![](_page_0_Figure_0.jpeg)

# <span id="page-1-0"></span>**Configuring the New Lima Art Scene**

This book examines the contemporary art world in Latin America from an anthropological perspective and recognises the recent reconfiguration of Lima's art scene.

Giuliana Borea traces the practices of artists, curators, collectors, art dealers and museums, identifying three key moments in this reconfiguration of contemporary art in Lima: artistic explorations and new curatorial narratives; museum reinforcement and the strengthening of Latin American art networks; and the rise of the art market. In so doing, Borea highlights the different actors that come into play in activating and de-activating directions and imaginations. The book exposes the practices of the local, the global, indigeneity and politics in the arts, and reveals that the strengthening of the Lima art scene has fostered the expansion of dominant art views and formats mobilised by transnational elite actors.

Featuring analytical chapters interspersed with personal stories, Borea's book presents an in-depth analysis of a specific art scene to open up a new way of understanding contemporary art practices in relation to globalisation, neoliberalism and the city.

**Giuliana Borea** is a Lecturer of Anthropology at the Pontificia Universidad Católica del Perú and a Marie Curie Postdoctoral Fellow in the Department of Sociology at the University of Essex, UK.

# <span id="page-10-0"></span>**[Acknowledgements](#page-7-1)**

This book draws from collaborations and expertise of several people over the years. My greatest gratitude to the artists, curators, museum directors and personnel, art collectors, critics, art dealers and other cultural workers who have shared their experiences, perspectives, dreams, archives and work with me, and whose names appear throughout the following pages My gratitude to all those who I have not mentioned in this book but whose work inspire my interest in the arts. My special thanks to Fernando Bryce, Natalia Iguiñiz, Rember Yahuarcani and Elena Damiani for our fascinating exchanges of ideas, which constitute specific sections in the book. I am also deeply grateful for rich conversations with Alfredo Márquez, Jorge Villacorta, Rosario Orjeda, Gustavo Buntinx, Claudia Coca, Giuseppe de Bernardi, Christian Bendayán, Lucia de la Puente, Miguel López, Christians Luna, Milagros de la Torre, Rochi del Castillo, Cecilia Gonzales, Ramiro Llona, Jose Carlos Mariátegui, Natalia Majluf, Carlos Marsano, Diego Costa Peuser, and Alejandro Zaia.

My research has been enriched in dialogue to art works, and particularly from those focusing on the art scene: my special thanks go to Juan Salas, Sandra Gamarra, Christians Luna, Pablo Ravina, Susana Torres, Claudia Coca, José Carlos Martinat, Alberto Borea, Marcel Velaochaga, Miguel Aguirre, Huanchaco, Primitivo, Valeriana and Venuca Evanán, Valeria Ghezzi, Brus Rubio, Eduardo Hirose and Rocio Gómez, whose work has contributed to the visual-curatorial section. I hope those who have collaborated on this book find their voice and work reflected accurately. Quotes from my informants and the Spanish bibliography are my own translations, and the presentation of the information and the analysis provided in this book is my responsibility.

This book juxtaposes research, projects and thoughts that I developed over more than a decade. These ideas expanded in new ways as I pursued my PhD in Anthropology at New York University. I owe my deepest debt to my PhD advisor, Fred Myers, from who I received the best of mentorship. His insightful suggestions and interest on my work have been a constant motivation. I am also indebted to other members of my doctoral committee. All my thanks to Arlene Dávila for her inspiring work on culture, the arts, neoliberalism and race, and her generous support in commenting on an early draft of this work. My gratitude goes also to Thomas Abercrombie, who constantly reminded me to look at history and ethnicity – he is no longer in this world, but his teachings remain with us. I am enormously grateful for the generosity of Edward Sullivan in providing me with a regional historical perspective on arts, and for his optimism about how my research speaks to art history. My work, and specially its methodology, has also benefited from work and conversations with George Marcus. I appreciate his fascinating participation at a conference that I organised in Lima during my fieldwork and his insightful advice.

The research for this book was supported by the Tinker Foundation Summer Research Grant from the NYU Center for Latin American Studies and a Dissertation Fieldwork Grant from the Wenner-Gren Foundation, which enabled me to pursue the multi-sited fieldwork. I also very thankful for the Stipendiary Visiting Fellowship from the Institute of Latin American Studies of the School of Advanced Study at the University of London, which gave me the academic environment I needed to turn my dissertation into a book: special thanks to ILAS's Director Linda Newson and to Mark Thurner.

My thanks to the Anthropology Department and the Master's Program of Visual Anthropology at the Pontificia Universidad Católica del Perú (PUCP), which have been key in the development of my ideas. I am particularly indebted to the students on my courses, Sociology and Anthropology of Art, and Approaches and Encounters: Anthropology and Contemporary Art Practices. My special gratitude to my students, now my colleagues, whose theses on art I supervised, particularly to Mijail Mitrovic, Carlos Zevallos and Jorge Juárez, and whose dissertations and publications have enriched my work. My thanks to the Department of Anthropology for facilitating my participation at various conferences where I received helpful feedback.

I am very much indebted to John Earls. My interest on interdisciplinarity and interconnecting systems derives from his brilliant way of thinking outside the box by bringing together multiple factors. My recognition also goes to Alfonso Castrillón, whose research and work in art and museums and his attitude towards the art circuit, have always inspired me. I want to thank my friends and colleagues Gisela Cánepa and Raul Castro for our various conversations on cities and neoliberalism, and to Guillermo Salas and Juan Carlos Callirgos for their insights in issues of race. I am also in gratitude with art historian Gabriela Germaná, with whom I have a constant dialogue about rethinking art categories and practices. My very great thanks to my friend and colleague Lee Douglas for her anthropological advice throughout the writing of this book.

David Flores-Hora, Diana Guerra and Santiago Alfaro have repeatedly helped me with specific data on Peru's arts and cultural policies: I am very thankful to them. I am also grateful to Luis Guillermo Lumbreras, who gave me the opportunity to work at the National Institute of Culture, providing me with a privileged view of Peru's cultural policies, and to the Instituto de Arte Contemporáneo, and especially George Gruenberg, for giving me the opportunity to coordinate the MAC-Lima, which was an important experience for the development of my thinking on this project. I also appreciate the help of the Museo de Arte de Lima, particularly that of Natalia Majluf and Sharon Lerner.

### xii *Acknowledgements*

I wish to extend my thanks to the anonymous reviewers who made generous comments on the book manuscript; their insightful suggestions have helped with the final strengthening of the book. My thanks also go to the editorial team of Routledge, specially to Katherine Ong, my editor, and her assistant Kangan Gupta for helping to see this book finally in print. # <span id="page-13-0"></span>**[Introduction](#page-7-2)**

My three-year-old daughter took out her toy camera, stepped back and faced the big tiger hanging from the ceiling, then moved closer towards some Hello Kitty figures hanging on a wall. We walked on, and after some meters she sat on the floor to look at herself in a big painted mirror. As she made her explorations I was making my own asking art dealers about their participation in and expectations of the new art fairs in Lima. A Brazilian gallery owner told me: 'We came to see and be seen, to introduce our artists and position ourselves. Now Sao Paulo, Buenos Aires, Bogota and Lima are regarding each other. Before we only looked at London and the US. Today it's easier to travel. Ten years ago we didn't cross the Andes'. The reference to 'regarding/looking at each other' [mirarnos] was constantly repeated. 'Who's curious about how we look at each other is Tate, MoMA', an enthusiastic speaker at an art fair said. The *mirarnos* reference not only was announcing the active work of seeing, but also pointing out a regional scale of connections and aspirations, and showing the directionality of actions. It was announcing processes of change. As my daughter adjusted her body in relationship to those objects as well as to see her image in that mirror, this *mirarnos* indicates the active recalibration of art agents' various strategies at specific moments and in specific scenarios.

This book is about the formation of the contemporary art scene in Peru's capital and its mechanisms of articulation over the last 20 years. It argues that the transformation of Lima's art scene needs to be traced in relation to Peru's social renewal following the end of Fujimori's regime; to Lima's neoliberal transformation and recasting as a creative city; and to the strengthening of Latin American art networks and changes in the larger art world. It is at this intersection of political economy and transnational articulations that the book locates its analysis. With a focus on actors' strategies across multi-scale arenas, it specifically highlights how Lima's contemporary art scene is shaped at local, regional and mainstream sites and networks. Consequently, this book draws on anthropological studies on transnational connections and, taking a critical approach, brings together literature on emerging art worlds and the repositioning of Latin American art with anthropological and urban scholarship on art, cities and neoliberalism. While these two bodies of literature address the art and its roles today, their discussions are limited to different fields and scales of analysis. Through in-depth examination of a <span id="page-14-0"></span>specific case, this book contributes to show the ways in which two projects, 'the global art world' and 'the creative city', closely articulate and fuel a particular organisation of the arts.

In arguing that the configuration of Lima's contemporary art scene started towards the end of Fujimori's dictatorship and was consolidated by the remaking of Lima as a creative city, I locate my analysis between 1997 and 2014. During this recent period this book contributes by identifying three particular moments. The first, from the late 1990s to 2006, comprised the launch of the Lima Biennial in 1997 (cancelled after three editions) as part of municipal neoliberal urban policy and the turn of art practices to deal with socio-political concerns at the end of Fujimori's dictatorship. These two mechanisms, with different directionalities, began to reconfigure Lima's art world. The time of the transition and early democracy was an effervescent period during which artists embarked on inter media explorations and collaborations, the figure of the curator with a local agenda was consolidated in Lima, and the limits of contemporary art expanded to include indigenous art. During this time neither the museum nor the market played important roles. The second moment, from 2006 to 2013, was marked by the debate on the museum and the new role of the Museo de Arte de Lima in contemporary art, articulating – partially – the production of new art and narratives. The younger elite generation leading the Museo de Arte de Lima boosted the figure of the collector and pushed their agenda to connect Peruvian art to the larger art world, as well as fuelling the art market. Peruvian collectors and other Latin American agents articulated and acted in leading art nodes, reinforcing the region. The third moment, from 2010 is characterised by the rapid growth of an art market interwoven with the consolidation of Lima as a creative city. The rise of Lima's art market peaked with the simultaneous launch of two art fairs, uncovering dramatic confrontations between a new Peruvian elite and international promoters competing to manage the Lima art market.

By identifying these three moments that can be summarised as of artistic and curatorial explorations; of institutionalisation and regional strengthening, of market growth and market-driven logics, this book illuminates the changing notions and practices of the local, the global, the social and indigeneity, and sheds light on when, by whom, and how different articulations have activated and deactivated, at least momentarily, directions and imaginations. Issues of class, race, gender and generation are brought to light as I look at actors' practices and aspirations. I show how contemporary art and art agents enabled an arena for intercultural agendas during the first half of the 2000s when art was not on the city's radar, becoming gradually a means of urban gentrification and a vehicle for art agents' transnational participation in international art venues and networks of power. I show the mechanisms by which these agents fuel Lima as an art node and promote Peruvian art abroad, and conclude that the strengthening of Lima's art scene and its articulation into larger art circuits has fostered not the spread of new art values and forms but rather the connection and expansion of dominant art views and prototypes through grounded articulations mobilised by transnational elite actors. Moreover, I argue that we are witnessing the overlap, friction and assemblage <span id="page-15-0"></span>of large programmes such as the 'global art world' and 'the creative city' in the management of art, both of which fuel market logics and engage with leading standards and values.

Exploring the Peruvian case through processes and actors, this book also looks more widely at the mechanisms of the 'contemporary' and reveals the possibilities and paradoxes of participation and of today's politics of being 'on the art map'. My intention is to raise broader questions about the processes of the configuration of the contemporary art scenes and the projects, actors and formats that these arrangements foster.

# **Locating the analysis: Processes, arenas, articulations and dreams**

The art world today, especially its art markets and the 'emerging' art scenes, receives enormous journalistic and scholar multidisciplinary attention. Millionaire prices at auctions, the proliferation of art fairs all over the world and growing financial investment in artworks are among the factors that have boosted this exponential interest in the market. Scholars have investigated how art dealers set prices for contemporary art works in Western art markets (Velthuis 2007), the importance of branding and publicity as tactics for raising value (Thompson 2008; Horowitz 2011), and the most influential art world with an emphasis on the art market (Thornton 2008). There has been a growing interest in emerging art markets such as Olav Velthuis's BRIC project and Iain Robertson's (2011) publication on the Asian art market. In addition, scholars, curators and museum projects have introduced topographical metaphors such as 'new geographies of art', 'the global art map', and 'new art worlds' to describe connections to and recognition of alternative art worlds. 'Emerging' art scenes are receiving increasing attention related to the aim of decentring the dominant art world. But questions arise such as: Who are the 'cartographers' who draw this map and its geography? What forms of art and which agents are located on this map? While scholars have announced 'the rise of new art worlds' in the making of the global contemporary art (Belting et al. 2013), and that contemporary art today 'comes *from* the whole world' (Smith 2011: 9), providing an overview of different processes at various locations, this book contributes with a detailed ethnography of the reconfiguring of an art scene. Its grounded approach raises concerns on how today's market overexposure tends to cancel out previous processes and how the narratives of the newness of geographies of art tend to overshadow those alter art worlds' longer histories. I look critically at the celebration of the booming Peruvian and Latin American art world and the Lima art scene, teasing out regional art agents' networks and agendas. The art scene that I discuss stands not on the Euro-American art world's awareness of it but on a set of processes of which that awareness, while crucial, is just one aspect of the factors that make up this art scene. My aim is to underscore these processes and show how multiple actors in tangible arenas interplay with, confront and participate in reinforcing and modifying the art scenes.

<span id="page-16-2"></span>I approach my analysis from an anthropological perspective. I draw on a growing anthropological discussion that has emerged since Marcus and Myers's *The Traffic in Culture* (1995) exhorted anthropologists to analyse how Western and contemporary art worlds operate – in a turn of the anthropological gaze. These authors also call for a critical anthropology of art that rethink the relationship between art and anthropology. Their ground-breaking volume contributed to broader anthropological studies of art by prompting scholarship on affinities – and practices – between art, anthropology and curatorship (e.g. Schneider and Wright 2006, 2010, 2013; Andrade 2007, 2017; Marcus 2010; Sansi 2015; Elhaik 2016; Borea 2017b, Schneider 2017[1](#page-30-0) ) and on the mechanisms of contemporary art worlds (e.g. Myers 2002; Winegar 2006; Fillitz and Van der Grijp 2018). My book situates in this second line of research, but as an ethnography of contemporary art it also tackles the links of art and anthropology and social concerns.

<span id="page-16-0"></span>By looking at once at the peripheral art scene, this book is in productive dialogue with Jessica Winegar's (2006) ethnography of the Egyptian art world. Her book is about the making of art narratives and value during a period of social, economic and political transformation in contemporary Egypt. She explains how people working in the arts produce versions of modernity and modern art by engaging in universalist discourses and offering their own versions of authenticity, progress and generation, showing the crucial role that the Egyptian state plays in the definition of this art sphere. Like Winegar, I discuss the transformation of an art world in the process of political and neoliberal changes, emphasising on the agents' practices and versions of the contemporary. Yet I show how the notion of the contemporary in Lima develops in relation not to narratives of authenticity but to other sets of discourses and transnational practices, such as on narratives of the political. Furthermore, this book focuses on an art scene in the capital city of highly centralised Peru, whose cultural work has been divided between elite groups and the state, with scant participation of the latter in the national politics of art. This book also resonates with Thomas Fillitz and Paul van der Grijp's more recent volume, *An Anthropology of Contemporary Art: Practices, Markets and Collectors* (2018), in which the editors bring together contributions that explore several components that organise the contemporary art world. Chapters explore the art markets, biennials and participatory art in different areas of the world; of particular interest for this study is Dayana Zdebsky de Cordova's analysis of Brazil's booming art market and the role play by the rhetoric in reinforcing this boom. One of the contributions of my monograph is to explain the processes and forms of interplay of the different components that shape the Lima contemporary art scene, allowing to underscore moments of reconfiguration, art players, multiscale arenas and networks of class and power throughout its making, practices and policies, and from there to question postulates of the global diverse art world.

<span id="page-16-1"></span>My anthropological approach also draws on a corpus of knowledge produced in Latin America during the 1970s and the early 1980s, known as the Social Theory of Art.[2](#page-30-1) It addressed a social understanding of art by questioning the emphasis on production over the 'system of art' (Acha 1981; 1992) and became crucial to understanding the relation of power between the Latin American and <span id="page-17-0"></span>Euro-American art worlds. I relate to the call of this work in considering the socio-historical processes, power relations and agents embedded in the production, distribution and consumption of art, with a distance on monolithic divisions of the West and the others. Much of this work is located at the intersection of theory and cultural policies, which I see as a tendency in much Latin American scholarship that needs to be addressed as a way of producing knowledge and not as a lack of art history as has been suggested (see Elkins 2007: 5). My work stands on this analytical-political commitment.

While Bourdieu's conceptualisation of the 'field of cultural production' (1993) remains important in my discussion, I engage with it critically. Bourdieu's 'field' has the virtue of allowing us to think about the unstable borders of the field and the multiple tactics used to define it. Yet, as Fred Myers (2013) explains, it is too coherent for an understanding of objects and agents' circulation across different regimes of value which is messier and more chaotic, and in which competing strategies are undertaken not only within the nation and among a group of actors, but also through transnational and multi-ethnic agents. This book pays attention to how issues of class, race and gender play out in local and transnational networks (Ong 1999), and adds analysis through its focus not on the limits of the field but on the various processes, events and modes of articulation involve in making and shaping an art scene. Within and through these events and articulations, struggles unfold and borders are produced, but they are produced less as the limits of a bounded place and closer to multiple arenas and articulations. In this sense, this work is in close dialogue with Victor Turner's (1974: 13–17) concept of 'arena', which he defines as intermediate, concrete tangible settings in which paradigms and values of the 'field', are mobilised, and 'social actions' take place as situations of performance and contestations; and it also follows James Clifford's (2001) understanding of articulations as a set of political processes and manoeuvres of connection and disconnections – exclusions – across the local, the regional, the national and the global. In this book, I look at the interplay of arenas on different scales, and at the multi-scale practices that configure Lima's art scene. By focusing on arenas my aim is to present the texture, the atmosphere, the aesthetics and the materiality in and through the projects and articulations have been produced and engrained, while capturing the ways in which these projects and practices have also shaped those arenas. I look at moments, settings and modes of articulation in order to focus on the configuration and transformation of the Lima art scene. I show specific tactics and trajectories but also dreams and aspirations, following Arjun Appadurai (2000), who announces the centrality of the role of the imagination as an engine for people and groups to act in the global world. I also reflect on my position and how I myself act across arenas. As the reader will note, I have witnessed many of these transformations at first hand and participated in some of them from different positions on Lima's art scene.

In my approach to actors and articulations, this book situates critically to Bruno Latour's perspective on actor network analysis. For Latour (1996; 2004), the ways in which actants (human and non-human) explain, connect and frame their acts and understandings should channel the analysis. From this perspective, <span id="page-18-1"></span><span id="page-18-0"></span>small/large, micro/macro, bottom/top and inside/outside distinctions dissolve. What matters to Latour are the scales and connections given by the agents avoiding ideas of context and affirming that 'in-between' networks does not exist.[3](#page-30-2) I also take issue with the idea of context as an overarching background that limits the understanding of the networks' and arts' capacity to act and their agency to create social relations and situations (Gell 1998; Giunta 2014). Keeping proximity to actors enables us to trace, for instance, twists, joins, textures, different rhythms and explanations that we will not estimate (Latour 1996; 2004), but I also make visible the joins, disjunctures and overlapping projects that actors have not always estimated (Rabinow and Marcus 2008: 70). Following Rabinow and Marcus (2008), I examine and make visible a dynamic grid of connections composed of overlapping different projects as well as by agents' practices and emotions as 'inseparable aspects' (Bateson 1958: 3). The articulations that I announce are not intended to show a whole or a complete story, nor Lima's art scene as a totality; rather, my purpose is to expose, through the concrete processes and work, rather than in abstract enunciations and overarching models, the mechanisms of the construction and articulation of the dominant art scene in Lima in its 'irregular shape' (Appadurai 2000: 33).

When I talk in this book about social and global articulations and networks these are situated articulations. This work connects with literature that exhorts us to see how global work (Sassen 2000, 2002), processes of neoliberalisation (Peck et al. 2009) and universal categories engage, friction and expand (Tsing 2005) 'through a range of contextually specific, conjunctural struggles' (Peck et al. 2009: 110). These concrete forms of articulations allow us to underscore the differential processes and actors at play, and their different aspirations. Anthropologists (Ong and Collier 2004; Marcus and Saka 2006) call us to understand the 'global' through specific, situated, heterogeneous, shifting and unstable global articulations, and point out that along with heterogeneous connections there are multiple disjunctures (Appadurai 1990, 2013; Castells 1996). In terms of the arts, vast geographies and people's art practices are out of play from today's 'new art worlds'. I show how there are multiple filters in these local/regional/global arenas that operate in ways of being visible and participate on the new art map. Although I use the notion of globality, my work stands in critical relation to it. Scholars have already relativised and provincialised the West. In the same way, we need to relativise 'the global' and the notions of 'global art history', 'global museums' and 'global art markets' in order to unveil the production of power under conditions and reiterations of globality.

# **Contemporary art, social articulations and the place of anthropology**

I approach the category of 'art', and more precisely of 'contemporary art', as – following Fred Myers (2002) – 'signifying practice' through which projects, processes and products become 'contemporary art'. My own understanding of art follows Howard Morphy's enterprise (2007: 3–4) of a 'cross-cultural definition' <span id="page-19-0"></span>of art, and Ella Shohat and Robert Stam's notion of 'polycentric aesthetics' (2002). However, this book looks at what the actors, institutions and politics on Peru's art scene and abroad understand, mobilise and value as 'contemporary art'.

As the art historian Terry Smith suggests easier than define what contemporary art is, is 'to ask how we arrive to it' (2011: 9). With a focus on Latin America, art historian Andrea Giunta argues in her book *When Does Contemporary Art Begin?* that some of the first symptoms of contemporary art 'is when the real world barges into the artworks' world' (2014:110) and exceeds the artwork format, with the change in the spectator to a growing demand for participation and the engagement of art with more radical political agendas. According to Giunta (2014: 213–14), contemporary art in Latin America began to take form in the 1960s in response to dictatorial regimes and was consolidated in the 1990s by the expansion of biennials, the consequences of the Washington Consensus and the emergence of the internet and technological changes. Giunta identifies that among the strains that contemporary art from Latin America addresses are the unresolved character of history and issues of memory, work on reiterations provided by the internet and technology, and reflections on cartography and the experience of living in big cities. In this case, Giunta's work correlates with Terry Smith's (2011) identification of three main strains, among the many diverse contemporary art practices today. Smith identifies how first-world countries' leading artists tend to address the sensational or go back to modernist aesthetics; that artists in thirdworld countries, tend to produce a postcolonial art that approaches the topics of identity, history and globalisation; and that the younger generation of artists investigate place, time, mediation and ethics in small and more local projects. Smith claims, 'Contemporary art is – perhaps for the first time in history – truly an art *of* the world. It comes *from* the whole world', 'and tries to imagine the world *as a whole*" (2011: 8; 13, italics in original). However, the question is what these main strains of doing contemporary art imply and do in the longer term. What are the consequences of expecting that artworks coming from postcolonial countries need to grasp 'social' issues? The question is whether the processes that Giunta describes have become today formulas for being 'contemporary'.

The shift of some art towards the social, the cultural and the political has received important scholarly attention (Foster 1995; Bourriaud 2002; García Canclini 2010; Bishop 2012; Sansi 2015; Kelley and Kester 2017). Claire Bishop has traced the interrelation of the arts with the social in Western Europe. One question this raises is when and how the engagement of art with the social and the cultural has taken place in other specific locations. Covering a period similar to that of this book, Bill Kelley and Grant Kester's volume *Collective Situations: Readings in Contemporary Latin American Art, 1995–2010* (2017) focuses on collaborative, socially engaged art practices in Latin America. The volume highlights a vibrant art practice across Latin America which, they argue, is executed not for the mainstream art world but for other circuits, establishing different relations with society. In the case of Peru the book focuses on Colectivo Sociedad Civil with Gustavo Buntinx's article. My book explores the characteristics of the latest turn –there other previous ones – in Peruvian art to engage with social <span id="page-20-0"></span>issues and the paradoxes that this brings today. It highlights the various collective groups that acted in the recovery of democracy and explains the moment and processes that triggered both socially engaged art practices and the rise of institutional-market oriented artists, arguing that the development of such art practices are not always fully divergent. In the time frame of my analysis I identify two different periods – one during the collapse of the dictatorial regime and the effervescent art explorations of the new democratic period, and the other the period since 2008 involving more dynamic global interrelations in which dealing with politics and the local becomes an asset of validation. This is particularly true in Latin America, which has tended to be situated in the Euro-American imaginary as a site of revolution and as a place for finding and showing 'the politics'. I argue that there is a mobilisation of the political without politics, and with this there is a new role for anthropology in contemporary art that is related not only to aspects of methodology (e.g. Schneider and Wright 2006, 2010, 2013) or the conceptual (Sansi 2015, Borea 2017b) but also to validation of art. The fast relocation of anthropology in the art world, from its link to exoticism and 'primitive art' or '*arte popular*' and its consequent expulsion from the discussion of 'the arts', to being asked today to comment on contemporary art practices is a shift that begs more critical reviewing and awareness. I also add an analysis of indigenous contemporary art that disturb the art strains mentioned above, and that today has become the new interest of top museums and local art platforms, which I reflect upon in my epilogue.

# **Creative cities: The role of art on neoliberal agendas**

There is a growing body of literature (Ciccolella and Mignaqui 2002; Dávila 2016; Boano and Vergara 2017) that looks at Latin American cities and their strategies for global connection and the transformation of the urban space through neoliberal projects. This literature on the Peruvian case is also increasing with an important focus on uses of and battles for the public space (Ludeña 2002, 2004; De Mattos and Ludeña 2011) and the changes in urban subjectivities (Cánepa and Lossio 2019). This book contributes to these studies by locating the Lima art world as the focus of analysis through which I trace the unfolding of the neoliberal economy with its transformation of space and subjectivities, and the regionally and globally relocation of Lima.

As scholars have demonstrated, culture, and particularly art, plays an important role in the global economy and neoliberal spatial renewal. The value of culture in the form of creative industries is organised in an instrumentalised way: culture is called to contribute to the economy, generate jobs and promote multicultural rights and social inclusion. As George Yúdice (2003) and Arlene Dávila (2012) argue, this expanded role of culture is a central neoliberal strategy. The civilising aura of art and its new social role facilitates the urban expansion of art spaces that inject new value into an area and its surroundings and tend to displace poor people and their practices to marginal zones. In Lima, this expansion is tangible in the Barranco district, as I discuss in [Chapter 6,](#page-201-1) and in the process of gentrification <span id="page-21-0"></span>through art taking place in an impoverished area of El Callao. In this context, Matteo Pasquinelli denounces 'creative cities' as a chimera in which 'the mask of culture is used to cover the hydra of concrete and real-estate speculation' (2008: 126). Sharon Zukin (1995) and Miriam Greenberg's (2008) studies show how the social and economic power of elites is materialised in the organisation and visibility of cities through public art and architectural projects, which at the same time enable cities to increase their symbolic and economic value and frame themselves as entrepreneurial and creative. In [Chapters 2](#page-65-1) and [6,](#page-201-1) I discuss how the making of the global Lima through real estate projects has induced a reorientation of the city towards the sea that favours a new central axis composed of San Isidro (the financial district), Miraflores and Barranco, leading to various processes of gentrification. This axis is also the landscape in which the dominant art scene operates at the same time that shapes it. I show how discourses on the arts mobilise projects based on notions of 'lack', for instance of art museums and art fairs, and on private agents' 'gifts' to the city. These lacks and gifts are ultimately narratives and aspirations based on a set of mandates for a city to become a modernising, neoliberal, city.

Multiculturality is a key component of global cities. The ways in which societies perceive cultural diversity, and their urban policies addressing it, affect the ways in which global processes and neoliberal practices develop in specific arenas and influence the ways in which the capacity for innovation is understood and studied. In Western global cities, the terms 'innovation' and 'creativity' have generally been conferred on scientists, entrepreneurs and artists in relation to practices promoted by the cities' elites (see Florida 2002; Currid 2007). Yet scholars have noted that although service workers, migrant people (e.g. Castells 1996; Hannerz 1996), and grassroots artists (Dávila 2012) are excluded from the creative discourse and have minimum labour rights, they are equally important in global urban life. However, in Latin America, the relationship between cultural diversity and global urban processes is seen differently (García Canclini 1995; Yúdice 2003). Existing studies do not restrict creativity to the professional managerial class and artists. In contrast, it is explored looking mainly at what has been called 'popular culture' and its actors – even though ultimately this has been appropriated and mobilised by the upper middle class and academics.

Over the period covered by this study I show three main projects and agendas through which cultural and social diversity have been perceived and managed: hybridisation, interculturality and creativity, briefly described here. Studies of globalisation in Latin America during the late 1980s and 1990s approached urban globalising processes with a focus on consumption, the media and identities, and examined people's manoeuvres through the global market (Barbero 1987; García Canclini 1989, 1995; Ortiz 1999). These works question the static explanations of identity promoted by nationalist governments and the dichotomies of imperialism, exhorting us to see identity as multifaceted (see Yúdice 2003: 88–93). In this context, Nestor García Canclini (1989) contributes with the notion of 'hybridisation', understood as creative and selective processes through which individuals and groups from peasant immigrants to elites combine structures, objects and <span id="page-22-0"></span>practices, in response to new conditions of production and the market (García Canclini 2001: iii–iv).This perspective highlights the transits and juxtaposition of 'tradition' and 'modernity' and the coexistence of different logics of development and 'multiple temporalities'. The so-called 'popular culture' has received considerable attention from this perspective.

The other project to be consolidated by the end of the 1990s and early 2000s was that of interculturality, addressing issues of citizenship, public policies with an emphasis on education policy, and decentralisation. It drew close to the agendas of indigenous movements in Latin America claiming an equal right to citizenship and the right to difference. Many promoters of interculturality mark its difference from multiculturality, promoted, for instance, in the United States. The influential Peruvian anthropologist, Carlos Iván Degregori (2002: 109), explains: 'For multiculturalism, tolerance is enough. Interculturality requires dialogue. I would add affective dialogue'. According to Fidel Tubino (2002: 73), interculturality needs to be understood 'as an ethic-political proposal, as a must-be-possible, as a feasible utopia'. During the early 2000s, in the transitional period between the dictatorship and democracy marked by the hope of creating a new society, several works (e.g. Degregori 2000; Fuller 2002) were published on this topic.

The agenda of interculturality continues: however, the main project through which diversity is perceived and managed today is Peru's national branding policy and creative industries. As scholars note, branding plays an important role in mediating the perception of localities and ethnicities (Greenberg 2008; Comaroff and Comaroff 2009; Dávila 2001). It can shape and reanimate people's cultural identity or even turn the brand into an identity, as Gisela Cánepa (2014) argues for *Marca Peru*. In this book I show the transition of the image of Lima from a *criollo* white colonial city to its new presentation as a contemporary culturally and socially diverse city, alongside the new social construction of feeling/ being '*cholo*' and its promotion by the government. This transition has taken place through the fostering of different cultural industries – gastronomy, fashion, music and art – and the highlighting of creativity and the contemporary. Thus, the interrelation of the transformation of the contemporary art scene in Lima and the making and reframing of Lima as a modernising global creative city is central to my argument.

# **The management of art and culture: Class, race and centralisation**

In Peru there has long been a division between elite groups and the state in the management of culture (Borea 2006, 2017a). This division responds, on the one hand, to the Peruvian government's management of national identity understood in its relation to the past and its 'traditional' legacy, and on the other to elite appropriation of art management as symbolic capital (Bourdieu 1984). This division crystallised between 1930 and 1950 when the Peruvian art field became organised in a system divided structurally between 'high art' and 'popular art' (*arte popular*), with the promotion of 'high art' since then in private and elite <span id="page-23-0"></span>groups' hands through the Institute of Contemporary Art (1954) and the Patronato de las Artes (1954), which created the Museo de Arte de Lima in 1961; and the promotion of 'arte popular' through the Museo de la Cultura Peruana founded by the government in 1946. This separation between 'high art' and '*arte popular*' is not only in terms of categories and channels of circulation, but also is a division that crosses issues of class and race. My analysis of the Lima art scene shows what artists, curators, art dealers, collectors and art promoters do, as well as who they are in Peru and Lima's social spectrum. The mediations of art and the building of networks, and moreover transnational networks, cannot be discussed only as art narratives or strategic practices but must include who these actors are in terms of class, race and gender.

Perhaps the most revealing study of Peruvian elites is Julio Cotler's *Clases, Estado y Nación en el Perú* (Class, State and Nation in Peru, 1978), which describes the roles and tensions of the Peruvian bourgeoisie concerning the control of power. Cotler shows the confrontations among traditional landowners and the commercial elite group that emerged in the Guano era in the 1840s. The latter group would lead governmental policy and make the state its political instrument (Cotler 1978: 128). Cotler's socio-historical analysis extends to the 1970s. Analysing current globalising times, William Robinson's *Latin America and Global Capitalism* explains the emergence of a transnational capital class as a continuation of elite families:

A dynamic new breed of transnationally oriented entrepreneurs emerged with the opening to the global economy … the general pattern with regard to the rise of this class group appears to be as follows: (i) it is concentrated in finances, telecommunications, retail, commercial, and other services, along with nontradition export activity, precisely those sectors most associated with the global economy; (ii) the new transnational capitalist groups are not selfmade. The leading TCC [transnational capital class] family networks date their fortunes and class status to the late nineteenth and early twentieth century as capitalist developed in Latin America. But they underwent a qualitative transformation in the 1980s and 1990s, experiencing an unprecedented windfall in the amassing of wealth and power, propelled by privatizations and other opportunities opened up by neoliberal globalization, including new types of access to the world market and forms of association with extraregional transnational capital.

(Robinson 2008: 171–172)

The mechanisms by which elite groups make the Peruvian state its political instrument have recently been analysed in Crabtree and Durand's *Peru: Elite Power and Political Capture* (2017). The authors explain the influence of economic elites in state decision-making on specific governmental offices, particularly those related to economic issues. In this book I contribute to these studies by showing how the state has relinquished the management of art to elite groups, and how the latter manage the arts and its politics. I explain the elites' different roles in leading <span id="page-24-1"></span>Lima's art museums ([Chapter 4\)](#page-123-1) and their participation in fostering Lima's art scene and Peruvian art through their transnational connections [\(Chapter 5\)](#page-171-1). I place the construction of art networks and networks of power at the centre of the discussion, and look at the ways in which art provides Peruvian agents with the possibility of acting as a transnational cultural class (Ong 1999, Robinson 2008). In addition, I show the tensions between generations of the traditional elite, and between transtional art agents and more local elite groups regarding the management of contemporary art.

This book also illuminates the different ways in which indigenous art (Amazonian and Andean art and agents) as well as '*chicha*' and '*cholo*' art and agents participate in the configuration of the contemporary, and the tensions involved. These problematic categories of 'indigenous' and '*cholo*' require explanation. As many scholars have pointed out, in Latin America issues of race intersects with class; and as Marisol de la Cadena (2004) explains the categories, for instance, of *indio*, *mestizo* and '*gente decente*' (decent people) are not fixed but arenas of dispute (see also Poole 2000). Andean and coastal indigenous people have called themselves, and have been called, 'indian', 'indigenous', 'peasant' and '*runa*' (people), depending on the historical and political context, their interlocutors and their claims. Amazonian people have called themselves, and been called at different times and contexts, 'natives', 'indigenous' and by the names of their ethnic groups. During large part of the 20th century, self-representation in the Andes has constructed over class terms and in the Amazon over ethnicity[.4](#page-30-3) Since the 1990s, with the reinforcement of native organisations at different levels through their claims to their territory and culture in interrelation with environmental politics and the International Labour Organization's Indigenous and Tribal Peoples Convention (C169 in 1989), the notion of 'indigenous' gained strength as a global political actor, opening channels to participate and obtain visibility. In Peru, Amazonian groups have been closer to these politics of 'indigeneity', and I explain how the emergence of Amazonian art as contemporary art interrelates with indigenous political claims, while Andean art has been attached to the idea of 'arte popular'. I want to clarify, however, that Amazonian and Andean art are not exclusively indigenous forms of art (Borea 2010), and moreover are produced not only in the Amazon and the Andes but also in other locations, such as Lima.

<span id="page-24-0"></span>*Cholo* is another problematic category constructed by the relationship between indigenous and urban settings, and particularly constructed from Lima (Cánepa 2007). While the genealogy of the term can be traced to colonial times, in the 20th century sociology and anthropology began to use it to refer to individuals or a social sector of the indigenous world that contacted with the 'modern' and the urban world. The *cholo* was understood as a liminal character who mediates between these worlds. In the 1960s and 1970s, sociological texts attempted to account for Peru's transformation from a traditional to a modern society. Aníbal Quijano's *Dominación y cultura: Lo cholo y el conflicto cultural del Perú* (Domination and Culture: The *cholo* and Peru's Cultural Conflict, 1980), written in the late 1960s, refers to the *cholo* as the new actor in this process of change and particularly within the context of Lima's urbanisation. Along this positive <span id="page-25-0"></span>view of the *cholo* from the academia, the same term has negative connotations in cities. While an *indio* generates a compassionate, yet paternalistic gaze, the *cholo* generates a sense of fear and threat: an indigenous person that is out of place and pollutes cities. However, it is not a stable category. In this book, and particularly in [Chapters 2](#page-65-1) and [3](#page-93-1), I explain how in the early 2000s a new social and empowered sentiment about being *cholo*, in interrelation with the expansion of the market, began to emerge and to transform Lima and its urban subjectivities. I highlight the different ways in which the *cholo* is included in art narratives and as an actor, as well as the problems that arise from scholars, artists and curators' exaltation of the notions of '*cholo*' and '*cultura popular*'.

I conclude this section with two further remarks. Peru is an extremely centralised country in terms of its population, one-third of which lives in Lima, and of its institutions and the administration of power, the art scene being no exception. Moreover, the lack of government participation in the arts at a national level has created an extreme centralisation of the mechanisms for the validation and visibility of art, augmented by the global up-scaling of Lima and the role that the arts plays in this. Although there are active art projects and smaller-scale art scenes in Ayacucho, Cusco, Iquitos, Ucayali, Trujillo and Arequipa as well as in different areas of Lima (Fioralisso 2016), my analysis focuses on the dominant art scene, which has mainly unfolded in Lima and on international platforms. Therefore, this book avoids representing Lima as synonymous with Peru, as it is often the case: this work is about the Lima art scene and its power and mechanisms to shaping the notion of what Peruvian art is. Finally, throughout these pages, I also highlight the gendered distribution of roles in Lima's contemporary art scene, in which women have predominantly assumed roles in the market and art management, while men have participated more in the curatorial and discursive arenas and as art collectors. This distribution shows some changes today, and I hope that my voice contributes in challenging readings and versions of an art scene that have been built around male-centric discourses while opening up more diverse explorations and debates.

# **An expanded fieldwork: Interventions, memories, visualisation and the sensorial**

A constant question that has appeared while I was researching for and writing this book has been how I can provide an anthropological perspective and voice in the study of a contemporary art scene. Of course there is no a standard way, and it has been complicated because, on the one hand, most of the available texts are written by the authoritative male voices of curators and journalists; on the other hand, I was actively involved in museum projects and cultural politics before embarking in this research. I realised that first I needed silence in order to get a critical distance from my own involvement in cultural policies. This, however, did not imply not acting in the field but rather doing it in a reflexive way by acknowledging how these different positionalities in different times and arenas were composing <span id="page-26-2"></span>my fieldwork *itself*. Thus, the research for this book was based on a multi-sited anthropological analysis in four dimensions.

First, the main fieldwork was conducted in Lima between 2010 and 2014, together with research in New York, Miami and Buenos Aires, as I followed actors, agendas and arenas. Second, this research was nourished by my experience, feelings and memories as I witnessed the transformation of Lima's art scene from three unique perspectives: those of my work in cultural policy; my teachings of anthropology of art to art students and anthropologists, and my curatorial projects. These experiences have expanded my fieldwork not only in space but also in time. Third, the research was also based on a series of interventions in the field, some planned and others the result of fieldwork practice. One of these 'contraptions', following George Marcus (2017) – one of the key speakers with Fred Myers – was the international conference Studies and Dialogues between Anthropology and Art that I organised in Lima on November 2014. The conference occurred at a key moment of growing reflection on the topic and a lack of events, especially in Spanish-speaking countries. The conference exceeded my aims and got its own dynamism (see Borea 2017b).[5](#page-30-4) For me, it allowed the bringing together of artists, anthropologists, art historians and philosophers from different locations – primarily but not exclusively from Latin America – and cultural traditions to debate, using different formats, on the studies and the relations between art and anthropology; and it amplified the aware of this relationship for larger audiences. Perhaps as consequence, different art agents invited me to participate in artistic events as a speaker or curator, highlighting their interest in the anthropological point of view. Thus my anthropological research was shaped not only through my strategic decisions and experiments but also by the strategic decisions of others, not only by allowing me to participate or interview them, but also by including me in their projects and invitations according to how they saw the role and value of anthropology and of anthropologists as concrete agents. My research has enriched profoundly for this interdisciplinary and intersectoral conversation.

<span id="page-26-1"></span><span id="page-26-0"></span>Finally, and fourth, my fieldwork research has also taken place in my office in interaction with my different forms of archives, notes, images and the space. My archives in their material forms and location have also been participants. My written, visual and audio archives not only allowed me to evoke fieldwork in a process of analysis, but through their re-assemblage at my office space they produced new connections, ideas and feelings enlarging fieldwork (Borea 2018)[.6](#page-30-5) I have systematically collected clippings about art, urban and cultural policies and indigenous representation, exhibition catalogues and pictures from indoor and outdoor exhibitions since 2000. This archive was my subjective starting point of information, which I have supplemented with further archive analysis. Arranging and rearranging these archives while I was in the process of researching and writing provided me with different visual-textual information about processes and connections. In organising and visualising the information as I traced agents, practices and processes I used two types of visual image: a chart, in which I organised the information as a timeline with flexible columns for local, regional, and larger arenas and events; and hand-drawn network diagrams connecting people, sites, objects <span id="page-27-1"></span>and processes which I stack on the walls. This process of connecting, drawing, juxtaposing and viewing allowed me to rethink my research at several points during the process, being aware of the turning points in my process of inquiry. In addition, my interviews have not only been a tool to collect information but also a site for the analysis – verbal, sensorial, emotional – where I and my interviewees, in several cases experts in the topic, built an arena for thinking together. Despite transcription is tedious work, I opted to make full transcriptions as a mechanism for thinking through writing and listening, and to inscribe the information and my own feelings as part of the process of making sense of them. I understand the interviews for this book as thick processes in themselves from which I sought to grasp the aesthetics of art stakeholders' narrations, circulations and engagements, understanding the form, emotions and meanings in their trajectories, dreams and frustrations.

In acting, tracing and thinking through this extended fieldwork in time and space, I have woven the narratives for this book. My narrative presents juxtapositions and detours, articulations and gaps, and different levels of analysis in my own text. With the slow process of grounded research, the intersection of different layers that cross my analysis and my positionality in the text, this book offers an anthropological perspective and voice amongst the ecology of other discourses and practices.

### **Chapters, trajectories and artworks**

<span id="page-27-0"></span>This book comprises six chapters, intercalated by four sections called 'Trajectories' and a visual-curatorial section called 'Artists on Art: Meta-reflection on the art scene'. Each format has its own temporality. In the chapters I move slowly through processes and practices. The first period, of art explorations and socio-political renewal, is traced in [Chapters 1](#page-31-1) to [3.](#page-93-1) The second period, of institutional articulation and regional networks, is tackled in [Chapters 4](#page-123-1) and [5.](#page-171-1) The third period, of the growth of the art market, is analysed in [Chapters 5](#page-171-1) and [6.](#page-201-1) [Chapters 2](#page-65-1) and [6 p](#page-201-1)lace emphasis on Lima's urban transformation and the role of art in this. Each Trajectory section covers the trajectory of one artist, adding and also confronting references in the chapters. In the visual section I work as a curator, bringing together the work of artists that reflect through their art practice on the art scene. Here I draft a short explanation of my perspective of this group of work, while each image displays its own information for the viewer to feel and reflect. Although this is a book about contemporary art and its organisation, I decided not to include images throughout the book; instead, these can be found but only in the 'Artist on Art' section. This decision responds to the atmospheres, flow and rhythm that I want the text to provide and to the role images play in this book. I refer to and describe many artworks and practices, and include the names of the artists and their work, so the interested reader can find them on the internet – I hope my narration generates that interest.[7](#page-30-6) The images included are of those art practices that research and explore the art scene, providing other textures and approaches to the topics that I address.

<span id="page-28-0"></span>[Chapter 1,](#page-31-1) 'Triggering change: The Lima biennial and the social re-engagement of art', starts by situating the reader in Fujimori's authoritative regime and Lima Mayor Alberto Andrade's whitewashing urban policy. The chapter focuses on two parallel mechanisms with different directionalities and aims that triggered the redefinition of the Lima art scene: the Lima Biennial and the demonstrations against Fujimori's dictatorship. I analyse the Biennial as a cultural technology of Andrade's neoliberal policy for the 'recovery of Lima' and as a means of the local art agents' aspirations of global articulation. While the Biennial is frequently discussed as a unity, in this chapter I follow its transformations and analyse how its calls started to configure 'the contemporary art' and who their agents would be. The chapter demonstrates how, in parallel with the indoors Biennial, a fight to recover democracy was taking place in the public space in which artists and indigenous leaders stood as political actors and triggered the shift from art practices centred on the self to those dealing with larger social questions.

[Chapter 2,](#page-65-1) 'Democratic effervescence: The renewal of Lima and artists' experimentations', analyses what I argue was a moment of explorations and new conditions for the redefinition of contemporary art. It examines how the return to democracy led to a period of effervescence that fostered the formation of new subjectivities based on the empowerment of '*lo cholo*'; mobilised interculturality agendas; and opened up an arena for experimentation and aspirations in the cultural field. This renovation played out alongside a growing neoliberal economy linked to transformation of the urban space. The chapter focuses on how artists' individual and collective initiatives fuelled this powerful period.

In [Chapter 3,](#page-93-1) 'Enlarging the contemporary: The rise of the curator and indigenous artists', I explore the processes of consolidation of the figure of the curator in conjunction with the modernisation of infrastructure for exhibitions and the transformation of the city. I explain how this first curatorial moment was primarily based on a local work in relation to new urban subjectivities expanding the limits of contemporary art. In this process, I show the mechanisms and agents that enabled the articulation of Amazonian art to definitions of contemporary art in Lima, and explain why Andean art has been largely marginalised and primarily categorised as '*arte popular*'. [Chapters 2](#page-65-1) and [3](#page-93-1) address the same moment of dynamic explorations on the art scene triggered by artists and soon after by curators. During this time, neither the museum nor the market played an important role.

[Chapter 4,](#page-123-1) 'Reinforcement of the museum: Debates and institutional articulations', investigates how the institution of the museum was strengthened by articulating the artistic explorations and vibrant atmosphere of the first period. It explains the intense debates that emerged regarding the notion and practices of a museum of contemporary art that took place, predominantly, in the blogs. The chapter explores the different competing museums' agendas on defining and promoting contemporary art, that of the Museum of Contemporary Art, the Museo de Arte de Lima, Museo de Arte de San Marco, Micromuseo and of the artistic museological project LiMAC, and argue that the image of the museological arena <span id="page-29-0"></span>has been built looking at large and small private museums but not at the intermediate scale and public managed museum. In the second part of this chapter, I focus on how the Museo de Arte de Lima's board located the museum as the main institutional actor and reinforced the figure of the art collector enabling elites to participate as transnational art agents in their aspirations to become global art players.

In [Chapter 5,](#page-171-1) 'Networking Latin America: Transnational actors and the new role of art collectors', I expand the focus of exploration to analyse the mechanisms of the connection of Lima's art scene with the Latin American region as well as with the United States and Europe. The chapter looks at how Latin American art agents' participation in leading art venues has grown since the mid-2000s, and focuses on the role of Peruvian stakeholders in locating Latin American and Peruvian art on larger art platforms. I explain that the networking of Latin America and its internationalisation of art is not a new enterprise and I define four key moments, extending the analysis of the last of these, which I describe as one of transnational participation. I show how this moment includes the position of Latin American collectors as trustees on leading museum's boards; the making of transnational curators; and the rise of art fairs in the region.

[Chapter 6,](#page-201-1) 'The celebration of culture: Creative Lima and the growth of the art market', explores the rise of an art market in Lima in close interrelation with the repackaging of Lima as a creative city and the new lifestyle. I explain how the notion of Lima as a creative city is consolidated through new cultural policies and rebranding at the national and municipal levels. In this new Lima, there is a growing place for culture and art that foster the aesthetics and rhetoric of 'diversity' and the 'political', but I argue that the previous political strength has diminished. I focus on how the new urban lifestyle in Lima promotes the rise of the consumption of art, trace the role of art galleries and their mechanisms of operation in the new Lima and transnational arenas, and analyse the simultaneous launch of two art fairs showing the confrontations between their organisers. I demonstrate how these art fairs respond to two overlapping but different projects, the 'creative city' and the 'global art world', and argue that both expand Western formats, elite power and market logics that wrap the contemporary art scene.

Intercalating the chapters, Trajectories are based on my conversations with the artists Fernando Bryce, Natalia Iguiñiz, Rember Yahuarcani and Elena Damiani. I selected these artists as they and their work have played important roles in the different processes I describe, and also I took into consideration issues of generation, gender, ethnicity and different forms of art practice. These sections add to and disrupt my analysis on chapters, and explore, beyond the artists' CVs and portfolios, the processes, connections and passions that move them to take the decisions and that shape their different achievements. Written in a conversational style, Trajectories are exchanges and make visible the collaborations in knowledgeproduction. Finally, in the visual section called 'Artists on art: Meta-reflections on the art scene', I work as a curator. Over the past 20 years, Peruvian artists have reflected with great intensity and anxiety on the Lima art scene and the art world at large, and some have made of these reflections their own art projects in a form <span id="page-30-7"></span>of visual para-ethnography. In this section, I group this body of work providing a third form of analysis.

I conclude that new connections and geographies of art do not mean new art values or epistemologies *per se*. Articulation with the larger art world requires the synchronisation of art worlds' ways of working, talking, showing and creating value in art, and the removal from the public eye of local battles that have worked in the reconfiguration of specific art scenes. However, as I show, this process involves the direct participation and dreams of Peruvian actors through their multi-scale strategies at local, regional and Euro-American levels. Over time, the arrangements that I discuss in this book gain different forms. What is at stake, however, is whether the larger programmes remain. In the following pages, I seek to grasp the processes, arenas, articulations and dreams that have fuelled the configuration of the Lima contemporary art scene, and from there to advance the debate on art worlds, globalisation, cities and neoliberalism.

# **Notes**

- <span id="page-30-0"></span>[1](#page-16-0) Schneider's and Borea's volumes, published in 2017, expand the voices of the debate on the relations of art and anthropology including, and from, Latin America.
- <span id="page-30-1"></span>[2](#page-16-1) Like the sociological studies of Howard Becker (1982, 2008) and Pierre Bourdieu (1984; 1993), the Social Theory of Art distances from the aura of art objects and artists to understand the phenomena of art in relation to the different agents and institutions that participate in the legitimation of art (Borea 2017b: 19–21).
- <span id="page-30-2"></span>[3](#page-18-0) Latour affirms that a 'great economy of thinking allowed by the notion of network is that we are no longer obliged to fill in the space between the connections' (1996:5).
- <span id="page-30-3"></span>[4](#page-24-0) In their study in Andean Peru, Bourque and Warren explain (1978) that nobody wanted to define themselves as indian/indigenous: their informants of different peasant communities told them that *indios* live further away from their community never finding someone that define as such. The emphasis on class rather than ethnicity was also an agenda on Juan Velasco Alvarado's government that changed the category of *comunidades indigenas* (indigenous communities) in Andes and the Coast, proclaimed during Augusto Leguía's *Indigenista* government, to *comunidades campesinas* (peasant communities), and following the Andean territorial model recognised part of Amazonian groups' territory as *comunidades nativas* (native communities).
- <span id="page-30-4"></span>[5](#page-26-0) The conference's impact exceeds the aims of this research: it acquired its own dynamic and enabled the creation of important networks, already materialised, e.g., in the organisation of panels on art and anthropology of people that met there.
- <span id="page-30-5"></span>[6](#page-26-1) I have developed this methodological reflection in 'Expanded Fieldwork: In-Site Arenas, the Actant Archive and the Sensorial Studio' (2018).
- <span id="page-30-6"></span>[7](#page-27-0) In a similar way, Andrea Giunta's *When Does Contemporary Art Begin?* (2014: 107) does not include images but suggests that the reader look for the images on the internet as a 'way for accepting our contemporary condition'.

# <span id="page-31-1"></span>**1 Triggering change**

# <span id="page-31-0"></span>[The Lima Biennial and the social](#page-7-3) re-engagement of art

Cultural policies were at the battle front in Lima's 2014 municipal elections. Mayor Susana Villarán's cultural programme was one of the key strengths in her campaign for re-election, and the lack of a cultural emphasis was one of the main weaknesses of the leading opposition candidate, Luis Castañeda Lossio. As Mayor of Lima from 2003 to 2010, Castañeda had focused on *obras:* building infrastructure ranging from steps up the hills of poor neighbourhoods to an immense water-fountain park. In contrast, Villarán's government had focused its efforts on culture, institutional organisation and the reinforcement of citizenship. Several Facebook messages and emails circulated warning of the danger of the elimination of culture in Lima's urban policies. A group of artists and cultural promoters signed a declaration titled *Emergencia Cultural* (Cultural Emergency), which among other things pointed out that in Castañeda's municipal period the 'budget for culture was minimal, and one of his first actions on taking power was to cancel the art biennials, successfully promoted by Alberto Andrade [ the previous mayor]' continuing, 'Today we reject the possibility that the candidate dismantles the work of the Office of Culture, initiated by this municipal government' (email, September 2014). Despite such opposition, Luis Castañeda Lossio was elected with a majority of 50.73 per cent.

Launched in 1997 and with its last edition in 2002, the Lima Biennial was a significant cultural endeavour during Alberto Andrade's two periods as mayor from 1996 to 2002. It consisted of two alternating events: the Ibero-American Lima Biennial (1997, 1999 and 2002) and the National Lima Biennial (1998 and 2000). Since its cancellation, several municipal candidates have promised its reinstatement, including Susana Villarán in her 2010 campaign. In this chapter, I examine what I argue are two parallel mechanisms with different directionalities and aims that triggered the configuration of Lima's contemporary art scene: the Lima Biennial and artists' re-engagement with social and political issues. I analyse the Biennial as part of Mayor Andrade's 'recovery of Lima' neoliberal policy at an urban level; in relation to Fujimori's political and economic regime at the national level, and as a means of articulating Lima and its art scene at the regional and global levels. While the Lima Biennial is frequently discussed as a single artefact, this chapter follows its development and shows how the last two editions not only responded to policymakers and art stakeholders' dreams of global engagement

### <span id="page-32-2"></span>2 *Triggering change*

but also opened up an arena for exploring forms of social engagement at the local level. I look at the transformation of art production from self-centred tropes to socially engaged practices, as artists responded in various ways to the dictatorial regime and embraced a renewed awareness of socio-political issues at large. This chapter advances scholarship that addresses the social, cultural and political turned of art (Foster 1995; Bourriaud 2002; García Canclini 2010; Bishop 2012; Sansi 2015) by exploring its particularities in the Lima art circuit as I connect the transformation in the arts to political economy processes.

# **The 'recovery of Lima' policy and the neoliberal project**

'Why a Biennial in Lima?' was the opening question that the art critic and President of the Lima Biennial, Luis Lama, raised in 1997 in the first Ibero-American Lima Biennial catalogue:

It happens that after almost three decades of isolation – imposed by the economy and ideology that marked the times – it is necessary for Lima to once again become part of the international circuit to which it had always belonged. We should add that with the recovery of the historical centre of our city, Lima is gradually becoming again a place of great cultural attraction once again, because among us, as in other few Latin American capitals, a phenomenon of cultural integration is taking place: an encounter not just between the capital and the provinces but also between the country and the world.

(Lama, 1997: 8)

<span id="page-32-1"></span><span id="page-32-0"></span>Lama saw the Biennial as a mechanism for the relocation of Lima and its art scene on the international circuit which, he stated, had been articulated three decades earlier. His mention of Lima's previous connection to the international art circuit referred to internationalisation projects stimulated by local elites during the 1950s and 1960s.[1](#page-50-0) His reference to the 'decades of isolation' included Juan Velasco Alvarado and Francisco Morales Bermudez's 12 years of leftist military government and agrarian reforms from 1968 to 1979 to Alan García's first period of office from 1996 to 1990, with his refusal to pay the National Debt and Peru's consequent withdrawal from the world economic system, leading to hyperinflation. This was a period marked by terrorist action and the internal civil war from 1980. By the first Lima Biennial, the main leader of the Shining Path terrorist group had already been captured, and the turn towards the neoliberal economic project was leading to macro-economic growth. Alberto Fujimori had been in power since 1990. After winning the general elections against the renowned writer Mario Vargas Llosa, Fujimori adopted a set of drastic structural changes that led the country to a neoliberal era. The international monetary system approved his actions and turned a blind eye to his subsequent political manoeuvre: a self coup d'état[.2](#page-50-1) An anaesthetised political reaction went hand-inhand with the failure and discrediting of political parties, allowing a rapid move towards pragmatic governance. Undermining politics with emphatic neoliberal <span id="page-33-3"></span>action became a way of *making* politics both in Alberto Fujimori's dictatorial regime (1992–2000) at the national level and in Lima mayor Alberto Andrade's urban government (1996–2002).

Andrade, previously mayor of the Miraflores district, was elected Mayor of Lima in 1996 and re-elected in 1998. His municipal plan had as its pivotal programme the 'recovery' of the historic centre of Lima as the heart of the city, and included the restoration of its colonial heritage and its re-establishment as a hub for political and financial decision-making and international tourism. He began an aggressive and immensely popular programme of restoring, cleaning and reordering Lima's centre, led by privatisation and white-washing polices firing 1,238 municipal workers, privatising much of the municipal work and removing approximately 20,000 street vendors; plazas and façades were refurbished, colonial balconies restored, and Lima's 'traditional' inhabitants were called to return and use the centre for residency, leisure and investment.[3](#page-50-2) The recovery plan was accompanied by the slogan *Volver al Centro* (Return to the Centre). To show their faith in the project, Andrade and his family moved from their house in Miraflores district to the Casona Gildemeister, a large mid-19th-century house in the city centre. While street vendors had been using the Lima Centre's public space as a big market place since the early 1980s and many people were already inhabiting and making sense of Lima's historical centre through different activities, they were not the mayor's target: he sought to attract elites, traditional Limeños and international tourists.

<span id="page-33-1"></span><span id="page-33-0"></span>Return to the Centre sought to reverse the elite's gradual abandonment of the centre since 1920, when they had moved away from their *casonas* (large historical houses) to the sunnier coastal districts of the south, motivated by the construction of new roads connecting Lima to the suburbs (Ludeña 2004). Strong internal migration, triggered by the dispossession of peasants' agricultural land in the highlands and the 1940 earthquake, reinforced the elites' exodus, and the newcomers found places to live in the already crumbling and divided *casonas*. [4](#page-50-3) Immigrants also decided to took over Lima's surrounding hills and fought to own a piece of land, build more spacious houses and obtain water, electricity and other services. In 1980 a second exodus from the Andes and the Amazon, motivated by the rise of terrorism, compounded Lima's transformation. Its streets became marketplaces for street vendors whose stalls transformed the organisation and visuality of the space.

<span id="page-33-2"></span>During the 1980s numerous social scientists studied this internal migration and transformation of Lima (e.g. Altamirano 1984; Matos Mar 1984; De Soto et al. 1986; Golte 1987; Degregori et al. 1987; Adams 1991), looking at its processes, organisation, decision-making, modes of living and the new urban culture categorised as 'popular culture'. Many social scientists saw this process with optimism suggesting that it was producing a transition from invaders to citizens (Degregori et al. 1987) from a traditional to a modern society (Quijano 1980): an optimistic narrative that has remained a constant in social sciences as well as in the arts. This transformation of the city also captured the attention of architects and artists such as of the collective E.P.S. Huayco (1980–1982)[,5](#page-50-4) that approached 'popular

### <span id="page-34-3"></span>4 *Triggering change*

culture' by recognising and appropriating its visuality in art projects displayed both in the public space and in the official art circuit. The influential art critic Mirko Lauer' comment, 'In today's Peru only the popular is modern', written for a Huayco exhibition in 1980, clearly highlighted the impact of the so called 'popular culture' in the arts, as Mijail Mitrovic explains (2016: 59), and the enthusiasm for what the 'popular' was producing.
