The 1980s witnessed the emergence of art collectives whose works approached the new urban aesthetics and the organisation of the migrant population, as well as the disturbing period of violence and the exploration of gender issues. It also saw, however, a decline in the state's role in culture previously promoted by the military government with a wider notion of art in terms of cultural narratives, diverse art agents and public platforms, which gradually shrank into an art world based on private galleries[.6](#page-50-5) A Huayco artist told me that under the new democracy they could not find sustainable channels through which to connect their work with a larger population and at the same time stay in the art world. Although these art collectives were short-lived, most lasting only two or three years, they showed to new generations of artists a different way of making art, of being an artist, and of relating to social aspects.[7](#page-50-6) 

<span id="page-34-1"></span><span id="page-34-0"></span>Yet, while the public art events promoted by the military government came to an end, it is important to note that in the 1980s and at the urban level, the socialist municipal government of Mayor Alonso Barrantes from 1984 to 1986 also promoted an expanded version of culture with his Cultural Participation Plan, which stated: 'We all produce culture. This should not be the privilege of artists, poets and specialists. Culture should be driven by broader sectors of the population' (Herbert Rodríguez's archive). One materialisation of this policy was Carpa Teatro (the Theatre Tent), a platform for cultural events under the Santa Rosa Bridge, which links the predominantly low-income district of Rimac with the centre of Lima and in which, for instance, Los Bestiarios participated[.8](#page-50-7) Alfredo Márquez, at the time a student of architecture and an active Bestiario, and now a renowned Peruvian artist, described me what was for him the Lima of 1985, when it was ruled by the leftist party Izquierda Unida and the young Alan García had just become president:

<span id="page-34-2"></span>There was a pulse in the city that was about having the whole of Lima's centre full of street vendors. Try to imagine: the centre of Lima was all street vendors, a festival of popular creativity, amazing! Where others saw poverty, unemployment, chaos, disorder, we saw a living city. There were the *subtes*  [underground musicians] selling their tapes. Poets were selling their poems. Informalist painters were selling their stuff on the street. It was as if everyone was on the street. The street was alive. The cultural centre, by definition, was the Museo de Arte de Lima, not for its exhibitions but for its film centre [*filmoteca*]. Everyone went to watch films at the film centre! Everybody, whether a *subte* living in Carabayllo or a pretty girl from San Isidro. The place where we all were was the Lima Film Centre. There were those who lived from that point towards the centre, and others who arrived there and then returned.

That city is gone. (Interview, January 2014)

<span id="page-35-0"></span>The Lima Centre recovery policy aimed to transform Lima from a 'chaotic' to an 'ordered' city, with notions of order and beauty dictated by Andrade's social taste. In her vivid ethnography of Alberto Andrade's urban project, Daniella Gandolfo (2009: 81) states that his victory was to naturalise his aesthetic project. Yet Andrade's urban policy was not an isolated but rather a global trend promoted by transnational organisations such as UNESCO's World Heritage Site programme. A private organisation, the Patronato de Lima (the Patronage of Lima), and the Peruvian government started the procedure for Lima's recognition as a World Heritage Site, which was granted in 1991. However, from 1991 to 1995 neither the central government nor the local authority worked to what John Collins (2008) calls 'UNESCO templates'. It was Alberto Andrade who took on this challenge; specifically, his urban plan was based on the 1996 Second Habitat Conference in Istanbul, which insisted that metropolitan restructuring must happen through the recovery of the urban centre. The engagement of Andrade's policy with UNESCO's views had rapid consequences, as noted in *Caretas* magazine:

During the recent stay in Lima, the general director of UNESCO, Federico Mayor Zaragoza, offered UNESCO's full support to the municipality and announced that the IDB [Inter-American Development Bank] will also assist in the recovery of the centre with a sum of between 5 to 12 million dollars. Calling for a truce in their 'battle for' customers, the Lima, Santander, Latino, Commerce and Credit Wiese banks, all of which own buildings in the financial centre of Lima, have joined this cause.

(*Caretas,* 25 April 1996)

Andrade's aesthetic project for Lima as a simultaneously colonial, *criollo* and modern city was well-received by UNESCO, by businessmen and traditional Limeños, and widely promoted by the media. Their aesthetic views of Lima coincided. Thus, these actors actively participated in the process of naturalising Andrade's project as the template to follow. Andrade's success was considerable and confronted Alberto Fujimori's as the leading figure. Yet Andrade neither opposed nor supported Fujimori's regime: the two coexisted, engraining Lima and Peru into the neoliberal project – more precisely, into a first wave of neoliberalisation; taking Peck et al. (2009; 2010)'s suggestion of rather to understand neoliberalism as a static notion and totality, to approach to the phenomenon as a dynamic conception (neoliberalisation), this is as the succession of an uneven path-dependent neoliberal restructuring.

Scholars have widely explained how neoliberalism is not just a set of economic policies that seeks to reduce state power and its participation in the market by promoting deregulation, privatisation and the reduction of state-subsidised social services. It is not just an economic doctrine, but a 'technology of governing' (Ong 2006), a 'state-led project of social engineering' (Taylor 2006) that modifies the relationship between the governing and the governed and strengthens institutions that advance market power while reducing the power of those who seek to limit it, in particular collective institutions (Bourdieu 1988). The intersection of Peru <span id="page-36-1"></span>and Lima's governmental strategies at different levels, would gradually raise neoliberalism not only as a set of policies but as a mode of subjectivity and common sense (Foucault 2008; Harvey 2006), as I will further explain in [Chapters 2](#page-65-1) and [6.](#page-201-1)

# **The silence of culture and self-representation as a trope**

On 5 April 1992, Fujimori staged a self coup.

I was fifteen years old and was at home when somebody said, 'There are tanks in the streets' and that the Senate has been closed. I did not yet understand the consequences. I thought the military would protect my father, a senator at the time, from the Shining Path's attacks. This was very far from what actually happened: Fujimori closed the Senate and the Chamber of Parliament with the immediate approval of 82 per cent of the population and began the persecution and oppression of his opponents.

On 12 September 1992, the leader of the Shining Path, Abimael Gúzman, was captured.

<span id="page-36-0"></span>We all saw it on TV: something huge was covered by a large curtain, which was slowly removed. A zoo-like cage was uncovered. In it, in a striped convict suit, was Abimael Guzman, who with fist raised proclaimed his defiance.[9](#page-50-8) Fujimori made a visual spectacle of the Shining Path leader's imprisonment. These images circulated widely and stayed in the memory of the people of Peru.

With the terrorist leaders under arrest, the economic growth, the arrival of new products, the opening of fast food outlets – the first McDonald's opened in 1995 – and the expansion of telecommunications, there was a general feeling that things were getting better, fuelled by Fujimori's immense campaign as the leader of a new era. Fujimori's power and national approval boosted Peru's entry into the global economy. The government reinforced its power with widespread corruption, controlling the media, parliament and the judiciary and using a surveillance system that closely monitored universities, art schools, political opponents and the public space. Many people, among them academics and artists, were arrested and kept for days or even years under suspicion of involvement with terrorism. There was no place for critical thought or opposition. Repression was the norm, and gatherings in universities generated government's distrust. In the name of pacification, innocent people, mainly those with indigenous features, were killed, such as in the La Cantuta and Barrios Altos massacres, and nearly 2,000 women were illegally sterilised.

Leisure time was promoted and moved indoors; telecommunications networks grew rapidly. Fujimori's power was reinforced by the new tabloids and mocking slap stick comedy celebrating an attitude of '*le metio la yuca*' (inserting the yucca into someone: this phrase, with sexual connotation, means tricking another to obtain what you want) in an extreme individual pragmatism. Fujimori succeeded in mobilising a pairing of economic growth with pacification, 'with its costs', in which politics was not part of the visible equation. This equation of 'economic growth + peace – politics' reinforced a market-driven, non-political and dreamlike attitude on the art circuit, with a few exceptions.

<span id="page-37-2"></span>In 1994 the art student Eduardo Villanes presented his installation *Gloria Evaporada* at the National University of San Marcos. Gloria is the name of a popular brand of canned evaporated milk whose cans are distributed in cardboard boxes which are variously reused, even for storing objects of Peruvian heritage and as beds for babies. In 1994 the police returned the remains of nine students and a professor, killed by the paramilitary Colina Group, to their relatives in cardboard boxes, many of which were Gloria boxes. Villanes's installation was in reaction to this. Later, when Fujimori granted the Colina Group amnesty, and in the context of a Protest against Impunity, Villanes called on the protesters to cover their heads with cardboard boxes. Bringing together the meaning of 'evaporation', the use of Gloria boxes as a container to return the human remains from a murder orchestrated by the government and as a container to cover the head, implying the erasure of identity/life, the flyer for this action announced: 'The government industry for evaporating people'. I met Villanes at an exhibition in New York while pursuing my PhD. He was living between Maine and New York, having left Peru after the intervention. In New York he tended to sleep in his truck. Sometimes he phoned me and asked if could stay at my place, when the truck was too cold. We frequently talked about him going back to Peru. In 2012 he returned – and cannot go back to the United States. Paradoxically, his work was featured in the 2014 big exhibition *Urbes Mutantes: Latin American Photography 1944–2013* at the International Center of Photography in New York and the New York Times made use of his work title in its headline about the show: 'In an Ironic Way, a Latin Myth Evaporated'. (*New York Times*, 2014) At the time of Villanes's *Gloria Evaporada* installation the dominant art scene was promoting other types of work.

<span id="page-37-0"></span>With the rising of the terrorist violence of the late 1980s, many Limeño artists engaged with figurative expressionism as well as fantasy-based works on subjective modes of expression[.10](#page-50-9) The arrival of the 1990s with the Fujimori's regime did not put a stop to these disquisitions and practices but rather enhanced them. As various art critics and curators explain (Del Valle 1999; Quijano 2001; Hernández Calvo and Villacorta 2002), during the first half and more of the 1990s, most artists carried out an 'aesthetic of subjectivity' reinforced by political repression, the removal of the public space as an arena for discussion, and the beginning of a consumerist era. The making of portraits and self-portraits as forms of individual and internal experimentation and the mastery of technique became central in the Lima art scene. The art schools in Lima – the School of Fine Arts (Escuela Nacional Superior Autónoma de Bellas Artes del Perú), the Art Department of Católica University, and the new art school for high- and upper-middle-income students, Corriente Alterna, founded in 1992 – promoted this aesthetic in which the self was the main landscape of exploration, particularly through painting. The gradual insertion of photography into the Lima art scene would also provide a new medium for exploration and further experimentation – this fomented by the new Centro de la Imagen created in 2000 based on the Gaudi Institute (1993–1998).[11](#page-50-10) 

<span id="page-37-1"></span>Art was promoted through an indoor circuit of commercial art galleries, noncommercial galleries and cultural spaces: the museum was not a relevant agent

#### <span id="page-38-3"></span>8 *Triggering change*

<span id="page-38-0"></span>for contemporary art. Financial entities, telecommunication companies and other businesses became key sponsors of the art world and promoted diverse awards and competitions as they penetrated the territory. In addition, embassies, municipalities and banks opened cultural centres and non-commercial galleries. As the artist Claudia Coca remembered, 'In the 1980s and 1990s there were many galleries. Not as it is today. All banks had a [non-commercial] gallery. You walked down Larco Avenue and you found galleries everywhere' (interview, April 2015). Art galleries and art schools joined together to promote exhibitions and competitions for recent graduates, including *Jóvenes Valores* (Young Talent) at the Forum Gallery, which featured the work of final-year students at the Católica University; *Encuentro de Arte Joven* (Encounter of Young Art) at the Praxis Gallery; *Estímulo Arte Jóven* (Young Art Incentive) at the 2Vs Gallery; and *Concurso de Arte Jóven*  (Young Art Competition) at Miraflores Municipality building.[12](#page-50-11) Art graduates had a convenient and dynamic local circuit through which their work was promoted in the context of the growing economy and consumerism. There was an active local art market. The gallery owner Cecilia Gonzales insisted: 'There was a market, but not those buyers between 35 and 45 years old that there are today, because there was not that idea of buying. There were no institutions. There was support from banks. … There was the same [market] but on a small scale' (interview, November 2013). In [Chapter 6](#page-201-1), I discuss the form of today's art market, but I want to draw attention here to the fact that for a significant part of the 1990s there was a small but dynamic circuit driven by art galleries, private awards and media coverage. It was a vibrant Lima centric circuit, distanced by its narratives and mechanisms from the socio-political context of the first of the dictatorship years, contributing to the apolitical atmosphere. The sense of repression and surveillance reinforced the solipsistic art practices and socially disengaged art scene. By 1998 the political manifestations to recovery democracy and specially the economic downturn led to a reduced art market and less private support for art competitions and awards[;13](#page-50-12) meanwhile, a new reorganisation of the art scene began to emerge.

<span id="page-38-2"></span><span id="page-38-1"></span>A note: while art schools in other Peruvian provinces, where the political violence was widespread, also promoted dream-like and subjective centred art practices in relation to the modernist trope and the repressive Fujimori's regime, other producers were responding to the political situation and their daily experiences. Artists seen by the dominant art sphere as 'popular artists' were already referring to and inscribing the injustices and violence in their work. They were producing it in their *talleres* (workshops) such as in Ayacucho and Lima, but their work was not yet known to the capital and its art scene; it was promoted first by human rights NGOs and social scientists towards the end of 1990s, as I explain in [Chapter 2.](#page-65-1) At the same time, while many art collectives had disappeared by the early 1990s, other art collectives and spaces in the form of cultural associations appeared in the 'peripheral' areas of Lima in response to the political violence. For instance, the cultural association Arenas y Esteras (Sand and Mats) in Villa el Salvador district was founded in 1992, in response to Shining Path's assassination of the community leader Maria Elena Moyano, with the aim of promoting community art and a culture of peace.[14](#page-50-13) However, these collectives and art spaces <span id="page-39-2"></span>are not part of the narrative of political art, clearly showing how the dominant art scene establishes its actors, genealogies and limits.

# **Solipsistic biennial and mechanisms of global connectivity**

<span id="page-39-0"></span>In 1997 the Union of Ibero-American Capital Cities proclaimed Lima the *Plaza Mayor de la Cultura Iberoamericana* (Main Plaza of the Ibero-American Culture).[15](#page-50-14) With this recognition the municipality organised the International Festival of Lima and the main square's name was changed from Plaza de Armas (Army Plaza) to Plaza Mayor (Main Plaza), removing its martial reference and restoring its colonial name. The Ibero-American Lima Biennial was created to enhance the Union of Ibero-American Capital Cities' recognition of Lima as the Main Plaza of the Ibero-American Culture. The Biennial's name and scope were directly related to this award. The municipal government sought to revamp the city through the management of culture as a resource. Gandolfo explains that 'Andrade hoped to redefine the notion of tradition so that it would transcend its opposition with modernity' (2009: 81), and contemporary art would play a key role in Andrade's project of 'urban recovery'.

Tracing the worldwide rise of art biennials, Elena Filipovic et al. state that despite biennials' global aspirations, many have been possible 'because of decisive 'local' events and issues' (2010: 18) that marked a turning point in the history and political-economic process of a city, country or region. The authors mention how Documenta was set in Germany's post-war reconstruction; the first Johannesburg Biennial marked the end of apartheid and the Gwangju Biennial commemorates South Korea's transition to democracy. The Lima Biennial was set as part of Andrade's urban recovery project and claimed to mark the reconnection of Lima with an international circuit. The Biennial facilitated the recovery of several historic buildings and connected the city with the global biennial phenomenon and its formats. It enabled the conjuncture of Lima's historic colonial legacy, the '*criollo*' and the 'contemporary' as a project that sought to reframe the city through policies – and versions – of order, renovation and embellishment.

<span id="page-39-1"></span>The Lima Biennial became Peru's main *indoor* art platform for visibility and networking. However, it was not the experimental and 'unstable institution', in contrast to the more established, rigid and authoritative institution of the museum, that Carlos Basualdo's (2003) calls for the biennial phenomenon. In 1997 Lima had no modern or contemporary art museum; the Museo de Arte de Lima had not yet assumed an active role on the art scene and the promotion of the contemporary, and the smaller Museo de Arte de San Marcos provided only, eventually, space for artistic exploration. Consequently, the Lima Biennial gained an authoritative voice at the time when it was also acting as an experimental platform in relation to the work promoted on the Lima art circuit. It became both the 'museum' and the 'experimental site' shaping the emergence and conditions of Peru's contemporary art and Lima's art scene. Through its format and calls for participation, the Lima Biennial began to establish the generation of artists, preferred forms of media, and in general the mechanisms for contemporary art[.16](#page-50-15) Young artists received <span id="page-40-4"></span><span id="page-40-1"></span><span id="page-40-0"></span>new and vibrant attention, while older generations, and even artists at the limit of the Biennial's rules for participation despite their solid local art market, were outside of the scope for the emergent strategies to connect art globally.[17](#page-51-0) The first Ibero-American Lima Biennial overwhelmingly represented Peru responding to the sense of immense opportunity that the event provided;[18](#page-51-1) although following editions imposed a sense of friction between generations. Participation was open to artists with 5 to 25, and later to 20, years of professional work. Mainly it enhanced the work of artists aged under 45 and of two generations: those born in 1954–1964, and in 1965–1974. For the second Ibero-American Lima Biennial in 1999, Luis Lama stated: 'Certainly, we reassert our conviction that the Biennial is a young event and that we need to privilege a generation that has not seen the advantages of our intense exchanges in the 1960s, promoted by transnational entities of that time' (1999: 22).

The biennials explicitly fostered installations and video art, aspiring to connect local art with 'contemporary' international tendencies. Artists embraced this new media on the Lima art circuit and continued to work on subjective narratives. According to the curator Max Hernández-Calvo (2009), who participated in the biennials as a young artist, the biennial created a 'landscape of possibility', with an edition of the Ibero-American or the National Biennials every year, offering artists possibilities for media exploration fostered by no other art space. The Biennial led to new ways of imagining the arts. For instance, in an interview a journalist asked artist Sandra Nakamura 'How did you get into art?' She replied: 'It was at the first Lima Biennial of 1997 that I felt that excitement when I first saw an installation. I had never seen anything that worked with a space in that way. That's when I said, "I want and need to do that"' (*El Comercio*, 7 July 2014).

<span id="page-40-2"></span>The Lima Biennial also reinforced the centralisation of Lima in the definition of what counts as Peruvian contemporary art. The first Ibero-American Lima Biennial established a selection process that privileged the art circuit in Lima. To try to overcome this Lima-centrism, subsequent selection processes for Peruvian participants were decided in a National Lima Biennial that included the winners of Regional Salons – however, the committee also invited directly some artists to participate. From 1997 to 2002, Lima had a biennial edition every year[.19](#page-51-2) The National Biennial provided a key arena to see what was happening in the country; although the exhibition was held only in Lima and did not circulate to other regions. The selection process for the Ibero-American Lima Biennials by an international jury confirmed that art produced in Lima responded best to the biennial's aims[.20](#page-51-3) Therefore, despite efforts of decentralisation, the Biennial played a role in confirming Lima's centrality in thinking and making contemporary art.

<span id="page-40-3"></span>The activation of the biennial format enabled the bringing together of diverse art agents in a single location at one time. It articulated local art agents, enabled (momentarily) the reconnection of Peruvian artists living abroad with the Lima art circuit and brought various international art agents to the city, creating a space-time of exchange, knowledge and connections with other art stakeholders, art tendencies and debates. Biennials, and also art fairs, are landmark events that provide a city and its art scene with visibility through the peregrination of <span id="page-41-0"></span>art agents, transnational encounters, and a display of symbols ranging from the artistic to the financial and the intellectual: a pilgrimage through the regional and global art landscapes that provides, for those who can and are invited to participate and attend, a sense of community and belonging. The Lima Biennial became a new site in the geography of art of the Ibero-American region. What biennials and art fairs do resonate with my research (Borea 2001) on the making of territory in Andean communities through the *Linderaje, a* border-making ritual, which involves walking through territorial limits and stopping and celebrating at milestones, mutually reinforcing both the group and the territory. Biennials and art fairs constitute, on different scales and levels of importance, milestones on the art stakeholders' regional and global pilgrimage. Community members – in this case artists and other art players – participate and perform, with their bodies, symbols and parties, marking the art landscape. As in the *Linderaje*, on the art circuit some milestones are more important than others, and therefore receive more attention as well as more symbolic and economic investment. While not all transnational art stakeholders passed by all of the milestones, many of them gathered, or at least aspired to gather, at the most important landmarks on the art circuit. As in the place-making of the Andean community, art geographies, with their landmarks and peregrinations, are crossed by articulations of power. The level of the Lima Biennial's global connectivity needs to be addressed: it was 'global' in terms of its engagement with the Ibero-American circuit – this is on a regional level – and by rooting global models and awaking aspirations. Discussion of the 'global' in the arts needs to be brought down to the political-economic position of events, places and agents, and its relation to other geographies.

Finally, the first Biennial's theme was cultural plurality, which was a new discourse for the local art circuit. While Latin American thinkers were focusing on the structural power relations of art circuits and proposing ways of thinking about art from Latin America in the 1970s and early 1980s, from the late 1980s scholars questioned the same category of Latin American art and criticised the forms in which European and US scholars and curators represented Latin America. As I have previously explained (Borea 2017a: 21-22), this critique was fed by the impact of new urban studies from Latin American scholars who focused on consumption, media and identities across Latin America in times of globalisation (Barbero, 1987; García Canclini, 1995; Ortiz, 1999). They proposed to understand identities as dynamic, multiple and hybrid rather than as homogeneous essential attributes. Néstor García Canclini's *Hybrid Cultures: Entering and Exiting Modernity* (1989) would achieve a particular influence with his notion of 'hybridisation', understood as creative and selective processes through which individuals and groups – peasant migrants, indigenous groups, elite groups, etc. – reconvert their knowledge, objects and practices to respond to, and reinsert them into, the new conditions of production and market (2001, pp. v–vi). Paying special attention to popular and artistic strategies and creativity, García Canclini added complexity to the study of Latin American identities by suggesting their multiplicity and instability, widely influencing the fields of anthropology, art history, art practice and public policy.

#### <span id="page-42-1"></span>12 *Triggering change*

<span id="page-42-0"></span>It is not surprising then that 'cultural plurality' became the theme of the first Ibero-American Biennial. However, while the selected international and Peruvian artists represented different Ibero-American countries, they did not represent cultural pluralism. Artists showing at the Biennial and on the dominant art circuit at large were predominantly West-oriented, white-mestizo and middle-upper class. In addition, most Peruvian artists were not dealing with cultural plurality as a topic in 1997; they were looking at introspective issues as I have explained[.21](#page-51-4) In a larger paradox, the Biennial with its theme of cultural plurality took place in the historic centre of Lima, where, in terms of citizenship cultural plurality was not at the core of the 'recovery' policy. Until 1999 the biennial editions engaged directly with the municipality's aspirations to make Lima a new, modern, ordered, and interconnected city, turning a blind eye to the political situation and to social diversity. The selected Peruvian works drew on explorations of the self in contexts of rising protest, mobilisations and demands for democracy and citizenship.

# **The social re-engagement of art in the late dictatorial period**

Three months before the second Ibero-American Biennial the School of Fine Arts withdrew its offer of an exhibition room for the event which was to feature work from the Peruvian artists selected for the first Ibero-American Biennial, who according to the Biennial's terms, would then have an exhibition space at following editions. Among these artists was the performer Emilio Santisteban, who was planning to present his edgy performance-installation *Crisis*. With the aim to produce his performance during the Biennial, Santisteban found another venue, but found it too large and dilapidated and therefore asked the art critic Gustavo Buntinx to hold together an exhibition. Organised in response to the cancellation of the Biennial space and censorship, this exhibition featured emerging artists who were dealing with the political regime in critical terms and was called *Emergencia artística: arte crítico 1998–1999* (Artistic emergency: critical art 1998–1999).

In a small dark room flanked by a Peruvian flag with a *bolero* song, *Poquita Fe* (Little Faith), the artist, dressed in black and with a hand on his chest, stood in front of each spectator-participant as they entered the room one by one. Denise Leigh (2006: 175) explains that Emilio Santisteban's action sought to trigger participants' personal and social commitments. 'Have you seen Emilio Santisteban's work yet?' was a question that I remember circulating among university students. Santisteban repeated his performance 1,235 times. Outside the exhibition building, the artist Christians Luna set up his installation *Muerto por un pan* (Dead for a loaf of bread). Simulating the chalk line that the police draw around a dead body on the ground, Luna drew a man's silhouette with fresh bread and registered the different forms of interaction with his work from the people who entered the exhibition or passed by. Making use of different media the artists grouped in *Artistic Emergency* questioned the political-economic context and Fujimori's propaganda about progress, receiving a large number of visitors. Claudia Coca, artist and director of Corriente Alterna Art School from 2011 to 2018, remembered:

<span id="page-43-1"></span>When I was studying, the enchantment of the arts, the manual work, lasted almost my complete career, because in the School of Fine Arts the manual mastery is its issue too. … There are many deficiencies in schools. … But I think that in Corriente [Corriente Alterna School] we have found a midpoint. The first year you fall in love with art; slowly the discourse on contemporary art enters with history of contemporary art courses. In the second year it starts to change, and in the third year the conceptual, the theoretical is introduced. Everything is thought out progressively. Because we know that if we are harsh on theoretical and conceptual issues we'll scare the students, and they won't explore and discover what they can do now with contemporary art.

This process was much longer for me in the ninetieth. It was very long, because I finished art school and was looking for a discourse, but perhaps in a very naive way. Unfortunately for the country, but somehow good for me, we were under a dictatorship. I think reflections, criticism, move us when the state of affairs is complex. A dictatorship with violence and corruption, as it was, affected me a lot and triggered other issues and worries. I realised 'I am Peruvian, I am a citizen, and I'm not in a bubble'. I started to work on selfrepresentation in a very playful way. One thing was my artistic development and the other was my political concerns. But that changed in 1997.

(Interview, April 2015)

New politically engaged art projects started to appear between 1998 and 1999 in a gradual transition from an art scene based on works around the individual subjectivity. The Peruvian artist Fernando Bryce, who was based in Germany, debuted his series *The Progress of Peru* at his exhibitions in Lima at the Forum Gallery in 1998 and at Casa Mariátegui in 1999. Using drawing as his medium and displacing the single work by a large series of small formats, Bryce focused on the making of history. Selecting and copying drawings, texts and images from the official propaganda of Oscar Benavides's dictatorial regime (1933–1937) and Manuel Prado's second term (1956–1962) that he found in a library in Berlin, Bryce called attention into the government's ideas and aspirations from a period understood as one of Peruvian progress and modernisation which was entangled, according to the art critic Rodrigo Quijano, 'with the most absolutely discriminatory and exclusive part of the *criollo* imaginary' (1998: 37). Quijano called Bryce's method 'mimetic analysis' for the dismantling of the national imaginary. The connection with what was happening under Fujimori's government was latent in both Bryce's work and Quijano's text. Bryce also challenged the art format and protocols of viewing that were still dominant in Lima: instead of one large painting, he presented a large series of smaller ink drawings that required an active displacement of the visitor by the gallery space[.22](#page-51-5) This shift in the conditions of what art could be had an impact on art students, as Wylly Zumaeta recalled:

<span id="page-43-0"></span>It's a really cool memory. After the '90s boom in media coverage of Tola, Llona and Szyszlo, and I as an artist who came from La Catolica from painting, I was exposure to that ... The ninetieth ended with the style of Llona's

#### <span id="page-44-1"></span>14 *Triggering change*

<span id="page-44-0"></span>large 3 × 3m paintings[.23](#page-51-6) And then comes an artist like Bryce with his little drawings – thousands, weren't there? That made a change … small work in drawing, simple. People worked on larger and larger formats. And this small format was as powerful and easier to transport.

(Interview, February 2014)

Like Bryce, the photographer Flavia Gandolfo tackled the making of history, in this case the history of contemporary times, in her exhibition *Historia* at the Miraflores Cultural Centre in 1998. Using unconventional media for the local art circuit, Gandolfo displayed a collection of visual documentation such as pictures of students' drawings of Peruvian maps, collected over two years visiting public schools. Her exhibition evidenced mechanisms by which Peruvian history is taught and imagined. Adding to this atmosphere, in 1999 the exhibition *El Laberinto de la Choledad* (*Choledad* labyrinth), organised by Jorge Villacorta, Julia Pesce, Augusto del Valle and María Paz Ortiz de Cevallos, was presented simultaneously in four locations. The project reviewed the production of art from 1980 to the late 1990s through the lens of cultural diversity. *El Laberinto de la Choledad* took its name from the sociologist Guillermo Nugent's (1992) book about racism and everyday forms of exclusion in Peru. The thinking of the exhibition was based on a set of talks given by social scientists and other scholars, whose reflections contributed to the exhibition display. Finally, it is important to mention that the alternative cultural space El Averno was founded in 1998 and became a platform for rock music, alternative art and activism. Thus during 1998 and 1999 the transition from the solipsistic art scene was taking place at the hands of artists and of art critics acting as curators, and socially engaged art produced during the 1980s would be gradually re-discovered. While the political situation fuelled the transformation of the art scene, the art also acted upon the political situation, contributing to the recovery of democracy.

# **A turning point: Towards the recovery of democracy and the 2000 national Lima Biennial**

With the economy growing, media manipulation and weak political opposition, in 1995 Fujimori obtained a strong electoral victory against his closest opponent Javier Pérez de Cuellar, previously UN Secretary General from 1981 to 1991. As soon as his second period in office began Fujimori started to create the conditions for his re-election by enacting the *Ley de Intepretación Autentica*  (Authentic Interpretation Law). While the 1979 Constitution had prohibited immediate re-election, Fujimori's 1993 Constitution allowed it. When he won the election in 1995 his party insisted that as this was Fujimori's first period under the 1993 Constitution he could run in the presidential election again. Three Constitutional Tribunal magistrates supported the repeal of the Law, but parliament – a one-chamber parliament created after Fujimori's abolition of the bicameral congress, controlled by his party – dismissed them. This intrusion into juridical power gave rise to the mobilisation of those who were already tired of <span id="page-45-0"></span>such abuse: among whom were university students. June 1997 saw the first of several *marchas estudiantiles* (students strikes) against Fujimori's regime, with thousands of students protesting in the streets with slogan such as 'We are students, not terrorists' and 'The fear is over'.

In 1995 I enrolled the private Católica University to study anthropology. With the exception of small groups, students from private universities rarely took part in social protests, perhaps because previously more robust political parties had represented their interests, and the politics of the street had not yet had the impact for them that it would attain later. Days after the march in June, 30 university leaders organised the Foro Universitario (the University Forum) to support the initiative of Foro Democrático – a small but important group of politicians and intellectuals who had been working to recover democracy since 1993 – to collect the million signatures required to call for a referendum on Fujimori's candidacy in the next presidential election. The Foro Universitario's collection of signatures predominantly took place with the slogan: 'One student, one signature' in Plaza San Martin, in Lima's historic centre, which was the platform of the first Ibero-American Biennial only four month later; yet the Biennial and the works featured in it did not allude to the political situation. Although more than a million signatures were obtained the referendum was denied, and parliament allowed Fujimori to stand again as a candidate in 2000. More protests from different sectors of society were to come.

The 2000 presidential election arrived. Corruption and extortion were at their peak. Both Fujimori and Alejandro Toledo got to the second round, but Toledo exhorted not to mark the electoral ballot and participate in the Marcha de los Cuatro Suyos (Four Sides March) on 28 July, Peru's Independence Day. The indigenous Quechua Stanford economist Alejandro Toledo articulated, in political terms, the growing disenchantment with Fujimori's regime, which announced the end of terrorism as its achievement but acted against human rights. In addition, the recession from 1998 to 2000 had revealed growing social inequality in the process of Peru's integration with the international economic system through neoliberal programmes (Gonzales de Olarte 2007). As an indigenous person, Toledo mobilised the notion of the *Tawantinsuyo*, the Quechua name for the Inca empire, which translates as four segments joined together in the Inca capital of Cusco. In the Marcha de los Cuatro Suyos people marched from the four corners of Peru to the centre of the capital. This political and symbolic was replicated in various central plazas throughout Peru. Millions of people participated. The Peruvian and Tawantinsuyo flags were waved in protest and calls for democracy. The Cuatro Suyos mobilisation was tainted by the death of six guards in a massive fire started by government infiltrators. Days later, on 5 August, a group of people began to wash Peruvian flags in trays of water and hang them on a line in Lima's Main Plaza and in front of the Government Palace. This action, named *Lava la Bandera (*Wash the Flag*)*, was immediately replicated in plazas across the country, washing the flag, to wash away Peru's corruption and pain in the hope that it would become clean again. The Colectivo Sociedad Civil (Civil Society Collective), composed by artists and cultural agents, planned and started this and other symbolic actions

<span id="page-46-1"></span>after the first round of elections in 2000. The Collective built upon the experience of previous initiatives such as *Artistic Emergency* exhibition, according to Gustavo Buntinx (2008), one of their founders. In the words of Jorge Salazar, a member of the Collective and of the Press and Society Institute, 'our fight … is to rescue symbolic power for civil society with protests that allow those who do not identify with political parties and who cannot find a way to channel their discontent with this government, to do so through the proposals of the collective: this is a civil opposition' (Méndez 2000). The Collective channelled the opposition against the regime with symbolic actions that were easily replicable. It also appropriated the aesthetics on which Fujimori had based his electoral campaign, redefining the content. The *tecnocumbia* is Amazonian Andean urban fusion music that Fujimori appropriated in *El Baile del Chino* (Chinese Man's Dance), which he performed at his rallies accompanied by the famous singer Rossy War and showgirls, turning the rallies into massive artistic political events. The Colectivo Sociedad Civil appropriated the style, colours and format of the *tecnocumbia* concerts announcements to claim, in a play on words*, Cambio no Cumbia* (Change not Cumbia). In this way the agents of Fujimori's campaign and his various opponents began to make strategic use of culture and the arts for political aims. As Gisela Cánepa (2006: 16) points out, culture became not only a means of expression but also a source for action. However, the Colectivo Sociedad Civil's actions, which have received considerable attention from scholars and curators, was not an isolated symbolic event. It was part of a larger arena of dissident action and claims. For instance, the Colectivo Resistencia (Resistance Collective) produced *Muro de la Vergüenza* (Wall of Shame), a wall of more than 15 metres long presenting pictures of corrupt politicians with the aim of motivating people to write their opinions on the wall as a way of regaining the public voice in the public sphere (Vich 2004). Other collective, Aguaytones, created the *Micro de la Democracia*  (Democracy Bus): a bus made of spray-painted cardboard boxes into which people were invited to get in to demonstrate, in the words of artist Jorge Miyagui (2009), their 'combative citizenship'.

<span id="page-46-0"></span>Carmen Ilizarbe (2017) explains that the demonstrations against Fujimori established a new form of political participation from the street and a process of politicisation of society that has since gained strength.[24](#page-51-7) Tracing the number and types of protest between 1997 and 2003, Ilazarbe's revealing study found that 50 per cent of the mobilisations had been marches and the delivery of memorials, and 7 per cent had been symbolic action. Her study also found that while unions and fronts had organised most of the protests, those organised by civil associations and students had gained much more visibility, and that the media saw these latter actors as the protests' leaders against Fujimori. Since then, the use of aesthetics and symbolism at demonstrations as a means of expressing claims, bringing people together and achieving media visibility has grown. Carlos León-Xjimenez (2017) explores the art-activism in Fujimori's era to diverse demonstrations during the democratic period and growing neoliberalism which include issues of human rights, women's rights, LGBT rights and solidarity with the claims of indigenous peoples. The author affirms that artists and activists have widened the <span id="page-47-0"></span>spectrum of political participation and increased the creativity of forms of protest. Yet the question is what happen with other forms of protest perhaps less symbolic and artistic? Is it that the politics of the street give actors who connect with certain aesthetics to protest a privileged role, at the time that devalue other expressive tools for demonstrations? Artists and other cultural producers are mediating much of this street political voice today, which has its paradoxes when we link it to the making of creative cities and the role of art in neoliberal gentrification processes, as I elaborate in [Chapter 6](#page-201-1).

The year 1997 saw political reactions against the dictatorial regime that ranged from protests in the street to the installation of a non-government-owned cable TV channel, from students and artists' demonstrations to those of workers; from the claims of politicians to those of indigenous groups, NGOs and other civic organisations: there was a growing climate of discontent. On 14 September 2000, Peruvians witnessed the level of government corruption and surveillance when Cable TV's Canal N showed a video of Vladimiro Montesino, Fujimori's key assessor, bribing a congressman recently elected to the opposition party to join the ranks of the ruling party. More videos then came to light, showing the corruption of politicians, owners of TV channels and juries. These videos, known as 'the vladivideos', became visual evidence of the corruption. In October 2000 the second National Biennial was inaugurated. In November Fujimori left the country and resigned as president by fax from Japan. He was protected there until he fled to Chile in 2005, wishing to return to Peru to run in the 2006 presidential election. However, Chile was going through a process of prosecuting human rights violations during Augusto Pinochet's dictatorship, and that atmosphere facilitated Fujimori's capture and extradition to Peru. In 2009 Fujimori received a custodial sentence of 25 years for, among other charges, human rights violations.

Held in October 2000, the second National Biennial became a new cultural-political arena: it revealed a new attitude amongst several of the artists. Various works featured a different sensibility: social engagement with the political-economic situation. The committee included Silvio de Ferrari, Luis Eduardo Wuffarden and Moiko Yaker, and for the first time Emilio Santisteban and Gustavo Buntinx, the organisers of *Artistic Emergency.* It was not just an individual artist or art collective who surpassed the arenas of subjectivity: the Biennial as a whole showed the changing attitude in Peruvian contemporary art at large through a turn towards dealing with diverse social factors and the use of a wider variety of media. Some works reviewed the processes and narratives comprising Peru's history, including Felix Alvarez's installation that symbolically executed Peru's official history with the aim of re-constructing it in collective terms through the creation of a new flag; Fernando Bryce's *Turismo/ El Dorado,* which included 50 drawings based on two tourist magazines of different periods, the first published during the government of Oscar Benavides (1933–1939) and the second published by the new Tourist Promotion Office, PROMPERU, launched during Fujimori's government, highlighting the similarities in their promotion of Peru: which emphasised an exotic culture and denied the political moment and poverty. Other works were Mario Curasi's altar, on which taking the style of Cusco's traditional Corpus Christi <span id="page-48-1"></span>altars, he placed images of Fujimori as a saint, questioning his claim that he was not aware of his regime of corruption, and Luz Letts's installation *A Country To Be Born*, which drew on perpetual Peruvians' frustrations, as did the work of La Pererra, composed by the artist Natalia Iguiñiz and the sociologist Sandro Venturo: *We the Peruvians Are Losers*.

Other projects focused on issues of violence and injustice, such as Milagros de la Torre's *Objetos Punzo Cortantes* (Sharp Object), and Alfredo Márquez's project on Peruvian jails. Alex Angeles critical of the methods used to produce and disseminate information changed the word on a TV journalist's vest from 'prensa' (press) to 'piensa' (think) calling journalists and viewers to act as emancipated actors rather than acquiescers. Other artists focused on gender issues, from reflections about women from Patricia Bueno and Alice Vega to homosexuality as in the work of Christian Flores. Others discussed ethnicity, such as Claudia Coca with *Mejorando la raza* (Improving the Race). Coca's painting focused on the *mancha indigena* (Mongolian spot) and the secrecy associated with having the *mancha* as it is a physical sign of indigeneity. Her work on prejudice and aspiration resonated with Miguel Aguirre's family portraits, which he digitally created after interviewing couples about their desired family. Exploration of technology and communication was Iván Esquivel's focus; the growth in consumption and globalisation was tackled by Giuliana Migliori, and the criticism to the art world was addressed by Aldo Chaparro's new white painting for art spaces and their white cubes, and by Susana Torres (Susy Xeroz)'s *New Inka Museum*. Reflections and representations of different traditions, aesthetics and cosmologies were also included, in the works of Jano Cortijo, Rafael Diaz and Christian Bendayán.

Thus art practices started to re-engage with the political situation and with the society at large in a recalibration of the production of art, and subsequently of curatorial activity towards the social. I discuss the latter in Chapter 3. The transition from a solipsistic art scene to the production of art addressing social concerns was triggered by greater awareness of Fujimori's corrupt dictatorial regime after the mid-1990s and the active participation of some artists in ending his dictatorship. Their awareness of the political context led artists and others to understand themselves not only as individuals but also as citizens. In addition, the Biennial offered the possibility of exploring the use of new languages, formats and spaces, and of locating the production of art in new narratives, from more dynamic approaches to identity to unveiling the mechanism of construction of history, nations and subjects.[25](#page-51-8) 

<span id="page-48-0"></span>For these new explorations, some artists began to approach to fieldwork practices, in which not only the survey – used by artists in the 1980s – but also the interview and interaction with people, the collection of grounded data and the corporeal experience thrived processes of social reflection. The artist Giuliana Migliori, a Lecturer of the Art Department and a MA student of Visual Anthropology at Católica University, explained in a course I taught in 2014, 'My work in the 1990s has a component that intuitively noted an anthropological interest: to be in contact with the public during the process of making'. In one of her projects in 1999, Migliori appropriated a very consumed Peruvian chocolate <span id="page-49-0"></span>branded Sublime Donofrío, and using the same typography she renamed it Sub-Lima Migliori. The new name announced her interest in the city and its people, Inside the wrapper Migliori included small papers with phrases about herself and her interests. Like a street vendor, she sold these chocolate-artworks in streets and plazas, connecting with people through the sale of, as she says 'an absurd object' that resembled a popular widely consumed product.

Female artists made a significant contribution to this new moment of social engagement and experimentation. Reflecting on their daily life experiences, they embraced social matters through direct contact with people and an extended reflexivity connecting with others' experiences, turning their projects into something larger than the materiality of the work itself. They started to value not only their final works and what they announced, but also the making process and the interconnection between the work and the public: what was at stake was the interrelations that their project created. While this can be read through Nicolas Bourriaud's 'relational aesthetics' (2002), it is important to note that this work was produced before the launch of this influential book, which informs much current art production. Artists such as Giuliana Migliori, Elena Tejada, Claudia Coca and Natalia Iguiñiz (see Trajectory 2) embarked on new ways of making art using a methodology based on experimentation, reflexivity and social interconnections. As Giuliana Migliori affirmed: 'These were projects derived not from a previous research but were experimental and empirical projects' (personal conversation, 2018). Moreover, the social re-engagement of Peruvian art in the wake of the dictatorship at the turn of the century was not exactly an ethnographic turn, as Hal Foster (1995: 302) describes it – 'it is now the cultural and/or ethnographic other in whose name the artist often struggles', it was more an ethnographic turn in terms of what Carlos Iván Degregori (2000) suggests as the purpose of Peruvian anthropology: not only to deal but also to engage with others, who are not only 'others' but also the self as the other is located in the self. The ethnographic turn in the art was predominantly a social turn with an important reflection of and engagement in terms of citizenship:

To see the Other within ourselves helps us place ourselves in lanes of an intercultural perspective. … The purpose of anthropology is to build a Diverse We, demanding the right of each person to equality and difference. The right to equality, not as a synonym for uniformity but to overcome the existence of first and second categories of human beings. In other words, equality before the law, which has to do with citizenship, democracy, human rights, social justice. And from this equality, the right to the differences that deal, among other issues, with gender equality, pluralism and multiculturalism.

(Degregori 2000: 62–63)

With the revoke of Fujimori's authoritative regime and the recovery of democracy an effervescent moment for the claim of rights and intercultural ideals, for the formation of new subjectivities and for new artistic explorations unfolded. The Mexico-based artist Francis Alys's project *When Faith Moves Mountains*  <span id="page-50-16"></span>for the third Ibero-American Lima Biennial, held in 2002, captured this energising moment of Lima's transformation and the dreams of its inhabitants. Working together, thousands of volunteers with shovels literally moved a mountain a few centimetres: this project took place in a time of hope and various explorations, as I explain in the next chapter.

# <span id="page-52-1"></span><span id="page-52-0"></span>**[Trajectory](#page-7-4)**

# [Fernando Bryce](#page-7-4)

In the mid-1980s, when terrorism was on the rise in Peru, a group of youngsters that had just finished school decided to go abroad to study art. Three of these young people were Fernando Bryce, Milagros de la Torre and Aldo Chaparro. Each one went to a different destination: the first to Paris, the second to London and the third to Mexico DF. Then they moved on to other cities. The three had been born in 1965 and had finished their art studies by the mid-1990s. Although they had some connection with Lima, particularly during the time of the Lima Biennial (1997–2002), the Lima art scene and media did not follow their growing international art trajectories during the first half of the 2000s. It is only since 2007, when interest in the emerging transnational trajectories of young Peruvian artists arose in Lima as a result of its museums, art collectors and curators' transnational strategies to position the Lima art scene and Peruvian art into the larger art circuit, that the work of Peruvian artists living or acting abroad came gradually into the Lima art scene's spotlight. These artists open channels of circulation for themselves and for the others to come. In doing so, they contributed with the visibility of Peruvian art and of Latin American art at large.

Fernando Bryce is one of Peru's highest-profile contemporary artists. His work has been shown at various national and international exhibitions and art fairs and acquired by both private collectors and museums. He is represented by galleries in Latin America, Europe and the United States. Furthermore, his work has been reviewed in multiple publications and has influenced a younger generation of artists. This section is based on my three-hour conversation with Fernando on 19 December 2013 at his house and studio in Miraflores, where I found that he is not only an important artist but also a brilliant narrator. We talked about his trajectory and how intertwined with those of others in the artworld. He told me about his first jobs, the importance of having a studio, his work, and creating networks; we talked about love, parties, commitment and more.

<span id="page-53-0"></span>Fernando Bryce left Lima in 1984 when he was 19 years old: 'I couldn't find what I was looking for, and I was tired of the city'. He went to France, a natural destination for him as he had studied at a French school in Lima. Before finishing high school Fernando had started to attend art workshops. For almost two years he went to Cristina Gálvez's workshop until she died in 1982. That year he began his art studies at Católica University, but left the university after the first semester. He remembers, 'At that time there were several painting workshops that were alternatives to the School of Fine Arts and Católica University, and a diploma was not so important'. So in 1983 he attended another workshop with artist Leslie Lee. Fernando's practical knowledge lay in matters of colour, shade and techniques such as still life. He enjoyed the conversations with Leslie Lee and found the experience instructive, but very formal and traditional.

In general, Fernando feels that he had very little in common with previous, and especially older, generations. He recognises that his professors and established artists such as Fernando de Szyszlo were very good, but their work was not something that interested him, and he remarks 'I was not alone in this'. He did not find himself attracted to the abstraction of a generation of artists, such as in Ramiro Llona's famous work; indeed, Llona's way of being an artist was different to what Fernando expected of himself. He was interested in Enrique Polanco's style of being an artist, but his tendency to expressionism did not attract him. He also found Armando Williams and his work appealing. Williams taught in Collective Huayco's workshops: 'I first went to Huayco with Rodrigo [Quijano] in 1981. When I was in the last year of high school in 1982 we returned to Huayco for a screen-printing workshop with Armando Williams, where we met Hebert Rodriguez, Charo Noriega and Quique Polanco, who also taught courses at the Franco Peruano school'. These artists were no more than ten years older than Bryce. 'I remember that at the time I was enthusiastic; they were doing something more interesting than the standards of that time. I saw them at Huayco parties'. While older artists gave him reasonable advice, for him the Huayco members were something new. In addition, the underground scene attracted him. 'The underground scene wasn't just a party – it was <span id="page-54-0"></span>a discovery of a new aesthetic, a new style and a new attitude, even new ways of making music. What mattered was performing'.

In 1984 he left Lima for Paris, where he studied Fine Arts at the Université Paris VIII. Despite taking interesting theoretical courses he wanted to be in a studio, painting. After two years he was accepted by the École des Beaux-Arts, where he studied for almost four years. While in Paris he became very interested in German art and contemporary music, and after meeting some Germans he went with them to Berlin. Motivated by a close relationship, by the end of his studies he found himself with one foot in Germany and the other in Paris.

Fernando had not yet found his own artistic language; he was exploring. His early work transited between fantastical drawings and geometric and cubist paintings. In Berlin he began experimenting with symbolic elements in a more expressionist language. When in Paris, Fernando occasionally went to the artist Christian Boltanski's studio to show him his work. During this time he established contact with some prominent Peruvian artists living in Paris. He remembers Jorge Piqueras as a very gentle and interesting person, and meeting the Peruvian painter Herman Braun-Vega and sculptor Alberto Guzmán. He had no further contact with Guzmán, but continued to see Braun-Vega, liking his work. Once, after returning from Berlin in the mid-1990s, he called on Herman Braun-Vega to show him his new work: 'I had been painting things and practicing for years, and I called Herman to show him something new, because it was the start of something, and he told me "Why are these abstract things there? Just take them out!" [laughs]. It was a good piece of advice, and I kept just the figure'.

From 1988 to 1991 Fernando moved between Paris and Berlin, returning occasionally to Paris before finally moving permanently to Berlin. 'To me Berlin was a great discovery; it was a *terra incognita*'. He did not speak German. Everything caught his eye because 'it was very different from Paris. The Wall was still there in 1988'. When he went to Berlin he had already acquired some confidence from having attended Boltanski's workshop in Paris. In Berlin, which was then much cheaper than Paris, Fernando had his own studio for the first time, and it was a big one. 'For me it was a great thing, that I hadn't had before'. In parallel with working in <span id="page-55-0"></span>his studio he delved into Berlin's underground scene, participating in some music performances and frequented techno music clubs. He also started to take part in small shows at galleries outside the main scene: 'There were exhibitions in basements, of people who were literally underground, because there was a very powerful underground scene, a local scene that's very difficult to imagine today because Berlin has become an open, international city'. During that period he was not in contact with any institution. He jokingly tells me 'From when I got to Berlin in 1988 until I exhibited at Barbara Thumm in 1998 there were ten years in which, in political terms, I was in secrecy'. Things changed slightly in 1994 when he started working in a studio house (*casa taller*), where he met his future art dealer, Barbara Thumm, who was an artist at the time. 'It was a workshop where there were young artists with the intention of engaging with the system, which gives you another kind of visibility because it was an open gallery. In addition, the place was managed by the municipality as part of its cultural programme, and suddenly gallery owners popped up there'.

In 1996 Barbara Thumm left the studio house and began to do curatorial work. At first she ran a gallery with a partner, but later she opened one by herself. Fernando was looking for a gallery at the time as he felt he had something to show, and he heard about Barbara's space. In a reflexive tone, he tells me about his trajectory: 'That has been my path. It was not that I finished school and the curator came along. … I won't say that everything is that easy now, and it's not the same for everyone, of course, and neither does it necessarily depend only on the work. But I do believe that there is a kind of homogenisation now. That's not the right word! Let's say there are ways of making art very safe, very conformist, for which you learn a formula, you learn a couple of speeches, in very superficial way … you put together a small product and you're in the gallery, you're already circulating. Maybe you can start like this and end up with something very powerful, it's possible – let's say there are ways of entering the market today'.

By the time Barbara Thumm opened her gallery Fernando was already using images from archives, but not systematically. When he had finished *The Progress of Peru*  <span id="page-56-0"></span>series in 1997 he took it to Barbara because he thought it was somehow good. He showed her as well previous paintings on paper and some series of drawings, and Barbara agreed to hold an exhibition in February 1998: 'It was my first show in a serious, established gallery'.

The 1990s were years of disconnection from friends he had had in the 1980s, such as the Peruvian artist Milagros de la Torre, a very good friend. They had seen each other frequently while both were living in Paris, but then she went to Mexico and he to Berlin and they did not meet again for many years. Fernando returned to Lima only sporadically. In a visit in 1994 he showed of the Trilce Gallery some of his paintings that were a combination of abstraction and figurative motifs of fruits, cookies and little balls, and they agreed to a show in 1995. Later, returning to Lima in 1997 he agreed a show with Claudia Polar at the Forum Gallery in 1998. He found out he could present what he was doing in Berlin at galleries in Lima.

In 1998 he decided to return to Lima. He was getting divorced and wanted to start a new chapter in his life. With the important achievement of his exhibition in Barbara Thumm and a clearer idea of the path to follow 'and with that particular enthusiasm that you feel when you believe you've discovered something', Fernando went back to Lima and resumed and strengthened his contacts, particularly with the curators Jorge Villacorta, Gustavo Buntinx and Rodrigo Quijano, whom he had known for a long time. He had known Villacorta from his visits to Lima since 1994 and they always met, as Fernando remembers, at artists Mariella Agois's New Year celebrations and Moiko Yaker's parties. He was also in constant dialogue with Gustavo Buntinx: 'There was a kind of mutual enthusiasm for what we both were doing, and with Rodrigo Quijano as well'. Through Buntinx, Fernando met Kevin Power, a professor of English literature, art critic, poet and art promoter based in Spain and they became good friends. Kevin Power was very interested in Latin America and organised exhibitions of Latin American art. Later he became deputy director of the Museo Reina Sofia from 2003 to 2005. Power was the adviser of the Santa Barbara art collector and creator of the TV series *Alf*, Tom Patchett, and suggested that Fernando should send him some images. <span id="page-57-0"></span>Patchett liked Fernando's work, which led to his first big sale. In his show at the Forum Gallery, Fernando exhibited *The Progress of Peru*. He laughingly tells me 'I sold some things, to my dad and other relatives'. When the show finished, Forum Gallery kept only the paintings to sell. This ultimately benefited Fernando, who was able to sell the series of drawing himself: he split one series up and he sold the other to Tom Patchett. 'The sale to Tom Patchett motivated me; it was like a first contact'. This was at the beginning of 1999. The Peruvian collector Juan Carlos Verme also bought Fernando's work for the first time: 'Juan Carlos Verme is the most important collector I have in Lima. We're not only friends; he has followed my work from the beginning'. Verme bought the 20-drawing *El Bosque de Rocas* series that was featured in Museo Casa Mariátegui as part of the exhibition *Museo Hawaii*. 'Verme was an emerging collector at the time. He began to discreetly put together a collection. At that time there was not such a significant discourse about who the collectors were'. The next year, Fernando received an invitation from Kevin Power to participate at an exhibition at the Espai D'Art Contemporani of Castelló. 'It was a great exhibition. They paid for my flight. It was the first time something like that happened to me. And then back to Lima. I had already begun *Atlas Peru* at the end of 1999, which I continued in 2000'.

He spent most of his time in Lima, although in 1998 and 1999 he returned to Berlin for two months because he had been commissioned to do a mural. In the studio house when he was living in Berlin he got together with Chilean artists and offered decorative murals. They decorated clubs, bars, hairdressers and shoe shops. He spent almost four years doing this to make money until 1999, when his work started to sell. Before, in 1993 he had a job for a year and half cleaning the Wild at Heart concert hall in Kreuzberg. 'It was legendary, because it was where the Sex Pistols first played in Berlin, and they paid the cleaners very well. It's a bit anecdotal, but it's true that that work required so much effort, I was four hours cleaning an area of 300 square metres, the bathrooms, etc., and afterwards I couldn't paint, which slowly led me into drawing. I had drawn before, but not in a systematic way'. He knew his cleaning job was temporary: 'I took it as something <span id="page-58-0"></span>Zen. I cleaned until everything was perfect. Back in my studio I couldn't paint because painting requires corporeal displacement. Instead, with drawing, I finally realised, you can be super-tired and all you have to do is just sit down. The relationship between the head and the hand always works and runs without problems. From that acknowledgement, drawing became liberating. I could work and draw after doing that job'.

Back in Lima for a short time in 1997, he started working on his series *Chronologies* about the Movimiento Revolucionario Tupac Amaru (MRTA) terrorist group's taking of hostages at the Japanese Embassy. Fernando collected press clippings, took pictures of the occupied site and worked on the series in Berlin. In doing this work, he remembered what he had learnt from Helmut Psotta during his short time at Católica University: to work with collages and series, something that was not widely promoted in Peruvian art schools at the time. Psotta had also urged students to read the newspapers and keep themselves informed about what was going on in Peru. Fernando already had that habit from his father, who had supported his desire to be an artist from the start. Fernando highlighted some remarkable learning experiences that influenced his artistic methodological inquiries: 'The serial thing from Helmud and reading the newspapers to work with reality. Then still life from Leslie Lee. And Boltanski told me that when you have a clear idea you must find the media that suits it'.

In Berlin he started going to the library systematically and began to read certain authors such as Foucault and explore topics including the history of museums and scientific taxonomy in an autodidactic way. These books explained different forms of classification. At the library he randomly found *The Progress of Peru*, a propaganda pamphlet for the Peruvian president Oscar Benavides in the 1930s. This discovery triggered an idea. He had already been drawing for two years, but this was the first time he worked from a specific document with the clear intention of reproducing the text and images. 'The result seemed sensational to me, especially the slightly ironic part in the sense that you are using a model, but in reproducing it you're reading it in another way. There's a contrast between the copy and the original that interested me, and I said, "Here's <span id="page-59-0"></span>a path." I had the idea of working with the archive as a powerful concept and working on images by understanding them in historic terms. Ultimately it's a cultural history, but with a political accent'.

Bryce improved his method and consolidated his ideas in *Atlas Peru*. At the beginning he bought some tourist magazines in Quilca – a street in Lima Centre selling second-hand books and music – and started to cut them up. At some point he decided to continue the work he had started with *The Progress of Peru,* working chronologically from the 1930s to the present. Today he continues with a similar dynamic. 'For me *Atlas Peru* was a very important work because it took me a year and a half, tweaking things and developing a working method'. While he was working on *Atlas Peru*, Kevin Power visited Lima, bringing Tom Patchett with him. Patchett had only seen his first two purchases via emailed images, and now he was travelling with Power to see the work in person. *Atlas Peru* was not yet finished and priced, but Patchett acquired it and let the artist continue his work and set the price later. In 2001 Fernando showed *Atlas Peru* at the Miró Quesada Gallery in Miraflores district as part of the *Puntos Cardinales* project promoted and curated by Jorge Villacorta.

While Fernando was working on *Atlas Peru*, Gustavo Buntinx asked him to get involved with the Colectivo Sociedad Civil. Even though he was concerned about finishing his work, he explains 'Of course I wasn't going to say no, because I was upset too'. In the mornings he involved himself in the Collective's political activities and then he worked until ten at night; later was party time. According to Fernando, many people got together in Barranco at night to comment on the political situation: 'Those years were super intense'.

Fernando remembers that artists were turning to new experimentations, that were less individual-based: 'We were coming out of this gap of the late 1980s and early 1990s. That was a kind of implosion of everything; that was a more intimate thing … with a more intimate symbolism. Suddenly there is this explosion of content and new intervention practices of doing new and different things'. In recalling this new art scene, Fernando mentioned 'there was Natalia Iguiñiz and Sandro Venturo with their actions. It was Micky Aguirre with his spectacular paintings, and Claudia Coca with her pop <span id="page-60-0"></span>project. The Biennial was very important. Surely it was the best thing Luis Lama has done. In 2000 I was one of the winners. There was the new scene. Alfredo Márquez with his work, and Bendayán too. There were new figures coming out. There was the political issue: the real struggle against Fujimori's dictatorship, when several artists began to organise; that also generated very interesting things'.

According to Fernando the few galleries that existed were aware that something new was going on but did not connect with what was happening. The Forum Gallery tried to engage somehow with this atmosphere through its participation in ARCO 2000. In this edition Gustavo Buntinx co-curated *Cutting Edge – South Cone*. 'To Cutting Edge, with the Forum Gallery, we went Flavia Gandolfo, Juan Enrique Bedoya, Natalia Iguiñiz, someone else – I don't remember now – and me. It was the first time I participated in an art fair'.

After *Atlas Peru* was shown in 2001 Fernando did not know what else he could do in Lima, so he returned to Berlin in 2002 after showing his work at the third Ibero-American Lima Biennial. The 2002 Biennial was held in a context in which Fujimori had already fallen and Alejandro Toledo was governing. Some of Fernando's friends had obtained institutional positions, Gustavo Buntinx became director of the Cultural Centre of San Marcos (La Casona). 'I did not know what to do for the Biennial. I was a bit lost, and I went to San Marcos a few times to talk to Buntinx. In one conversation that included Augusto del Valle, the fact that the Museum of Copies [a 1950s UNESCO project of European master paintings copies to cultivate art] was in storage cropped up. Immediately it clicked. Augusto del Valle was the curator [Director] of the Museum and told me the whole story, and I was fascinated'. Fernando reviewed the archives and works of the Museum of Copies and produced his project for the third Ibero-American Lima Biennial.

After that he dismantled his studio, left his things at his father's house and went back to Berlin. Between 1998 and 2002 he spent more time in Lima than elsewhere. This also coincided with a new relationship. During 2001 he resumed his contact with Barbara when he visited Berlin, and showed her *Atlas Peru*, which was already sold. Despite some discomfort about this <span id="page-61-0"></span>sale, as the terms of their working relationship had not been clearly established at that point, they agreed to have an exhibition in 2002 and show *Atlas Peru*. He left photocopies of the work and Barbara contacted the director of Künstlerhaus Bethanien, who liked it and agreed to an exhibition in June 2002. While Fernando was in Lima planning his participation in the Biennial, the curators of Manifesta 4 were in Berlin and visited Barbara Thumm to see the work of another artist who worked there. Barbara showed them the photocopies of *Atlas Peru*. Nuria Enguita, one of Manifesta's co-curators, liked it and invited Fernando to participate in Manifesta. He explained, 'That to me was a fluke, but we tried to make it happen. When I returned to Berlin and Barbara looked at my work we began to make things happen and the work was there to be shown. I communicated to Tom Patchett that I was invited to Manifesta with that work and he told me not to worry. He paid me in instalments. I sold *Atlas Peru* for 60,000 dollars. There were about 400 images. Market prices have changed now, but I was happy with that and I could live for a couple of years. Tom told me he was going to Manifesta to see the work; he went with Kevin Power, and both were happy'. That year Manifesta was held in Frankfurt and coincided with Documenta in Kassel. The exhibitions were only 100 kilometres apart and many people who went to Kassel also went to Frankfurt. Many people saw Fernando's work for the first time in the 400 drawings, which filled a huge space. 'This helped in a very strategic way, because for instance people from MoMA saw my work there'.

In Berlin, with a new studio, Fernando started a new chapter in his career. 'I was happy with the prospect of returning to Berlin as a professional artist, just starting out of course. I was 37 years old'. In the show at Barbara Thumm he presented his series *Vision of Western Painting*. It was like a second edition, another layer of copy to Museo de San Marcos's collection of copies. He took a picture of the framed copies and reproduced them in the original size and framed them again.
