That year he received an invitation from organisers of the Busan Biennale who had seen his work at Manifesta. 'It was my first invitation to a biennial outside Peru'. In October 2002 representatives from MoMA asked to see more of his work, so he visited their <span id="page-62-0"></span>office in Berlin. At Art Basel in 2002 MoMA bought his *South of the Border* series and new invitations began to emerge. 'I was invited to Istanbul in 2003, and to the Berlin Biennial in 2004, and started a fully international period. It was one invitation after another to biennials and other events'. In 2003 Fernando participated in the Latin American Pavilion at the Venice Biennale with Peruvian artist Gilda Mantilla. In 2005 a Tate board member, whom he had met in Basel, donated his work to Tate and bought the *Revolution* series about the Cuban revolution.

In 2009 Fernando won a scholarship to Rome, where he spent the whole year. He went with his partner, the Peruvian artist Nicole Franchy, and in 2010 they spent a year in Belgium because Nicole had won a scholarship in Ghent. In 2011 Fernando returned to Lima, and in 2012 he travelled and gave talks intensively with the travelling exhibition organised by the Museo de Arte de Lima (MALI), *Fernando: Drawing Modern History*, which circulated to the Museo de Arte Latinamericano de Buenos Aires (MALBA) and the Museo Universitario Arte Contemporáneo (MUAC) in Mexico. Despite continuing lack of support from the Peruvian government this exhibition received help from the new Peruvian brand's campaign: 'I became a Peruvian brand artist' (laughs). The exhibition was important to him because it allowed him to show his work to and communicate with more people. 'I feel that with these exhibitions at the MALBA and MUAC my work became more popular, not just at an institutional level but also for students and others who like my work. It's great to reach the people, and I can do that through art institutions'.

The exhibition at the MALI in 2011 was his first solo show since the Miró Quesada Gallery exhibition in 2001. During those ten years, his work was certainly included in group exhibitions in Lima, and the MALI had exhibited work it had bought from him several times, but he had had no solo shows in Peru at a time when he was becoming better recognised in other latitudes. An exception was the launch of *Atlas Peru* as a book. After Fernando and Jorge Villacorta had been in a tiring long process trying to release the book, the art promoter Martha Zegarra, whom Fernando met at a party given by the photographer Mario Testino in 2003',got involved with the project and the book was launched in 2005'. Juan Carlos <span id="page-63-0"></span>Verme was already the President of the MALI's board, 'At first Juan Carlos was a novelty. Once he invited me to a dinner with the American patrons of Tate in New York. It was a very dynamic market during those years; everything happened very fast. I remember Art Basel Miami in 2006, where I sold everything before the fair opened. That sort of thing was happening – now it has changed. How they buy now is different. They think ten times first. At that time everything was fast'.

Even though Fernando had studied in France and has spent more time in Europe, for him the United States is where his work has had the most successful reception in terms of acquisitions, as well as Spain. The first time he went to New York and the United States was in 2003, and since then he has been visiting regularly. He was invited to a MoMA exhibition in East Hampton, and then his work was shown at MoMA itself. Then he had different events in Pittsburgh, Minneapolis, MOCA in Los Angeles, and the Museum of Houston acquired his work. Since 2008 he has worked in New York with the Alexandre and Bonin Gallery, and had an exhibition there in 2011.

His first contacts in Spain were through Kevin Power at the exhibition at the Espai D'Art Contemporani of Castelló in 2001. In 2003 he was part of a group show at the Marlborough Gallery in Madrid, curated by Power, and his work was also exhibited at Fundación Antoni Tapies at the invitation of the curator, Nuria Enguita, in 2005. Later he started to work with the Joan Prats Gallery in Barcelona, where in 2006 he held a show featuring the series *Colonial Post*, which the Museo de Arte Contemporáneo de Castilla y León (MUSAC) later acquired. His work is also in private collections in Spain. As he had started to work with Barbara Thumm when she opened her gallery, in Spain he wanted a similar close and horizontal relationship with the gallery owner despite other, more established galleries also being interested in his work. The Joan Prats Gallery was under renovation and incorporating new artists, and Fernando thought was the best option. He remembers that a beginning was a little tension working with another gallery in Europe, but in the end it worked well.

His work with the established Lucia de la Puente Gallery in Lima was different. In 2005 he started collaborating with the Gallery via its participation at <span id="page-64-0"></span>ARCO. However, it was not until 2009 that he began to work with it on more formal terms. He had his first solo show with Lucia in 2013 in parallel to the first editions of the two art fairs in Lima, Art Lima and PArC. Fernando showed work at both art fairs, accompanied by extensive media coverage.

For Fernando, 'We [Peru] are already on the map, already visible. I think it's great'. He goes on: 'Many people buy art to have it in their houses, but there are very few collectors. There is an image based on Peru's boom, and of course on the fact that there are many Peruvian artists, more artists than ever before circulating on the international art circuits, which means that many talented people are doing things. But it seems to me that this does not necessarily have a counterpart in the local market'. He warns 'There is a somehow an artificial image. … Things have improved, but not by that much'.

During the interview Fernando told me about his and Nicole Franchy's plan to move to New York, where they married and lived from 2014 to 2017. After this experience, they decided to return to Lima and pursue their art careers from there. In Lima, after Lucia de la Puente Gallery closed in 2018, Fernando has started working with 80m2 Livia Benavides, while in Spain he left working with Joan Prats Gallery in Barcelona to join Espaivisor – the Visor Gallery in Valencia. He is still working with Barbara Thumm Gallery.

# <span id="page-65-1"></span>**2 [Democratic effervescence](#page-7-5)**

# <span id="page-65-0"></span>The renewal of Lima and artists' experimentations

A combi [urban mini bus] and colourful advertisement organised the exhibition space in which clothes printed with potatoes and corn were some of the objects for sale. The 2003 PLK Wardrobe exhibition brought the atmosphere of mass consumption and public space to the gallery, and at the same time exceeded the gallery space connecting to people via the creation of a clothing brand.

Billboards highlighting various urban manual jobs appeared in Lima, some including large portraits of such workers and information about their jobs. This 2004 project was called 'The Value of What is Useful, Visual Solutions to Social Problems'.

In the first half of the 2000s the artist Olga Engelmann developed a pioneering artistic proposal linking art, graphic design and advertising, which connected her work to a great number of people via consumer techniques that included the development of her clothing brand, PLK, and billboards in public spaces. She gave visuality and value to everyday elements and practices that characterise Lima and Peru, contributing to the generation of self-esteem and identity. Her work both fostered and was reinforced by an atmosphere of social renewal. In 2007 she was named Person of the Year by El Comercio newspaper: 'Olga Engelmann was the first to make engraving, migrant culture and clothing style a trademark of Peru's unity and diversity. This is a trend now' (El Comercio, 29 December 2007).

The recovery of democracy began a time of hope for redefinition at the political, social, cultural and material levels in Peru, intersected by a growing neoliberal economy that would gradually influence this transformation. In this and the following chapter, I examine processes, events and agents that boosted a vibrant period of experimentation and expansion of the limits of art from 2000 to 2007. In this chapter, I explore the formation of new urban subjectivities in conjunction with Lima's transformation as a modernising city, and show how artists' individual and collective initiatives registered and fuelled this powerful moment, working in and with a changing Lima, and revitalising the art scene. I argue that what occurred amidst the recovery of democracy was a strong mobilisation of agendas and emotions in terms of claiming citizenship through indigenous rights, the battles of memory and new urban subjectivities. The new democracy mobilised intercultural agendas and created a fertile arena for the art field. In Chapter 3, I explain <span id="page-66-0"></span>the consolidation of the figure of the curator in conjunction with the renovated exhibition infrastructure as part of Lima's spatial and architectural transformation and examine how curators responded to new compelling art production providing narratives for Peru's contemporary art.

# **Politics, memory and new urban subjectivities**

 After the democratic elections held by the interim president Valentín Paniagua, Alejandro Toledo took power in July 2001 and culture was mobilised at a national level. Toledo's government reinforced the neoliberal project, this time by promoting cultural diversity and exploiting indigeneity to attract tourism and foreign investment. Toledo appointed Pedro Pablo Kuczynski as finance minister and Richard Webb as president of the Central Bank. As Gonzales de Olarte (2007) explains, both were part of the 1989 Washington Consensus, which had set the global neoliberal policies, and their appointment therefore inspired international investors' trust. Peru's macroeconomy began to grow again. Optimism dominated the atmosphere, partly because of this economic growth but also because Toledo, who was born in an Andean community, migrated to the coast. He later studied at Stanford University, becoming an economist and then president of Peru, embodying the Peruvian dream of many socially excluded people – the poor, the indigenous, immigrants – who aspired to social mobilisation and economic capital by investing in their children's education. During his period in office, Toledo exploited this aspect of his identity, which linked him to Lima's migrant population of already successful businesspeople and cultural producers who were changing the city's image. The economic growth of the Andean migrant population in the capital also intersected with market agents' strategies for capturing these transformations and fuelling a new image of the nation and Lima to expand the market to include new consumers. At the same time, the government pursued a form of *neo-indigenismo* policy that included indigenous participation. Toledo's wife, the Belgian American – and later naturalised Peruvian – anthropologist Eliane Karp, became a strategic channel for native leaders to amplify their presence in the media and make themselves heard in Parliament. 'Today indigenous people are at the top of Peru's priorities', she emphasised in 2003 to the Spanish newspaper *El País* (26 April 2003). However, her prioritisation of indigeneity issues and her harsh attitude towards people with high incomes, as demonstrated by her references to the '*pituquitos de Miraflores*' (Miraflores's posh people), immediately created opponents. Yet one thing was clear: the colonial and *criollo*  Lima were being confronted by the government, by indigenous voices, by the emerging middle class, and even by the market.

The recovery of democracy brought centrality to political actors and indigenous movements, and in particular to Amazonian leaders who were demanding the protection and recognition of their land and their cultural identity in a nation that had largely ignored them. In the 1970s, the long-ignored Amazonian people began to organise to fight for their rights politically on the basis of their ethnicity. A decade later they had created national and regional Amazonian federations and <span id="page-67-1"></span>connected to the growing international indigenous movement. In the late 1990s and the first half of the 2000s, indigenous leaders were in Lima demanding their rights. Their political agency sparked the transformation of Peru's cultural representation, which had previously encompassed the coast and the Andes through notions of 'modernity' and 'tradition', respectively; it also influenced the dominant definition of the arts and Amazonian artists' aspirations to achieve a place within the arts. Indigenous movements and new curators would participate in the organisation of important exhibitions that expand the limits of art and the notion of the contemporary, as I explain in Chapter 3.

 The reclamation of memory and the quest for justice also became pivotal factors in the reconstruction of society. As a first step towards democracy, the transitional government created the Truth and Reconciliation Commission (TRC) in 2001 with the aim of investigating the country's internal armed conflict. In August 2003 the TRC's final report revealed that the conflict had resulted in 69,280 people dead and missing from 1980 to 2000, of whom over 40 per cent had been from the Andean region of Ayacucho, 75 per cent had spoken Quechua, and 79 per cent had lived in rural areas (CVR 2003). It stated that the Shining Path and Movimiento Revolucionario Tupac Amaru (MRTA) terrorist groups were ultimately responsible, but it also highlighted systematic human rights violations by the Peruvian state. It remarked on the political class's responsibility towards what had happened and civil society's indifference. Newspaper and media headlines announced, 'All of us Peruvians are responsible' (*El Comercio*, 29 August 2003). Indifference to the needs and pain of the country's most inneed people was exposed, shaking society and arousing feelings of responsibility and empathy as well as incredulity. The TRC introduced the need for justice and remembrance as a national endeavour. Towards this aim, they organised the exhibition *Yuyanapaq: Para Recordar* (To Remember) in Lima*.* As I have argued elsewhere (Borea 2004), this exhibition enabled Peruvians and particularly Limeños to engage with the 'sense of past' that the TRC proposed. Curated by Nancy Chappell and Mayu Mohanna, the exhibition, set in a derelict building featuring photographs of the civil conflict and ending on a patio with a view of the sea, was a metaphor for a ruined state and its healing; the exhibition became a ritual site of remembrance and emotional involvement. But when the TRC's work was finished, the different governments were consistently uninterested in the politics of remembrance. The creation, at the TRC's recommendation, of the Place of Memory, Tolerance and Social Inclusion, a national museum, finally became a reality after several years on the back burner. Architectural tendering did not take place until 2010, and for more than three years the museum was only a new, highly lauded, architectural project in the upmarket Lima's waterfront but without a museological discourse. The complexity of approaching the period of violence and memory from the perspective of a national museum resulted in the creation of different curatorial proposals. The museum finally opened in 2015 under Ollanta Humala's government.[1](#page-80-0) 

<span id="page-67-0"></span>Since the early 2000s, and particularly following the 2003 TRC report, various cultural-political projects ranging from exhibitions of memory to artworks, films,

<span id="page-68-2"></span><span id="page-68-0"></span> literature and academic publications have tackled the need for remembrance and justice. The TRC triggered the interest of many cultural producers in the civil war and its consequences, which became a central concern in the production of art (Vich 2015: 19) and favoured an arena to engage with work dealing with the political violence and its memory. In this context, works by Andean artists addressing memories of violence and war – even before the 2000s – gained new interest on the art scene, with the *retablo*, typically a small wooden box with two doors and two or three inner divisions containing figures made of potato paste and glue representing a particular event, as one of the most compelling art forms for the narration of violence and memory[.2](#page-80-1) *Retablo*s were originally religious artefacts representing heaven at the top division and life on earth at the bottom. The first big shift in the content and form of the *retablo* was in the representation of rituals and everyday cultural traditions, which occurred in Joaquín López Antay's work in the 1930s. The use of the *retablo* as a medium to express pain and horror was introduced in the early 1990s. A leading figure in this movement was Florentino Jiménez. This Ayacucho artist, and others usually labelled 'popular artists', revisited and materialised in their work the violence of the state's historical oppression and state and terrorist group action during the internal armed conflict that they had lived or witnessed at first hand. For Ulfe (2011) and Vich (2015), the *retablo* became a type of testimonial art denouncing social injustice and shocking events that could not be fully expressed in words. In the late 1990s, these works on violence began to circulate at events held by non-governmental organisations (NGOs) and social scientists. Gradually the *retablo* gained a place in Peru's mainstream art scene with its growing interest in political processes. Florentino Jiménez and his sons have produced compelling and striking *retablo*s expressing the brutality of the period of violence. One of Jiménez's sons is the artist and anthropologist Edilberto Jiménez, who was a TRC commissioner in Peru's southcentre area from 2002 to 2003, and worked in Chungui, one of the localities that suffered the most. His *retablo*s bring together people's testimonies and his own experience combining the modelling of figures and writing. His *Abuse of Women* (c. 2005) shows the military's cruelty to Andean women. The space inside the *retablo* simulates a mountain with two main sections: at the bottom are recurrent scenes of a soldier raping a woman; at the top, a soldier throws a beaten woman into an abyss while another drags a woman by the ear, perhaps to also throw her into the abyss. A fifth soldier is shooting a female archangel who is holding the scales of justice and a dagger. In the top corner, five women and two children, one with tied hands, are hiding from the soldiers. On the *retablo*'s doors is written the painful testimony of what happened to Antonia Ramírez Orihuela.[3](#page-80-2) The circulation of works like this since the early 2000s have acted as a reminder of what happened in Peru, and more precisely of what specific people experienced, exhorting the need for a different future.

<span id="page-68-1"></span>In the new democracy, claims for citizenship included the unmasking and combating of discrimination in racist Lima and in Peru more generally. In the early 2000s, some artists began to tackle the intricacies of racism, adding their criticism to important scholarly work produced in the 1990s about discrimination

<span id="page-69-1"></span> (e.g. Nugent 1992; Callirgos 1993, 1997; Flores Galindo 1994) and to an increasing number of reports to the police and in the media denouncing acts of racism. These works revealed various ongoing forms of racism. For instance, the artist Natalia Iguiñiz's 2001 ground-breaking photographic series, *La Otra* (The Other)*,*  shows in each picture two women, an employer and her *empleada* (housemaid), who were asked to pose together. The images display posture, ways of dressing, physical differences and similarities, distance and complicity, visualising the complex relationship of power and care. In 2004 the artists Claudia Coca and Susana Torres presented their exhibition *Peruvian Beauty: Centro de Estéticas* (Peruvian Beauty: Beauty Centre), challenging naturalised ideas of beauty in daily life and the arts and proposing a plural notion of aesthetics. In a process of appropriation and replacement that produced visual friction, their set of paintings locates their own bodies – Coca, more indigenous, and Torres, whiter – in Western classic artworks featuring white women such as Ingres' *The Valpinçon Bather* and Rubens' *The Three Graces* as well as Guaman Poma de Ayala's Andean drawings of Inca princesses. The exhibition included a performance in which a stylist changed the artists' appearance in the gallery, which had been set up a beauty parlour. A video by the artist Patricia Bueno, directed by the filmmaker Claudia Llosa, intercalated and juxtaposed this action with the artworks with messages such as 'A Greek nude is not the same as a *cholo calato* [naked *cholo*]' and 'I am who I want to be'. Later Claudia Llosa chose the non-professional Andean actress Magaly Solier to be the protagonist in her film *Madeinusa* (2006) and the international awardwinning film *La Teta Asustada* (The Milk of Sorrow, 2009), announcing radical transformations of Peruvian cinema.[4](#page-80-3) These and other projects confronted forms of racism and visual hegemony. Although events and projects such as La Molina municipality's building of a wall dividing the privileged La Molina district from the underprivileged Ate district in 2007, various social negative media comments about Magaly Solier, and the fact that housemaids were not allowed into the sea at exclusive beach clubs, which led to the *Empleada Audaz* (Brave Servant) march were reminders that racism still prevailed in Lima at a time of greater consumption of 'Peruvian creativity'.

#### <span id="page-69-0"></span>*'Cholo power': Market, agency and cultural production*

Intersecting the atmosphere and events mentioned above, new social sentiment about being *cholo* began to emerge in the early 2000s which along with the neoliberal market logic and its narrative would transform Lima. The Limeños' perception of a *cholo* as someone from the Andes who pollutes the city and avoidance of self-recognition as a *cholo* changed. Urban cultural politics changed, although not at the hands of the new mayor Luis Castañeda Lossio who, in power from 2003 to 2009 due to re-election, rejected an approach to culture in his aim to give priority to infrastructure works. Cultural politics changed with the capitalisation and marketisation of migrants' social processes in Lima into a history of success. In 2002 the publicist Rolando Arellano advised a group of businessmen to expand their markets, who then opened the Megaplaza Mall

<span id="page-70-1"></span> in North Lima, an area mainly of migrant descendants, at a time when malls traditionally targeted middle- and upper-class traditional Limeños. Surprisingly for the investors, sales exceeded those of other malls, and together with publicists they began to pay attention to this emerging middle class, its symbols and tastes, its forms of advertising and consumption. In 2004 Arellano published his influential book *Ciudad de los Reyes, los Chávez, los Quispes …* (The City of the Kings, Chavez and Quispes…)*,* a title in direct dispute with Andrade's elite urban project, in which he states that out of the awareness of traditional Limeños, migrants had acquired economic power, brought their traditions to the city and created a new middle class. His book argues for renaming large areas surrounding Lima, previously named as 'peripheric cones', as 'North Lima,' 'South Lima' and 'East Lima', emphasising the existence of multiple centralities. While urban migration and its transformation of Lima had been widely analysed by scholars and urban planners and tackled by various artists, Arellano repackaged these studies and other findings as a triumphant story at an effervescent intercultural moment and at the rise of market logics, impacting investors and publicists.

When I returned to Lima in 2004 after 18 months in Spain, I was surprised by an advertisement for telephones in Quechua and by the use of colourful iconography in publicity materials. Emerging sectors' screen-printing and lettering for advertising posters became of interest to the mass media publicity and the art circuit. Capturing this interest, journalist Benny Chueca interviewed Fortunato Urcuhuaranga in 2004: 'Whether making textiles or screen-printing, Fortunato Urcuhuaranga is an artist who has gone from handicraft to innovative graphic design. He invented the style of poster called *chicha*. His company is Viusa (Central Highway 13 km) and from there – together with his children – he distributes banners and phosphorescence'.[5](#page-80-4) In his interview with Chueca, Fortunato Urcuhuaranga explained his feelings and how he started to make banners:

<span id="page-70-0"></span>My spectacles company was called Procoinsa (Promotora Cultural Huancayo La incontrastable) and for the promotion we needed something more than radio and newspapers, and as I was knowledgeable about screen-printing, I created my banners myself. We begin with a banner for the workshop door. Each time it was bigger, until people started asking who was making them. … but I must be honest: at the beginning I was ashamed to say that I had done it. I was a cultural promoter, a conductor of radio programmes; now I was a banner-maker. But I understood that I had to take pride in my work and put a price on my service.

(*Peru 21*, 9 Oct 2004)

The media started to cover stories about achievements and triumphs such as that of Urcuhuaranga's family. In 2004 a TV series depicted the life of Dina Paucar, the main *huayno* singer at the time. The programme beat all of the ratings. Success, money, music, dresses, migration and a new canon of beauty were interlaced in this icon, who articulated the new identity, sentiments and tastes of Lima's large <span id="page-71-1"></span><span id="page-71-0"></span>migrant-descended population[.6](#page-80-5) What Paucar embodied grabbed the attention of traditional Limeños.

 Embracing and promoting this sparkling *cholo* moment, artist and curator Christian Bendayán, sociologist Santiago Alfaro, anthropologist Cesar Ramos and curator Alfredo Villar created Kacharpari Produccciones and organised the project *Sabroso, Encuentros con los Sentidos Populares* (Tasty: Encounters with the Popular Senses) in 2005. This project focused on provincial cinema, popular art, folklore and *chicha* music – these are terms that the project used – through the voices of their producers. They explained, 'We find it convenient that the protagonists of these multiple stories to be the ones who speak and tell of their experiences of producing modern art with provincial sensitivity, without intermediaries or interpreters' (in Oquendo 2005). Following this direction, in 2006 the National Library organised the colloquium *Lo Cholo en el Peru* (The *Cholo* in Peru) in its brand new building with the exhibition *Distintas visiones de la choledad* (Different Views of *Choledad*) curated by Cesar Ramos. The colloquium included round tables of mestizo scholars whose analysis was preceded by the testimony of a '*cholo'* cultural producer and entrepreneur. Beyond aiming for inclusion, the organisation of the round tables staged the difference between 'white'/mestizo academics and *cholo* producers. In her analysis of the colloquium, Gisela Cánepa (2007) highlights how it was held in a context of neoliberal economic policies and how the selected topics boosted the construction of Peruvian-ness from Lima. With its contradictions, the project contributed to positioning the renovated National Library as a platform for social encounters and for re-evaluation of other aesthetics. Thus, '*chicha/cholo* art' permeated the Lima art scene due to the impulse of curators and the media, the appropriation of this aesthetics by middle-upper-class 'white' artists and designers, and the promotion by the artists themselves during the first half of 2000. This tendency would develop novel dimensions within Susana Villaran's urban government and the re-launch of the Peruvian national brand in 2010 (see [Chapter 6](#page-201-1)), when, for instance, one of Fortunato Urcuhuaranga's son, Elliot Tupac (whose actual name is Elliot Urcuhuaranga), produced his compelling mural, *Cholo Soy* (I Am Cholo).

#### *From interculturality to creativity*

As scholars have pointed out, the perception and management of cultural diversity affects how global conditions and neoliberal practices spread in specific contexts and influence the ways in which the capacity for innovation is understood and studied. Scientists, entrepreneurs and artists are seen as the subjects of innovation and creativity in top global cities (see Florida 2002; Currid 2007). Yet some scholars have noted how low-paid workers and migrants, who are outside the creative rhetoric, are pillars of the development of these 'creative' cities (e.g. Castells 1996; Hannerz 1996). In Latin America, studies have not constrained creativity to the professional managerial class and artists; for instance the relationship between cultural diversity and global urban transformations was seen first as a process of <span id="page-72-0"></span>cultural hybridisation (see García Canclini 1995; Yúdice 2003), exalting 'popular culture's' creativity and responses to urban global processes.

The other project that was consolidated by the end of the 1990s and early 2000s was interculturality. This project articulated discussions on citizenship rights, public policies with an emphasis on education policy and decentralisation. It approached the agendas of indigenous movements in Latin America claiming an equal right to citizenship and the right to difference. Many promoters of interculturality also marked its difference from multiculturality promoted, for instance, in the United States. The influential Peruvian anthropologist Carlos Iván Degregori explains: 'For multiculturalism, tolerance is enough. Interculturality requires dialogue. I would add affective dialogue' (2002: 109). According to Fidel Tubino, interculturality needs to be understood 'as an ethic-political proposal, as a must-be-possible, as a feasible utopia' (2002: 73). In the hope of creating a new society in the post-crisis first half of the 2000s, interculturality became a feasible utopia and fusion became a creative arena for these encounters.

The mix of styles and aesthetics known as 'fusion' was perceived as a democratic materialisation of cultural hybridity and interculturality. Appropriations and cultural combinations became powerful means of cultural production and marketing. Music became the space par excellence for Peruvian fusion, where Amazonian rhythms gained relevance (see Rozas 2007). Peruvian cuisine, with its vocabulary of triumph and fusion, emerged as a materialisation of the renewed national identity through its main promoter, Gaston Acurio. Native ingredients and recipes were 'upgraded' by chefs to make new combinations and forms of *haute cuisine* that resulted in an explosion of new high-quality restaurants, first in Lima and then internationally. Later, fashion designers worked with migrant urban aesthetics and Andean and Amazonian elements, promoting native products such as alpaca wool. My argument is that throughout the transition to democracy and until the mid-2000s, interculturality and fusion were notions that organise dreams and political work about social encounters, recognitions and appropriations of what Carlos Iván Degregori defines as the 'diverse we' (see p. X). From 2004 onwards, Peru's cultural production started to be included in marketing strategies associated with notions of 'creativity' and being gradually categorised and understood as cultural industries. Fusion became an arena for grounding the neoliberal project in the uses of culture and the rise of creative industries – a way of playing in the global market by fitting in the new forms of alterity. A headline in *El Comercio*  in December 2004 celebrates how 'The fashion of the Andean *chullo* [woollen hat with earflaps] caused frenzy on the catwalks of Europe'; at the same time, the journalist Fernando Vivas wrote:

For some time now we have been getting over excited at every mention of Peru. … And the government has embarked on a strategy to introduce Toledo fully to the new trend. … In a country clinically diagnosed as having little identity, we have one that has expanded. … I'm talking about something else: of the desire to become more distinguishable as a nation while we multiply the connections with the planet; of the enthusiasm with which Peruvians, <span id="page-73-1"></span>with their previous inferiority complex compared to Mexican or Brazilian chauvinism, jumps up to say 'Hey, I'm here'.

(Vivas 2004)

 In 2006 the famous Peruvian soda Inca Kola, integrally connected with the Peruvian identity, launched its high-impact publicity campaign *Inca Kola: Peruvian Creativity*, which narrated the history of the invention of famous Peruvian artefacts such as the *chullo*, the *ceviche*, the *cajón* (a Peruvian drum), the *pisco* (a grape brandy), etc., showing first their local and then their worldwide circulation. Each advertisement ended, 'This is a Peruvian artefact that has astounded the world, but mostly makes the Peruvian people's hearts beat proudly'. This enthusiasm for Peruvian-ness grew rapidly in the following years. The consolidation of the creativity project came with Peru's economic growth in 2008, and from 2010 it reached new heights with the launch of the new Peruvian brand and the entrepreneur narrative. The conjunction of the growing neoliberal economy and the reinforcement of 'Peruvian creativity' at market, media and political levels strengthened Peruvians' self-esteem. Yet I argue that the possibility of an intercultural space in the field of cultural production (Myers 2002: 6) lost its political force when it became a formula for creativity. This gradual displacement would play out in the cultural field and the arts by moving from experimentation as politics towards celebration of the political. Creativity has become a standardised prototype that works in the localglobal economy. In [Chapter 6](#page-201-1), I return to this issue with a look at the mechanisms in which the rhetoric on creativity and market-driven logic superimpose as the dominant form of art organisation and imagination in Lima.

# **Relocating the city: Facing the ocean and new narratives of Lima**

<span id="page-73-0"></span>The transformation of urban subjectivities and imaginaries occurred in interrelation with the transformation of the physical Lima. While the heart of the colonial and *criollo* Lima is Lima Centro (see [Chapter 1](#page-31-1)), the face of modernising Lima is the waterfront. Lima turned to face the ocean. In 1970 the municipality of Lima began to implement its long-term plan to gain ground to the sea and green Lima's dry and abandoned seashore. The Costa Verde (the Green Coast) project was embraced by the six Lima districts along the coast[.7](#page-80-6) Despite many setbacks the project gained new dimensions by the 2000s with real estate development along the waterfront and the new social and economic value of living by the sea. The opening of Larco Mar Mall, an entertainment and shopping centre, which replaced a traditional park on the Miraflores waterfront in 1998, marked Lima's new directionality. Real estate companies began the transformation of the waterfront with luxury apartments and district mayors worked on new embellished promenades. Public art became part of the equation. Surfing acquired higher status particularly after the victory of the Peruvian surfer Sofia Mulanovich in the 2004 World Championships, which gave rise to the installation of surfing academies. Later it would come the establishment of paragliding facilities. This reorientation of the <span id="page-74-1"></span>city favoured a new central axis, with Miraflores as the recreational and commercial district, San Isidro as the financial district and Barranco as the art district. In 2000, the new Interbank building opened in San Isidro, its architectural design soon becoming a symbol of the new Lima financial district. Other new and refurbished buildings started to transform Lima's financial hub. In 2011 a new key landmark was built: the five-star Westin Hotel. In 2014 the main Peruvian bank, Banco de Crédito, replaced the concrete front of its headquarters with a transparent light-interactive façade, unthinkable in the 1990s when the building was a target for Shining Path terrorist attacks. Wiley Ludeña explains, 'While in times of terror these buildings became fortresses with an enclosed design, today they have been uncovered to look fragile, and moreover with a second luminal skin' (*Somos*, 17 May 2014). The bohemian district of Barranco gradually replaced Miraflores as the principal neighbourhood for art galleries, studios and new art museums, generating an ongoing gentrification. In particular the Lucia de la Puente Gallery's move to a refurbished Barranco old *casona* in 2002 was a milestone in the physical relocation of the art market and its forms of operations, as I discuss later. Old buildings were refurbished as art spaces: MATE (the Mario Testino Art Museum), inaugurated in 2012, was the epitome of this process. The consolidation of San Isidro, Miraflores and Barranco circuit benefited the new urban modes of consumption and the lifestyles of the traditional and new high-incomers, particularly young people, as result of Peru's economic growth. This became more evident around 2008 with the growing number of sophisticated restaurants, the explosion of new boutique shops, expensive brand stores, more luxury apartments and the rise of the contemporary art market. Peruvian creativity catered perfectly to the tastes of the new middle-high classes and urban styles.

<span id="page-74-0"></span> As a city, Lima increasingly became the subject of cultural narratives and forms of representation.[8](#page-80-7) The new narratives ranged from celebratory approaches to critical perspectives, including the launch of a TV programme about the city's history, *A la Vuelta de la Esquina* (Just Around the Corner) in 2005, which would have been inconceivable previously and which is still on air. In 2006 two books called for Lima's rediscovery. The journalist and travel writer Rafael León's *Lima Bizarra: Antiguía del Centro de la Capital* (Bizarre Lima: Anti-Guide to the Capital's Centre) depicts Lima beyond its colonial heritage by leading a traveller, understood to be a Limeño inhabitant, into hybrid modern Lima. The other, *Polis: Visiones y Versiones de Lima a Inicios del Siglo 21* (Polis: Visions and Versions of Lima at the Beginning of the 21st Century), edited by Gary Leggett, offers multiple approaches to Lima, examining the city through its fragments and exploring people's senses of belonging. A year later the renowned international photographer Mario Testino published his book *LIMA*, which includes various artists and poets' images of the city. Lima and its inhabitants became a focus of cultural production, not as an exception but as a crucial arena of inquiry. Visual art, literature and films began to register and address Lima as an actor with its history, transformations, tensions and new subjectivities from points of view ranging from celebratory to critical. This cultural production fed the sense of Lima in transformation.

<span id="page-75-2"></span><span id="page-75-0"></span>In her project *Postcards Lima\*Peru* (2002–2007), Gilda Mantilla refocuses the city. Far from its sea-facing blue-sky image, one postcard shows a panoramic view taken from an impoverished site in the mountains surrounding Lima under the city's recurrent grey sky. Rather than promoting new buildings and refurbished colonial houses, another postcard features unfinished but inhabited houses with naked iron rods protruding from the roof announcing the dream of the second floor. Mantilla presented this work as part of Peru's participation at the 50th Venice Biennale in 2003.[9](#page-80-8) Also making use of touristic technologies, a group of artists proposed an alternative promotional map of the city. They participated in the last Ibero-American Lima Biennial in 2002. Selected by the Spanish curator Estrella de Diego, the Spanish artist Rogelio López Cuenca decided to work in collaboration with the Peruvian artists Alex Ángeles, Giuseppe de Bernardi, Marco Durán, Natalia Iguiñiz, Carlos León, Iván Lozano, Alfredo Márquez, Giuliana Migliori, Cecilia Noriega, Javier Vargas and Alice Vega, who grouped themselves under the name TUPAC\*CAPUT. The project emphasised events and situations of pain, political violence and celebration in Lima during the previous 30 years which were not included in conventional and tourist maps of the city[.10](#page-80-9) The artists expanded Lima's imagination by including and marking other areas and histories in a project that saw itself as an action and open-ended process. I want to note here that the sense of Lima that these artists proposed captivated me, and I included the *LIMA I[NN]MEMORIAM* map as part of my mobile archive in my own trajectories.

<span id="page-75-1"></span> In 2006, the year that *Lima Bizarre* and *Polis* were published, a compelling exhibition entitled *Urbe y Arte: Imaginarios de Lima en transformación 1980– 2005* (City and Art: Imaginaries of Lima in Transformation 1980–2005) was held at the Museo de la Nación. The architect Pablo Dam, the musical researcher Luis Alvarado and the curators Jorge Villacorta and Augusto del Valle with the young curators Sharon Lerner and Miguel López grouped and analysed artistic and cultural works focusing on Lima over the previous 25 years and examined the links between the visual arts, music and architecture. When this exhibition opened I was working at the National Institute of Culture at the Museo de la Nación, and I remember visiting and revisiting this ambitious project, which materialised the significance of thinking Lima for the curatorial practice, at a time when, as I explain in the next chapter, curators were mainly working locally while producing key narratives for the arts.

# **Artist-run initiatives: Working together/ working closely, explorations and parties**

Artists felt the need to react to the rapid social and physical transformation of Lima and Peru in real time and an urgency to explore using different media. Some found that an abandoned family home, a deserted school or a lavatory were the right places for their individual and collective explorations and their engagement with the art circuit, the use of different media, the city and its inhabitants.

There were long queues outside an abandoned house in Miraflores in 2000. *Casa* (House) by the photographer and artist Jose Carlos Martinat was a site-specific <span id="page-76-0"></span>work. The house his family owns and lived in for decades in Miraflores became a place for his reflections and memories through the use of multiple languages. Pictures of objects conveyed a sense of loss, amplified by video and sound. Its novelty on the local circuit attracted many people. Martinat commented, 'That show made me experiment with media that I had never thought to use. Everything happened intuitively and by necessity. It affected much of what I do now, because I experimented with audio, space, sound, smells. I used a motion sensor for the first time, which opened the doors for me to all the technology projects I have done since' (interview, June 2015). It was his first individual show; his second, as a prominent Peruvian contemporary artist, followed ten years later in 2010 at the Revolver Gallery.

In this atmosphere of experimentation, other artists grouped together in artistrun spaces and projects. Working together, working closely, working with people from other disciplines, working through different media, working in public spaces, working and partying were means that triggered their explorations. A lavatory became a good place to work on new ideas and projects. In one of the few bookshops dedicated to art and culture, La Casa Verde, owned by Teresa Gruenberg, the artists Philippe Gruenberg and Pablo Hare turned the 2 × 2 m space into the Galería del Escusado (the Toilet Gallery, 2002–2003). They brought together different experimental projects such as Luz María Bedoya's ephemeral water writing on the toilet walls and Integro XO's site-specific performance of a healing ritual shown on the bookshop's TV screens. The gallery and its exhibitions challenged the agendas of established galleries just metres away, such as the established Forum Gallery and the traditional art market at large. Other initiatives, such as Festival Arte sin Argollas (Art without Rings), held in an abandoned school in Central Lima and in parallel with the third Lima Biennial, challenged the organisation of the art circuit. Organised by people in theatre, dance and visual art, this event questioned the small group that managed the art scene and the narrow public for which the arts operated, advocating that art should engage with society at large and produce social change. In a TV programme, the artist Jorge Miyagui explained:

 If we're talking about art that seeks importance and to create social change, painting pretty pictures and doing conventional neat performance shows is not enough. Further action is necessary. An attitude that is subversive and transformative is necessary. The problems of artistic institutions concern a very small group, the little art world which is only in the interests of artists and critics. I'm talking about small daily changes. When we all realise that we're making history every day; all those who reproach you as a pretentious wit *because they think utopia is impossible*. … I ask you, what would this world be if the hippie counterculture, the feminist movement or May '68 had never existed?

(*Sentidos* 2002; italics mine)

Most of the artist-run spaces – Tupac, Drama, Proyecto Barrio and La Culpable – were in Barranco. According to the artist and manager of Casa Tupac, Giuseppe

<span id="page-77-0"></span>de Bernardi, although the Lima art scene was precarious and very little was going on there was an effervescent atmosphere for doing things. He told me 'Tupac came from a blast of urgency. It was created in 2001, when shit was happening. … Many artists were leaving, in the brain drain. … I feel I am part of a network of active citizens' (interview June 2015). Tupac operated in de Bernardi's large family property, originally owned by his Italian grandfather. De Bernardi moved there in 2001 when he started to receive advance payment for his art, after the socialite magazine *Cosas* revealed that collectors George and Teresa Gruenberg owned one of his works. 'I lived with my father. I was *mil oficios* [a Peruvian expression that means someone who does many small jobs to survive]. Nobody gave you anything'. The condition for using the house was maintaining it, and for 15 years it survived despite financial limitations. With his decision to stay in Lima de Bernardi rejected an offer to study in Paris. The *Lima I[nn]Memoriam* project for the third Lima Ibero-American Biennial, discussed above, was already in process. The Spanish artist Rogelio López Cuenca and the members of Tupac Caput coordinated the project via the Internet, for which de Bernardi had to use an Internet booth: 'It was a pre-Internet time for many of us'. They also gathered at de Bernardi's place. Motivated by discussions that arose during the project, he called the house Tupac. He told me: 'Thinking in the long term, I opted for TUPAC: five letters summarising an idea of cultural resistance'. Tupac Amaru I was the last Inca leader of the Vilcabamba dynasty in the 16th century, when the Spanish were already in Inca territory; Tupac Amaru II was the name taken by an indigenous cacique in a powerful indigenous revolution against Spanish rule from 1737 to 1781 that triggered an indigenous renaissance. Later, the image of Tupac Amaru II was mobilised by Velasco Alvarado's military government as a symbol of reform, and the name was also taken by a terrorist group that operated from 1984 to 1997. In short, Tupac Amaru is related to issues of rebellion and Andean indigenous power. De Bernardi explained: 'Today Peru loves itself; before it was not like that. When I chose that name my friends told me "You're a fool. You want the project to be respected! You're crazy!"' (interview, June 2015). After the third Ibero-American Lima Biennial, Tupac art space was launched: in a party atmosphere, each of the artists who occupied the house opened their studio. In de Bernardi's words, 'there was this independent thing, which we have on another scale now'. At the time 'there was a gap between the Biennial and us, the citizens; it was all very precarious'. Tupac began as a studio and living space but soon became exclusively a place to work. Early on, another art collective, Perú Fábrica, moved into this space. As de Bernardi recalled, 'it was a beautiful moment, a very active time'. Those who had a studio began to gather together and created a space of independent management: a work culture. They created a residency programme for Peruvians and foreigners, held an annual auction and threw parties.

Drama was another active artist-run space. Its artists belong to a younger generation than de Bernardi's. In their last year of art school in 2003, Miguel Andrade from the Católica University and Alberto Borea from Corriente Alterna, the anthropology student Giancarlo Gomero and the poet Kennet O'Brian got together with the aim of finding a big space to work in and be

around people they would like to share with, experiment together and to throw parties. 'Our parties were a blast! Many artists came. Even Llona and Tola [renowned established artists]. We met them there', Alberto Borea told me. For a year and half they rented a large rundown house on the Miraflores waterfront. They rented out spaces to other young artists and producers and set up exhibitions in a central space. They started to curate their own shows and invited new young curators who had begun to work on the art circuit (see Chapter 3). With other friends such as the now theoretical musician Jaime Oliver, anthropologist Mauricio Delfin and Jose Aburto from Realidad Visual, they explored multimedia projects and experimental music in a combination of poetry, music and installations. However, to make way for the growing real estate development along Miraflores waterfront, the house was demolished and replaced by a luxury 20-storey apartment building. Before moving out they organised the edgy project-party *Demolition,* with performances and experimental music. In 2004 they moved to Barranco, where they operated almost under the same logic: working closely and exchanging ideas, setting up exhibition projects and parties. Drama brought together a new generation of artists, among them a strong group of women artists including Raura Oblitas, Maya Watanabe, Ana Cecilia Farah and Elena Damiani. As Alberto Borea is my brother, I was close to this young and frenetic project. Drama closed in 2008.

 Galería del Escusado, the project in the lavatory mentioned above, became a precedent for Espacio La Culpable (The Guilty Space). La Culpable lasted from 2002 to 2007 with two different periods. In 2008 it re-opened as La Ex-Culpable and lasted until 2011. In the first period, it operated as a workshop where artists explored with different media and projects, individually and together, looking at and engaging with the city and particularly with Barranco through projects and parties with other grassroots collectives such as Project Barrio (the Neighbourhood Project). At this point, La Culpable comprised the artists Pablo Hare, Philippe Gruenberg, Gilda Mantilla, Raymond Chaves and Luz María Bedoya – artists who had started to work professionally in the second half of the 1990s. In the second period from 2005, La Culpable intensified its strategy of participating in the public sphere by interrelating artistic production with critical discussion. It included the curators Rodrigo Quijano and Miguel López. Later the artist Eliana Otta joined the group. They wanted to create a space for thinking artistically and theoretically about the arts and the art scene (Cartagena 2008). One of their crucial mechanisms was presenting artists' portfolios in *Conversando con la Culpable* (Talking with La Culpable). I remember sitting on the floor with many people from artists to museum directors drinking beer, listening and looking at the artists' presentation of their work and explanation of their trajectories. As Philippe Gruenberg (2009: 57) wrote, La Culpable enabled an arena for 'discussion and horizontal exchange, without protocols, without hierarchies, more creative and festive'. The presentation of portfolios offered a closer idea of what Peruvian artists, mainly Limeños, living in the country and abroad were doing; they also invited foreign artists who were in Lima. For instance, the Peruvian artists David Zi Yink and Fernando Bryce participated in *Conversando con La Culpable* in January 2007. Both were living abroad with growing international trajectories but were little known in Lima in that time.

In this atmosphere, art students also felt the need to engage with the new exploration of and debates on art. In 2001 students of the School of Fine Arts occupied the school for three months demanding new professors, a new curriculum and an end to political interventionism. With a new director and enthusiasm for renovation, the students, coordinated by Guillermo Quiroz and Jucali Durand, organised three National Art Conferences between 2001 and 2007 – 'Where Are We Going?: Roles of the Artist in Peru' (2001), 'Between Institutions and Independent Groups' (2004) and 'Art and Politics: The Symbolic as Power and Strategy for Social Change' (2007) – that captured crucial transformations in Peru's art scene. Following a change of director in 2007, students took over the School again and another restructuring period started. That year the last edition of the conferences was held. Private education was not exempt from flaws and students' initiatives. Between 2003 and 2005 a group of students from Católica University published three editions of a magazine called *Prótesis*. In the words of Nancy La Rosa, one of its members, 'We felt that the magazine was a place of learning. Due to educational shortcomings in the field of theoretical discussion of art at the Art Department, the journal functioned as a prosthesis' (interview, June 2015). Students Nancy La Rosa, Sharon Lerner, Eliana Otta, Santiago Quintanilla, Juan Salas and Miguel López were part of *Prótesis* – after finishing art school around 2005 they would become part of the new generation of artists and curators. Despite its short life this publication gave them the chance to get familiar with the work of various artists, to interview those whose work they liked, to meet curators and network and to learn organisational skills:[11](#page-80-10) 'We did everything in the magazine. It was also a space for learning about organization and management. … It was great', La Rosa remembered.

<span id="page-79-0"></span>Tupac, Drama and La Culpable together with artist-run publications and other events, became vibrant platforms for the exploration of new forms of art and gatherings that shaped and fuelled a powerful moment of renewal on the Lima art circuit in the first half of the 2000s. While this was a precarious time for many of the artists economically with the shrinkage of the previous art market and the cancellation of private financial support in the early 2000s, it was a vivid moment of individual and collective exploration and social encounters. Conversely, it was a moment for the rapid expansion of real estate projects that would reinforce the relocation of Lima towards the sea, favouring the image of a modernising contemporary Lima. Artists, filmmakers and writers began to address Lima as a protagonist in their cultural production. Artists felt the urgency to respond to the shifting conditions of the city and the arts and for which they grouped together in self-managed art spaces and other initiatives. With a sense of freedom and experimentation, they crossed discipline boundaries in new media explorations and took control of the presentation and mobilisation of their work and of its forms of social engagement. Working together and sharing knowledge and skills, artists became managers, curators, installers, editors, organisers of events and programmes. Artist-run initiatives and spaces reconfigured the Lima art scene, although not from a parallel or alternative position to the main contemporary art circuit – as such initiatives are usually understood today (e.g. Besson 2017). Cultural centres (see Chapter 3) and these artist-run initiatives were the main arenas where contemporary art developed in a city where the art museums and the art market platform were still weak.

# **Notes**

- <span id="page-80-0"></span>[1 T](#page-67-0)he interdisciplinary curatorial team that provided the narratives with which the museum opened included the curator Jorge Villacorta, the artist Natalia Iguiñiz and the scholars Ponciano del Pino and Victor Vich, with the artists Eliana Otta and Santiago Quintanilla and the scholar Alejandra Hibbett as assistants.
- <span id="page-80-1"></span> [2](#page-68-0) Other compelling artforms addressing the period of violence are the *Tablas de Sarhua* These are painted boards from the Andean Ayacucho Sarhua district that originally were given as a present when someone completed the building of their new house narrating the family story. Later, the *Tablas* included the narration of other events such as processes of migration and the times of political violence, particularly through the work of Primitivo Evanan and the Sarhua Popular Artists Association (Asociación de Artistas Populares de Sarhua, ADAPS).
- <span id="page-80-2"></span>[3](#page-68-1) Edilberto Jimenez (2005) also uses drawings and texts to express the terror experienced by Andean people.
- <span id="page-80-3"></span>[4](#page-69-0) For an explanation of Peru's contemporary cinema in relation to issues of identity, violence and new approaches, see Sarah Barrow (2018).
- <span id="page-80-4"></span> [5](#page-70-0) El Comercio's *Publicidad en General: Las Estrategias de la Calle* (Publicity in General: Strategies from the Street, 2006) focuses on the aesthetics of emergent sectors' publicity and makes evident the influence of this production at different levels.
- <span id="page-80-5"></span>[6](#page-71-0) Santiago Alfaro (2015) studies the success of the genre of *huayno* with harp and the role of the singer Dina Paucar in the rise of this music and its industry.
- <span id="page-80-6"></span>[7](#page-73-0) The Costa Verde project was proposed by architect Ernesto Aramburu Menchaca, the mayor of Miraflores in the 1960s. Lima's relation to the sea began to materialise in the early 1970s, gaining more beaches and creating parks. In 1995 the Costa Verde Master Plan 1995–2010 was drafted and from the middle of the first decade of the 2000s, various luxury apartments were built along the waterfront.
- <span id="page-80-7"></span>[8](#page-74-0) Laura Poldaski's compelling book (2004) explores the transformation of Buenos Aires in its material and cultural representation during 1960. Tracing interrelations among the material city, urban cultural production, social relations and national policies, she shows how they 'all interact productively, shaping one another' (2004: x) in the construction of Buenos Aires. Poldaski explains how the renewal of the urban environment and the process of democratisation enabled some institutions to provide 'a less elite, more integrated vision of Argentina', although Buenos Aires's new cultural production and its reconceptualisation formed the basis of a new hegemonic project (2004: 7).
- <span id="page-80-8"></span>[9](#page-75-0) It is important to note that artists Gilda Mantilla and Fernando Bryce were selected to be at the Italo Latin American Institute Pavilion at the Venice Biennale, which provided a presence at the biennale for Latin American countries that did not have a national pavilion. However, Mantilla received very little financial support, and no support from Peru's government, just the Pisco sour for the opening. She had to sell her car to cover the curator's flight ticket and other expenses, showing how precarious economic support of the art scene was at the time. Mantilla's participation, with Raimond Chaves, at Peru's new pavilion at the Venice Biennale in 2015 took place within a very different moment.
- <span id="page-80-9"></span>[10](#page-75-1) For a detailed analysis of the Lima I[NN]Memoriam project, see Dorota Biczel (2013).
- <span id="page-80-10"></span>[11](#page-79-0) They held interviews with the artists Milagros de la Torre, Fernando Bryce, Miguel Aguirre, Christian Bendayán, Runcie Tanaka, Alfredo Márquez, Sergio Zevallos, Alejandro Jaime and Mayu Mohanna.

# <span id="page-81-0"></span>**[Trajectory](#page-7-6)**

# [Natalia Iguiñiz](#page-7-6)

Many artists who finished their studies in the mid-1990s and early 2000s participated in the manifestations to revoke Fujimori's dictatorship, and became actors of the effervescent moment of explorations that began with the new democracy. In precarious conditions, artists promoted different types of art initiatives and collectives. Various artists also decided to teach at art schools and take other jobs in parallel to their art practice. Some opted to produce art and found the value of their work in its engagement with the neighbourhood and other specific communities. This grounded mobility, with a social or environmental agenda, enriched the spectrum of the Peruvian art scene. Other artists went abroad, including Armando Andrade Tudela to London and Sandra Gamarra and Miguel Aguirre to Spain, and created international networks.

The art practice of Natalia Iguiñiz, born in 1973, has had a different trajectory of circulation from practices related to an art market circuit. Her practice engages with Peru's population, in particular with women, and has had a wide impact both on Lima and on Peru at large. This section is based primarily on a twoand-a-half-hour conversation with Natalia at the Centre of the Image in Miraflores on 7 February 2014, and on my pre-existing relationship with her and her work. In our conversation she emphasised the transformation of her work, its circuits of mobility, the anxiety associated with its circulation, how motherhood affects her decision-making, and gender inequality.

Natalia's career has been clear since she was young: she would go to university after finishing school. Her dilemma was in choosing between graphic design and painting. Her father worked for an NGO where he used a printing press where she saw the mixing of colours, the types of inks and paper used, etc. Natalia began to study graphic design at Católica University in 1990 but saw that for graphic design she needed solid manual precision and she 'had a strong personal urge to express life. I never finished studying design; I changed to painting, which at the time was about colour. When I was a little girl I ordered my stuff by its colours'. For Natalia, the Department of Arts provided her with what she was looking for: 'I got into a very traditional university department where the starting point was art as something individual, something self-absorbed and introspective, and in that sense it coincided totally with my personal need to express my deepest feelings. In that sense I fitted perfectly into the school, and I got in'. Until she finished her studies in 1995, Natalia's work was about self-representation. But then something changed: 'Issues related to being female and the practice of sexuality in a society like Lima began to appear. These issues began to emerge beyond the conventional self-portrait. There are a series of frictions in relation to what can be said, what cannot be said, how much you can expose yourself, that sort of thing'. She started to be aware that what was happening was not only happening to her.

Natalia finished university in 1995. 'There was a small graduate student exhibition scene. Several galleries organised such exhibitions. This hardly happens any more'. These various shows were the institutional support that accompanied artists who had just completed their studies. The career path for graduate students included participating in every group show and competition going. Natalia points out that 'the exhibitions did not usually have a conceptual framework as they do today, with themes. In those days 'Young Talents', 'PUCP Graduates', were the themes'. According to her the art scene was very dynamic in terms of art spaces. 'Even restaurants had a gallery on the second floor, and that sort of thing', and the Banco de Crédito and the Johnnie Walker Awards were very important but existed only briefly. This art scene was also very closed and Católica University's students were advantaged: 'I don't know if they were extremely elitist. We were together with the [public] School of Fine Arts in only a couple of galleries. At the time the Católica University was the most important art school. Corriente Alterna was not yet taking part [it was only founded in 1992]. It was Católica University and the School of Fine Arts. In the Art Week [at her university] we had a soccer game between schools. Today the students from the Católica don't know where the School of Fine Arts is. In the best scenario they have friends at Corriente Alterna. Class issues have widened the gap. I had very good friends at Fine Arts. I hung out with Claudia Coca and Emilio Santisteban. I frequently met up with Marcel Velaochaga. There were even galleries that brought us together. A gallery named Signos opened and the curators were, practically, Gustavo [Buntinx] and Jorge [Villacorta]. Jorge truly cared about the new graduates right from the beginning'. She met Jorge Villacorta when he judged several of the awards. Later they became friends: 'He has always befriended young artists. We had a ritual: we went to eat a *Chifa* [Peruvian Chinese food] at Christmas. He said things about your work and gave you advice. We had long talks about art and painting in general. I remember when we finished school Micki [Miguel] Aguirre and her girlfriend and I hung out with Jorge. There was Max [Hernández], who was becoming more defined as a critic, and we hung out with him too; Rodrigo [Quijano] was around. It was a small world, very enclosed, the Católica University one, but also very busy'.

Natalia started to get tired of this art circuit and its arrangement. 'I started to think that we could be together for another reason than because we'd finished our studies or because we were the young talent.' The other reason she got tired of this circuit was that 'at the beginning it was something that looked very institutional, like everything was channelled. But it was so narrow, so small, that it was the same people all the time'. In addition to the various group shows the early career path included a solo exhibition. Artists had to secure an art space for their solo show. 'The gallery that has always been the *non plus ultra* was the Miró Quesada [in Miraflores]. If you managed to get your individual show there, it was excellent'. Natalia participated in several small projects at commercial and non-commercial galleries, and her first solo show came three years after her graduation, at the Forum Gallery.

Before that, she participated in the First Ibero-American Lima Biennial in 1997 in the Emerging Artists section: 'I made my first installation because Luis Lama invited younger artists to work on installations at the Casa Barbieri, where I put a painting, a mirror, and the easel that I used for painting. It was like an optical illusion with the reflection in the mirror of whoever stood there next. It was called *Self Portrait with Passenger Companion*. It was based on my personal relationships at the time: the guys came and went in your life, which made me anxious'. Her solo show in 1998 was predominantly of painting, and almost the entire exhibition sold. She continues 'hence I felt that I'd had enough of the self-portrait and the selfreferentiality had reached its peak. At that time I had already worked on the theme of *La Perdida* [the Lost One] (1998), a thing that was less me, me, me, more playing with the stereotyping of how a woman who has a freer life in terms of her sexuality is seen and sees herself. I liked to play with the idea of *la perdida*'. At the time Natalia almost had an abortion, but she lost the baby first. 'Because I had personally faced the issue of abortion, I then created a work that, for me, was the breaking point from all those exhibitions'.

After her solo show Natalia worked on a photographic series of her sitting on the toilet with her white pants down, taken in different bathrooms, showing the anxiety of waiting for her period. She interviewed several girls about this topic. The work was featured at the group exhibition *El Último Lustro* (The Last Lustrum) at the First National Lima Biennial in 1998, where it won her an award. Her work consisted of 'an installation using headphones over which girls explained, with all their feelings, what pregnancy or abortion meant to them. You saw queues of students, queues of schoolgirls and boys wanting to hear the other side of the story. Then, and there, interpersonal and social relations emerged as a possibility, and then came *La Perra* [*The Whore*, another project]'.

The change in the direction of her work towards interpersonal and social relations occurred in conjunction with the new networks she was building through her work. She produced and channelled her work in order to reach people beyond the art circuit: 'This is what I care about. I didn't want to just exorcise my crap, I also wanted to know what people thought, but individuals; I didn't want a sociologist to tell me about gender relations, the coast, the highlands and the Amazon. I didn't want not to tell my story. I wanted to be a channel for everyone to tell the stories that are lived alone with so much anguish, ignorance and brutality. Something happened to me that made me think how someone can feel that it's their right to come and tell you that you're a bitch, just like that, with that violence. I was about eighteen to twenty years old, and you look around the world, thinking there cannot be that much aggression. Then, came *La Perra* [a project] with the sentence 'If you walk down the street and they scream "bitch" at you'. I was happy doing that. I have the soul of a sociologist. My whole family are sociologists. That big world of the university was crucial as a first stage of my formation as an individual. At first my networks were more to do with the art galleries and art awards in Lima, with their precariousness and detachment from the world, also because at the time nobody spoke of residencies or anything; you had no idea what was going on abroad. For me the networks that I built later were about not the art circuit but feminist NGOs. I started to work with all the feminist NGOs, with Manuelas [Manuela Ramos], Demus, with Floras [Flora Tristan], all of them. I began to participate in debates because Manuelas disagreed with the *La Perra* project while Las Floras supported it. This was around 1999 or 2000. Then I began a strand of work that I'm still doing, I started making posters for them that firstly were communicational. By 2001 I had *La Otra* [The Other] exhibition. Those were portraits based on what we talked about before. When I moved in with my boyfriend, he developed an allergy to washing powder, and in an act of love, as I washed my pants with soap I also washed his. I did it for love and I wondered what would happen if I had to do it for money if I could do nothing else for a living. This triggered *La Otra*. Another opportunity that opened up for me was working with the Domestic Workers Union. I started working with Casa de Panchita and with the Centro de Capacitación de Trabajadoras del Hogar (Centre for Training Domestic Workers), I was happy, and I began to make posters supporting their claims. Being in a room with thirty housemaids all telling me their stories was all that mattered. No one financed me for all this of course. The NGOs paid for the paper; for the poster they only paid you about 200 dollars. No one thought about what exhibition those posters were going to be hung at, or which international competition would participate, nothing, nothing'.

*La Perra* project was a poster with an image of a dog (*perro/a* in Spanish) that stated at the top 'If you walk down the street and they scream "perra" at you [which also means whore] they're right, because you're wearing a very short and provocative skirt.' Natalia and the sociologist Sandro Venturo, who was her partner for many years, worked on this project as the collective *La Perrera*. They put the posters up in the streets as an arena for communication and debate. In addition to how people commented on and reacted to the posters themselves there was an email address for further comments. The results of this work were featured as *perrahabl@: Calles de Lima y Calla* (Lima Street and Silence) at the Museo de la Nación in 1999. However, after the exhibition opened a congresswoman categorised the poster as suspicious and suggested that its authors should be investigated (Reyna 1999). This sparked huge debate among feminist groups, artists and others, across and beyond disciplines. Natalia explored the enormous potential of the street and the arts beyond the gallery. At the Second National Lima Biennial in 2000 La Perrera took part with their project COM.PE. This work also involved the use of the Internet and phrases that targeted people's common prejudices. It was featured at Casa de América de Madrid in 2001.

The possibility that Natalia has had to become more independent of the market and the international artist's career path has been largely due to her teaching. Having a regular income allowed her to explore beyond the limits of the established art scene. But she doesn't complain about the system itself, which, as she argues, provides artists with other possibilities. She needs a regular income and a more established residency without constantly having to travel because she is the mother of two young children. But let's go back.

Natalia began teaching early. As a student she worked as a teaching assistant, and as soon as she had finished university she started to teach: 'I don't know if it was a double-edged sword. … I got the job in the Católica and I still have it. I've never left the university [laughs]. I began teaching at Corriente Alterna. Immediately Lucho [Luis Lama] called me there. I made my living from education from the start, and eventually I sold things at exhibitions'. From 2002 to 2018 she also taught at the Centro de la Imagen.

Talking about collectors and the art market, she explains her priorities to me: 'It's difficult for me to make a career selling my work to art collectors and then tomorrow you're in a museum, etc. … But to sell something, yes, that's fine. Overall what satisfies me the most is the possibility that my work will provoke conversation, real-time discussion between people. If I create a work, it's to bring a topic or an agenda into the conversation'. For instance in her exhibition *Pequeñas Historias de Maternidad 2* (Small Stories of Motherhood 2) she engaged in conversations with many mothers. 'I felt I made a show for moms'. Some men wrote in the comment's book that she was an exhibitionist for showing her breast in the pictures, while some mothers approached to her to tell her how they breastfed their baby in public toilets and shops: 'The conversation at that exhibition was a collective catharsis', she tells me.

Natalia is not represented by any gallery. Since her first show at Forum she has had a relationship with it, but she knows that her current work would not interest the Forum's buyer. She was also close to the Vertice Gallery when the curator Miguel Zegarra worked there. 'He was a fan, we were mates, and he got me into a lot of exhibitions. We went to *Vía Satélite*  [a travelling exhibition]. I did a lot of things with Miguel [Zegarra]. When younger critics like Miguel López began to get stronger my work started to see another type of circulation, because [younger curators] are more sensitive to more political art, to something more related to archives, more testimonial, linked to the political life. They began to have that eye'. Nowadays Natalia's work has more vectors of circulation, promoted by a younger generation of curators and art dealers who suggest to collectors 'Buy Natalia Iguiñiz'. Some of them were her students; others, like Cecilia Jurado, who has helped Natalia with her photographic projects, are friends. Now Jurado has a gallery in New York. 'With the exception of the initial moment when I finished university, it is only now – twenty years later – that I have started to somehow connect again, when people who are more akin to me began to become part of the institutions, like Sharon Lerner at the Museo de Arte de Lima. They bought some of my old pictures that nobody was going to buy. Some collectors began to be interested in some things I'd done, some friends started to buy posters I'd made ten or fifteen years ago'. While not inclining towards the art market, Natalia thinks it is important that collectors are acquiring works of hers that previously they would not buy. Although, her aim is that her work circulates broadly and to have the possibility to being copy. She insists that the art market reduces both the ability to reproduce and the distribution rights, just because a few people have bought an artwork at stratospheric prices. 'I don't understand, at least I'm not working for that. … I'm not saying I won't be satisfied if my work sells. Of course I will. But the question is to whom it is sold, for what – what life would you want it to have?' Natalia is concerned about the circulation of her work. Although art dealers tell her that she cannot control it she reaffirms 'But I know why I do my work, I know the sense of it, and if tangentially the MALI can have some of my posters and protect them, good. … This is something only institutions can do. You can't close your eyes and say "I'm not working with institutions because one of the trustees is a mine owner", or "I don't relate to this institution because it pays its workers too little, or because their patrons pay their children's nannies less than I do, even though they have much more money". If you do so you couldn't live. But you choose your battles and certain things are non-negotiable'.
