When we talked about dreams of having work in an important international museum, she affirms 'I'm not doing what I need to do for that. But if someday MoMA's curator comes and tells me 'I will feature your work', yes. … I would have to be living abroad'. She mentions two artists, Sandra Gamarra and Fernando Bryce, friends of hers whose work fascinates her and whose career trajectories have allowed them to achieve that goal. Natalia thinks living abroad for a period would have given her more potential to attract the interest of international museum curators and expand her perspective: 'I feel that my work is in dialogue to what happens here, not in the art world but in worlds outside the art world. That encourages me. But I feel that my view of things is a bit isolated. For example when you're in New York everyone is there. There's a Bolivian, a Colombian, a Chinese, a Mexican, this one, that one, the other one. That's a great perspective. Every time I go abroad it broadens my view. It expands not only when I go here to *comedores populares* [soup kitchens] but when I'm abroad too. I remember, for example, when I was at a party with historians in New York and a guy from Puerto Rico told me 'I saw one of your pictures on the cover of a book about domestic service.' We exchanged emails, and now in his classes on the history of colonialism he shows my photos from *La Otra*'.

She continues, 'I dream that my children will grow up and I'll connect with people who are doing these things elsewhere, that I'll have more theoretical capacity to reflect on what I'm doing, of course I want that. And if on top of that had a residency that allowed me to concentrate on what I do, obviously I'd be happy. But now I'm very into maintaining my house, my two children, and being with them while they're at the age to be with me'.

Motherhood has led Natalia to make certain decisions that directly influence her career and has also been a topic that she has explored throughout her career. She has concentrated these explorations in her project *Historias de Maternidad* (History of Maternity), of which she has completed three series. Her work, which questions the generalised narratives of the happybeautiful pregnancy and the good mother, has been my closest reference to the complexities of pregnancy and motherhood. At the time of our interview, she was organising *History of Maternity III*. 'I'll include a couple of works from the other two exhibitions, other people and friends' work on motherhood, and ceramic from Quinua [a Peruvian Andean town famous for its ceramic work] about maternity in the Micromuseo style [in a dialogue of different artistic traditions]. I want to do an exhibition like you're a curator, you're an artist, you're a collective. … In addition, I write my own texts'. She held this exhibition in 2015 at the Luis Miró Quesada Gallery. When I entered the show, I saw headphones hanging from the ceiling: I put them on and listened a child calling: Mama, mamaaa, mum, ma, mamaaaa. I kept listening. I couldn't take them off. Tears rolled down my cheeks. Natalia's sensory work again connected with my own experience. To combine an early academic career with motherhood is not an easy task (see Borea 2018).

Natalia's international circulation is mainly through academic circuits and her work is about issues of gender, workers' rights, human rights and the consumer society. 'I was fully involved in Fujimori's expatriation. I did several posters for APRODEH [the NGO Asociación Pro Derechos Humanos] about the repatriation of funds that he took. I've worked on human rights, women's rights and sexual rights claims and in relation to the internal armed conflict'. Natalia was a member of the curatorial team of Lugar de la Memoria, la Tolerancia y la Inclusion Social (Museum of Memory, Tolerance and Social Inclusion). 'It is that. All these years of getting there. Not just making images but developing my arguments. I went to Argentina to a conference on sexuality and explained my posters, combining my talk with an exhibition. My relationship with the international world is more on the academic side than the art market side'. Natalia did an MA in Gender, Sexuality and Public Politics at San Marcos University from 2005 to 2007, and in addition to reading at the Católica's Art Department has been a lecturer at the Visual Anthropology MA programme.

Natalia participated actively in the symbolic-political actions for the recovery of democracy. She explained to me that the *Cutting Edge* exhibition at Arco Madrid (2000), curated by Gustavo Buntinx, brought together artists with whom she was already friend, and provided them with an arena to exchange ideas about the sociopolitical situation in Peru, leading to further activities. It 'was fun because it was Flavia [Gandolfo], Fernando [Bryce], La Perrera [Natalia Iguiñiz and Sandro Venturo] and Juan Enrique Bedoya. That was nice. There was a little gig. The Colectivo Sociedad Civil was envisioned there. I knew Gustavo [Buntinx] and Susana [Torres]. He presented my work at some exhibitions, but right then in 2000 we met Gustavo and Susanna, Sandro and I, and we said "Let's do something, we don't know what, perhaps at the Manu Chao concert, no, where shall we do it? On the street".' Other people joined the group including Emilio Santisteban and Claudia Coca, artists at the School of Fine Arts: 'We produced *The Burial of the ONPE* [Peruvian National Office of Elections] in front of the Palace of Justice. We did the poster *Cambio No Cumbia* [change not *cumbia*]. Fernando Bryce joined, Lucho Garcia Zapatero joined, and then came *Lava la*  *Bandera* [Wash the Flag; see [Chapter 1\]](#page-31-1). But for Natalia the group then became very demanding. When started to say 'Hey, I saw you at a party, and you're not working on the poster'; I said to myself 'impossible' … . Well, Sandro and I left. *Lava la Bandera* was a little more radical for us. I mean, we did an exhibition later, *Patria* [at Pancho Fierro Gallery, 2001], washing the flag with lye. I've always been against militarism, and the question of nationalist ideology. For me there are problems with the constitution of nation states. At the time they weren't yet calling the group Sociedad Civil. They consolidated as such with *Lava la Bandera* and what happened next'. She insists: 'I admire that [*Lava la Bandera*] action and I think it was important for the symbolic overthrow of the dictatorship, as Gustavo [Buntinx] says'.

While Natalia's participation in these actions was of importance to her, the political and social engagement of her work mainly has come through an extension of her personal experience, which in connection with others' experiences becomes a dense and multi-layered work, showing complexity and contradictions: 'When I breastfed … I started working on lactation; and it was like that with the different topics happening in my life'. In another part of our conversation she mentions that 'In general I like the critical reflection on advertising and consumer society […] but I also love fashion. If they're not my own contradictions, I feel that the things have no flesh. I'm not interested in pointing out saying "You're the bad guys, they are bad, the *gringos* are the bad guys". I'm interested when you yourself have the contradiction, when you discover your own racism, your own sexism, your own ability to consume'. It is a reflection that connects with, contributes to and acts upon the social world. It is in looking at how to provide different depths and textures in her work that she approaches anthropological methods: 'To do a portrait of Maria Elena Moyano I used many anthropological strategies, interviewing everyone who knew her. I collected all the photos and newspaper archives. I took pictures of all the archives. That is happiness'. I ask her 'Why did that make you happy?', and Natalia replies, 'In this case because I was creating a portrait, which I felt was lacking in Peru at the moment. Female leadership. … There is still a problem between

### 62 *Trajectory*

political authority and being a woman, not because they're incompatible but because our society is organised so that power is in the hands of those who don't have to care for anyone'.

In 2018, under the curatorship of Miguel López, Natalia´s anthological exhibition *Energías sociales/ Fuerzas vitals: Natalia Iguiñiz: arte, activismo, feminismo (1994–2018)* (Social Energies/Vital Forces: Natalia Iguiñiz: Art, Activism, Feminism) was presented at the Miraflores ICPNA, together with a publication that includes both analyses of her work and her own reflections.

# <span id="page-93-1"></span>**3 Enlarging the contemporary**

<span id="page-93-0"></span>[The rise of curators and of indigenous artists](#page-7-7) 

One. The exhibition *El Ojo Verde* (The Green Eye) opened its doors at Centro Cultural Telefónica in December 2000. Lima had never seen Amazonian cosmologies and material culture on display at this level. Promoted by the Amazon organisation the Interethnic Association for the Development of the Peruvian Amazon (AIDESEP) and sponsored by Fundación Telefónica, *El Ojo Verde* featured the different ways in which Amazonian groups give significance to their tangible and intangible worlds. The curators, Gredna Landolt and Pablo Macera, had worked with native specialists, selected by their federations, who described and depicted myths and beliefs representing their culture. This exhibition attracted an overwhelming response from visitors and the media, creating a precedent for subsequent exhibitions about the Amazon that intersected with the new governmental agenda on indigeneity, and encouraged indigenous artists to claim their place on the artistic spectrum.

Two. In 2001 the project *Puntos Cardinales 2001: Cuatro Artistas Visuales Peruanos* (Cardinal Points 2001: Four Peruvian Visual Artists) opened at the Luis Miró Quesada Garland Gallery. This project, curated by Jorge Villacorta and sponsored by the telecommunications company TIM, consisted of four consecutive installations by Iván Esquivel, Fernando Bryce, Sandra Gamarra and Gilda Mantilla, artists who had emerged in the mid-1990s. The exhibition brought together the transformation of the space and the new artistic-sensorial languages through which the artists responded to the socio-political situation, consumerist culture, and the growth of globalisation and communication technologies. The project provided an arena in which different audiences could become more deeply involved with installations that were promoted by the Lima Biennial but rarely by other art spaces. The catalogue included curator Rodrigo Quijano's (2001) compelling review of the art scene over the previous decade, describing the political and economic situation under Fujimori's authoritative regime.

These two exhibitions have been analysed separately as landmark exhibitions heralding a new contemporary art scene. While Natalia Majluf (Villacorta 2013: 324) suggests that *Puntos Cardinales* marked a turning point from painting towards other artistic languages and sensibilities such as installation, I explain (Borea 2010) that *El Ojo Verde* brought visibility to Amazonian art and enlarged, with other subsequent exhibitions, the limits of the Lima contemporary art circuit. Looking at different exhibitions, in this chapter I show the ways in which curators brought together artists' explorations that were transforming the arts and provided new art narratives particularly via group exhibitions. I show how the figure of the curator became consolidated in Lima between 2000 and 2006 in conjunction with the modernisation of art spaces and the gradual transformation of the city, and argue that during this period, Peruvian curators primarily acted locally; their participation abroad would follow with the formation of transnational networks [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1)). I also explore the exhibitions and agents that have enabled the articulation of Amazonian art to the Lima contemporary art circuit, as I expose how Andean art is still primarily linked to the category and platforms of *arte popular*.

The history of exhibitions and curators has received increasing scholarly attention in recent years (e.g. O'Neil 2012; Altshuler 2013; Obrist 2014). In general, the literature has tended to concentrate directly on top curators who act through mainstream art nodes, however, there is a lack of work analysing the significance of curators acting in their specific cities as agents of their transformation. This section contributes to show how curators became dynamic agents providing new art and social narratives as well as moving culture and art in the new Lima for which culture was becoming a growing resource. Thus, I highlight a threefold link that played out simultaneously: the rise of the curator validating and fuelling the arts in a nation and capital city in renewal, in conjunction with the renovation of the infrastructure of cultural centres.

### **From art critics to curators**

During the 1980s Lima's dominant art scene operated primarily through art galleries, accompanied by vibrant Marxist-oriented criticism in newspapers and magazines. The magazine *Utópicos*, launched in 1982, provided important space for the development of art critique with a social perspective on the analysis of art. On the other hand, exhibitions were usually organised without a visible curator. Paul O'Neill (2012) explains that the process of demystification of an autonomous art system made the agents involved in the process of art mediation visible. In Europe, the emergence of the independent curator – an agent with no ties to a specific institution who participates in the organisation and provides the narratives of exhibitions – was fully evident in 1977 with Harald Szeemann's *Documenta 5*: *Questioning Reality, Pictorial Worlds Today*. With the rise of global-scale exhibitions such as the biennials in the 1980s – a moment that coincided with the intensification of the global neoliberal economy – the independent curator gained further space for action and visibility. As Reesa Greenberg, Bruce Ferguson and Sandy Nairne affirmed in the 1990s, 'exhibitions have become *the* medium through which most art becomes known' (1996: 2, italics in original). The group exhibition, through which curators articulate art works and provide the frame and modes of mediation, became the privileged way of dealing with and giving value to art. In the 1990s, every exhibition in the mainstream contemporary art world had 'Curated by X', attached to it, confirming the move towards curation (O'Neill 2012: 46–47).

In Lima the figure of the curator appeared only timidly and sporadically during the 1990s and was reinforced in the first half of the 2000s, although there were earlier explorations. In 1987 Gustavo Buntinx organised *Algo va a Pasar* (Something Will Happen) at the Centro Cultural Miraflores, now the Miró Quesada Garland Gallery, for which he brought together artists who, to different degrees, were interpreting the terrorist violence that afflicted Peru. Buntinx acted as curator of this exhibition without naming himself as such. The exhibition drew strong criticism from the Argentinean Lima-based art critic and art dealer, Elida Roman, who accused Buntinx of creating non-existent relationships between works alluding to mummy bundles, on the one hand, and the terrorist violence, on the other. In 1990 Buntinx set up another group exhibition *Restauración/No Restauración*  (Restoration/No Restoration) as director of the Museo de Arte Italiano. Working with the notion of the refurbishment of the museum this exhibition focused on the art system and established a dialogue with previous work by the conceptualist artists Hemilio Hernández, *El Museo Borrado* (The Erased Museum), and Rafael Hastings. It included a performance by Susana Torres, Guiseppe Campusano and Beto Montalva in which war images were displayed and Susana Torres modelled with canvas on her body, accompanied by Madonna's *Material Girl* sung slowly as a criticism of the local art scene. Only a year later, Jorge Villacorta embarked on his first curatorial work, explicitly assuming the role of curator for a show of five ceramicists at the Tadeus Gallery. Later, other curatorial exercises took place at the Parafernalia Gallery, run by Villacorta and Giancarlo Figallo and opened from 1993 to 1997. Further curatorial explorations took place at the Spanish Cultural Centre under the direction of Teresa Velázquez, who had extensive previous experience in New York. It is revealing that the first appointment of a curator at the Museo de Arte de Lima took place in 1995, with Natalia Majluf, later the museum director, taking over this position as part of the museum's gradual transformation.[1](#page-109-0)

<span id="page-95-0"></span>During the mid-1990s, some art critics were transitioning to the role of curator while others expressed their doubts about the centrality of curatorship. Many exhibitions were still being produced without a visible curator and others left this function to the exhibition organiser. In the late 1990s, fostered by the first Lima Biennial and its subsequent editions, the figure of the curator in Lima began to gained strength. The Biennial mobilised the notion and value of contemporary art and of the independent curator as the central mediator, articulator, and discoursemaker about what contemporary art is. Throughout the Biennial's editions, the figure of the curator grew in significance. While a biennial committee selected the artists for inclusion in the first edition in 1997, the final 2002 Ibero-American Biennial was fully organised from the curators' gaze. The committee chose a curator for each participating country who selected the artists and presented their work.[2](#page-109-1) In addition to those who transited from art criticism into curatorship, a group of young curators emerged from the Art History programme at the San Marcos University and from the art practice itself.

<span id="page-95-1"></span>In this conceptual and discursive curatorial landscape of evaluation and mediation, the male Limeño voice has predominated with female participation mainly <span id="page-96-0"></span>related to art management at both the market and the museum level. Elida Roman, and later Natalia Majluf, are perhaps the only women who had a strong voice in the discussion of contemporary art before 2007. However, Majluf primarily participates from the angle of museum management, and in terms of research and curatorship her work has focused on the 19th and early 20th centuries. Roman addresses 20th-century and current art, but despite her art critique column in *El Comercio*, the leading voices that circulate most strongly in the public domain framing, defining and imagining what contemporary art is, and the ones usually consulted by journalists, artists and art managers have been predominantly male.[3](#page-109-2) The art critics Gustavo Buntinx and Jorge Villacorta have had particularly influential roles in providing curatorial styles, lines of inquiry and narratives for Peruvian contemporary art. Moreover, I argue that Buntinx and Villacorta themselves became the validating 'institutions' of contemporary art since the late 1990s, in a terrain where institutions were significantly weak[.4](#page-110-0) Several scholars have pointed out how, due to the lack and weakness of institutions throughout Peru's history, individuals rather than institutions have organised the distribution of work and power. The validation of contemporary art to its stakeholders and the media was provided not by institutions, which were not on position to do so, but by the iterations of two curators who enabled the opening up of narratives across aesthetics (Buntinx) and diverse media (Villacorta); however, at the same time, their iterations set specific directionalities and constrained other modes of imagining. In the next lines, based on my extensive interviews with Gustavo Buntinx (in August 2015) and Jorge Villacorta (in July 2015), I show their trajectories with the aim of demonstrating their significance and influence, and the ways in which they achieved their authority in the art scene.

<span id="page-96-1"></span>Gustavo Buntinx was born in Argentina and moved to Peru when he was five years old. His parents had grown tired of Argentina's dictatorial regimes and saw the Peru of the 1960s as a booming country. They lived in rural Chaclacayo, and Buntinx attended the Santa Margarita private school. He explained, 'I grew up with very diverse people, and that gave me a progressive spirit'. They travelled across Peru in their Volkswagen Beetle, and for Buntinx that 'awakened a love of Peru in me'. As a teenager he wanted to be a painter. He painted Andean highlanders in Chaclacayo's plaza. He also wanted to be a priest. The first period of his life in Peru suddenly ended with the Velasco dictatorship and his parents' decision to move to Miami. In Miami he was only interested in finishing school, from which he graduated with honours. Buntinx studied history and literature at Harvard University, where he participated in theatre groups and in a leftist political intellectual movement. He graduated with honours in 1978 but decided to abandon his academic career and return to Peru. At that time the Trotskyist Hugo Blanco had returned to Peru from asylum abroad and had founded the Workers Revolutionary Party. Blanco was elected to parliament and ran for president in 1980. According to Buntinx 'There was this crazy fantasy that the first successful Trotskyist revolution was happening in Peru'. In parallel with his political activity he gradually began to write about art in the leftist magazine *Revista Marka*. As his previous studies had not been directly based on the arts, he set an intense programme of readings for himself. Then, at first under the pseudonym Sebastian Gris, he began to write for the newspaper *La República* where, he remembers, there was space for three pages about art. In the 1980s the art historian Alfonso Castrillón asked him to teach art history at the San Marcos University, in parallel to which he read Postgraduate Studies in History at La Católica, which brought him into contact with the historian Alberto Flores Galindo and his *Revista Sur*  project. He was close to the thoughts of the researchers involved in the so-called Social Theory of Art, which addressed the need to understand Latin American art from a regional perspective, and included looking at poverty and popular culture.[5](#page-110-1) For the Third Trujillo Biennial in 1987, under the general coordination of Alfonso Castrillón, Buntinx co-organised the symposium Modernity and Province, which brought together several Latin American thinkers such as Juan Acha and Ticio Escobar.

<span id="page-97-1"></span><span id="page-97-0"></span>Buntinx's interests have revolved around politics, popular culture, religion and the museum. His theoretical and political projects have guided his work as an art historian, art critic and curator. In the 1980s he was primarily attracted by the art collectives E.P.S Huayco and Taller NN,[6](#page-110-2) and in his early curatorial exploration he organised the exhibitions *Algo va a Pasar* (1987) and *Restauración/ No Restauración* (1990), described above. In 1992, feeling persecuted under Fujimori's government, Buntinx moved to Argentina, to return to Peru around 1997. Upon his return, as I explained in [Chapter 1,](#page-31-1) he presented *Emergencia Artística* exhibition and was a co-founder of the Colectivo Sociedad Civil; he sat on the committees of the National Lima Biennial in 2000 and the Ibero-American Lima Biennial in 2002, and was part of the curatorial team for the exhibition *Cutting Edge: Cono Sur* held at ARCO Madrid in 2000. From 2001 to 2006 he was the director of the Centro Cultural de San Marcos; after this position he would reinforce his Micromuseo project (see [Chapter 4\)](#page-123-1).

<span id="page-97-2"></span>Buntinx has built a conceptual architecture to approach to what he promotes as Peruvian contemporary art. In 2002 he curated the exhibition *Lo Impuro y lo Contaminado: Retornos críticos de la pintura 1997–2002* (The Impure and the Contaminated: Critical Returns of Painting 1997–2002) at the Centro Cultural España, in which he worked on his pivotal frames of 'the impure and the contaminated'. Showing the work of Miguel Aguirre, Christian Bendayán, Claudia Coca, Sandra Gamarra, Mónica González, Gilda Mantilla, Alfredo Márquez, Cecilia Noriega-Bozovich and Marcel Velaochaga, Buntinx highlighted a change from the formal painting of the 1990s to one that revealed new forms of expressing disputes and negotiations of a 'marginal (post)-modernity, crossed by discontinuities and fractures' (Buntinx 2002). The notions of both marginal post-modernity and political fractures comprised Buntinx's 'Neo-Baroque' concept, built in close relation to artists Alfredo Márquez and Alex Angeles' multi-layered work *Caja Negra* ('Black Box', painted in 2001), a large-format painting that simultaneously unravels various violent periods of Peruvian history through the reinterpretation of a pictorial colonial language.[7](#page-110-3) In developing his ideas, Buntinx has also been influenced by Christian Bendayán's paintings and via Bendayán by Amazonian urban art. In 2007 he put together his conceptual landscape for the international <span id="page-98-0"></span>exhibition *Lo impuro y lo contaminado: Pulsiones (Neo) Barrocas en las rutas de Micromuseo* (The Impure and the Contaminated: (Neo) Baroque Pulsations in Micromuseo's Trajectories).[8](#page-110-4) Buntinx has contributed to expanding the delimitations of Peruvian art in terms of multiple aesthetics and to promoting a theoretical reflection emerging from local experiences. However, the centrality of the curator and the baroque style of his discursive repertoire have sometimes overshadowed artists' perspectives and voices while moving forward the validation of art through the curatorial voice. It is important to note that while Peruvian curators were working predominantly locally during the first half of the 2000s, Buntinx's ideas were circulating in the international curatorial and academic arenas[.9](#page-110-5)

<span id="page-98-1"></span>Working in parallel with Gustavo Buntinx is the art critic and curator Jorge Villacorta. Villacorta has fostered transits between media and disciplines by focusing on various intersections of art, music, technology and science through an interest in conceptual art and projects that address the audience. His family instilled in him an inclination towards music and the arts. Villacorta told me that the first exhibition that strongly affected him was the *Carlos Quizpez Asin Retrospective,* organised by the art historian Alfonso Castrillón as director of the Banco Continental Gallery. Castrillón was for Villacorta his main benchmark for exhibition projects and writing about art in Peru. After finishing his studies at the private Markham School, Villacorta travelled to England, where he studied biology and obtained a degree in genetics at the University of York. In England he immersed himself in punk, a major cultural phenomenon at the time. Through visits to museums and exhibitions he developed a fascination for conceptual and video art. Villacorta explained: 'When I went to Europe I remembered Castrillón as a director of exhibitions, but I did not know the word 'curator' yet … I first heard it in Europe'. Only a year after his arrival in England, the Pompidou Centre opened in Paris, where Villacorta saw three exhibitions curated by Pontus Hulten which strongly influenced him: *Paris-Berlin, Paris-Moscow,* and *Paris-Paris*. The work of Harald Szeemann in his role of curator-creator was also persuasive. In 1982 Villacorta returned briefly to Lima but was soon back in Europe, this time in Switzerland, pursuing his study of medical genetics. Here he saw factory buildings being turned into exhibition spaces. In 1985 he returned to Peru.

<span id="page-98-2"></span>Back in Lima, he started working on tissue re-generation at the Hospital del Niño while also gradually involving himself in the art scene. His participation in these two diverse fields has created the image of Villacorta. He started to work at the Instituto de Arte Sabogal, and for the 1987 Trujillo Biennial he went to sell paperback books published by the Institute[.10](#page-110-6) It was not until 1989, on the recommendation of his schoolfriend, the artist Runcie Tanaka, that he began producing art criticism for the magazine *Oiga.* Two years later he was working on what would become his first curatorship at the Tadeus Gallery, described above. Things began to change for him when he and Giancarlo Figallo opened the Paraphernalia Gallery, focusing on the work of young artists. Here Villacorta explored the curatorial practice; for example in collaboration with Buntinx he curated the exhibition *Arte No-Venta* (Art No-Sale), which brought together several of the young artists who had participated in the Trujillo Biennial and whose work had attracted good reviews but few sales. Parafernalia closed in 1997. In the 1990s Villacorta was already acting as a juror on several art competitions.

Villacorta is perhaps the most prolific curator of individual and group exhibitions in Peru. As Buntinx has built a corpus of concepts, Villacorta has provided directionalities by grouping artists and making sense of recent periods of art practice which have become the legitimised versions. These exhibitions include *Documentos: Tres décadas de la fotografía en el Perú, 1960–1990)* (Documents: Three decades Decades of Photography in Peru, 1960–1990) curated with Natalia Majluf at the Museo de Arte de Lima in 1997 at the time of the first Ibero-American Lima Biennial; *El Laberinto de la Choledad* (The *Choledad*'s Labyrinth)*,* curated with Julia Pesce, Augusto del Valle and María Paz Ortiz de Cevallos in 1999, which included art from 1980 to 1998; *Urbe y Arte: Imaginarios de Lima en transformación 1980- –2005* (City and Art: Imaginaries of Lima in Transformation 1980–2005) at the Museo de la Nación in 2005, curated with Pablo Dam, Luis Alvarado, Augusto del Valle, Sharon Lerner and Miguel López; and, with Carlo Trivelli, *Fotografía Después de la Fotografía* (Photography after Photography) for the Second Lima Photography Biennial in 2014 (Buntinx curated the first edition), which sought to trace a turning point from a photographic towards a postphotographic practice.[11](#page-110-7)

<span id="page-99-0"></span>Villacorta has also explored the role of curator-creator in three exhibitions at the Miró Quesada Garland Gallery. *Terreno de Experiencia – Por la Alteración del Uso del Espacio* (Land of Experience – On the Alteration of the Use of Space), in 2000, put the exhibition space itself forward as an arena for multiple explorations incorporating art publications, a daily newspaper, performances and interactive video installations. A year later, *Puntos Cardinales,* mentioned at the beginning of the chapter, opened. In 2004 Villacorta pushed the limits of curatorial experimentations further with *Cubo Blanco Flexi Time* – a name that alluded to the White Cube gallery. In the curatorial text, he explains, 'This is an unconventional exhibition project that aims to bring to the public, in an unconventional way, an idea of what the curator's job entails'. Artists set up radical proposals: one of these was *Epicentric*, in which Jose Carlos Martinant and Kiko Mayorga moved Villacorta's possessions from his house to the gallery and asked him to write about his ideas and feelings about the show, subverting the relationships between artists and curators, the private and public space, intimacy and the public gaze. *Cubo Blanco Flexi Time* hinged upon a larger curatorial programme of compelling proposals that was developed at the Miró Quesada Garland Gallery, as I will explain.

Apart from curating, Villacorta has invested much of his time in teaching and mentoring. He taught History of Photography at the Gaudi Institute from 1993 until it closed in 1998 to become the Centro de la Imagen, where he has taught since it opened, and at the Department of Art at La Católica University from 1999 to 2013. He has also promoted the work of younger curators: for instance, when *Centro de Arte Ego* – the Spanish curator Carmen Cantón's project that focused on the mediation of art – reached Peru in 2003, Villacorta suggested seven young curators – Emilio Tarazona, Carlos León Xjiménez, Manuel Munive, Juan Peralta, Jose Carlos Mariátegui, María Fé Nevares and Miguel Zegarra (Tarazona 2006) – to have a platform for experimentation. Specifically, Villacorta has contributed to tutoring two curators who were his assistants between 2005 and 2006, Miguel López and Sharon Lerner. These two curators, I argue, have generated and belong to a new moment in curatorship in which curators act both locally and internationally, and where the female voice has started to achieve more validation in the field: Sharon Lerner is the curator of contemporary art at the Museo de Arte de Lima since 2012.

### **Cultural centres, spatial renovation and curatorial work**

During the first half of 2000s, the Lima art scene saw the material renovation of non-commercial and of, the few, commercial galleries, in response to the new forms of art, the new requirements of exhibition display and the material renewal of Lima. The curatorial work consolidated in relation to these renovated infrastructure of art spaces that also envisioned an attractive cultural programme – with an important participation of foreign cultural diplomacy's programmes and centres. Curators became key agents in the putting in motion and visibility of culture and art in the cities. Their methods of research in general faster than the slow pace of academic work, and their texts and catalogues shorter than the academic monograph has enabled a dynamic cultural agenda fostering the urban consumption of culture. Thus, I argue that their form of work is fuelled by, and fuels the role and space of culture in, neoliberal cities.

In 2000, the Lima Municipal Art Gallery Pancho Fierro, located in Lima Centre, launched its renewed space for the promotion of art with particular focus on contemporary art, under Major Alberto Andrade's policy on art and culture. Other municipal space that saw material renovation was the influential Miró Quesada Garland Gallery – perhaps the most important art space before the Museo de Arte de Lima took the leading institutional position. In a feat of remarkable municipal management, Miraflores district manages one of the most prestigious cultural spaces in Lima, commanding a privileged location at the corner of Parque Kennedy and the busy Larco Street. The Miraflores Cultural Centre, later renamed the Miró Quesada Garland Gallery, opened in 1984 under the strong direction of Luis Lama, who led the space until 1999. Since its opening, this venue has been disposed towards the contemporary. In 2000 Jorge Villacorta became its coordinator through the Quidam Cultural Action Association. The space went through a full renovation under the architect David Mutal. With this new infrastructure, Villacorta began to promote experimental art projects as well as situating curatorial work as a creative tool. In 2002 the management changed and in 2003 the cultural promoter Cecilia Sarria took charge, launching a visible arena for curators and their narratives, to which I will turn later.

In 2001, the Peruvian North American Cultural Institute (ICPNA) also reopened its refurbished spacious gallery in Miraflores district. With its Director Fernando Torres and the Sub-Director Pedro Pablo Alayza (1999–2009), ICPNA <span id="page-101-2"></span>promoted curatorial projects dedicated to the exploration of 20th-century Peruvian art, which was an important task due to the scant institutional work examining the recent history, as well as on Peruvian photography, contemporary art and '*arte popular*' – the latter co-organised with the Instituto de Investigaciones Artísticas y Museológicas of the Ricardo Palma University, directed by Alfonso Castrillón and as part of his long-term commitment to a diverse Peruvian art scene. It was also Castrillón who launched Peru's first MA in Museology at the Ricardo Palma University in 1999 in response to the growing importance of professionalisation in culture and museums.

The Museo de Arte de San Marcos saw the conclusion of its architectural refurbished, as part of the long restoration of the San Marcos University Cultural Centre's historical building in Lima Centre, with the support of the Spanish Agency for International Development Cooperation (AECI). With the new democratic government, Gustavo Buntinx was appointed director of this Cultural Centre and curator Augusto del Valle (2001–2004) and artist Armando Williams (2004 to 2006) were appointed directors of the art museum. With the infrastructural renovation, del Valle sought to organise the museum into specific areas (which could not happen), including an Experimentation Room. As he explained: 'There was a space for creativity to embark on new things' and 'we were the avant-garde of exhibition display'. During this time, the Museo de Arte de San Marcos acted primarily as an important exhibition platform with edgy curatorial projects.

<span id="page-101-1"></span><span id="page-101-0"></span>Located close to the historic Lima Centre and opened in 1996, the Spanish Cultural Centre also provided a space for artists and curators to experiment in innovative discourses from video art, photography, street art, *conceptualismo*  to different artistic traditions, attracting diverse audiences. Its directors Teresa Velázquez (1997–2002), who was the assistant curator of video art and performance at the Museum of the Moving Image in New York, and the art historian Ricardo Ramon (2003–2009) played significant role in opening the space to the various artistic and curatorial experimentations that were taking place. They also fostered the Ibero-American project through the promotion of travelling exhibitions and projects across the Spanish Cultural Centres located in Ibero-America, especially during the booming Spanish economy before 2008.[12](#page-110-8) Velázquez and Ramon's close engagement with the Lima art scene led them to continue to promote Peruvian artists and curatorial projects after they left Peru, extending the art circulation for Peruvian agents. From 2004 to 2006, Teresa Velásquez was the director of Museo Patio Herrariano, where she promoted the project *Videografías: A Selection of Recent Video Art from Latin America (2000- 2005)*  curated by Jose Carlos Mariátegui and Jorge Villacorta with the institute of Alta Tecnología Andina (ATA, High Andean Technology). The exhibition circulated in Spain and at Spanish Cultural Centres in Panamá, Costa Rica, El Salvador, Honduras, Venezuela, Colombia, Peru, Argentina and Uruguay, and according to Mariátegui, it was 'the first exhibition of video art from Latin America' (interview, June 2015). It is important to note that it was the vibrant Peruvian video art scene and the curators connected with it that first circulated internationally[.13](#page-110-9) In 2005 the Space Telefónica Foundation, of the Spanish telecommunications <span id="page-102-2"></span><span id="page-102-0"></span>company, was fully upgraded with the latest technology fostering exhibitions that linked art, technology, sound and digital culture[.14](#page-110-10)

I want to note here that Spain, with historical ties with Peru, not only provides governmental aid for the restoration of historical buildings and for art projects, but it also was an important destination, particularly during the boom of the Spanish economy up to 2008, for artists and for those interested in professional careers in heritage and museums via the Spanish scholarships system. This professionalising expertise created frictions with an older generation whose work was based on an important practical knowledge. My professional career as a museologist is part of this process of professionalisation in culture. I lived in Spain in 2003 and 2004, and as part of my Carolina Foundation Scholarship in museology we were shown, as privileged witness, the architectural expansion and refurbishment of Spanish museums including the expansion of the Museo Reina Sofia with the Jean Nouvel project, the soon-to-be-inaugurated Museo de Arte Contemporáneo de Castilla y León (MUSAC), the revitalisation of the Museo de Arte Contemporáneo de Barcelona (MACBA), and the renovation of the Museo Patio Herreriano, where I was studying. In 2008 the financial crisis exploded. With it, the position of Spain as a leading reference for work on museums and the arts was disputed, compounded by Peruvian agents' new connections and dialogues with global sites such as London, New York and Berlin, as well as with the new forms of articulation across Latin America, as I discuss in [Chapter 5](#page-171-1). In general, more research is required on the role of foreign cultural diplomacy on Lima's and Latin American contemporary art scene[.15](#page-110-11) I suggest, however, that this was more influential before the reinforcement of the Museo de Arte de Lima and the Peruvian art stakeholder's transnational connections.

<span id="page-102-1"></span>Finally, not only cultural centres underwent infrastructural renovation; also the few commercial art galleries did. For instance, in 2001 the influential an oldest art gallery, Forum, launched its totally refurbished space in Miraflores while Lucia de la Puente Gallery opened its new space in a capacious old *casona* (big historical house) in Barranco district becoming a milestone in the transformation of the gallery scene for contemporary art ([Chapter 6\)](#page-201-1).

# **Curatorial consolidation: Challenging exhibitions/engaging locally**

In this chapter, I have explained the transition of art critics to curators, and how the renewal of Lima and its cultural spaces is interrelated with the growing importance of curators. The figure of the curator begins to appear in Lima in the 1990s, but is only consolidated at the beginning of the 2000s, gaining significant momentum with a proposal promoted in the Miró Quesada Garland Gallery by its coordinator Cecilia Sarria: 'One Month/One Curator' in 2003 and 2004. This mechanism provided an attractive format and motivated curators to develop and show their existing lines of inquiry in this central art space, much of which was connected to and sought to make sense of the changing urban subjectivities.

Curators organised challenging exhibitions in an effervescent moment for the city and the mobilisation of culture. Expanding the limits of the Lima art scene, <span id="page-103-1"></span>the exhibition *Perlas y Lentejuelas* (Beads and Sequins, July 2004), curated by Alfonso Castrillón, showed the labour-intensive skirts of *huayno* music singers for the first time in an art space pointing out the creativity and complexity involved in the making of these garments and relating them to other contemporary artworks. Another exhibition, *Soporte* (Support, January 2004), curated by Rodrigo Quijano, featured the work of graffiti artists Ente (Joan Jiménez) and Pésimo (Edwin Higushi). This was the first exhibition of graffiti at a Lima gallery. In a talk that I organised with colleagues about urban policy and graffiti in 2015, the artist Christians Luna reminisced about this exhibition: 'It was wonderful to see the community that that exhibition brought about. That group of people was different!' Gustavo Buntinx curated the shows *Del puñal al pincel: Lu.Cu.Ma. en Iquitos* (From the Dagger to the Brush: Lu.Cu.Ma in Iquitos, August 2003), which focused on the work of Luis Cuevas Manchego (Lu.Cu.Ma), a street painter and ex-prisoner from the Amazon city of Iquitos, and *Neón Colonial* (Colonial Neon, September 2004) that featured political posters used at public demonstrations to regain Peru's democracy. The posters employed the aesthetics and forms of publicity created by Lima's migrants to announce concerts and other events. This exhibition showed the work of Elliot Urcuhuaranga, later known as Elliot Tupac, for the first time on the art scene. Other exhibitions explored other ways of expanding the arts, such as *Intersections*, curated by Augusto del Valle, which looked at the relationship between art and science, and *Cubo Blanco Flexi Time*, curated by Villacorta, which highlighted the curatorial work.

The organisation of group exhibitions along the lines of 'new artists' common during the 1980s and 1990s lost its appeal with the new discursive landscape that curators were mobilising in their construction of the contemporary, and which artists found more attractive and in relation to the larger art world. Thus curators became central agents in the mediation of art practices and guarantors of art value. In *El Comercio*'s 2003 yearly review, Carlo Trivelli (2003) wrote 'It is healthier that more commercial galleries and cultural centres are calling for a curator to produce their shows', and predicted strong interest in curatorship in 2004. It is crucial to note that the impactful Peruvian Truth Commission and Reconciliation exhibition, *Yuyanapaq: Para Recordar* (To Be Remembered), was launched in 2003. Its curators Nancy Chappell and Mayu Mohanna, created a strong metaphor relating the ruined building in which the exhibition was held with photographs of the conflict and of Peruvian society (Borea 2004), showing the potential of curatorship and exhibition display. *Yuyanapaq* was recognised as the best exhibition of the year (Lama 2004), receiving 80,000 visitors of varying ages and socio-economic status over eleven months and locating the discussion of violence, inequality and memory in the public sphere and as an issue of increasing interest for artists and curators.[16](#page-110-12)

<span id="page-103-0"></span>By 2004 the figure of the curator was consolidated in Lima. At the end of 2004 Luis Lama wrote in his art critique column in *Caretas* magazine: 'It was a year in which curators took their egos out of the closet with prominent exhibitions, but also with the opposite' (Lama, 30 December 2004). In December 2005, the Museo de Arte de Lima launched a significant curatorial programme <span id="page-104-0"></span>which, with its new committee board and management, marked a shift towards the museum's new role as the leading institutional agent on Lima's contemporary art scene. For this long-term project (running from 2005 to 2007), called *Miradas de Fin de Siglo* (Views from the End of the Century), the museum asked four curators to explore the history of contemporary Peruvian art; seeking, as they explained, to 'promote work with our collection and facilitate public debate about contemporary art while enhancing the value of our important collection of contemporary art' (Museo de Arte de Lima 2008: 25). The four selected independent – male – curators and exhibitions were: Gustavo Buntinx with *Revelaciones: Poéticas* the political and cultural claims of Millenium); Augusto del Valle with *Constelaciones* (Constellations)*;* Jorge Villacorta with *Tránsito de Imágenes: Puntos de Fuga Hacia el Arte Último* (Transit of Images: Vanishing Point Towards the Art Today); and Rodrigo Quijano with *Popular/Pop: Vanguardias, Conflicto y Modernidad visual* (Popular/Pop: Vanguards, Conflict and Visual Modernity). Together these proposals provided narratives for Peruvian contemporary art, delineated gaps in MALI's collection that a new acquisition programme would address, and played a key role in locating MALI as the most prominent institution defining Peruvian contemporary art, as I discuss in [Chapter 4](#page-123-1).

### **The articulation of Amazonian art and the predicaments of '***arte popular***'**

At the same time new art practices and narratives were reconfiguring the limits and value of contemporary art, the political and cultural claims of Amazonian indigenous leaders were achieving visibility in different social domains. It is only at the beginning of the 2000s that the Museo Nacional de Antropología, Arqueología e Historia and the Museo Nacional de la Cultura Peruana opened their Amazonian halls (Borea 2006); before that, both focused on Andean and coastal material culture and history, and did not include the Amazon in the national imaginary. The image of Peru as a nation was essentially composed of two regions: the coast and the Andes. The coast was linked to notions of progress and modernity, and the Andes to ideas about national roots and traditions. In the 1970s, Amazonian indigenous groups started to raise their voices against inequality, but unlike Andeans, who raised their voices on the base of class (Calderón 2000), the Amazonians based their movement on issues of ethnicity. The 1980s and 1990s saw the consolidation of indigenous movements and the birth of their international networks, and the late 1990s and early 2000s was a period marked by their national intense political participation and visibility, in which native leaders as political actors put their cultural symbols in motion (Conklin 1997): until then, Amazonian symbols and culture had never been empowered as assets.

I have argued elsewhere (Borea 2010) that the late visibility and mobility of Amazonian art and artists has favoured their incorporation into Peru's dominant art scene, in contrast to Andean art and artists which have been categorised as '*arte popular*', a parallel category in hierarchical relation to 'art'. In the early 2000s, Amazonian art began to circulate in a new conceptual landscape for the <span id="page-105-2"></span>arts, new urban subjectivities and a shifting art scene in Lima. A new group of art agents – curators, artists and art promoters – created more inclusive agendas regarding the poetics and politics of representation (Karp et. al 1991), articulating Amazonian art in innovative curatorial discourses and displaying it in strategic ways and venues. Below I discuss four consecutive cornerstone group exhibitions between 2000 and 2008 which contributed to situating the Amazon in the cultural and artistic sphere.

I began this chapter with a description of two exhibitions, one of which was *El Ojo Verde*. *El Ojo Verde* revealed the different ways in which Amazonian groups give value and meaning to their material and immaterial worlds. This show was curated by Gredna Landolt and Pablo Macera, sponsored by Fundación Telefónica and promoted by the Interethnic Association for the Development of the Peruvian Amazon (AIDESEP). Looking for adequate ways to display Amazonian cosmology and include native people in the process of representing their culture, the curators called for native specialists, selected according to their respective federations, to depict and display their cosmology. The stunning visitor and media response opened the way for the subsequent exhibition, *Serpiente de Agua* (Water Serpent), launched in 2003. The curatorial proposal and display strategies were similar to those of *El Ojo Verde*, but the scope, political support and location of this exhibition placed the Amazon at the height of visibility. *Serpiente de Agua*  was curated by Gredna Landolt and Alexandre Surrallés, supported by Fundación Telefónica and other organisations, and was specifically promoted by Peru's first lady, Eliane Karp, together with the Amazonian organisation AIDESEP. Desemparados, a 19th-century train station in Lima Centre was converted into a cultural venue, and artists such as Rember Yahuarcani arrived in Lima for the first time for this exhibition, which included several workshops and presentations.

<span id="page-105-1"></span><span id="page-105-0"></span>Two years later, in 2005, *Amazonia al Descubierto* (Amazonia Uncovered) went on display. With this exhibition, other artists including the Uitoto-Bora artist Brus Rubio began their connection with Lima. Rubio would go on to develop a growing international artistic trajectory fuelled by winning the second prize in the prestigious Passport for an Artist award, promoted by the French Embassy, with which he travelled to Paris[.17](#page-110-13) *Amazonia al Descubierto* was composed of seven projects and focused on Amazonian art. One of these, *La Soga de los Muertos: El Conocer Desconocido del Ayahuasca* (The Dead's Rope: The Unknown Knowledge of Ayahuasca) curated by Christian Bendayán, specifically addressed Amazonian contemporary art. Bendayán brought together the work of 30 *mestizo*  and native contemporary artists – whose artworks showed their experience with the *ayahuasca* plant. In Bendayán's words, '*La Soga de los Muertos* unites the erudite and the popular on the same terms … popular art is entering new spaces, influencing artists from the established circuit, returning them to their origins and inviting them to be recognised as part of a community with history, traditions, and aesthetics' (2006: 7)[.18](#page-110-14) In 2008 Bendayán curated the exhibition *La Piel de un Río: La Amazonía en el Arte Contemporáneo* (The Skin of the River: The Amazon in Contemporary Art) at the Museo de Arte de San Marcos, reinforcing his project on the recognition of cultural diversity in contemporary art and <span id="page-106-1"></span>fostering the idea that Amazonian art belongs not only to Amazonian artists but also to all artists who engage with the Amazon.

This new cultural panorama also saw important solo exhibitions of contemporary artists – including Limeño artists, urban Amazonian artists, and native artists – that referred to and used Amazonian symbols and topics. In this process of re-assembling the limits of contemporary art it is important to note that the Vertice Gallery opened its doors in 2007 with *Emergencia* (Emergence), the exhibition of the Limeño artist Harry Chávez, which put forward cross-cultural readings emphasising Amazonian cosmologies. Additionally, in 2008 the 80m2 Gallery, which started promoting emerging artists with an expanded view of Peruvian art, held the first exhibition of an indigenous artist at a commercial gallery. This was Rember Yahuarcani's show *Llanchama: Solo Pieles* (*Llanchama*: Only Skin), curated by Armando Williams and Doris Bailys.[19](#page-111-0)

<span id="page-106-0"></span>The curators Gredna Landolt and, particularly with regard to contemporary art, the artist, curator and art promoter Christian Bendayán both played a pivotal role in this process. Bendayán, who was born in Iquitos, the capital of the Amazonian province of Loreto, has set his sphere of action in Lima and Iquitos. He was accepted to study at the Art Department of La Católica but did not enrol. Instead he attended classes at the School of Fine Arts (ENSABAP) and for a short period attended workshops with the artist Enrique Polanco at the new art school of Corriente Alterna in Lima. In 1993 he embarked on a journey down the Ucayali River (Germaná 2007: 128). In 1996 he converted his studio in Iquitos into a social project called The Restringa, offering theatre workshops for abandoned children and teenagers. Soon it became a kind of cultural orphanage that continues its work to this day. By the end of 1996 Bendayán had decided to establish himself in Lima. Broadly speaking, his work focuses on transvestites and prostitutes of the Amazon, middle-class aesthetic tastes, religious images and sensual motives using a colourful palette. Bendayán's artistic and political agenda is the visibility and articulation of diverse traditions and agents, both on his canvases as an artist and in the gallery space as a curator. In 2012, I organised the panel Art, Circulation and Connection at the Encuentro Nacional de Cultura meeting held at Lima's School of Fine Arts, to which I invited Christian Bendayán, Gustavo Buntinx and Natalia Majluf. During this session Bendayán remembered:

The following year [1998] I painted this picture solely from the need to develop things that had to do with my tastes, aesthetics that interested me, topics that interested me, and I tried desperately to gain exposure for it by presenting it to different galleries and submitting it for an award for which it did not qualify, and so on. The painting was completely disregarded. But that painting represents my statement; my statement of my interests, my aesthetic interests, and what I thought and felt about Peru at the time. The painting is called *Estética Center* [Aesthetic Centre]. And then in 2000 I submitted another work for the same award, and the work was included in the final selection. The painting is called *Portrait of Luis Cueva Manchego or Lu.Cu.Ma*. Again, the painting didn't gain any greater recognition in the competition, <span id="page-107-0"></span>but that year the competition held a secret public ballot and my work won the audience award, and that to me was an indication that I should move forward with things that interested me. This painting represents the presence of other artists in my work, and my interest in working with other artists or with the work of other artists. I have the good fortune to share this table with representatives of institutions that now house the two works. The first is in the MALI collection, and the second is in the Micromuseo collection.

In 2002 Bendayán set up four projects in Iquitos and was invited to exhibit one of these in Lima at the Miró Quesada Garland in Miraflores. The project, *Puro Sabor: El Arte Popular Urbano de Iquitos* (Pure Flavour: Popular Urban Art from Iquitos), featured the work of eight urban painters who, as Bendayán emphasised during his talk on the panel mentioned above, 'were not only not recognised as artists but did not perceive themselves as such either'. Names such as Lu.Cu. Ma, Luis Sakiray and Miguel Saveedra started to gain visibility in Lima. For Bendayán, *Puro Sabor* (2002), *La Soga de los Muertos* (2005) and *La Piel de un Río* (2008) were three phases of a large project through which he sought to promote Amazonian art in Lima (interview, 2008). In this he enhanced unknown aspects of the Amazon, such as the colourful Amazonian urban aesthetic and the ayahuasca experience and associated parallel worlds. Later in 2009 Bendayán organised *Poder Verde* (Green Power) at Centro Cultural España, which had four editions; one, in Buenos Aires – to where the Spanish cultural promoter Ricardo Ramon was assigned after being in Peru. In addition, Bendayán has curated individual exhibitions, produced videos, and became a committee member of one of Lima's art fairs, as I explain in [Chapter 6](#page-201-1). In 2012 he won the Creativity category of the re-launched National Culture Prize. During the award ceremony he mentioned the Andean artist Joaquin López Antay, who had received the same award almost 40 years earlier, unfolding intense debate on the art scene at that time. Latest, Bendayán has been the selected artists for the Peruvian Pavilion for the 58 Venice Biennale (2019), with the curatorship of Gustavo Buntinx and the project: *Indios Antropófagos* (Anthropophagous Indians).

Before I discuss the intricacies of Andean art and '*arte popular*', I want to make a further note on the development of curating and the narratives of contemporary Amazonian art. During the first half of the 2000s, AIDESEP and Amazonian organisations were directly participating, in collaboration with curators, on the representation of their culture. However, with the re-organisation of indigenous strategies and their focus on defending their territories from the growing threat associated with the exploitation of hydrocarbons, and with the consolidation of the curatorial voice and Lima's art scene interest in the Amazon, the agents mediating Amazonian culture have changed. What began as a collaborative process representing Amazonian indigenous culture and arts has turned to a moment in which others mediate Amazonian indigenous art, and where there is still no indigenous curatorial voice. In addition, promoting the Amazon as unknown and colourful worked to trigger an interest in the dominant artworld of Lima, but the mysterious, the sexual Amazon, the exuberant nature and ayahuasca – topics that <span id="page-108-0"></span>once enabled the expansion of Peru's art limits, are now, with their reiteration and lack of depth explanations, becoming a reinforcement of otherness. There is still a need to develop vocabularies, criticism and mechanisms to stimulate further narratives of Amazonian art and dialogues across art traditions, without constraining them into tight *tropos*.

The circulation of Andean contemporary art in Lima's dominant contemporary art circuit has met with further obstacles. In terms of art narratives, the construction of what Peruvian art was throughout the 20th century has included continuous reference to the Andes and the Andeans and their appropriation by *mestizo* urban artists, from romantic visions to the *Indigenismo* movement; from abstractionism with pre-Inca roots to brief political recognition of Andean artists and the appropriation of Andean urban icons (Lauer 2007). Despite constant reference to the Andes, there has been no dialogue on equal terms with indigenous Andean artists and their artistic proposals, which have consistently been labelled '*arte popular*'. As I explained in the introduction to this book, the division and management of 'art' and '*arte popular*' as a dual organisation of art was consolidated between 1930 and 1950. *Arte popular* is related to practices associated with the 'traditional', despite innovation, and predominantly to certain bodies: to the 'others', most frequently, to the Andean people (Borea 2017a). The National Culture Prize for art awarded to Joaquin López Antay by the military government in December 1975 shook this dual organisation between 'art' and '*arte popular*', leading to heated debate between López Antay's defenders, who considered his *retablos* art, and those who considered them handicraft. However, the discussion soon abated, and the hierarchical distribution continue (Borea and Germaná 2008).

*Arte popular* is a catch-all category that operates across Latin America. For instance, in his influential book *Arte Popular del Perú*, the art historian Francisco Stastny (1981: 15) defines it as consisting of crafts created in the last century for the wealthy provincial middle class; plastic creations used by Andean peasants; artistic objects made by native groups in the Amazon; and handicrafts manufactured to meet the new urban and tourist demand. He also adds a fifth group: marginal creations produced in cities such as graffiti and decorated buses. The category of *arte popular* is used by the artists themselves who found in it an arena for recognition, mobilisation and a market with an aura of 'authenticity', as well as by the government and some anthropologists and folklore scholars who see it as providing potential for tourism and as a niche for work. I have argued (Borea 2017a: 111) that the art forms and agents of '*arte popular'* according to Stastny, which received little attention from *indigenismo* and folklore throughout the 20th century – that is to say Amazonian art, migrant urban art and Andean art upon political issues[20](#page-111-1) – have more successfully removed from the category of *arte popular*.

Until 2018, only few of the various Andean art exhibitions that were launched, intentionally challenged the category of *arte popular* in their narratives and display, in which quantity and the mixing up of pieces as timeless and authorless prevailed. The anthropologist and art promoter Cesar Ramos's *Manos Artesanas* (Craft Hands) exhibition in 2006, sponsored by the association Central <span id="page-109-3"></span>Interregional de Artesanos del Perú (CIAP), was an important project in this area. Ramos's aim was to establish an equal dialogue among different art traditions and artists. For the exhibition, the curator used the categories 'artist artisan' and 'urban artist'. The artists worked in pairs. Although the exhibition was still based on a dichotomy, it allowed an intercultural exchange during the making process in which the artists got to know each other and produced joint objects. Micromuseo opts for the categories 'artefacts' rather than 'art', and 'artificers' rather than 'artists' with the aim of avoiding differentiation. But while this is not a problem for 'artists' already recognised as such, calling '*artistas populares*' 'artificers' removes them from their achievement of being recognised as 'artists'. The notion of the artificer has not been adopted by any of these agents, as I learnt from my interviews. Nevertheless, in terms of display, Micromuseo exhibitions have enabled productive dialogue and encounters outside the categories of 'high' and 'low' art. In 2008, Gabriela Germaná and I curated the travelling exhibition *Great Masters of Peruvian Art: Mérida, Rojas, Urban and Sánchez*, with the support of the Toronja publicity agency and Transportadora del Gas del Perú. The name of the exhibition challenged the dual system of art, as Edilberto Mérida, Santiago Rojas, Jesús Urbano and Mamerto Sánchez were recognised with a government award as 'great artisans', but not as 'great artists'. The exhibition focused on the artists' specific work emphasising their contribution to the arts, including reflections in their own voices in videos and presentations, and criticised the ongoing hierarchical differentiations; however, journalists' headlines continued to locate the show in the field of '*arte popular*'. In 2014 I attended a talk on the 30th anniversary of the municipal Luis Miró Quesada Garland Gallery, where *Great Masters of Peruvian Art* exhibition was held. During the talk one of the curators who had managed the space at the time of the exhibition recalled it as an attempt to show how the gallery encompassed diversity, saying 'And you, Giuliana, organised Great Artisans here'.

'No, the exhibition was precisely *not* about that', I thought.

### **Notes**

- <span id="page-109-0"></span>[1](#page-95-0) See [Chapter 4.](#page-123-1)
- <span id="page-109-1"></span>[2 L](#page-95-1)aunched in 2002, the magazine *Arte Marcial: Arte, Crítica y Ensayo*, edited by the artist Walter Carbonel and art historians Daniel Contreras and Jose Peralta, included in its first volume an article of the artist and curator Max Hernández Calvo (2002) which asked if curatorship existed in Peru, and after analysing the curatorial practice suggested that curatorship was nascent in the local art system but growing.
- <span id="page-109-2"></span>[3](#page-96-0) During what I argue as the moment of consolidation of curatorship in the Lima art circuit, a group of young women artists and art historians including Lala Rebaza, Valeria Quintana, Cecilia Badillo, Gabriela Germaná, Maria Eugenia Illia and Kelly Carpio from the San Marcos University questioned the predominance of the male voice in terms of curatorial evaluation and proposed an expanded view of contemporary art; they do so, with the launch of their magazine *Vanalidades* (2004–2006), in which first edition in 2004, of only three more, announced: '[The magazine's aim] is to show all kinds of art, although mostly Peruvian, from prehistory to the present, either done here in Lima and Ayacucho or in the Amazon. This is a magazine published only by women, and its first issue is written only by women. The truth is that we are tired of the fact that

- <span id="page-110-15"></span>almost everything that is written and said about art is done by men. So we hope it will be a slightly different view of things (fewer underpants and more panties, as a friend said)' (*Vanalidades* 2004: editorial).
- <span id="page-110-0"></span>[4](#page-96-1) Along this same line of argument, the art critic Luis Lama was the strong voice from the late 1980s to the early 2000s, and perhaps before that the art historian Alfonso Castrillón.
- <span id="page-110-1"></span>[5 S](#page-97-0)ee [Chapter 5](#page-171-1) for an explanation of the Social Theory of Art.

- <span id="page-110-5"></span>[9](#page-98-1) For instance, his proposals influenced Harvard University's *Cultural Agents: Arts and Humanities Civic Engagement* project directed by Doris Sommers and the Peruvian art historian Jose Falconi, and the Ecuadorian anthropologist X Andrade's *Full Dollar Collection project*.

- <span id="page-110-10"></span>[14](#page-102-0) This space has worked in close relation with ATA, which is a key promoter of video-art and art and technology on the local and international level since its creation in 1998. See Hernández Calvo et al. 2018.

These institutes offer language lessons and promote cultural activities.

The central components are the iron tower blended with plants and birds, Brus Rubio self-portraited acting as a shaman and the water fountains transformed into a pond with leaping Amazonian dolphins.

I have suggested (Borea 2010) that this acceptance came about because important prior exhibitions opened the way for more challenging proposals in relation to Amazonian art, and the people who managed the Museo de Arte de San Marcos had, and still have, a special commitment to more inclusive art agendas.

Before this, the Museo de Arte de San Marcos had acquired works by the Uitoto artists Rember Yahuarcani and Santiago Yahuarcani for its collection of contemporary art, marking a turning point in the politics of museums' acquisition of contemporary art.

# <span id="page-112-1"></span><span id="page-112-0"></span>**[Trajectory](#page-7-8)**

# [Rember Yahuarcani](#page-7-8)

In the first half of the 2000s, in parallel with white mestizo artists' vibrant art exploration and new curatorial narratives, indigenous Amazonian artists started to claim their place on Lima's contemporary art scene, proposing new themes, aesthetics and accountabilities as well as ways of being a contemporary artist. These artists, in relation with curators and art promoters with an interest in Amazonian art, started to participate in key group exhibitions. With their gradual involvement in the mainstream art circuit their approaches, media and narratives would be reformulated in response to their new inquiries and strategies for participating in the art circuit. In this process some indigenous Amazonian artists such as Rember Yahuarcani and Brus Rubio decided to move to Lima and participate in the art circuit from there; others such as Roldán Pinedo and Olinda Silvano were already living in the capital before they became involved in the art scene; and some opted to work between Lima and the Amazon, such as Elena Valera. With remarkable mobility across the Peruvian territory, these and other, indigenous Amazonian artists have played an important role in the growing visibility of Amazonian art and in pushing the limits of Peru's contemporary art. Since 2010 some of them have also been participating on international platforms, although their place in the art market both locally and internationally is still small.

The first time I saw the Uitoto artist Rember Yahuarcani's work was at the *Rope of the Death* exhibition at the 2005 *Uncovering Amazonia* project. Two years later we met. Born in 1985, Rember Yahuarcani is a key player in the local and international positioning of Amazonian contemporary art, and one of the most renowned young Peruvian artists. I researched his work for my MA dissertation (Borea 2009) and the article 'Personal Cartographies of a Huitoto Mythology: Rember Yahuarcani and the Enlarging of the Peruvian Contemporary Art Scene' (Borea 2010). In 2009 he asked me to curate an exhibition of his and his father's, the artist Santiago Yahuarcani, work on their approach <span id="page-113-0"></span>to the Uitoto calendar, which I did with the curator David Flores-Hora. This exhibition was called *Eleven Moons*. Since then, Rember and I have established a curatorial-research collaboration that keep us in communication, and together we presented a project for the curatorial competition for the Peruvian Pavilion for the 57th Venice Biennale, which became a finalist. The conversation that follows is based on a two-hour interview on 7 December 2015 at my house in Barranco. Rember told me about his trajectory, emphasising issues of belonging and his homecoming, accountability, and projects with his father. In this section, I also include parts of another long conversation we had in 2009.

The first time Rember Yahuarcani went to Lima was to participate in the *Water Serpent* exhibition (2003). Although his father, Santiago Yahuarcani, had been invited, he decided that Rember should go in his place. Rember tells me 'I'd finished high school at 16. A year and half later I was still at home; there was no other option. My elder sister was studying at university and all the money that was earned was for that. Because I had no choice, I stayed, painting, and this opportunity turned up and my dad told me "You're here doing nothing. I think it's better that you go in my place." Then he literally sent me'. Rember and his siblings embarked on painting and sculpting because their father taught them how. 'If my father had been a bricklayer or a carpenter, I would have been that'. His family learnt different crafts, and made masks and paintings for the tourist market. 'Craftsmanship was the way in which my dad taught us, fed us', he states.

At the *Water Serpent* show he met the National Library's Director of Intangible Heritage, who bought a painting and proposed an exhibition of his work at the National Library in Lima Centre. He went back to Pebas, his home town in the Amazonian region of Loreto, and return to Lima in August 2004 with, as he says, 'the paintings under my arm' for his first solo show *Lagrimas del Piri Priri* (*Piri Piri's* Tears), where he sold only three or four works. Back in his hometown he thought 'I'm going to paint here and go back to Lima'. He arrived in early 2005 for the large group exhibition *Uncovering Amazonia*, and 'I realised then that things were not so simple. … It was not about going back and having <span id="page-114-0"></span>a place to exhibit in Lima'. With very little money, Rember wondered what to do. 'I thought "I have no money and here I am. If I'm at home I don't need money. I have food and land, there's cassava. I had somewhere to stay in Lima, I had food, but it wasn't enough." And I started to wonder: a university course is about five years, a technical course takes three years. … I'll dedicate myself to painting for three years, and if I can make a living from it, I'll continue. If not, I'll quit. … So I decided to stay in Lima in 2005, 2006, 2007, 2008 to 2010. … I didn't return to my home, to Pebas, for six years'.

He produced his work in Lima, establishing new networks and exhibiting. 'I met people – I met you'. He had several shows. 'I was focused on doing that. To work, to see if one could actually live, if I could live on what I knew best at the time, which was painting. And that's what I devoted myself to'. As I have explained elsewhere (Borea 2010), at *Water Serpent* Rember met several key people including the curator Gredna Landolt, the historian Pablo Macera and the artist and curator Christian Bendayán. He also met Victoria Morales, an employee at the Museo de Arte de San Marcos, who would become his best friend and his main guide to the Lima art circuit. 'She told me "If you want to survive here, you have to learn many things. … And these are the people that can help you with the things you do"' (interview, 2009). She also recommended that Rember should visit museums and galleries and see as many exhibitions as he could. His new experiences in Lima would lead him to his own artistic exploration and transform the concerns and materiality of his work. During this time, Rember transited from working with natural dyes on *llanchama* bark to working with acrylic paints on canvas in a larger format. His topic moved from tourist art depicting indigenous life to an individual artistic language based on Uitoto mythological figures. He changed and shortened his signature, leaving out the information about his ethnic group and reducing it to the single authored signature (see Borea 2010).

Returning to my conversation with Rember in 2015, I tell him 'This introduction, as you call it, is great, because the last time we talked in depth was in 2009. You stayed in Lima until 2010 and then you decided to go back. What happened?'. Rember's reason for going back to his community in the Amazon was that his paternal grandmother became sick. During his time in Lima his maternal grandmother and grandfather, with whom he had been very close, died. 'When my grandmother, from whom I had learned the myths and everything, was sick, I decided to go home. It was the end of 2010, and I'd been away for about six years. I went on a plane. I think I stayed at home for about three months'. Now he goes back whenever he has the chance. From late October 2012 to September 2014, he spent more time in the Amazon. He explains: 'I was forgetting very important things. I needed to speak the language; if you don't practice you forget. … Here [in Lima] I was talking about the myths and the stories. I was talking so much about them, but not realising that it seemed that the more I talked, the faster I forgot. And how did I notice that? Because when I went back and started talking to my dad I realised that he was making me remember many details of the myths that I'd heard at some point, that I knew from before, but I'd forgotten them – important details. Then I said 'I've spent almost seven years talking about myths in Lima and I've actually forgotten almost 20 per cent of the very important details,' and I said 'No, it cannot be.' So I decided to stay at home, to learn all these things again, because I started to feel that somehow the house and the family remind you that your home is there'.

Rember always paints when he returns to Pebas. 'This time I took materials with me', he tells me. At the beginning of his career in Lima, he had brought *llanchama*  and natural dyes from the Amazon. In Lima, he gradually explored painting on canvas with acrylic paint; these are the materials he now takes back to the Amazon. 'I've started to not waste time in the Amazon. I returned to be with the family, with the myths, but at the same time when I was there I remembered all the things that happen in the city such as the art system, the history, anthropology with its perspectives, because somehow they have allowed me to understand the gaps in the indigenous world and where one must contribute. … Contribute to constructing, because indigenous peoples have always set things up. That's in the myths'. He recounts a myth to me about how men defeated the anaconda, the otorongo and the toucan, and continues: 'And after defeating the evil they asked themselves "What shall we set up? What <span id="page-116-0"></span>shall we do?" And there are the questions that the West also asks: "Where do we come from? What are we? and Where do we go?" But unlike the West, what these men do is the following: when these men wonder about life during the daytime and think about how they can answer these questions, they chew tobacco at night and their spirits return to where they came from.
