Their spirits return to the bottom of the earth, and their grandparents or their ancestors tell them what to do. That, then, is the importance of returning to the origin'. He adds 'What the West has done is a lot of fragmentation. It has fragmented many things in order to figure them out. So when they find cultures like mine that are not fragmented, somehow there's an obstacle to understanding them because most people come out with their whole knowledge and therefore they cannot understand'. Yet Rember feels much indigenous knowledge is being lost, and insists that 'on a small scale [this] happened to me during those six years I stayed in Lima. Thus I talk about what I'm seeing, and in another way I'm also telling you what happened to me'.

Something happened in October 2012 that reinforced his sense of belonging and challenged his art. The La Chorrera Uitoto - the Colombian Uitotos of Rember's extended family group – asked Rember's father to work on a mural: 'I was an assistant. I mean, I wanted to go, I was curious. It's like you're always hearing about a place that your grandfather has talked about all his life, and you get the opportunity to be there. So I went. … Any indigenous person who lives in Apiaco hears about La Chorrera. Being there was totally different. Before, when we said that we belong to the Aymenu family of the Heron Clan, some said it doesn't exist. Even the oldest said that. My grandmother always said that my great-grandfather was from the Heron Clan, that she was from the Heron Clan, that my dad was from the Heron Clan, and there was nobody to corroborate this. Then when I went there with my dad and he talked with the old people they said the Heron Clan is a big clan in La Chorrera that is divided into a lot of subgroups of other clans. That was a surprise. … Hey, what my grandmother said was true! My dad has always been sure, but the fact is that for me, as the son of my father and from a different generation, I had some doubts before 2012'.

<span id="page-117-0"></span>Rember and Santiago Yahuarcani had the challenge of painting a mural about Uitoto beliefs in this Uitoto settlement. According to Rember, this was very difficult for two reasons: first, because in Pebas they work in private but this was a mural to be painted publicly. The second reason was, he told me, that 'I've always painted to tell something to people who are complete strangers, who don't know if I'm telling the truth or inventing something, but that wasn't the case there. They're people who know our history from A to Z, so you can't be inventing anything. We took it as a challenge to how truthful what we were doing was … and whether we could really translate an oral aspect, something immaterial, into something material'. The people of La Chorrera were surprised that they were male painters. Rember noticed that painting is traditionally a female occupation: 'Painting is something very old in the indigenous world. There are painted columns … when a new house is finished there's a party and its columns are decorated. The designs are somehow abstract, but they are designs of the anaconda, the jaguar, of the most powerful animals. There's body painting, painted cloths, painted columns, and some utensils are painted; but not as elaborately as in other cultures. Painting is women's work most of the time'.

The people of La Chorrera were happy with the mural, and for Rember and his father that was revealing. I ask him if this translation from the immaterial to the material surface is the main focus of his artistic practice, to which he answers that it might has been but that has changed. 'I feel a great responsibility; it cannot be that after the 900 years that my ancestors have kept this, my generation is going to lose it. That responsibility has manifested for me in the need to find a language, to be able to explain to you, first in your own language, to make it easier to communicate. I think this is the moment to find a good language, a good way of communicating to you who I am'. For Rember, that good communication involves different media: 'Through painting, literature, and I hope later photography, video and these different languages. … We can appropriate these languages to explain who we are. … I speak of art because this is what I know. I imagine there are many languages. But we must begin with what we are familiar with'. He has started to do drawings <span id="page-118-0"></span>with a brush, and tells me, 'Now I don't feel limited. … I try to use these languages or these techniques, and introduce my characters, which are in fact indigenous characters with a history. I used to think that if I painted like this or used other techniques I could lose who I am, but at this point I think not'.

The importance of the artistic language and the means to communicate and express what he wants to convey has gained predominance in Rember's work in recent years. During the last years he has participated mostly in group shows, but two things have been particularly relevant: the launched of his book *Fidoma y el Bosque de Estrellas* (Fidoma and the Forest of Stars) and an international residency. His book is bilingual, written in his native language, Mɨnɨka, and in Spanish. Rember is also a writer and has already won awards for his writing: 'I began to understand; how good can it be that an indigenous person speaks of myths and language but doesn't have the ability to present a publication written in his own language? It's as if I'm trying to talk about how good Peruvian food is and I forget about the ingredients. That book has been important in this regard, and also because I did it with my dad. We both sat down to translate and somehow research, because there are many words that don't exist in an ancestral language. Thus it is a way to research and to invent, to make a language understandable. We worked with a Spanish dictionary and a Mɨnɨka dictionary. … That was a challenge, because it was new work for us'.

He brought this experience to a residency for which he was selected by the Montevideo Spanish Cultural Centre as one of 40 emergent artists from Spanishspeaking countries. The previous director of the Lima Spanish Cultural Centre, Ricardo Ramon, who directed the Cultural Centre at Montevideo, was there, and he has contributed to fostering some of these networks. Rember tells me, 'I've been in an art residency, not for indigenous art, but of art, art … I was surprised that there were artists working on performance, photography, painting, sculpture, and very good. The speakers were artists in mid-career whose work is in galleries, and curators like Rafael Doctor from Spain, who has been a curator of Casa de Las Americas; check it out on the internet. He was the one who organised this whole event. … From that, I began to say that one has to appropriate <span id="page-119-0"></span>these different art languages. … What caught my attention was this: I wrote down the words that the forty participants used most, which were "identity", "I'm searching for", and the other was "the need". … Then I said to myself, "I know where I come from, my identity. … I do not have to look for these answers, what I have to do is research my world and amplify it." That's what I must do; find a way to communicate as best as I can, to have a talent for communicating in painting, drawing, everything'. His presentation was the secondto-last. He explained his work in Spanish, while his PowerPoint presentation was written in Mɨnɨka. 'I told them, "I'll tell you who I am, where I come from, and where I want to go. I am Uitoto, and we speak this," and I started to talk about my work and my painting, and I explained the research I'm doing with my dad and why I want to take it to painting and literature'.

Rember participated in the travelling exhibition *¡MIRA! Artes Visuais Contemporâneas dos Povos Indígenas*  (Look! Indigenous People's Contemporary Visual Arts), which brought together indigenous artists, art promoters and academics from Brazil, Bolivia, Ecuador, Colombia and Peru. He thinks it was a good experience, but it could have been better. From his experience with MIRA, he talks to me about the expertise needed to manage a project involving indigenous art and the limits and possibilities of anthropology and art: 'For instance, you're an anthropologist who knows, I think, the experience of an indigenous person, the process of knowing the traditions, but you also have the other side which is the artistic aspect, the aspect of art. I think, people have to have these two sides – or if they have more sides, even better. But in this case the people involved were people who had dedicated their whole life to the world of anthropology – I don't know what to call it, traditional anthropology, maybe – who somehow did not know how the artworld works. It's about not only connections but also experience, I mean, all what this means. From my point of view it was good but a very academic event'.

While Rember's participation on the institutional circuit is large, his participation in the art market is still weak as he is not yet part, or totally part, of any gallery. In 2008 he had his first solo exhibition at the 80 m2 Gallery, curated by Armando Williams <span id="page-120-0"></span>and Doris Bayly. Later he has been connected to the Enlace Gallery, which promoted a show of his work in an off-site gallery space at Corte Ingles in 2013. In our conversation about the art market I asked him if he had finally had a show at the Enlace Gallery, but he had not yet: when he spoke to the owner in early 2015, he said he wanted to continue watching his work, to which Rember replied, 'You have seen it for four years'. While he has not had a show in the gallery yet, he did present his book there. 'For me it was a surprise that the gallery agreed. I think of it [the book presentation] as a kind of test to see what I was offering. There were a lot of people'.

Despite not being represented by a gallery yet, he insists, 'There are new spaces that have opened, you know. … I've been asked to exhibit at those galleries, but I … prefer the traditional galleries, because I think that's the fight you must fight coming from a world that people don't know. Because these alternative places somehow will be there, but your work will always be seen as work by an alternative artist, as alternative painting; I think it will be very different if work is presented in a more established place. I think it will be a turning point'.

By the time of our interview, Rember had not met the main art collectors on the Lima art circuit yet. He explains to me that he sells his work by other means and to other people. In the past he usually sold to 'people from San Isidro, Miraflores' but, he says, they have stopped buying now. Recently however he has discovered a new market: 'There is now a group of people who aren't collectors and aren't on the art scene, who have begun to have money. In fact they aren't this group of local people, but migrants from outside Lima who have a lot of money and spend five, six and seven thousand. … A lot of people in the North [Lima North], for example, have money, and all they need is to be introduced to the value of artworks. A museum, someone should invest in the north'. Rember continues, 'There are people who have businesses. I explain to them why a work is worth so much, and they say "I want it."' Rember has just sold a 2 × 2 m piece to a businessman who owns printing shops, for two \$3,500 instalments. He explains that they met by chance. He went to the theatre and someone told the man, 'he writes books'. They began to chat <span id="page-121-0"></span>about the play and other things. The man told Rember he was starting to buy some artworks by artists such as Victor Humareda, and that he had just bought a Teofilo Castillo, but not a big one. The man asked Rember where he lived. Both lived in Lima North, so the man gave him a ride. Rember tells me, 'He had a fabulous 4x4. And he asked "Where can I see your work?" I told him to come to my studio. He explained to me that he had just bought a house that was being refurbished'. The man has a daughter who studies at the private Lima University and a son at school. According to Rember, both enjoy art. 'At my father's exhibition at the Museo de Arte de San Marcos the son told his father "I would like this work for my room" and the father bought it. The boy is fourteen years old! There's a lot of money. And it isn't that I don't know where this money comes from: people work, and work a lot. People work from Monday to Saturday and have their money now, and they don't work eight hours, they work more'. But as Rember points out, these new highincome people, most of them with a history of internal migration, are not included in targeted strategies for expanding the number of Peruvian art collectors.

We also talk about '*arte popular*' (popular art/folk art) and the market, as indigenous Amazonian art tended to be identified as such. Rember emphasise that producers use the *arte popular* category and circuits 'because people also need to survive – that's inevitable. While I take the risk of doing these things [as art] I also sacrifice many other things, because I could be selling my work at 500 or 400 dollars and I could live by selling four or five a month, and I'd live well. But I prefer to do other things, because I believe that a change is necessary, and I think in the future, in ten or fifteen years, we will be responsible for this pigeonholing of what we did, whether it is Amazonian art or whether it is indigenous art. Those responsible will be us: Brus [Rubio], I and those who will appear. Because right or not, we are the face of that group of people here: I can say that this is Rember's painting, yes, but Rember is Uitoto. And the Uitoto are such and such. Then there's a whole thing that follows behind me'. With this sense of accountability, at the end of our conversation Rember insists on a new dimension of his work and voice: 'When I went to Montevideo I realised that artists also investigate their world, wherever they come from. … Why

### <span id="page-122-0"></span>92 *Trajectory*

can't indigenous artists investigate their world, their myths, their customs, their food, everything? That's what I'm doing now – researching'.

Rember has expanded his international mobility considerably: in 2015 he travelled to Europe for the first time to visiting museums and galleries, and won a fellowship to participate in the Latin American and Caribbean Art Festival in China, a country that he visits frequently and with which he has established a productive relationship leading to exhibitions, participation in a biennial, and gallery representation in Shanghai.

# <span id="page-123-1"></span>**[4 Reinforcement of the museum](#page-7-9)**

# <span id="page-123-0"></span>Debates and institutional articulations

This chapter focuses on the rise of the museum as a leading actor in the management and definition of contemporary art in Lima in the second half of 2000s, which I have identified as the second moment in the configuration of Lima's contemporary art scene. I argue that the museum articulated and networked the dynamic explorations of artists and curators, and boosted international articulation and the figure of the collector as a transnational art agent. Initially I examine the strengthening of five museum projects and their competing agendas in dealing with contemporary art. Exploring the tensions across museum projects I expose the strategies and aspirations of different generations of elites in their roles as art patrons. I trace art stakeholders' claims about the need for a museum of contemporary art in a modernising city, and confrontations with the community on the use of public space. I show how during this confrontational moment blogs became a crucial platform for information and debate in the arts and local politics, and artworks themselves became political agents. I introduce my reflexive voice as coordinator of the Museum of Contemporary Art in 2007–2008. The second part of the chapter analyses the strategies that the Museo de Arte de Lima set in motion to make itself the main institutional Peruvian art agent and an apparatus of social capital in the new Lima fostering the international mobilisation of art and agents. I analyse the museum's role in creating a new generation of art collectors who have allowed elites to act as transnational art players and foster global connections for Lima, the arts and themselves. I locate this analysis in a set of practices and networks on a regional and international scale, which I discuss broadly in [Chapter 5.](#page-171-1)

# **A museum of contemporary art for the new Lima: Confrontations and competing agendas**

The role of the museum in managing Peruvian contemporary art moved from a site of little activity to a zone of debate and consolidation. From 2006 to 2009, two overlapping museum debates took place: one concerned the relationship between the museum, the community, and the sense of public space, while the other involved the management of contemporary art. In this moment and through these discussions five different museum projects enhanced and shaped their <span id="page-124-0"></span>agendas: the Museo de Arte de Lima; the Museum of Contemporary Art project; Micromuseo, the Museo de Arte de San Marcos, and LiMac.

The Museo de Arte de Lima and the Museum of Contemporary Art are managed by two private institutions founded in 1955: the Patronato de las Artes (Patronage of the Arts) and the Institute of Contemporary Art (IAC), respectively. These institutions comprise elite Lima families with financial, political and cultural influence and prominent intellectuals, and have been in charge of promoting Peruvian art, fostering a division in the management of culture between the elites managing 'art' and the Peruvian state managing '*arte popular*' (popular art) and heritage, as explained in the introduction. On the one hand, the IAC originated as an institution supporting abstract art at a time when figurative *indigenismo* art dominated the art scene, intending to situate contemporary Peruvian art in relation to modernism and its universal notion of art. With the aim of engaging with international art, the IAC facilitated a space and organisation for international and national exhibitions. Its aims were closely linked with the renovation of Peruvian architecture, previously fostered by the Agrupación Espacio (1947). Among its founder members were the architects Luis Miró Quesada Garland, and Fernando Belaunde, who served as president of Peru from 1963 to 1968 and 1980 to 1985. Paradoxically, the IAC lacked a permanent exhibition space of its own, and this would become its Achilles heel – it only managed to launch its museum of contemporary art in 2013. On the other hand, the Patronato de las Artes began with the clear intention to create a museum of fine arts based on the collections it already held and the new donations expected to would receive. Leveraging the political and cultural influence of the Patronato's members, they obtained from the Municipality of Lima the use of the historical Exhibition Palace, originally constructed in 1872 to hold Peru's first large public exhibition. After restoration supported by the Peruvian and French governments, the Museo de Arte de Lima opened in 1961. At the time, Manuel Prado, a banker, was serving his second term as president of Peru. The Prado family donated an extensive collection to the museum which defines its long historical scope: 3,000 years of Peruvian art. Both the Museo de Arte de Lima and the Institute of Contemporary Art, with its later Museum of Contemporary Art, shared the role of representing the arts. However, the implicit division between the institutions in focusing on different art periods was disrupted when the IAC's Museum of Contemporary Art project was enmeshed in a series of disputes and the Museo de Arte de Lima decided to manage contemporary art.

#### *The 'gift' and discontent: Museum, community and place*

For more than ten years the IAC has been working to donate a museum of contemporary art to Peru, which it has lacked until now. Since its foundation more than half a century ago, the collectors, artists, businessmen and professionals who have comprised its board of directors have sought to give the country a museum that cannot only permanently exhibit its valuable collection of more than 150 contemporary paintings but also provide a space where <span id="page-125-1"></span>young artists and the Peruvian community in general can share knowledge, expression, discussion and access to knowledge to modern contemporary work that could bring together, thanks to its good international reputation.

(IAC website 2006)

In 2006 the Institute of Contemporary Art's museum project was in a delicate situation after years of confrontations with the Municipality of Barranco and some of its neighbours and artists. The problem of the location of the IAC's dreamed museum of contemporary art was longstanding. During the first government of President Fernando Belaunde, himself an IAC member, use of the public Museum of Italian Art was given to the IAC. Later the military government took it back for governmental management. With this situation and the exodus of elites abroad during the military government, the importance of the IAC diminished radically. Back in democracy in Belaunde's second government, the IAC started to look for a place for its museum, following the Patronato de las Artes's strategy of securing a public space. The mayors of Miraflores and San Isidro offered locations, but no firm agreement was ever reached. After the difficulty in finding a public space the IAC organised an auction supervised by Sotheby's in the convoluted year of 1999, with the intention of buying a big historical house in Barranco with the proceeds. Many artists donated work to the auction, which raised \$560,000; more money was needed. However, in 2000, Barranco's municipal administration, close related to the IAC administration, assigned the use of a public area for the building of the Museum of Contemporary Art, which would also be responsible for maintaining the adjacent park. This was a recreational space for the neighbourhood with an artificial lake, a park and a cultural centre. The reason given by the municipality was that the park had deteriorated and it lacked sufficient finances to maintain it. At the request of Barranco's administration, the National Institute of Culture (INC) revoked the place's heritage classification and the municipality ceded its use to the IAC. This unleashed a long series of disputes. According to the residents, the authorities did not ask for their approval, and they wanted their park back.

The IAC increased local tension. Thick, unfriendly bars fenced the perimeter, the lake was drained, the cultural centre was demolished and the museum's structure began to rise. Yet suddenly construction stopped, leaving a metal skeleton in a closed park. There were no more funds to continue, and opposition to the project grew. The board said that they could not complete the building because the dispute prevented them gathering financial support; the district government, now under another party, held that the board had not fulfilled its promise to build on time and must forfeit the space. For some neighbours, the battle was about memory and their public space.

<span id="page-125-0"></span>The IAC repeatedly stated that it wanted 'to give Peru a museum' (see quote above). But three factors undermined this 'gift' (Mauss 1925)[.1](#page-140-0) First, the 'gift' was based on the search for a public space in which to set up a private museum, as was also the case of the Museo de Arte de Lima. Second, the 'gift' from this elite group to society was not wanted by many in the neighbourhood, while others <span id="page-126-0"></span>did not want to receive it passively and demanded to be part of the project. Third, the givers were giving an interesting but small collection – most acquired in exchange for exhibitions– and apart from one member, they were putting very little of their own money into the project. What they were actually giving, while reinforcing their social position, was an elite symbolic appropriation of the notion of the 'museum of contemporary art'. I suggest that in the distribution of cultural management between the state and the elites, the latter's management of art tends to be announced as a gift as part of the elites' agendas and dreams of shaping the nation. The narrative of the 'gift' and 'dreams' also conceals the cultural work and public places in which these – elite – dreams take place. Moreover, and following Sharon Zukin (1995), I see these museums as the materialisation of the political, economic and symbolic power of the groups behind them.

The struggle between the community, the artists and the museum peaked in 2006. Intending to give the stale project new momentum, George Gruenberg, the Peruvian-Swiss businessman, important art collector and business partner of the School of Art Corriente Alterna, took the position of chairman of the IAC board. Gruenberg sought to increase public enthusiasm and financial support by naming the museum Fernando de Szyszlo, after the recognised Peruvian artist and founding member of the IAC. Other established artists objected publicly to this intention, seeing the museum as a collective project that had received donations from many artists, and De Szyszlo did not represent Peruvian contemporary art. These struggles amplified the residents' claims. These reactions were reported in newspapers and emerging blogs. Artist Gerardo Chávez announced, 'I wanted to knock on Szyszlo's door and tell him: "Don't do this" [but] I've changed my mind, because talking with my colleagues, we realised that we aren't used to speaking out' (*Perú 21*, 16 March 2006). The IAC stepped down regarding the name. In parallel, the artist Gerardo Chávez opened his Museum of Modern Art in the northern city of Trujillo.

After working at the National Institute of Culture as Coordinator of museum projects and Director of Museum and Cultural Heritage, I thought that my anthropological and museological experience could contribute to improving the relationship between the museum and the community, as well as to rethinking the museum project itself, which so far was mainly architectural. I knew that while I remained in the public sector a museum of contemporary art would be 'something I would have to work on at night', as an INC superior informed me when, as Director of Museums, I insisted on its importance. Historically, it was not part of the government's agenda. In addition, I wrote an academic article examining the controversy over the IAC's Museum of Contemporary Art project (Borea 2006). Thus my involvement with the project as its coordinator between January 2007 and August 2008 was not naïve, although I have to say it became a very challenging experience as it condensed discriminatory perspective from IAC members as well as from the municipality; amplified power relations at a time of emerging urban gentrification and of what I propose was a turn of the museum institution (I explain this below), and put my anthropological concerns and museological ideals in contention with my work as a museum manager. In <span id="page-127-1"></span>the following lines I describe an event that demonstrates the museum–community struggle and the diverse responses of and to the museum.

<span id="page-127-0"></span>One of the strategies that we planned for rethinking the inconclusive museum situation and handling conversations with the different actors involved in the controversy, was the site-specific exhibition *La Construcción del Lugar Común*  (The Construction of a Place for All), curated by Jorge Villacorta and Miguel Zegarra. The curators invited 15 artists to work on the museum's structures and surroundings.[2](#page-140-1) The exhibition was seen as an arena for debate but also for healing the tension. However, the Mayor of Barranco, Antonio Mezarina, decided not to participate in this arena, which the museum ultimately mediated, and opted for another strategy: to close the 'museum' down the afternoon before the opening. In this context, the museum based its right to function on a judicial injunction that protected its activities; the Barranco Municipality based its right to close the place on its authority in the district; and artists discussed by sitting on the huge municipal banner, which stated 'Closed by municipal regulation'. In the end, the municipality placed the banner at the entrance to the museum, but the museum could not be closed and the exhibition opened as planned. During the event a group of neighbours protested, holding an image of the demolished cultural centre with the lake, which they remembered as 'La Lagunita' (the little lake). Different actors mobilised different sets of strategies and objects – judicial resolutions, municipal banners, their bodies, and images of the past – as evidence of their right to use the place. The next day artists modified the municipality's banner in a quick and ironic play on words intervention, changing '*clausurado por disposición municipal*' (closed by municipal regulation) for 'inaugurado por exposición municipal' (inaugurated by municipal exhibition). Although the municipality restored the original message, the intervention had already been documented and circulated in the printed and social media. With this event the different groups expressed, and most importantly could express their points of view, unleashing emotions and confrontations. Yet neither the Municipality of Lima nor the Institute of Culture expressed their opinions in this controversy, while the blogs rose importance as an active platform for the debate.

The discontent reached the artworks as they activated questions, anxieties and hopes. For instance, on Elena Damiani's *Reconstructing the Landscape*, a computer graphic that redrew the museum's structure according to its latent potential to be (or not) a museum, someone wrote in black spray paint '*Esto es una cueva de mercaderes "de arte"*' (This is an 'art' merchants' cave) and crossed out the image of the structure, cancelling its possible existence. The artist Christians Luna planned his work to explicitly engage with people's dissatisfactions. He attached cardboard panels to the fence surrounding the museum with the question, *'*What does this look like?' with proposed answers such as 'parking', 'hospital', 'school' and 'jail'. Someone answered: '*Es una mierda*' (it's a piece of shit). The exhibition provided different actors with the opportunity to express their points of view and disagreements. Artists and the museum staff worked with the community on a project titled *Art in Your Park*, which widened the channels of communication between the museum and the community, creating

<span id="page-128-1"></span>

the potential for a new museum–community social relationship. Although, the relationship between the museum and the community was going to operate within an emerging museum, neoliberalising, logic: that of the museum as a key player in the growing processes of urban gentrification, with the rise of art collectors' control of museum management and curators taking the lead in museum activities.

### *Elites in debate: Who manages the contemporary?*

In June 2005 the young Peruvian-Swiss businessman and art collector Juan Carlos Verme was elected chairman of the Patronato de las Artes in charge of the Museo de Arte de Lima. Verme's election brought strong dynamism to the museum and he introduced new trustees from a younger generation. The new board, the museum's director and the museum's professional staff turned the MALI into Peru's main institutional actor for contemporary art.

The MALI's new focus on the contemporary produced tension with the Institute of Contemporary Art, which was struggling with its contemporary art museum project. The private management of art with the implicit time-frame division between the Patronato de las Artes with the Museo de Arte de Lima and the Institute of Contemporary Art was disrupted. Although the agendas and generations of the people involved were different, the agenda of the older, elite IAC members being to fulfil their long-held dream of building a museum of contemporary art, while that of the new MALI board, comprising a younger elite, was to locate the MALI and themselves as the institution that define and mediate Peruvian contemporary art in the local and wider art circuits. They differed in organisation as well: the Museo de Arte de Lima was in the process of reinforcing its internal organisation, while the other lack a group of specialists for the different museological roles.

The following excerpts from two interviews in 2009, one with the leading Peruvian abstract artist and a member of the IAC, Fernando de Szyszlo, and the other with the director of the Museo de Arte de Lima, the art historian Natalia Majluf, offer some insight into this debate:[3](#page-140-2)

<span id="page-128-0"></span>*Fernando de Szyszlo (Perú 21, 29 November 2009):* It's simple: a museum, in principle, is the repository of a country's art. And the MALI should be a repository of Peruvian art. The board that was formed for its creation had this idea: to show two thousand years of our art. … The directors of the Museo de Arte de Lima believe it has an avant-garde role, and the truth is that that does not correspond to them: their fixation on modern art may be commendable, but it is a lateral concern which has nothing to do with the objective of [the museum's] formation. This task should be left instead to the Museum of Contemporary Art. (*Perú 21*, 29 November 2009)

*Carlo Trivelli (in interview with Natalia Majluf, El Comercio 6 December 2009):* Regarding the art installations of Centro Abierto promoted by the MALI and other institutions, Szyszlo said they seemed to him very nice but

<span id="page-129-1"></span>lacked seriousness. He added: 'Being Peruvian means saying something that makes sense for us, and not thinking as if we were in New York or Madrid'.

*Natalia Majluf:* It was the same Szyszlo who said with regard to Agrupación Espacio and the publication of the journal *Las Moradas* that 1947 was the year when we Peruvians were contemporaries with all men for first time. Should we cease to be contemporaries today? I believe not. And what the museum does not want and will not do is to retreat to a provincial position and only look inward. I think the great dynamism that Peruvian art has achieved in the last five or ten years has to do with the opening up that the Peruvian scene has shown since the Lima Biennials, and with the work of the museum itself, which, with much effort, has brought to Lima exhibitions of artists such as Cornelia Parker, On Kawara, Joseph Albers, the big show of Fluxus with support of the Goethe Institute. … All of them have helped develop a new perspective on the art in Peru.

(*El Comercio*, 6 December 2009)

What was under debate, then, was the management of contemporary art as well as the directionality of the strategies. As Matthew Bird explains in his article 'Institutionalizing the Lima Museum of Art' (2014), changes at the Museo de Arte de Lima began in 1993, when Walter Piazza assumed presidency of the museum in the context of Fujimori's dictatorship and the beginning of the neoliberal economy. As both Minister of the Economy in the military government for a short period and an engineering and construction businessman in the private sector, Piazza's aim was to rescue the museum from its crisis – a crisis due to many of the museum's patrons going abroad or devoting themselves exclusively to protect their businesses in the military government and later of the terrorists. Piazza's administration included trustees who set up a vigorous museum with which young people began to get involved. For instance, Armando Andrade became a museum patron in 1995, Juan Carlos Verme and Susana de la Puente in 1997, and Nicolas Kecskemethy in 2001. The personnel at the museum included two young professionals: Natalia Majluf and Pedro Pablo Alayza.

<span id="page-129-0"></span>Natalia Majluf became museum curator in 1995. This position already hinted at the museum's renovation, as it was the first time the museum had included the post of curator. Previously its exhibitions had been put together by an exhibition organiser. Majluf studied for a BA in Art History at Boston College, obtained her MA at New York University's Institute of Fine Arts, and pursued her PhD at the University of Texas in Austin. Returning to Peru in 1995, she was offered the position of museum curator. Pedro Pablo Alayza, who had previously been deputy director at the National Museum of Archaeology, Anthropology and History of Peru (1992–1994) and had a BA in Art History from the Université Libre de Bruxelles, was appointed director of the museum. With different perspectives on what counts as art,[4](#page-140-3) both Majluf and Alayza have had crucial roles in the politics of art: the former from the MALI and the latter from various positions in significant institutions.

<span id="page-130-1"></span>To promote the museum, Piazza's administration launched the *Amigos del Museo* (friends of the museum) membership programme and the museum's annual auction. With the help of international cultural diplomacy the museum hosted touring international exhibitions. Retrospective exhibitions were also introduced. But the museum had not yet become a visible agent fostering the transformation of the art scene in Lima when a new crisis occurred. With the economic recession of the late 1990s, Piazza's business company found itself facing major problems and he devoted himself entirely to its recovery. Alayza left his position as museum director at MALI to work as deputy director at the Peruvian North American Cultural Institute's Cultural Office. In 2002 the directorship was offered to Natalia Majluf, who told me: 'In 2002 I assumed the management of the museum. We were in a very delicate economic situation, and we began to work more systematically to start resolving it. But let's say 2004 is about when we began to feel the change' (interview, January 2014). For instance, the museum founded its Pedagogical Institute to revitalise its financial situation, offering professional careers in culture, which were already in demand. A plan to refurbish the whole museum was convened, and travelling exhibitions of contemporary art were hosted.

An important turning point in the MALI's focus on contemporary art arrived with the launch of its series *Views of the End of the Century* from December 2005 to 2007*.* With this series of four consecutive exhibitions (see p. 74) the museum grasped the new vibrant curatorial moment (Chapter 3), bringing it into its institution. Four Peruvian curators – Gustavo Buntinx, Augusto del Valle, Jorge Villacorta and Rodrigo Quijano – were invited to look at the strengths and weaknesses of the museum's collection of contemporary art and provide new perspectives and narratives. Each curator presented a project in a subsequent display. The strength of the new curatorial narratives, discussed in the previous chapter, gradually, both enlarged the museum's collection and widened its perspective. Juan Carlos Verme had already been chairman of the museum's board since mid-2005. Before explaining the specific strategies of the Museo de Arte de Lima's new board, I will refer to other museum projects that were reinforced during this period.

## *Micro-scale and the invisible middle: Micromuseo and the Museo de Arte de San Marcos*

<span id="page-130-0"></span>Curator Gustavo Buntinx advanced his Micromuseo project after being director of San Marcos's Cultural Centre and in the heat of the museum debate, where he reinforced his argument about the importance of a critical project upon the museum as a site. Since the 1980s Buntinx had been reflecting on Peru's lack of a museum of contemporary art. In 1986 he presented his Alternative Museum project, which was a first answer to the IAC's search for a place for its museum. His proposal focused on integrating migrant people relating to what many authors were already calling 'popular modernity'.[5](#page-140-4) His aim was to increase the range of people participating in what museums considered their community. While this <span id="page-131-5"></span><span id="page-131-0"></span>project did not flourish, these ideas would give form to the Micromuseo project[.6](#page-140-5) In 2001, in light of the controversy over the Museum of Contemporary Art in Barranco, Buntinx wrote *Museotopias: Tres textos utópicos sobre el vacio museal del Perú* (Museumutopias: Three Utopian Texts about the Museum Void). The idea of 'the museum void', which announces the lack of both a museum of contemporary art in Lima and a diverse community as part of thinking the museum (Buntinx 2006), strongly permeated the discourses about Peru's institutional field. The play on words in the project's name, 'Micromuseo', synthesises the proposal: 'micro' means small and also refers to the small buses that circulate chaotically in the city, predominately used by middle- and low-income commuters. These buses, *micros*, are usually identified in the Peruvian academic world as the materialisation of popular culture and an index of its aesthetics and values. The project, announced as a move away from the elite's aspirations to the global by looking to empower the local, addressing, 'popular modernity', small-scale and circulation and bridging discussion on 'high' and 'popular art' (Buntinx 2005b, 2006). The Micromuseo project contributed to reconfiguring the map of what counts as Peruvian art, questioned the elite's aspirations and influenced local and international thinkers. Equally important, the husband and wife team, Gustavo Buntinx and artist Susana Torres, have put together a significant collection through purchases and donations in line with Micromuseo's political project[.7](#page-140-6) Rather than finding a permanent home, Micromuseo set up *paraderos* (bus stops), small exhibitions at certain pubs and restaurants, although Buntinx has also dreamt of a permanent space[.8](#page-140-7) Yet Micromuseo has had little circulation across wider Lima or Peru: this 'mobile museum' has had its Limacentric routes, which are fortunately expanding in the last years[.9](#page-140-8)

<span id="page-131-4"></span><span id="page-131-3"></span><span id="page-131-2"></span><span id="page-131-1"></span>The debate about the museum revolved around big private projects and alternative museologies. However, it did not consider the middle scale: the university art museums. Two of which opened in Lima in the 1970s. One is the private Universidad Católica's Museo de Arte y Tradiciones Populares (Museum of Art and Popular Traditions), created in 1979 after the Andean artist Joaquín López Antay won the National Arts Award triggering heated debate about whether his work was art or *artesania* (craft). Many of this debate took place at the Riva Aguero Institute in which the museum is located. The other is the Museo de Arte de San Marcos, which belongs to Peru's largest and oldest public university, San Marcos University. This museum was created in 1970 with three significant collections: popular art (*arte popular*); contemporary art, including an important body of work from the university-run National Art Salons of the 1950s and 1960s; and a collection of copies of paintings by European masters as part of a UNESCO project to cultivate art during the 1950s[.10](#page-140-9) Later it added the *Pintura Campesina* (Peasant Painting) collection of Andean people's drawings of their experiences in the terrorist state's civil war as part of an NGO project that used painting as a means of healing. In Chapter 3, I mentioned how the Museo de Arte de San Marcos became an important exhibition platform in the first half of the 2000s. However, little was done in terms of collections and to overcome its categories of '*arte popular*' and 'art'. In 2006 the administration of the Cultural Centre changed, and with it <span id="page-132-2"></span>the museum director became German Carnero Roque, who with the curator Juan Peralta – who had participated in the organisation of the Lima Biennial and curated the travelling exhibition *Descentralizarte* (Decentralised, 2007) which looked at Peru's contemporary art at large – and the support of the art critic and curator Elida Roman, enlarged the museum's collections. While the university museum lost the dynamism it had attained with Buntinx, Del Valle and Williams's administration – many visitors, mainly those related to the dominant art scene, stopped attending the museum when its administration changed – it reinforced its collection and national accountability. In celebration of its 40th anniversary in 2010, the Museo de Arte de San Marcos presented its new collection of contemporary art. In contrast to the other museums' agendas the collection provided a nationalscale narrative involving artists from diverse Peruvian regions with different visual languages and working with a variety of media. Remarkably, this was the first museum to acquire indigenous Amazonian art as contemporary art for its collection with its inclusion of Santiago Yahuarcani and Rember Yahuarcani's works. However, this museum and its politics were not at the centre of the participants' imagination and narratives regarding the museum debate, partly because of its medium scale, although it holds a significant collection, but perhaps mainly because it belongs to the public San Marcos University and issues of class and race influenced in the invisibility of this platform in Lima art scene's debates. Would the story be the same if it were a museum of art belonging to the private Universidad Católica? I believe not.

The narrative of 'Lima being the only big city in Latin America without a museum of contemporary art' was constantly repeated. I used it myself to support the importance of the Museum of Contemporary Art in Barranco. However, this argument locate the city, infrastructure and cultural projects in a type of evolutionary process of urbanity and culture, linking a specific type of a big museum of contemporary art, with private, elite, management, as part of the equipment of a modernising city, excluding the work of middle-sized, public museums with *cholo* administration.

#### *Blogs and the [virtual] LiMAC: On the museum and the public space*

<span id="page-132-1"></span><span id="page-132-0"></span>As mentioned, much of the museum debate during 2006 and 2009 took place in blogs. Blogs emerged as a new platform and way of mediating and fuelling discussions, becoming a crucial arena for opinions and discussion of politics, the transformation of Lima, and the arts. Two different types of blog were involved in the disputes I refer to here: those concerned with Barranco's neighbourhood politics and the use of public space, and those concerned with the art scene[.11](#page-140-10) The latter gave visibility to the growing activity on Lima's art scene and became a new form of and arena for art criticism, covering discussion of not only artworks but also the art scene with attention to the role of museums and the arts in the public sphere.[12](#page-141-0) Blogs provided a dynamic space for young curators to position themselves as knowledgeable agents and demonstrate understanding of contemporary theory and broader debates. However, as Franka Winter (2013) notes in <span id="page-133-3"></span>her doctoral thesis on Peruvian blogs and politics, these blogs mainly amplified the voices of young middle-class males and did not facilitate the engagement of a wider audience. I suggest that while the aim of art bloggers was to open up the public sphere to wider discussion, they became sites of conceptual debate, mostly written in elaborate language, that reinforced the same dominant art scene, yet via a new channel.

<span id="page-133-0"></span>In this context the Chilean art critic Justo Pastor Mellado's phrase that to construct is not the same as to build (*construir no es lo mismo que edificar*) first published in his blog, became a cornerstone of reflections on blogs about the museum, particularly in relation to the Museum of Contemporary Art controversy.[13](#page-141-1) His statement was a direct answer to the imposing, sophisticated and experimental architecture of the new museums, and particularly of those of contemporary art, in which architecture was gaining far more relevance than their collections and the work of the museum areas themselves. But the critique of the over dimension of architecture leaned the balances towards the discursive aspect of the museum and the role of curators. The idea of constructing based not on materiality but on a critical project enabled curators to claim a privileged space in the significant institution of the museum. In this way the various museum and art professionals consolidated into a hierarchical order, with the curator in a predominant position of knowledge and power. However, this debate paid little attention to the wider museological plan.[14](#page-141-2) Less evocative in discursive terms, the museological plan is the result of teamwork regarding the spirit of the museum, its aims in its various and multiple areas, the focus of its collection and its relation to the community, including the community's voice[.15](#page-141-3) The predominance of the museum building and its sophisticated architectonic projects has been replaced by a discursiveconceptual museum, which Claire Bishop calls 'radical museology' (2013); but I suggest that both perspectives lack understanding of the integral museum and of its commitment in its surroundings. I argue that during this time the museum as an institution has shifted: from the New Museology proposal that advocated reconnecting the museum to its territory and community – a museological way of thinking which Latin America was key in developing – to a museum that privileges transnational articulation and participation, an aim fostered by the stronger role of art collectors and curators in the management and practices of museums.

<span id="page-133-2"></span><span id="page-133-1"></span>Paradoxically, in the museum debate the museological plan was discussed and applied mostly by a new 'fictional' museum which became tangible in the growing virtual space and through exhibitions. With the Spanish Marcelo Botín Fellowship, the Peruvian Spain based artist, Sandra Gamarra, created LiMac, the Lima Museum of Contemporary Art. This was Gamarra's answer to Peru's museum scene. LiMac presented a museological study and plan on its website: with museum guidelines and organisation, information on the collection and temporary exhibitions, artist fellowships, the friends of the museum policy, the shop and merchandise, etc. The positioning of this virtual museum art project as a 'real' museum was such that when people wanted to contact the Lima Museum of Contemporary Art many wrote to LiMac: Sandra Gamarra contacted me as coordinator of the MAC-Lima to tell me about these emails. Moreover, Claire Bishop <span id="page-134-0"></span>(2013: 11) mentions LiMac as a concrete university museum of contemporary art. In 2006 Gamarra presented her first LiMac exhibition, *New Acquisitions*, in Lima. Through her painting she appropriated of other artworks and included them as part of LiMac's collection. This is a project that Gamarra is still developing by introducing different layers of complexity in her thinking about the museum and the art scene. Her work and reflections about the museum and the art scene have been an important reference in my work. For her 2006 exhibition, she wrote:

Not having a place to commemorate the past affects a present that recreates itself constantly, unable to project a future. As a result the works of local artists end up in foreign countries, in private hands or sleeping in studio collections because Lima is still unable to house them. The past is perverted and subverted into a constant present, the present overlaps and multiplies into a mess of disconnected layers. LiMac seeks to order these times, even if it means its own funeral. The creation of a 'real' museum in Lima will not end the fiction of LiMac but will serve as a natural boundary, leading to similar entities that can create a dialogue that in turn convenes more participants.

(Gamarra 2006: 1)

In this arena of competing museum projects and bloggers' voices, the Peruvian government – with no cultural policy on contemporary art – characterised for its silence and moreover for its censorship. In 2007 the National Institute of Culture censored part of Piero Quijano's exhibition *Dibujos en Prensa 1999– 2007* (Drawings in Newspapers 1999–2007) at Casa Mariátegui Museum, which belongs to the National Network of Museums. Minutes before the exhibition opened, the director of the National Institute of Culture asked the museum director to remove three drawings: a satirical representation of the then president Alan Garcia, an ironic comment about cultural policy, and a drawing strongly critiquing the actions of the military during the civil war. Director Armando Williams did not open the exhibition and resigned. The National Institute of Culture's censorship was rapidly and widely criticised in blogs and the media, and all of Piero Quijano's drawings were moved to the artist-run space Espacio La Culpable and exhibited there (see [Chapter 2,](#page-65-1) p. 48).

This vibrant discussion of the museum, and particularly of the contemporary art museum, slackened by the early 2010s. The role of blogs decreased and Facebook took their place. The Museo de Arte de Lima opened a refurbished first floor for temporary exhibitions, and after the inaugural show held a large retrospective of work by the modernist painter Fernando de Szyszlo. The MALI had never held an exhibition of De Szyszlo before; who, as mentioned, was keeping a critical eye on the MALI's new interest on contemporary art. At a massive opening inaugurated by President Alan Garcia, the museum chairman Juan Carlos Verme pointed out that with this exhibition the MALI was filling a gap. In highly contested municipal elections, a new mayor for Barranco was elected. He supported the MAC-Lima. The role of culture and the arts in the transformation of Lima was no longer in debate. The Mario Testino art space, later the Mario Testino <span id="page-135-0"></span>Museum (MATE) opened in Barranco in 2013 with a cosmopolitan celebration. The MAC-Lima, under the direction of the artist and cultural promoter Alvaro Roca Rey, also opened its doors in 2013. Part of the Micromuseo collection is shown, on loan, at the MAC-Lima, in a museum that the Micromuseo's director continuously criticised.

Going with my daughter to play in the park surrounding the MAC-Lima is somehow peaceful: all seems to have become calm, and I see many people I know there, but perhaps it is *too* harmonious, with *too* many known faces. Antonio Mezarina was elected mayor again but did not demand that the MAC-Lima fulfil all of its 2007 promises. The waters are calm: the reflecting pool surrounding the museum is meant to suggest 'La Lagunita', especially to the few museum visitors and park users that remember it in a confrontation that is now over. However, a question revolves in my head and heart: has the MAC-Lima indeed become *a place for all*?

# **Contemporary MALI: Creating collectors, fuelling the museum, boosting the market**

Juan Carlos Verme's connections with the Museo de Arte de Lima began when he returned to Lima in 1988 at the age of 23. His family had left Peru for Switzerland, where they had relatives, during the military regime. He went to secondary school in Zurich and attended many exhibitions. Back in Lima and looking for the cultural life he had enjoined in Zurich, Verme began to attend the Filmoteca (film archive and cinema) at the Museo de Arte de Lima, which screened film classics (Bird 2014: 1). Assuming a position in the family enterprise, he became director of the Banco de Crédito del Perú in the early 1990s and later occupied important positions on various Romero Group boards, attaining recognition in the business world. He started meeting artists and critics at social events. In 1997 Verme became a trustee of the Museo de Arte de Lima. He was already an emerging collector of contemporary art who purchased the series *El Bosque de Rocas* (The Rock Forest) by Fernando Bryce at his exhibition at Casa Mariátegui in 1999. Therefore when Verme was elected Chairman of the Museo de Arte de Lima's board he brought with him not only his business expertise and knowledge of art but also his knowledge of and position on the contemporary art scene.

Another tactical actor in the reconfiguration of the Museo de Arte de Lima has been Armando Andrade, who in 1995 became a patron of the museum, and under Verme's administration was made president of the museum auctions. After declining a fellowship to study art at the Sorbonne in Paris, because his father, a landowner, would not agree to it, he studied law and attended the School of Fine Arts at night to learn design. In the late 1970s he created a design studio with his partners and soon began to work in publicity. With six agencies, he runs one of Peru's leading communication and publicity groups. Under Toledo's government Andrade worked as a Communication Consultant to the Ministry of Defence and an external consultant to the Prime Minister. Andrade has been a key agent in providing Lima with a renew identity from the publicity sector, aspect explained <span id="page-136-0"></span>in [Chapter 2](#page-65-1), and has applied his expertise at the Museo de Arte de Lima. One of the agencies of which he is a partner was responsible for the museum's successful rebranding. He owns one of the most significant collections of Chancay pottery, textiles and contemporary art in the country, and has played an influential role in art collecting in Lima since the late 1990s.

To revitalise the museum, the new board increased the number of young patrons; fostered a branding campaign that created the museum's acronym, MALI, whose repetition sounds like 'Lima'; refurbished the whole building and enhanced its internal management; and reinforced the museum's orientation towards contemporary art. The museum achieved its central position in the management and definition of Peru's contemporary art through the launch of three programmes since 2007: the Contemporary MALI Programme, the Contemporary Art Acquisition Committee (CAAC), and the Summer Auction and Party.

The Contemporary MALI Programme was the framework for the museum's increased effort to boost Peruvian contemporary art. According to a museum report (MALI 2008a: 35) it was created to enrich the MALI's collection, support research, contribute to open the local art scene and generate structures to make Peruvian art more visible, support the work of contemporary artists, promote critical debate, and expand access to art. The post of a Curator of Contemporary Art, which was not limited to Peruvians, was created to support the programme. Tatiana Cuevas, the ex-curator of Mexico's Museo Tamayo, was the first to take this position and remained in it until 2011. In this way MALI prevented disputes among curators in Lima on the one hand, and materialised its international aspirations on the other. In 2012, in an attempt to consolidate the local milieu, the Peruvian curator Sharon Lerner was appointed to the post, which she still holds.

The MALI Contemporary Art Acquisition Committee (CAAC), launched in 2007, is based on the Tate Gallery's system: members make an annual financial contribution to a common fund for purchases. Two external curators and the museum's curator recommend a list of works, explaining the reasons behind their choices. Committee members can present proposals too, and the acquisitions are decided by the collectors' vote. I argue that the CAAC fostered the development of new Peruvian transnational art collectors and also brought dynamism and growth to Lima's art market. The CAAC started as a learning apparatus for both collecting and acting within the art world. By being on the Committee, emergent buyers define themselves as collectors and seek to make good decisions. Curators' explanations about their selections provide collectors with artistic and contextual knowledge with which they act as mediators in the formation of the museum's collection and promotion of Peruvian art. However, behind the scenes there is tension regarding the skills for evaluation. A collector told me that curators explain the significance of the recommended works to the Committee, but in the end collectors are guided by an aesthetic taste they already 'possess' (conversation in 2008). From the collector's point of view, while they may lack historical knowledge, which can be learned, they do not lack aesthetic discernment. To increase their knowledge and broaden their experience as collectors the Acquisition Committee participates in various art events on the local, regional <span id="page-137-1"></span>and mainstream art circuits. In the MALI's 2008 Institutional Report, the president of the CAAC stated:

This year we have undertaken activities that meet an important objective of the Committee, which is to promote collecting and interest more people in contemporary art. Members of our Committee have participated in various art fairs such as: ArteBA, ARCO, Art Basel Miami and Basel, MACO and Pinta. The Committee has also organised visits to the important collections of Alfredo Barreda, George Gruenberg and Walter Piazza, whom I thank especially for opening the doors to their homes to share their experience as collectors with us.

(Jorge Basadre, MALI 2008b)

The Peruvian collectors rapidly achieved recognition and participation in the region (see [Chapter 5\)](#page-171-1). Natalia Majluf told me that 'at ArteBa, people said "There are the Peruvians"'. There is an emergence of collecting with an international force' (interview, July 2011). Today many of these art collectors feel confident about their art knowledge and have embarked on promoting different projects from art galleries to residencies on the Lima art scene, and some are invited to participate in expert panels in the international art circuit.

<span id="page-137-0"></span>The CAAC choose what they consider valuable in the light of history, artists' trajectories and their own tastes. It should be said that when the CAAC was launched, contemporary Peruvian art historiography had been little explored and curators were gradually working on exhibitions that revealed art practices and narratives.[16](#page-141-4) The members of CAAC were aware of the MALI's powerful legitimising role in the creation of a Peruvian contemporary art historiography and of its decisions and omissions, as the President of the Acquisition Committee described:

It should be noted that several of the artists who have been selected by the CAAC in these two years have recently had work included in the collections of major international museums such as the MoMA in New York, the Tate in London, the Los Angeles County Museum of Art, the Museo de Arte Latinoamericano in Buenos Aires, the Museo de Arte Contemporáneo de Castilla y León, as well as the prominent private Daros-Latin America Collection in Zurich and The Diane and Bruce Halle Collection in Phoenix.

(Jorge Basadre, MALI 2008b)

For Peruvian artists, to have their work in the MALI's collection is a milestone in their careers: it anchors their work as part of the Peruvian art legacy and increases their mobilisation in the art world, even if they already have international exposure. As part of the MALI, their work articulate new power relationships (Myers 2002: 5) and acquires new value.

The launch of the MALI Summer Auction and Party, which takes place at exclusive beach clubs, was the third strategy. Even though the MALI has held <span id="page-138-1"></span>auctions since 1999, the pairing of the summer auction with the new urban lifestyle of the growing upper middle class and the increased visibility of contemporary art in the media raises the exposure of these events. Auctions in Lima constitute a 'community of the privileged' (Braudillard 1981: 117), although not precisely as tournaments of value, as disputed bids rarely occur, but as events of reinforcing an urban upper class in which being at the auction and the after-party is what matters. The MALI's auctions challenge the traditional boundary between the art market and the museum that Olav Velthuis (2007) explains with reference to the New York and Amsterdam art markets in three main ways. Velthuis notes how art dealers and auction houses have different strategies and agendas for attracting collectors. According to the author, art dealers maintain ongoing relationships with artists and their work beyond a specific transaction, calculating prices over the long-term perspective of the artists' career rather than basing them solely on market forces. Museums are perceived as outside the market, but their acquisitions and practices influence it. However, auction houses do not maintain long-term relationships with artists and prices are determined according to the moment. In Velthuis's analysis, museums do not play a prominent role in auctions unless they are interested in acquiring a masterpiece from them. The MALI's auctions cross these traditional limits between the art market and the museum.

First, the Museo de Arte de Lima participates directly in the art market by promoting its auction. Moreover, this is the only established art auction to be held in Lima. People respond well to its auctions as the museum is a private non-profit organisation and the money raised is used to improve it. In the catalogue accompanying the 2009 Summer Auction (MALI 2009:1), Juan Carlos Verme wrote:

In recent years the MALI's auctions have become an important element of our museum. It is thanks to the auctions that we have made progress on many fronts, with projects that have helped to enrich the collections, improve our programme and reach an increasingly broad and diverse audience. But now, with the beginning of the renovation work that we have undertaken, auctions acquire greater importance for the economy of the museum, because the funds we raise will serve to complete the equipping of the new infrastructure on the first floor, funded by the Plan National Copesco Mincetur, bringing us closer to our goal of giving to the country a museum at the importance of its history.

<span id="page-138-0"></span>Second, MALI's auctions bring different agents on the Lima art scene together. Instead of disputing the market between art dealers and auction houses, galleries want their artists' works to be in the auctions.[17](#page-141-5) The museum has managed to bring together different agents of the Lima art scene through its auctions, materialising the dominant art network of Lima' art scene and of Peru at large. In the process of selecting the galleries to propose works for the auction, the museum itself becomes a referee of the hierarchy among galleries. The third difference is the still small size of the Lima art market and art scene in general, whose institutions function in overlapping and alternative ways: a museum holds auctions, galleries <span id="page-139-0"></span>include gift and coffee shops, an incomplete museum hosts educational activities, biennials are cancelled while the art scene grows, the state government scarcely participates, and so on. Yet despite the imbalances that the museum-led auctions bring into the Lima art scene, these auctions are not much criticised as celebrated by the media, young artists and art dealers as a way of creating dynamism on the art scene. Among the few early dissonant voices was that of the artist Ramiro Llona:

I welcome the appearance of the Museo de Arte Contemporáneo de San Marcos, and I reiterate my concern with MALI's museum planning policy. They are growing a lot and have performed extraordinary institutional work, but I think it is a huge mistake for the MALI to have art auctions, because a museum cannot be a place of speculation. That is a violation of all the rules. It is too much responsibility: they are tilting the balance too far to one side and it is in hands of a very few people, and with that the great history of Peruvian art is undermined.

(*El Comercio*, 11 March 2010)

Besides setting up the Contemporary MALI Programme, the CAAC and the Summer Auction, the MALI has refurbished its building and reinforced its internal structure in terms of management, personnel and finances. The refurbishing of the museum and adaptation to international standards of administration and technical requirements for exhibitions facilitates the museum's participation in exhibitions abroad, locating it as a viable node in the international mobilisation of art and strengthening its relationship with other museums and projects. When Verme took the position of chairman of the MALI's board he announced to the media that his dream was to refurbish the exhibition rooms, particularly the second floor for the permanent collection (*El Comercio*, 13 June 2005). The Ministry of Foreign Affairs and Tourism provided the funds for the museum's restoration as it sought to strengthen Lima's tourist appeal. The museum closed its doors on June 2008 with the aim of reopening the new museum by late 2009 (*El Comercio*, 29 June 2008). But the reopening was going to take longer than the trustees thought: the renovation was not fully completed until September 2015.

However, the reopening of the ground floor temporary exhibition rooms in 2010 secured the MALI broad international media coverage, forging international networks and materialising its leading role in a burgeoning Lima. The reopening took place with the jet-set Peruvian photographer Mario Testino's exhibition *Portrait*, with guests including the top model Kate Moss, making visible today's celebrity logic at the art scene (Velthuis 2012)*.* Months later, and as part of its aim of positioning the MALI on the regional-global radar, the museum displayed its enhanced and expanded collection of contemporary art at Estacao Pinacoteca in Sao Paulo, planning its opening to coincide with the Sao Paulo Art Fair, SP-Arte. The exhibition, named *Arte al Paso: Museo de Arte de Lima Contemporary Collection* (2011), brought together almost a hundred pieces produced by Peruvian and Peru-based artists from 1968 to 2010 and was the MALI's <span id="page-140-12"></span><span id="page-140-11"></span>first international exhibition of its collection of contemporary art. Through it the museum highlighted its collection of contemporary Peruvian art and the work of Peruvian collectors on the booming Sao Paulo art scene. In addition, the MALI started to plan and co-curate large exhibitions that circulated through museums across Latin America, transforming itself into an agent producing art narratives that travel locally and across Latin America.[18](#page-141-6) These travelling exhibitions, and the programmes and strategies explained above, have contributed to the positioning of the MALI in the region as well as to its connections with key international museums at a time when world-leading museums are seeking to connect with institutions in different parts of the world with the aim of engaging with 'new' art worlds, as I explain in the next chapter.

### **Notes**

- <span id="page-140-0"></span>[1](#page-125-0) In *Art, Anthropology and the Gift* (2015) Roger Sansi explains the importance of, and differences between, the notion and practice of the gift in anthropological thinking and artistic practices. In this section, I add how elite groups mobilise the notion of the gift in their art management and narratives.
- <span id="page-140-1"></span>[2](#page-127-0) The artists invited to participate in the exhibition were Miguel Andrade, Sergio Abugattás, Victor Castro, Elena Damiani, Guiseppe de Bernardi, Nicole Franchy, Alejandro Jaime, Christians Luna, José Carlos Martinat, José Miyashiro, Raura Oblitas, Ishamael Randal Weeks, Iliana Scheggia, Manuel Larrea and Martín Jiménez Paz (MAC-Lima 2008).
- <span id="page-140-2"></span>[3](#page-128-0) Fernando de Szyszlo died in 2017 at the age of 92. Natalia Majluf left office in 2018 after running the MALI for 16 years.
- <span id="page-140-3"></span>[4](#page-129-0) See [Chapter 6,](#page-201-1) pp. 174.
- <span id="page-140-4"></span>[5](#page-130-0) 'Popular modernity'is understood as the processes of recombination of diverse cultural forms that occurs in, and in answer to, urban settings produced mainly by the urban experiences of migrants.
- <span id="page-140-5"></span>[6](#page-131-0) Micromuseo Productions organised the *Artistic Emergency* exhibition of 1999 (see [Chapter 1\)](#page-31-1). For an exploration of the Micromuseo project in relation to other Latin America museum projects, see Carla Pinochet Cobos (2016) and Manuel Kingman (2012: 39–64).
- <span id="page-140-6"></span>[7](#page-131-1) Susana Torres's public voice in relation to the Micromuseo project has been louder since 2010.
- <span id="page-140-7"></span>[8](#page-131-2) Its first *paradero,* in 2008, was in the Habana pub with an exhibition of the work of Victor Castro, a Mexican artist based in Lima, who was then my husband and initially helped me in the exploration of the Lima art scene.
- <span id="page-140-8"></span>[9](#page-131-3) For instance, more recently, Micromuseo has developed a project in the Andean region of Chavín de Huantar in collaboration with the local people and the National Museum of Chavín.
- <span id="page-140-9"></span>[10](#page-131-4) For an analysis of Museo de San Marcos's arte popular collection, see Germaná and Yllia (2008).
- <span id="page-140-10"></span> [11](#page-132-0) As a coordinator of the MAC-Lima, one of my first actions was to organise a meeting with bloggers to communicate and exchange opinions about the museum as a project. Among the most active and influential blogs on Barranco politics were: [http://elmorsa](http://elmorsa.blogspot.com) [.blogspot.com/](http://elmorsa.blogspot.com) (of the archaeologist Roberto Bustamante) and [http://playasdebarranco](http://playasdebarranco.blogspot.com) [.blogspot.com](http://playasdebarranco.blogspot.com) (of Enrique Delucchi); on the art scene: [http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com/](http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com) (of curators Miguel López and Emilio Tarazona); [http://escuela-de-marte.blogspot](http://escuela-de-marte.blogspot.com) [.com/](http://escuela-de-marte.blogspot.com) (a project of artists Felipe del Águila and Carlos Eduardo Risco, researcher Sonia Pérez Unzueta and a most active participation of curator David Flores-Hora); [http://](http://miraaeselococalato.blogspot.com)

- <span id="page-141-7"></span> [miraaeselococalato.blogspot.com/](http://miraaeselococalato.blogspot.com) (of curator Felipe Mayuri Poma); and covering various topics including the arts was – and still is – [http://lahabitaciondehenryspencer.com/](http://lahabitaciondehenryspencer.com) (communicator Luis Carlos Burneo) and [http://zonadenoticias.blogspot.com/](http://zonadenoticias.blogspot.com) (of the poet and literary critic Paolo de Lima).
- <span id="page-141-0"></span>[12](#page-132-1) Art historian and museologist William Lopez´s (2005) genealogy of art criticism in Colombia is of great interest. The text exposes the strong tradition of art criticism in this country, its weakening in the early 1990s due to the distancing of the art from society and the loss of its cultural sense, and the emergence of a new generation of critics in 2000 who opened new platforms of criticism, now on the internet, such as the influential blog Esfera Pública. Lopez shows how these online platforms have generated new models of art criticism and broadened the topics of discussion, however, explains that with texts full of theoretical references that in many cases lack intellectual rigour, they have not being able to expand their relationship with the wider audiences.
- <span id="page-141-1"></span> [13](#page-133-0) See, for instance, [http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com/2008/07/la-pelea-por-el-museo-3-re](http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com) [spuesta-de.html.](http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com)
- <span id="page-141-2"></span> [14](#page-133-1) Miguel Lopez's post 'La Pelea por el Museo' (The Fight for the Museum), published on 11 July 2008, provoked a large number of answers and re-posts (see [http://arte-nue](http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com) [vo.blogspot.com/2008/07/la-pelea-por-el-museo.html](http://arte-nuevo.blogspot.com)). See the compilation of part of this debate and the terms of the discussion at Esfera Pública 17 July 2008 (see [https://](https://esferapublica.org) [esferapublica.org/nfblog/la-pelea-por-el-museo/\)](https://esferapublica.org).
- <span id="page-141-3"></span>[15](#page-133-2) For what constitutes a museological plan, see SGME 2005.
- <span id="page-141-4"></span>[16](#page-137-0) See [Chapter 5](#page-171-1), p. 150.
- <span id="page-141-5"></span>[17](#page-138-0) The auction committee selects the artworks through different strategies: by directly asking artists to present their work; by visiting galleries or asking them to propose artworks from which the committee selects those they want; and by accepting artworks from private collections.
- <span id="page-141-6"></span>[18](#page-140-11) These exhibitions include *Gordon Matta Clark: Undoing the Space* in 2010, curated by Gabriela Rangel and Tatiana Cuevas, which travelled to the Chilean National Museum of Fine Arts, the Sao Paulo Museum of Modern Art and the MALI; *Fernando Bryce: Dibujando la Historia Moderna* in 2011–2012, which toured the MALI, University Contemporary Art Museum in Mexico DF and the Latin American Art Museum of Buenos Aires; and, in 2013, the MALI with the Americas Society co-organised the simultaneous exhibition of Peruvian New York–based photographer Milagros de la Torre's *Indicios* at the MALI and *Observed* at the Americas Society, curated by Edward Sullivan.

# <span id="page-142-1"></span><span id="page-142-0"></span>**[Artists on art](#page-7-10)**

# [Meta-reflections on the art scene](#page-7-10)

Over the last 20 years, Peruvian artists have reflected with great intensity on their position on the art scene and on the ways in which it develops and operates. Some of them have made this process of reflexivity their own work, becoming a kind of para-ethnography of the art scene. From different perspectives, artists approach the ways in which art has been framed, the politics and practices of museums and what exhibitions do, the art markets and art collecting, the place of Peru in international art circuits, and their personal aspirations and anxieties in the art circuit. These approaches become ways of researching, imagining and re-mapping today's circuits, platforms and categories of art. This section, therefore, provides another layer of information about the configuring of the contemporary art scene in Lima and at large from Peruvian artists' reflections. I have been in dialogue with this corpus of work that has triggered ideas and feelings in me during my research process.
