### **Beyond Missing Institionalities**

During the 1970s and 1980s, Peruvian artists reflected on the art scene and its precarity – for instance, Emilio Hernandez Saavedra's *Museo de Arte Borrado*, Grupo Parentesis announcements' *Se Necesita un Mecenas*  and Mariela Zevallos' *Baja en el Museo de Arte Moderno*  in E.P.S. Huayco's folder. Curator Gustavo Buntinx brought together these earlier works to develop what he calls the missing institutionality and the museum void. A similar interpretation was developed by the curators Tatiana Cuevas and Rodrigo Quijano in the *Arte al Paso: Museo de Arte de Lima's Collection* exhibition held in Sao Paulo in 2011. They divided the exhibition into three sections; the first was 'Critique Towards a Missing Institutionality and the Redeeming Power of the Past', which focused on the interrelationship between the lack of art institutions and art policies and the priority given by public cultural policies to archaeological and heritage legacy in response to the ways of <span id="page-143-0"></span>imagining Peru. These curators traced a genealogy from 1970s artworks to the most recent practices that tackle the – missing – institutionality.

While this is a direction of curatorial explanation, Peruvian artists are exploring the art scene from various other angles that need to be addressed. This is not only because there is already an institutionality – with its flaws – but because these are art practices that go beyond the institutional critique. I suggest that to remain under this scope limits the understanding of how artists are reflecting on and analysing the art scene in which they participate and which they shape. The aim of this section is to group part of this body of work which has not been put together in this way before. I have grouped the works into four intersected sections. There are more recent art projects in this direction, however, with one exception, my selection covers until 2015 in relation to my research time frame.

# **Art geographies: Mapping, tracing and archiving**

Work in this section maps the art geographies, traces the art circuits and captures the atmospheres of a time. Juan Salas's *Flows* is a series of digital impressions in which he tracks the origins and flows of artists to biennials and fairs into specific cities ([Figures 1](#page-150-2) and [2](#page-150-2)), with an emphasis on the south, to then elaborate a condense image of worldwide circulations over the year [\(Figure 3\)](#page-151-3). This is an open archive in which Salas documents the art nodes and the expansion of the geography of art. While Sala's work focuses on mobility and nodes, Alberto Borea unfolds the node, allowing us to see the art market geography through his series Skyline ([Figures 4](#page-151-3) and [5](#page-151-3)). Making use of art gallery guides of Latin America and of New York, Borea develops a landscape of skyscrapers composed by the list of galleries. The relation of art market spaces and the shaping of the city becomes central as he exposes the predominance of New York City with its large number of galleries and skyscrapers in relation to those under a Latin America region. Despite the growing transnational mobility of art players and the expansion of art spaces in the city, Valeria Ghezzi points out that the art scene is a *Little World* [\(Figure 6](#page-152-2))*.* Making use of and thinking through her <span id="page-144-0"></span>personal archives of business cards which she places on small easels over a small canvas, she suggests that the artworld is shaped more by art agents' connections and networks. Sandra Gamarra also makes use of her own archive. Her work *Correspondence I* [\(Figure 7](#page-152-2)) is part of a four-painting series based on the invitations and catalogues she received for art openings*.* In reproducing the invitations, Gamarra's painting allows us to see the exhibitions that took place in a specific time, 2000–2001, and the interest of the Lima art scene in that moment. Juan Salas' *Brief Guide 2008* [\(Figure 8](#page-153-0)) does something similar, but here the artist presents the exhibition display. Both Gamarra and Salas provide an atmosphere of a moment. Salas also reflects on the recent history of museums and exhibitions, with *Views of the End of the Decade* [\(Figure 9\)](#page-154-0), a title that plays with the name of MALI's *Views of the End of the Century*  series of exhibitions. While analysing his own process of selection and montage, Salas' work brings together projects and events also discussed in this book – e.g. Centro de Arte Ego, Limac's New Acquisition exhibition and Elena Damiani's work with a graffiti intervention – as well as others such as Guiseppe Campuzano's Museo Travesti del Perú or Alberto Casari's *La Alfombra de Villacorta*. The methodology of these works of tracing, connecting, making visible articulations and artscapes, and using personal archives (see also Rocio Gómez's work below), resonate with my own methods of research. I have had a particularly close dialogue with these artists' ways of thinking-making.

### **Categories and belonging: What? who? and how?**

These works reflect on the construction of the category of art and revise issues of aesthetics. Artists approach to what becomes art and who becomes an artist, evidencing the construction of these limits and, therefore, the possibility to widened them and the strategies to be within them. In the times in which the Peruvian art scene was enlarging its approach to art, the work of Susana Torres and Claudia Coca, Christian Bendayán and Fernando Gutiérrez, known as Huanchaco contributed to the discussion. Susana Torres and Claudia Coca put together the *Peruvian Beauty: Centro de Estéticas*  (Peruvian Beauty: Centre of Aesthetics) project and <span id="page-145-0"></span>exhibition, challenging the notion of beauty which has tended to privilege white people's features even in a diverse country as Peru, and proposing a plural idea of beauty. In a process of appropriation, replacement and visual friction, they include their own bodies replacing those of women in famous paintings and other images: Coca, more indigenous, situates herself replacing white women from Western classic and modern artworks [\(Figure](#page-155-2)  [10\);](#page-155-2) and Torres, whiter, includes herself in those representations of Andean and black women, such as in Guaman Poma de Ayala's drawings of the Inca queens ([Figure 11\)](#page-155-2). The exhibition included a beauty parlour where a stylist changed the artists' appearance, while a video produced by the artist Patricia Bueno under Torres and Coca's concept, juxtaposed this action with the artworks and messages such as 'A Greek nude is not the same as a *cholo calato* [naked *cholo*]'. Some years before, Christian Bendayán had also contested ideas of beauty and their protagonists with his painting *Estética Centre* (Aesthetic Centre, 1998). This work features two travesties – one the stylist, the other the client – in a beauty centre in which can be read 'unisex' and which space reflects a popular taste in Bendayán's colourful vivid style. Invited to the colloquium *Arguedas and Peru Today* (2004), Bendayán decided to produce a video, *I Have a Cousin Who Is a Painter* ([Figure 12\)](#page-156-1), as his presentation. This compelling video is a register of advertising mural painting and lettering produced by local painters in Amazonian urban Iquitos capturing people's aesthetics taste, while a male Amazonian voice-over offers analysis with humour: 'I've been told that for the upper class what I like is not fashionable … that of what you are talking about, that of minimal, conceptual, abstract, critical, technological, I don't understand, I've not seen it either. It is far from my world'. In a similar direction and in an ironic fashion Huanchaco's *Kill Bill* project ([Figures 13](#page-157-2) and [14\)](#page-157-2) combines the appropriation of Quentin Tarantino's celebrated film *Kill Bill* and Lima urban street culture. This project is composed of a video and paintings in which famous local actors and Huanchaco himself act. He, 'the artist', in the Bride's (Uma Thurman) yellow outfit, combats the reproduction of art and street creativity, which are strongly defended by Bill and his gang of workers. On the other hand, and in the <span id="page-146-0"></span>times of the booming art market, Pablo Ravina focuses on those ingredients that contemporary art 'must have' to be recognised and valued as such in the art scene [\(Figure 15](#page-158-2)). Each method, which range from a solid discourse [\(Figure 16](#page-158-2)) to self-referentiality, is deployed in a written and visual form, composing *Six Infallible Methods to Recognise Contemporary Art* and through which Ravina highlights the making of contemporary art as following a recipe (see also Miguel Aguirre's work below).

### **On the art museum: Fragility, expansion and appropriation**

At a time of weak institutionality in the mid-2000s but also of a growing debate about the museum and its gradual reinforcement in Lima (Chapter 3), artists reflected on this institution becoming active agents of discussion, imagination and museological proposal. Sandra Gamarra's LiMAC (Lima's Museum of Contemporary Art) online art project emerged as a museum platform itself in response to the ongoing lack of a museum of contemporary art in Lima [\(Figure 17](#page-159-2)). From a virtual museum to the management of tangible temporary exhibitions to LiMAC's art collection via Gamarra's appropriation of various artworks through painting – such as that of Ernesto Neto ([Figure 18](#page-159-2)), the LiMAC art project has become an agent in the art museum practices and debates. The fragile situation of the contemporary art museum was also explored by artists at MAC-Lima's 2008 *Construction of Place for All* exhibition. The disputes over the museums impacted the possibility of their participation in the show, when the municipality intended to shut down the exhibition and the museum. Using their bodies, artists sat down on the banner that announced '*Clausurado por disposición municipal*' (Closed for municipal regulation), and when the municipality finally could place the banner up, the group of artists changed it to '*Inaugurado por exposición municipal*' (Inaugurated for municipal exhibition) [\(Figure 19](#page-160-1)). These interventions immediately circulated in the public sphere and became part of the museum debate. In this specific exhibition, Christians Luna formulated a question that engaged with neighbours' dissatisfaction with the museum project: *What Does This Look Like?* [\(Figure 20\)](#page-161-2), a question written on cardboard panels and with proposed answers such <span id="page-147-0"></span>as 'parking', 'jail', 'school' and 'hospital', which were attached to the museum's surrounding fence [\(Figure](#page-161-2) [21\)](#page-161-2). Someone decided to include one more answer: '*Es una mierda*' (It's a piece of shit) while homeless people tore out part of the work to use the cardboards for their beddings. As some artists reflected on the struggles for a contemporary art museum, others focused on MALI's renewal and expansion, both on its physicality and collection. Through a photographic project, Eduardo Hirose's *MALI Intervention* series [\(Figure 22\)](#page-162-1) reveals that liminal moment in which artworks are covered or moved awaiting for their new location in the new project, and in which the building has the marks of the previous display also awaiting to its full refurbished. His photographs of buildings and objects without people in them allow us to see human decisions and projects, condensing the past, the hope of the future and the uncertain liminality of the present. While Hirose set his focus on the moment of physical expansion, Brus Rubio does it in the MALI's enlarging of its collection to include Amazonian art. *MALI's in the Mitayo* [\(Figures](#page-163-2) [23](#page-163-2) and [24](#page-163-2)) is a series of paintings produced after MALI acquired Rubio's work and highlights its inclusion in the museum. *Mitayo* means the hunted prey that is shared with the family or community. In using Amazonian references, Rubio hunts the MALI, which allows him a sphere for intercultural dialogue in the city. Finally, Alberto Borea's work is also a reflection on museum collections, this time of a leading international art museum. Borea's *Entering the Guggenheim in a Sudaca Way* [\(Figure 25](#page-164-1)) is an intervention in an empty site of the New York museum. He produces and records this performance not as an invited artist but as a visitor: as an artist with strategies and aspirations. Borea's work was acquired by the Guggenheim in 2015, this time 'entering the Guggenheim' as part of the widening of the museum's collection of Latin American art.

### **On the art market: Collecting, formats and anxieties**

Art collectors and the art market receive large attention not only from the academia but also from art practices. Jose Carlos Martinat's *Collector's Trap* [\(Figure 26](#page-165-1)) <span id="page-148-0"></span>is an intervention, and register, in MALI´s *Filmoteca*  (cinema archive and theatre) during its refurbishment, that includes a theatre seat and illuminated letters which read: 'theatre seat is offered to continue the work'; but whose work? The renovation of the Filmoteca or the artist's work? The work criticises the fetishist consumerism of art collectors who are willing to buy art which, although may be mocking them as in the title of the work, it is 'a Martinat'. In this way, Martinat enters into a play of critique, or of its same dilution. Showing the links between money, market, power and art, Miguel Aguirre's *Following Tips for Artists Who Want to Sell* [\(Figures 27](#page-166-2) and [28](#page-166-2)) 'follows' conceptual artist John Baldessari's *Tips for Artist Who Want to Sell* in a re-enactment for contemporary times, also uses Oliver Stone's *Wall Street* film – a film that shows the mechanisms and greed of the market. In a dual appropriation, Aguirre selects images of Stone's film that for him can be linked to Baldessari's instructions, and paint them reinforcing his questioning of the art market and also showing his knowledge of and relation to it. Further paradoxes of art production and art collecting are tackled by Velaochaga's work. *Living in Peru*  ([Figure 29\)](#page-167-2) shows a luxurious neat space produced in a Lichtensteinian fashion in which drastic different art – abstract artwork and a mural of Peru's armed conflict based on an actual terrorist's propaganda – is put together producing a feeling of disturbance and raising questions about the consumption of issues of violence as decoration in elite scenarios and the larger inequalities in contrasting ways of living. Velaochaga advances this contrasting relationship in featuring the photographer Mario Testino posing next to the terrorist leader Abimael Gúzman's portrait ([Figure 30\)](#page-167-2), in an ironic comment that the photographer could even attribute the taking of that picture to himself. In this way, Velaochaga criticises the branding of Testino as 'the Peruvian photographer' when he had spent most of his life abroad.

Other artists reflect on the market with a focus on specific art fairs. In *Exhibition Selling of Arte Popular* [\(Figure 31\)](#page-168-2), Primitivo, Venuca and Valeriana Evanán, members of the Association of Popular Artists from Sarhua (ADAPS), show the format, exhibition and selling practices of '*arte popular*' fairs in which their <span id="page-149-0"></span>painted boards are usually recognised. In a text at the bottom of the board, ADAPS recognises the role of the Ministry of Culture's Ruraq Maqui: Handmade art fair in promoting their work and culture. In the middle of the setting, artist Primitivo Evanán is represented playing the Andean horn, while others are producing their work at the sight of the visitors who take pictures. This piece can be analysed in dialogue with Venuca Evanán's later work of 2018, *Self-representation* ([Figure 32\)](#page-168-2), in which after her first participation in the art fair Art Lima, she reflects upon her position as an artist in this event, the location of her work and the protocols of showing and viewing in art fairs. The various dynamics and actors of Lima's art fairs are in the centre of analysis of Rocio Gómez's work ([Figure 33](#page-169-1) and [34\)](#page-170-1). With a focus on PARC, *An Image of an Exotic Land* combines different strategies and media to explore the Lima art market in which Goméz also participates. Becoming a sort of explorer, archivist and ethnographer, the artist penetrates this 'exotic land' of art fairs, registering and classifying like in museum practices the different activities, moments, actors and interactions that take place in these events. Gómez selects and appropriates photographic images of her archive and other sources through her painting to document and also to highlight certain issues, and her work shows something disturbing: all the booths are empty of artworks while the art fair is full of people who are talking, walking and 'looking'. In her decision of omitting the artworks she is analysing the event, its contradictions and her own anxieties as an artist belonging to, and shaping, today's art scene.

### <span id="page-165-1"></span>**On the art market: Collecting, formats and anxieties**

# <span id="page-171-1"></span>**5 Networking Latin America**

<span id="page-171-0"></span>[Transnational actors and the new role of art](#page-7-11)  collectors

At Art Basel Miami in 2013, a headline in one of the various newspapers handed out caught my attention: 'Why Latin America is hotter than ever. How a group of curators, collectors and scholars put a continent on the art-world map'. Laurie Rojas's article mentioned the Mexican curator Pedro León de la Barra and the art historian Mari Carmen Ramírez, and highlighted the roles of five collectors: New York–born and –based Estrellita Brodsky; Venezuela-born, New York–based Patricia Phelps de Cisneros; Cuba-born, Miami-based Ella Fontanals-Cisneros; Argentina-born, Buenos Aires–based Mauro Herlitzka; and Peru-born, Limabased Juan Carlos Verme, the latter, as I explained in [Chapter 4](#page-123-1), a collector and chairman of the board of the Museo de Arte de Lima.

Over the past few years, a growing body of journalistic and academic literature has celebrated the global positioning of Latin American art and other art scenes. However, there has been little work tracing the intersecting networks and agents' strategies across multi-level arenas which shape the process of 'putting on the art map', its mechanisms and consequences. In this chapter, I show how Lima's contemporary art scene is shaped in close and simultaneous interrelation with actors' multiscale practices and connections with regional and larger circuits. I illustrate here that the participation of Latin American art is due not only to the decentring of the Western art world, as some might imagine, but also to the strategic practices of Latin American art agents in articulation to the boosting of local art scenes. In previous chapters, I analysed the reconfiguration of the Lima art scene in relation to Peru's and Lima's political economy, and in doing so I have addressed aspirations to global connectivity. In this section I move my perspective, locating my analysis in the same transnational networks and agendas that cross Lima and through which Peruvian art and agents mobilise to participate across the globe. In [Chapter 4,](#page-123-1) I explained the different rearrangements of the Museo de Arte de Lima with its focus on the internationalisation of Peruvian contemporary art; of the museum itself; and of its members. Here I engage in further layers of analysis to uncover and understand the articulations of the Lima art scene. I place the construction of networks of power at the centre of the discussion, and trace the tactics and aspirations that produce articulations and disjunctures in the mobilisation and evaluation of art. At the same time, I distance my analysis from newspaper reports and studies that present the rise of Latin American art as unprecedented, <span id="page-172-2"></span>arguing that this perspective obscures previous regional articulations and greater complexities that are grounded-specific.

<span id="page-172-0"></span>I trace four particular moments in the networking of the Latin American region and its internationalisation of art. I briefly discuss the first three of these – the role of MoMA and of the Organization of American States; the Latin American project of the Social Theory of Art; and the US-led debate on museums' representation of Latin American art – as I bring events and processes analysed in previous chapters into relationship to these three moments[.1](#page-187-0) Then, I turn to the fourth current moment, which I describe as of transnational participation, and analyse three interwoven aspects: the position of Latin American collectors as trustees on leading museum's boards and their participation in reconfiguring the organisation of top museums; the transitional participation of Latin American curators; and the art-market-driven assemblages of the region, with a focus on Pinta: the Modern and Contemporary Latin American Art Show. Acting and networking through the category 'Latin America', collectors, markets, curators and museums have found a powerful strategy for mobilising and locating art practices from Latin America in the larger art sphere. Having witnessed the construction, circulation and deconstruction of the category 'Latin American art' throughout the 20th century, in the 21st century we are looking at its agency.

# **Three previous moments in the making of Latin American art**

It is not my aim here to undertake an analysis of the category 'Latin America'. Many scholars from social and literary studies have explained the region's colonial history and the problems deriving from this term (Coronil 1996; Quijano 2000; Mignolo 2005; Maldonado-Torres 2012). Neither is it my aim to discuss the category 'Latin American art', to which other scholars have contributed significantly (e.g. MOLAA 2011; Ramírez et al. 2012). Rather, I trace significant moments in the mobilisation of the notion of Latin American art and look at the ways in which Peruvian art and agents have participated, as not all Latin American countries are or have been part in the same way under the umbrella of Latin American art. As Arlene Dávila (2008: 126) suggests, this differential representation is related to different state policy across Latin American countries and to their elites' international work.

<span id="page-172-1"></span>With the opening of MoMA in 1929, the idea of 'Latin American art' was eagerly nurtured from the United States through the forming of collections and exhibitions such as the show *Latin-American Art in the Museum's Collection* in 1943[.2](#page-187-1) During this period Mexican art was represented more than that of any other country, followed by Brazilian, Cuban, Venezuelan and Argentinean art. Peru had a minimal presence. In the post-war period, MoMA's vigorous concern with Latin America declined as turned its focus to abstract painting from the United States. The leading role in the promotion of Latin American art was assumed by the Organization of American States (OAS) in Washington. In a compelling study, Claire Fox (2013) traces the agenda of the OAS Visual Arts Section and particularly of the Cuban José Gómez Sicre, its Chief Director between 1948 and 1976, <span id="page-173-0"></span>in creating a continental consciousness of Latin American art. The project gained strength after the Cuban Revolution and the consequent Alliance of Progress programme, which exhibitions were used as instruments to stop the advance of communism. Gómez Sicre promoted abstract and experimental art, distancing himself from social realism and any type of art with a clear political engagement. The spread of his project, which channelled notions of developmentalism and universalism on the continent, was possible through the cooperation of Latin American elites, intellectuals and artists who were perceived as modernising leaders by US diplomatic policy. As Claire Fox explains, 'Fundamental to the Visual Arts Section's activities was an optimistic, even triumphalist, view of the hemisphere as an egalitarian network of post-war 'international art centres' that emerged to displace Paris as the art capital' (2013: 5). Lima was part of this hemispheric urban network, particularly through the work of the Institute of Contemporary Art (see [Chapter 4\)](#page-123-1), whose international tactics and aspirations during the 1950s and 1960s should be understood within this wider scope. Moreover, the work of the Peruvian artist Fernando de Szyszlo was largely supported by this US programme. As Fox explains:

For many young Latin American artists, a show at the PAU's [Pan American Union] small gallery was a career turning point that enabled them to bypass restrictive or nonexistent national arts institutions and gain access to a broad international network of galleries, museums and publics. Among the wellknown twentieth-century artists who had their first solo show abroad at the union are Fernando Botero (Colombia, b.1932), … José Luis Cuevas (Mexico, b.1934), and Fernando de Szyszlo (Peru, b.1925)

(Fox 2013: 5)

From the late 1960s to the early 1980s it emerged a different, even opposite, agenda to that promoted by the United States. In this second moment, Latin America was networked through an internal project based on theoretical thinking and art practice that, in hindsight, were brought together as the Social Theory of Art (Lauer 1982; Eder et al. 1986) and conceptualism, respectively. The 1970s was an era of right-wing military dictatorships in several Latin American countries. Countries such as Chile, Argentina and Brazil received US support to control the advance of left-wing ideas. Nonetheless, Marxists ideas were already seeping into different realms of thought and action, and intersecting with local experiences of inequality. For instance in the social and economic arena, Dependency Theory was a response to Modernisation Theory and explained poverty as a consequence of economic disparity in the world system. Liberation Theology, conceived by the Peruvian Gustavo Gutierrez, was a Latin American attempt to think about poverty and justice from a religious point of view. In the arts, the Social Theory of Arts and conceptualism or *no-objetualismo* provided ways of talking and acting with regard to art from Latin America in which the focus on the art object and the artist was displaced to the larger art field.

Thinkers such as Damián Bayón, Juan Acha, Ida Rodríguez Prampolini, Victoria Novelo and Aracy Amaral as well as younger figures such as Rita Eder, Alfonso

<span id="page-174-0"></span>Castrillón, Nestor García Canclini, Roberto Miró Quesada, Mirko Lauer, Ticio Escobar and Adolfo Colombres, were part of this conversation. These scholars, many of them Peruvians (Acha, Lauer, Castrillón, Miró Quesada) questioned the transposition of art tendencies from Europe and North America to Latin America, pointed out the power structure of the world's art system and gave value to indigenous, African and popular culture. The Social Theory of Art brought art historians, critics and anthropologists to discuss and think together about Latin American art and addressed art as a broader phenomenon encompassing production, distribution and consumption, and entangled in relations of power and class – these ideas were largely developed by the Peruvian Mexican-based Juan Acha, who was also an active promoter of *no-objetualismo*. In relation to the latter, Luis Camnitzer (2007) emphasises that *no-objetualismo*, or conceptualism, was not the same as the conceptual art fostered in the European American mainstream, in which ideas and language became central in creating a new art style. 'The Periphery', he says, 'couldn't have cared less about a style and produced conceptual strategies instead. These strategies were formally open-ended and much more likely to be interdisciplinary' (2007: 2); 'art, politics, pedagogy, and poetry overlap' (2007: 21). However, as Camnitzer insists, these differences were erased in the writing of the art history with the recognition of conceptual art as the 'original' form.

The Social Theory of Art continental perspective was consolidated at two key events: the First Latin American Biennial of Sao Paulo, under the name *Mitos y Magia* (Myth and Magic) in 1978, and the First Colloquium of *Arte No Objetual y Arte Urbano* (No Object-Based Art and Urban Art) organised by the Museo de Arte de Medellin in 1981; neither event saw a second edition. The Marxist ideas of the mid-1980s were seen as obsolete, and in Peru were seen as related to the Shining Path's ideology. Some Latin American countries started to move towards a neoliberal economy in which art galleries and the art market would gain strong influence in defining art (Del Valle 2010). According to Mirko Lauer (interview, 2011), the Social Theory of Art provided a theory for a continent with no previous tradition of academic study of the arts. *Mitos y Magia* brought people who were working on a new art criticism together for the first time. Nowadays, the Social Theory of Art is being revisited by Latin American scholars in an attempt to relocate this work in history and answer new challenges under the conditions of globality. Conceptualism is also being re-examined. Some of the people who came together around the Social Theory of Art, including Juan Acha, Ticio Escobar, Mirko Lauer, Alfonso Castrillón, Gerardo Mosquera, Nelly Richard and Gustavo Buntinx spoke at the key *Coloquio Latinoamericano de Arte: Modernidad y Provincia* (see [Chapter 1\)](#page-31-1) at Peru's Trujillo Biennial in 1987.

Conferences, as Andrea Giunta (2007: 27–39) points out in her thoughtful answer to James Elkins's (2007: 5) assumption of a lack of art history in Latin America, have been significant channels in the making of this history. For instance, Giunta mentions the Congress of the Art History Association in Zacatecas, organised by Rita Eder in 1992, where two generations of academics came together for discussion triggering new networks:

<span id="page-175-0"></span>There, an entire generation of beginners found themselves face to face with the authors of the books they had read. … Friendship and working networks were forged that still remain active today. There we met Hadjinicolaou, García Canclini (who was exiled in Mexico), Aracy Amaral, Nelly Richard, Ida Rodríguez Prampolini, Jorge Alberto Manrique, Fausto Ramírez, Juan Acha and Gerardo Mosquera; for us they were all mythical figures. We also met colleagues from other Latin American countries, closer to our generation: Natalia Majluf, Renato Gonzalez Mello, Jaime Cuadreillo, Cuauhtémoc Medina, Justo Pastor Mellado, Gabriel Peluffo, Gustavo Buntinx, Clara Bargellini, Graciela Schmilchuk, Alexandra Kennedy Troya. … There were also people we couldn't speak with at the time because they didn't speak Spanish and we didn't speak English. … It was coming out of our generation: we were present as a group at an international congress, and to our surprise, we received recognition as the discipline's up – and- coming generation.

(Giunta 2007: 34)

It is this generation of art historians that currently holds leading positions at museums and in university programmes on Latin American art. They are active in promoting and defining art in and for Latin America, while working with and in parallel to a new generation of art historians and curators.

The third key period in discussing Latin American art took place in the 1990s when scholars criticised the monolithic ways that Latin American art was being represented. This debate unfolded predominantly in the United States through the work of art historian Mari Carmen Ramírez and also through the Cuban curator Gerardo Mosquera, among others. In reviewing a set of exhibitions, and particularly the show *Art of the Fantastic: Latin America 1920–1987*  at the Museum of Indianapolis curated by Holliday Day and Hollister Sturges, in which 'the fantastic' was suggested as a 'mode of expression in Latin American culture' and a vehicle for defining 'their special cultural identity that developed over a period of 400 years' (1987: 10), Ramírez (1992) warned against a static understanding of identity and the suppression of processes. She affirmed, 'At the core of this problem lies the inadequacy of the conceptual framework that informs North American curatorial practices' to deal with the complex logic that gave rise to modern art in a continent recently described by the Argentinean cultural theorist Nestor García Canclini as the continent of the 'semi', i.e. semi-modern, semi-developed, semi-European, semi-indigenous (1992: 61). As I mentioned in [Chapter 1,](#page-31-1) a new corpus of Latin American studies on urban anthropology provided art scholars and curators with new theoretical frameworks with which to explain the diverse identities and modernities on the continent, and to support their claims to more complex representation of the art from Latin America. It was with this international debate about the region's multiple and hybrid identities that the first Lima Biennial (1997) intended to engage.

# <span id="page-176-3"></span>**A fourth moment: From representation to participation, and its paradoxes**

<span id="page-176-0"></span>Today sees a fourth moment in networking the region, in which the strategy has moved from claims for representation to transnational participation, and in which elites as collectors are playing a leading role, particularly through museums[.3](#page-187-2) Art collectors have opened up new paths for the circulation of Latin American art and for other agents, particularly curators, as they have located themselves in crucial decision-making positions on the boards of major museums and have become a global cultural elite fostering global art politics and the art markets. In addition the mobilisation of Latin American curators to, and the collaborative work with, leading art institutions have enabled the widening of the dominant art narratives, which has meshed with the reassessment of the mainstream art world's hegemonic vision towards multiple modernities and new contemporary art worlds. Some of earlier concerns about the representation of Latin America are now being tackled with more encompassing museum collections and informed curatorial work (Ramírez 1992, 1996); however, others remain, while the paradoxes of participation arise:[4](#page-187-3) what are the mechanisms of participation, and ultimately, who defines the framework? Does it allow the different participants equal mechanisms for re-positioning? Who are the actors that participate and which epistemologies are mobilised? How is participation channelled, and most importantly, what does participation channel back?

## <span id="page-176-1"></span>*Latin American art collectors in museums: Driving global art policies*

<span id="page-176-2"></span>In 2006 MoMA established its Latin American and Caribbean Acquisition Fund, supported by the collector Patricia Phelps de Cisneros. In the same year, with an endowment from the collector and art historian Estrellita Brodsky, MoMA created a new position: the Estrellita Brodsky Curator of Latin American Art. Luis Pérez Oramas, previously the curator of the Cisneros collection, perhaps the finest collection of Latin American geometric art which has played a key role in readdressing this genre, holds this position. When Pérez-Oramas took the post, he stated, 'Geometric abstraction and Constructivism were minimally considered in the acquisitions and they must be better represented in the museum nowadays' (*IBLNEWS*, 2006). Consequently, the 2008 MoMA exhibition *New Perspectives in Latin American Art* included works of not only social realism, magic realism and fantastical art but also geometric art, abstraction and conceptualism. With this, MoMA displayed a more diverse and complex history of Latin American art[.5](#page-187-4) Other exhibitions, such as *Inverted Utopias* curated by Mari Carmen Ramírez (Museum of Fine Arts in Houston, 2004); *The Geometry of Hope: Latin American Abstract Art from the Patricia Phelps de Cisneros Collection*, under the curatorship of Gabriel Pérez-Barreiro (Blanton Museum of Art and NYU Grey Art Gallery, 2007); and *Constructive Spirit*: *Abstract Art in South and North America, 1920s–50s*, curated by Mary Kate O'Hare (Newark Museum, 2010), have clarified the inclusion of Latin American abstraction as an original movement rather <span id="page-177-0"></span>than as mere mimicry, stressing the links that have shaped both Latin American and North American abstraction. In this vein, exhibitions such as *Nexus New York: Latin/American Artists in the Modern Metropolis* (2009–2010) curated by Deborah Cullen, and *New York, 1613–1945* (2010) curated by Marci Reaven and Mike Wallace, both at the Museo del Barrio, also advanced in showing artistic influences and exchanges between the United States and Latin America (Sullivan 2010). However, Peruvian art was still only scantily represented at these exhibitions, and it was only after 2010 that this changed. The rescaling of geometric and abstract Latin American art at the United States and Europe, promoted by Latin American collectors, curators and scholars also impacted Latin American art worlds. For instance, in 2011, the Museo de Arte de Lima, aiming to show that Peruvian art is a part of this history, featured the exhibitions *Jorge Piqueras: From Structure to Explosion, a Geometry in Process, 1952–59,* and *Szyszlo: Restrospective*, among others*.* In 2011, MALI auctioned several works by the artist Benjamin Moncloa, one of the most important geometric abstract artists in Peru, who for decades was largely overshadowed by the overwhelming reiteration of Fernando de Szyszlo's work.

Tate applied similar dynamics to MoMA: in 1999 the Tate Americas Foundation established a permanent office in New York, and in 2003 the Tate Latin American Acquisition Committee was founded. In 2006, a year after assuming his position as chairman of MALI's board, Juan Carlos Verme became a member of this Committee and is one of the few people comprising the Tate Americas Foundation's board of trustees. In 2002, Tate Modern appointed Cuauhtémoc Medina as its first curator of Latin American Art, followed since 2009 to 2012 by Julieta González, who has been on the advisory curatorial team of MALI's Acquisition Committee. With an endowment from Estrellita Brodsky in 2012, Tate, like MoMA, opened the post of Estrellita B. Brodsky Adjunct Curator of Latin American Art to which the Colombian curator José Roca was elected. According to Tate (2012), the post 'reflects Tate's continuing commitment to broadening the geographical scope of the Collection'. With the aim of broadening the museum's interpretations, Tate Modern also launched the Space Initiative Project, based on:

peer-to-peer collaborations with cultural organisations from around the world. It brings together emerging curators from both the Tate Modern and international venues for contemporary art to work together on an exhibition that will be manifested in both locations. … Thus the exhibitions open up the possibility of introducing new work and interpretations within differing global contexts. (Tate n.d.)

This was the arena for *Ruins in Reverse* curated by Flavia Frigeri of the Tate Modern Gallery and Sharon Lerner of the Museo de Arte de Lima. Today, peerto-peer collaborations represent a significant mode of work among art institutions. Yet it is not surprising that in this participatory regime, the collaborations tend not to be between major museums or institutions but instead they temporarily connect art institutions on different scales around the globe, while this temporary <span id="page-178-1"></span>relationship hides a hierarchical structure and disguises this order as 'cooperation'. In addition, such joint projects are generally held in museums and institutions that already have the infrastructure and the administrative 'efficiency', such as the Museo de Arte de Lima in Peru, and the leading art venues' trust to embark on international collaborative projects.

The Guggenheim Museum also amplified its scope in 2012 with the launch of its large collaborative programme UBS MAP Global Art Initiative and subsequent focus on three regions: South and Southeast Asia, Latin America, and the Middle East and North Africa. For two years, a selected curator from each of these regions proposed explanations of the arts in the region and, in conversation with museum's curators, suggested works for purchase and educational programmes. In 2013 the Mexican curator Pedro León de la Barra was appointed as the Guggenheim's UBS MAP Curator for Latin America.

I returned to New York in September 2014. Two exhibitions of Latin American art were running in this central city: *Urbes Mutantes: Latin American Photography 1944–2013* at the International Center of Photography, co-curated by Alexis Fabry and María Wills, and *Under the Same Sun: Art from Latin America Today at the Guggenheim*, curated by Pablo León de la Barra. Both included the work of several Peruvian artists for the first time. The first was based on the collection of Leticia and Stanislas Poniatowski and included work from the Peruvian photographers and artists Luz María Bedoya, Milagros de la Torre, Flavia Gandolfo, Jesús Ruiz Durand, Herbert Rodríguez, Bill Caro, Pablo Hare, Susana Torres, Eduardo Villanes, TAFOS, Carlos Dominguez, Herman Schwarz, Daniel Pajuelo, Jaime Razuri and Nicolas Torres. The Guggenheim exhibition featured the work of the Peruvian artist Armando Andrade Tudela and Peru-based artists Gilda Mantilla and Raymond Chávez. León de la Barra organised the exhibition around six themes: abstraction, conceptualism, modernities, participation/emancipation, political activism and the Tropical. The once 'other' Latin America achieved a place at one of the major museums; its aspiration to acquire globality was celebrated in the 'global' site and in the 'localities'. At the same time, something was clear: if the show tackled indigeneity with texts including the words 'indigenous', especially in the Tropical section, this was only through the representation of 'Western' Latin American artists[.6](#page-187-5) Local processes expanding the limits of art to encompass indigenous art were not reflected at the exhibition. 'Participation' had its limits.

<span id="page-178-0"></span>In the 2010s, the relation between the Latin American and Spanish art circuits recovered its dynamism it had enjoyed in early 2000s. As I discussed in [Chapters 1](#page-31-1) and 3, Spain connected the region by fostering the Ibero-American project. I have shown how this project have operated through the promotion of a network of cities, scholarships and the establishment of cultural centres and travelling exhibitions. This nexus was also fostered through Spanish museums and the art market. The Museo Nacional Centro de Arte Reina Sofía held a project with five-parallel-exhibitions called *Versiones del Sur* (Southern Versions) with Spanish and Latin American curators: Mari Carmen Ramírez and Héctor Olea with *Heterotopías: Medio Siglo sin Lugar (1918–1968)* (Heterotopies: Half of Century <span id="page-179-0"></span>without Place); Gerardo Mosquera with *No es solo lo que ves: Pervirtiendo el minimalismo* (It's Not Just What You See: Perverting Minimalism); Mónica Amor and Octavio Zaya with *Más allá del Documento* (Beyond the Document); Carlos Basualdo and Zaya in *Eztetyca del Sueño* (Aesthetics of the Dream), and Ivo Mesquita and Adriano Pedrosa with *F[r]icciones* (F[r]ictions). In an interview I undertook with art historian Gabriela Rangel, she stressed the importance of this event for bringing Latin American and Spanish curators together and developing larger networks (interview, September 2014). Also in 2000, the Madrid art fair ARCO featured a section called Cutting Edge that included the exhibition *Cono Sur*. This exhibition presented works from Peru, Argentina and Chile under the curatorship of Justo Pastor Mellado, Marcelo Pacheco, Gabriel Peluffo, Gustavo Buntinx, Adriano Pedrosa and Sandra Antelo-Suárez. Four of these curators – Pacheco, Peluffo, Pedrosa and Buntinx – also sat on the selection committee of the Lima National Biennial in the same year.

In 2011 Latin America recovered its solid presence on the Spanish art market when ARCO launched its new section Solo Projects: Latinoamérica. The Museo Reina Sofía also reinforced its view towards 'the south' at the hands of its new director Manuel Borja (2008–present), previously the director of the Barcelona Museum of Contemporary Art (MACBA). Like the other top museums, the Museo Nacional Centro de Arte Reina Sofía launched its own foundation, the Fundación Reina Sofía in 2012, which, among other aims, cultivates collaborations with museums in Latin America through the Museums of the South Network programme. Amongst its founders are the chairmen of the boards of the Museo de Arte de Lima and Museo de Arte Contemporáneo-Lima, Juan Carlos Verme and George Gruenberg respectively, with Verme also its vice president. In addition, Manuel Borja has supported the Latin American curatorial network, Red de Conceptualismos del Sur (Southern Conceptualisms Network), which sheds light on art in the context of dictatorial and violent regimes in Latin America providing new attention to conceptualism.

In sum, the direct involvement of Latin American art collectors and curators in the operation of top museums has fostered a rise in the visibility and value of Latin American art showing its specificities, connections and contributions, such as in the promotion of Latin American abstract art and *conceptualismo*. Collectors are acting simultaneously in private and public, in national and international museums, in a transnational condition. However, it must also be remembered that these tactics require a huge amount of social and economic capital if they are to influence the formation of collections and the structure of major museums. The growing participation of elite groups is shaping cultural policy on large scales and transforming the conditions of the museum, as well as those of the market.

#### *From local to transnational curators and a curatorial network*

As I explained in Chapter 3, curatorship in Lima was consolidated during the first half of the 2000s with a predominantly local role, producing new art narratives in a changing Lima and Peru. Since 2007 the curatorial activity, in relation to the <span id="page-180-1"></span>new channels opened by Latin American art collectors in international museums, to the growth of Latin American art fairs and to the formation of curatorial networks, became a more multi-scalar practice for those who could connect on these circuits.[7](#page-187-6) I focus now on the young Peruvian curator Miguel López, whose work marks and shapes this new transnational moment on Lima's art scene.

<span id="page-180-0"></span>With an interest in writing, Miguel López started his studies in literature at the Católica University, but soon dropped them to take classes at the institute of photography Centro de la Imagen. López found himself fascinated with conceptual art to which he was introduced by the lecturer and curator Max Hernández. For his graduation project he traced Peruvian agents who had been named as curators in journals, catalogues, newspapers, and other sources, and photographed each of them, creating a visual map of Peru's 'Curators', as his project was titled. Soon López and Sharon Lerner, now MALI's Curator of Contemporary Art, became the pupils and assistants of the curator Jorge Villacorta, López's and Lerner's professor at Centro de la Imagen and La Católica, respectively. Working with Villacorta, they participated as co-curators in the exhibition *Urbe y Arte: Imaginarios de Lima en transformación 1980–2005* (see Chapter 3)**,** which López said was like 'doctoral training'. In parallel, he and curator Emilio Tarazona were starting their research for their exhibitions on Peruvian conceptualism.

In 2006, contemporary Peruvian art historiography was very little explored, with certain exceptions (e.g. Hernández Calvo and Villacorta 2002; Buntinx 2005a; Lauer 2007). In 2007, two revealing exhibitions were launched by Emilio Tarazona and Miguel López, *Persistencia de lo efímero, orígenes del no-objetualismo peruano*, *1965–1975* (The Persistence of the Ephemeral, Origins of Peruvian Non-Objectualism) at Centro Cultural de España, and *Arte Nuevo y Fulgor de la Vanguardia* (Arte Nuevo and the Glow of the Vanguard) at Miró Quesada Garland Gallery. These exhibitions documented the development of *no-objetualismo* in Peru during the 1960s and 1970s, a chapter of Peruvian art history that had remained invisible due to the decline of the Social Theory of Art in the early 1980s. Alongside other projects in the region, the exhibitions promoted the revival of this theory and shed light on *no-objetualismo* or conceptualism as a proposal from Latin America. For instance, Tarazona and López's research reintroduced to the art circuit the work of Teresa Burga, who in 2015 was one of the two Peruvians at Okwui Enwezor's *All the World's Futures*, the central exhibition at the 56th Venice Biennale.

Using a large part of his salary as Villacorta's assistant and already working for his exhibitions with Tarazona, López travel to Buenos Aires to meet the art historian Ana Longoni, co-author of *Del Di Tella a "Tucuman Arde": Vanguardia Artística y Política en el 68 Argentino* (with Mariano Mestman) and of *De los Poetas Malditos al Videoclip* (with Ricardo Santoni)*,* who for López has the sharpest approach to the Argentinean repression in the 1960s. According to him, the Red de Conceptualismos del Sur (RCS) was imagined in a coffee shop in Buenos Aires during a conversation he had with Ana Longoni, and was effectively created at Barcelona in 2007. In an interview we held in April 2014, López explained:

<span id="page-181-0"></span>I travelled to meet Ana, who had been very inspiring for me and I knew she was going to transform my work. And that was the beginning of the Red de Conceptualismos del Sur. I told her "I know this person and this person in Chile and Mexico who are working on this". And she said, "I know this person and this person in Brazil and Chile." And she proposed meeting. This was in August 2006.

She kept hold of the idea of getting together and had a contact at MACBA [Barcelona Museum of Contemporary Art]. She went for a conference, and a meeting was proposed to discuss conceptual art in Latin America. She sent me an email 'We have this opportunity.' The following year in March or April 2007 we had the first international meeting where we formed the network … in a bar over four beers, saying 'OK, we're gathered here – we're five Latin Americans [Fernando Davis and Ana Longoni (Argentina), Emilio Tarazona (Peru), Cristina Freire (Brazil) and Miguel López] – and we'll make it so the meetings we have won't be in Europe but in Latin America', because the project was to discuss local urgencies in our own contexts and to be able to create the infrastructure for storing those memories in our own localities.

*Me: What do you think the main contributions of the RCS have been?* 

*ML*: Its impact has been different at different times. To start with, the discussion had to do with a latent fear as the market was engulfing the patrimony of the archives to butcher and sell as conceptual art as if they were nothing but bits of paper. The first positioning of Red was to say 'The archives remain in our contexts and the work of we intellectuals, curators, museum directors, institutions is to build the conditions for the preservation and research of these materials'. That's the first statement we made: the archives remain in Latin America. And if a foreign institution wants to access those archives, it has to imagine some kind of policy for that, therefore we have to imagine horizontal policies that keep those documents here but at the same time allow them to be investigated in other contexts: but they stay here.

It was also a political project. It was a research project that had a clear time frame of work from the 1960s to the 1980s, when there was a series of violent and murderous military dictatorships, as well as contexts of extreme violence in Peru, Colombia, Mexico, and where these materials [documents] were not only materials that could intervene in the histories of art, but above all could take part in discussions about democracy, citizenship and human rights; these materials do not account for the history of art but for political struggles, social processes, which are critical to us. It was clear that the position regarding these materials was a political position. It was an impressive experiment, which dramatically politicised me.

Miguel López has built much of his network across Latin America and Spain. MACBA offered him the possibility of attending its new Independent Study Programme (PEI). This experience consolidated his perspective: 'It was an <span id="page-182-1"></span>experiment that mixed queer theory, activism, with economics, with geography, with neo-Marxism. It was a very political thing'. When the MACBA's director, Manuel Borja, became director of the Museo Reina Sofía in 2008, the museum reinforced its relationship with Latin America not only through the creation of the Fundación Reina Sofía mentioned above but also with its support for the Red de Conceptualismos del Sur (RCS). In 2010, RCS proposed a research project to the Museo Reina Sofía, which became the influential travelling exhibition *Perder La Forma Humana: Una imagen sísmica de los años ochenta en América Latina*  (Losing the Human Form: A Seismic Image of the 1980s in Latin America)*,* presented in 2012 at the Museo Reina Sofía, MALI, and the Museo de la Universidad Nacional de Tres de Febrero (MUNTREF) in Buenos Aires. The exhibition brought together a large body of archive material that showed how artists and art engaged politically in times of repression and violence, drawing attention to the sense and role of art in these contexts. Since 2018, Ana Longoni leads the Museo Reina Sofía's Public Affairs Office, consolidating the relationship between the Museo Reina Sofía and the RCS, and their interest in Latin America from a particular political approach[.8](#page-188-0)

<span id="page-182-0"></span>I want to clarify that the Red de Conceptualismos del Sur has not been the only transnational archive project working in Latin American art during the last ten years. While Red the Conceptualism del Sur has strengthened through its relationship with Spain, the Museum of Fine Arts of Houston and the International Center for the Arts of the Americas (ICAA) in the United States, under the leadership of Mari Carmen Ramírez, have developed a cooperative project with Latin American institutions to create a digital archive of 20th-century Latin American and Latino art. The first phase concluded in 2012, and included the publication of the volume *Resisting Categories: Latin American and/or Latino Art* edited by Mari Carmen Ramírez, Héctor Olea and Tomás Ybarra-Fausto. The project's partner and mediator for the archival selection in Peru, was the Museo de Arte de Lima with curator Gustavo Buntinx, as its first coordinator, who has active connections in the United States, where he also studied.

Both Miguel López and Gustavo Buntinx, who belongs to an older generation of curators, approach the art that engage with social and political aspects. Yet they do this on different terms: Buntinx stresses popular culture, contaminated aesthetics and political memory, while López reinforces the political agency of art through activism, bodies and subjectivities. López has increased the circulation and value of artists working in this direction. As artist Natalia Iguiñiz told me in an interview in February 2014: 'When younger critics like Miguel López began to have more strength my work started to find another type of circulation, because they are more sensitive to more political art, to something more related to archives, more testimonial, linked to political life. They have begun to have that eye'. López and other curators have shed light on the role of art in the context of dictatorial regimes and crises, and on how it impacts and is contested by the micropolitics of the body. While these curators have contributed to expand the art narratives and subjects in intersection to a socio-political dimension; I also argue that in the last years there have been an overused of 'the political', as the <span id="page-183-2"></span><span id="page-183-0"></span>mainstream's expectation of Latin American, and other postcolonial countries, art is for it to deal with political, historical and social issues[.9](#page-188-1) In this scenario, the political agenda and the capacity for imagination have lost their potential by becoming a formula mobilised by artists and curators themselves.

<span id="page-183-1"></span>López operates as a transnational Peruvian curator, or moreover as an 'art broker' (Ramírez 1996). He defines himself 'as a person who sets things in motion. … The materiality of this setting in-motion is uncertain. It may be an exhibition, a book, a talk, a network or a magazine' (interview, April 2014). Since 2012, he has been one of the curator advisors to MALI's Contemporary Art Acquisitions Committee, enabling – not without opposition among some of its members – the inclusion of political work in the collection. Today López is curator in residence of the Costa Rican project TEOR/éTica and has been awarded the 2016 Independent Vision Curatorial Award by twelve nominees around the world.[10](#page-188-2) He is also a cofounder and active member of Bisagra, an independent curator-artist run space in Lima. Working simultaneously in Lima, the Latin American region and on other art circuits, López has achieved a wide network mobilising information, projects and amplifying the visibility for Peru's contemporary art as he has positioned himself. He is part of a new generation of Latin American curators who started to perform across multi-level arenas and influence in the international understanding of the arts from Latin America.

### *A Latin American art fair at central art nodes: Boosting the market*

'Latin American art' as a market and collecting category exists since 1979 with the first auction of Latin American Art celebrated in Sotheby's and years later in Christie's. Since then, every year these auction houses hold two auctions of Latin American Art in New York, one in May and the other in November. The pioneering Latin American art dealer, the New Yorker Mary-Anne Martin explains, in the book chapter 'The Latin American Market Comes of Age: Some Thoughts on the Past Twenty-One Years', published in 1999, how the creation of this market took place at the auction houses and some galleries, while pointing out that museum curators, with few exceptions, had ignored this area. Yet, as I have just explained, this situation has shifted in the past years with the joint work of Latin American collectors and curators in leading museums. In addition, the market promotion of Latin American art has expanded and changed.

On one front, 'Latin American Contemporary Art' became a market category. In 2009, Philip de Pury and Company, the third auction house, started its thematic auctions of Contemporary Art in New York and London, with auctions based on the BRIC group (Brazil, Russia, India and China), Africa and Latin America, enlarging the market scope of the contemporary. On another front, art fairs started to proliferate becoming crucial market, and curatorial, platforms. This has also been the case in Latin America in which during the past 15 years, the number of art fairs has grown considerably (see Borea 2016). While the established ArteBA (Buenos Aires) was created in 1991 and MACO in Mexico City in 2002, most of the others have been launched since 2005: ArtBo in Bogota and SP Arte in Sao <span id="page-184-0"></span>Paulo in 2005; ChACO in Santiago de Chile in 2009; Art Rio in Rio de Janeiro in 2010, and in 2013 PArC and Art Lima in Lima. In this boom of art fairs, it is important to note that Art Basel Miami was created in 2002 and has been a crucial arena providing 'global' visibility for some Latin American artists, galleries and collectors. Another central art fair in the making of Latin American art is Pinta: The Modern & Contemporary Latin American Art Show, conceived by two Argentinean entrepreneurs with a network perspective, Alejandro Zaia and Diego Costa Peuser.

I met Alejandro Zaia at Philip de Pury and Company's Contemporary Latin American Art Auction in 2010. The work of two Peruvian artists was included in this auction's second edition: the emerging artist Alberto Borea and the established artist Ramiro Llona. As I was living in New York, and due to my research and my relationship with these two artists, one my brother and the other a good friend, I decided to go to the auction, although I confess that I felt insecure about what to wear and how to act as it was the first time I had attended an auction in New York and I did not know anybody. There I also met the Peruvian New York–based Cecilia Jurado, one of the owners of Y Gallery, which promotes young artists in New York with special attention to Latin American artists and the Colombian-born New York–based gallerist León Tovar. Months later, I attended Pinta, where I met the Peruvian art historian José Falconi, who was finishing his dissertation at Harvard University. José, my colleague and friend Lee Douglas and I went to the lounge for a drink. José introduced us to Alejandro Zaia, who, to my surprise, was one of Pinta's chairmen. We got in contact again months later. I thought he would be going to the Armory Show, especially because the second edition of Armory Focus, after Focus Berlin, was on Latin America, so I emailed him and asked for an interview.

We met in a hotel lounge with a mesmerising view of Manhattan at the corner of Rector and Greenwich Streets. He started asking me about my research, my professional experience and my interest in arts. It was not the first time an interviewee evaluated me before an interview. I had had a similar experience in 1998 in the Amazonian community of San Francisco when the renowned shaman Lucio Muñoz, after first refusing to talk with me, on my second attempt asked me several questions about my understanding of Shipibo cosmology. He then agreed to share his time and knowledge with me. In both cases I was with knowledgeable people in their fields and elite actors.

Alejandro Zaia is an Argentinean businessman who studied political science. In 1997 he founded a public affairs company in Argentina. In 2001 he left Buenos Aires to live in Miami, where he reoriented his firm towards the Latin American market, opening offices in Buenos Aires, Bogota, Sao Paulo, Mexico DF and Santiago de Chile, and co-created another group for the Hispanic market. In 2004 in Miami he met Diego Costa Peuser, another Argentinean entrepreneur with network perspectives who had co-founded the art magazine *Arte al Día*  with his father, Jorge Augusto Costa Peuser in 1980. Nowadays *Arte al Día* is one of the most influential Latin American art journals, along with *Art Nexus*. In 1998 the magazine's executive office moved to Miami, and years later it opened <span id="page-185-0"></span>offices in Mexico City and Caracas. In 2003 Costa Peuser launched Arteamericas, a fair dedicated to Latin American art, in Miami. He also organised art events in Buenos Aires and Lima as I explain in the next chapter. Zaia and Costa Peuser invited the Argentinean art collector and promoter Mauro Herlitzka to join their project to create a Latin American art fair in New York. Herlitzka was the chairman of Fundación Espigas, vice president of the Museo de Arte Latino Americano de Buenos Aires and a member of Tate Modern's Latin American Acquisition Fund, MoMA's International Council and the Latin American and Caribbean Acquisition Fund, and has been the director of the ArteBA art fair.

According to Zaia, one reason for setting Pinta in New York was that there was an 'important critical group' of artists, art dealers, and public and private institutions working on Latin American art, but there was nothing to bring them together at the market level. Pinta was seen as the connecting market platform and as a channel for the circulation of Latin American galleries, their artists and other agents. Zaia found Pinta a challenging idea: 'I like it, I feel comfortable with projects that develop brands, new things, which generate value. Branding and positioning strategies were applied to an art project' (interview, March 2011). Pinta was founded in 2007. Strategically, it took place in November, days before Sotheby and Christie's auctions of Latin American art. To participate, galleries must feature works by Latin American artists living in Latin America or abroad, or present the work of foreign artists who have had a long working relationship with Latin America. The plan was for 50 per cent of the participating galleries to be Latin American and the others from the United States and Europe. In 2009, the fair brought together 34 galleries, invoiced \$8 million, and received 7,000 visitors at its locations in the Metropolitan Pavilion and the Altman Building. In 2010, Pinta was held at Pier 92, where the Armory Show is held, with 54 galleries, sales of over \$10 million, and 12,000 visitors.

Pinta offers incentives to museums dealing with Latin American art to increase their collections through its Matching Funds Programme. Museums such as MoMA, the Museum of Fine Arts in Houston, El Museo del Barrio in New York, and the Museo de Arte de Lima have all been recipients of this fund. The condition for this support is that the museum's director, and preferably the board too, attend Pinta to make their acquisition. As most museum boards today consist of active art collectors, Zaia told me during our interview in 2011 that their attendance generally also leads to more acquisitions for their personal collections and their participation increases the fair's symbolic value. Yet Pinta has its detractors. Some point out that the reinforcement of a particular niche in the art market for 'Latin American art', reduces 'Latin American art' to culturally specific rather than 'universal' art. However, for the organisers participating in a Latin American art fair does not detract from the artists and galleries' potential to be 'global'; rather creates a platform for positioning the artists' trajectories of artists to move strategically from there.

Pinta's promoters decided to increase the scope of the art fair by launching Pinta London in 2010. Here the conditions were different: while Pinta's project in New York was to integrate different agents already operating there through <span id="page-186-0"></span>the market, in London the goal was to excite interest in Latin American art. The organisers sought support from Tate Modern and the University of Essex. In London, Pinta's promoters did not receive criticism about the categorisation of Latin America. On the contrary: White Cube Gallery, for instance, participated with the work by the internationally recognised Mexican artist Damián Ortega, expecting that Pinta would bring together a series of collectors who would be very interested in buying Ortega's works.

Pinta has been a channel for the circulation of Latin American galleries, artists, curators, collectors and other stakeholders. The reorientation of the Museo de Arte de Lima ([Chapter 4\)](#page-123-1) and the Pinta project have been closely connected since Pinta's debut. MALI's board members Juan Carlos Verme and Armando Andrade have constantly been part of Pinta's Host Committee, and MALI has recurrently taken part in the matching funds programme. Peruvian galleries, collectors and artists participate in this art fair, with the Peruvian artist Fernando Bryce Pinta's guest artist in 2008 and the curator Miguel López in charge of Pinta Forum (the art fair panels) together with Gabriela Rangel.

In 2012, Diego Costa Peuser and Alejandro Zaia's partnership was dissolved. Zaia moved to Madrid. They divided the ownership of the fair, Costa Peuser retaining the management of Pinta in the United States, and Zaia, the art fair in the United Kingdom. Pinta London had underwent various adjustments until 2015, after which Zaia and other partners launched Crossroads Art Fair, which focuses on questions of globalisation and cross-cultural interaction. With Diego Costa Peuser, Pinta New York has also sought renovation through the inclusion of more curatorial activity in the overall mediation of the art fair. In an interview, Costa Peuser told me:

Pinta New York has a new location in SoHo a very nice place, a two-story building. It is a fully curated fair. There are sections, and each has a curator who has a lot of prestige and experience and knows what they want to do. […] Curators invite the galleries. Curator-gallery work together. And what is asked of curators is first a selection of galleries, their help with the installation of the show to make it neat, and a lot of networking. What is really good at Pinta is that it is a fair with a big curatorial presence and many museum directors. Curators from JP Morgan and the biggest banks' collections go. Pinta's potential is as a platform, and from there it enables other events.

(interview, August 2013)

In 2014 Pinta moved from New York to the growing art node of Miami. Its strategy was to operate during the week of the imposing Art Basel Miami. Many Latin American galleries do not fulfil Art Basel's requirements, and Pinta and the parallel art fairs offer them other options to be and exhibit in Miami. In New York, London and Miami, Pinta has contributed to the mobility of Latin American galleries, artists and other stakeholders and the articulation of institutional and individual art agents from and with an interest in Latin America. Expanding their nodes and strengthening their art circuits, Pinta's organiser Diego Costa Peuser <span id="page-187-7"></span>and the Argentinean entrepreneur Gaston Deleau decided to launch an art fair in Lima in 2013, although this strategy was not uncontested, as I explain in the next chapter.

Final note: I was working on this book in NYU's Bobst Library when I received an email with the heading 'VIP Invitation, Exclusive Pre-Opening Pinta Miami 2015'. Despite my previous attendance at Pinta, this was the first time that I had been invited. What is interesting, apart from the *enchantment* of the invitation itself, is to identify the ways in which the process of my anthropological research became to act and be part of this art circuit, and to understand the current strategies of the art fairs. On the plane back to Lima, I was flipping through the magazine *American Way* and found an article about Art Basel Miami that pointed the creation of the Art Basel's new position of Director Americas in Noah Horowitz, author of *Art of the Deal: Contemporary Art in a Global Financial Market* and director of the Armory Show since 2011: 'He'll direct the Miami fair and act as a liaison between the organization and North and South America' (Michaud 2015: 60). It also referred to the changing mechanism of VIP invitations, as for instance my invitation to Pinta, for which Daria Brit Green, vice president of the SCOPE art fair, explained, 'The notion of what a VIP is is shifting, and it is starting to include people who are not necessarily over 60 with museums in their names. It also includes influencers and bloggers' (*American Way* 2015: 66).

### **Notes**

- <span id="page-187-0"></span>[1 T](#page-172-0)he recent exhibition *Vanguard Networks: Amauta and Latin America, 1926–1930*, curated by Natalia Majluf and Beverly Adams (Museo Reina Sofia, 2019), highlights how Jose Carlos Mariategui made of this journal – Amauta – a continental project that created networks in the region, that understood the revolutionary role of indigenismo in the continent, and that created vibrant dialogue between social thinkers, artists, art critics and politicians. It is of importance to locate this project in parallel to the MoMA's approach to Latin America.
- <span id="page-187-1"></span>[2 F](#page-172-1)or an in-depth investigation about the pioneering role of the MoMA in the creation of the paradigm of 'Latin American Art' through acquisitions, exhibitions and curatorship, see Basilio et al. 2004.

- <span id="page-187-3"></span>[4 I](#page-176-1)n the introduction to *Exhibition Culture: The Poetics and Politics of Museum Display*, the editors Ivan Karp and Steven Lavine point out various concerns about issues of museum representation (1991: 11–24) which apply to debates about museum representation of Latin America art.
- <span id="page-187-4"></span>[5 I](#page-176-2)n October 2016, Patricia Phelps de Cisneros donated 100 works of her collection to the MoMA. In January 2018, she donated 200 works to different museums including the MoMA, MALI, and Reina Sofia, and the MoMA reopened its new rooms in 2019 including an exhibition of the Cisneros collection on Latin American abstract art. In February 2018, Ella Cisneros Fontanals donated part of her collection to the city of Madrid reinforcing Spain's renew attention to Latin America. See La Nación 2018.

- Colombia and the current panorama with the international work of curators such as Jose Roca, the role of blogs in the new criticism, and different challenges and limitations.

# <span id="page-189-1"></span><span id="page-189-0"></span>**[Trajectory](#page-7-12)**

# [Elena Damiani](#page-7-12)

The strengthening of the Museo de Arte de Lima, the transnational role of art collectors and curators and the emergence of art galleries that operate internationally in relationship with Peru's peaking booming economy in 2008, boosted a generation of artists who were at the beginning of their professional lives. The Lima art scene would gradually offer artists the possibility of achieving an international profile while staying in Lima. Those who decided to live abroad, such as Elena Damiani, also chose to maintain a connection with Lima's art collectors, galleries and institutions that has fostered their circulation locally, regionally and beyond. At the same time, with the expansion of art markets across the world, the artists who already have some gallery representation have started to, or aspire to, work strategically with galleries in different regions, strengthening their global visibility and value.

Elena Damiani, born in 1979, is building a successful international career. The inclusion of her work in the central exhibition at the 56th Venice Biennale, Okwui Enwezor's *All the World's Futures*, in 2015 was a milestone in her trajectory. Elena was in Mexico setting up her installation *Sediments: An Assemblage of Remains* at the Amparo Museum in Puebla when we had our long Skype interview on 23 February 2016 for this section. This installation was first shown at the Contemporary Art University Museum Museo (MUAC). In our talk about her trajectory, she highlighted the mechanisms and agents that help her to promote her work, her experience at Goldsmiths: and the opportunities that this opened up, her work with Revolver in Lima and other galleries, the importance of Lima in producing her work and the importance of having a good portfolio to promote it.

Elena started to study architecture at the Peruvian University of Applied Science (UPC), but after three years she realised that it was not for her. She liked <span id="page-190-0"></span>the practical work but was not interested in working on projects involving clients. She decided to switch to art at Católica University but did not get in. She decided then to study at Corriente Alterna. 'I think that while it was not my first choice it was the best thing that could have happened to me'. Corriente Alterna is a smaller institution where she had easy access to professors, and from the first day she was already planning her personal projects. Luis Lama was a mentor for her. She says, 'I know there are many people who see him as a great tutor, and there are many others who stay away from him. But I just think his personality and passion are so visceral that they made me get along with him, and he made me see that art was for me. He opened the doors to many things for me, firstly through the course he taught on the predicament of contemporary art. … Other professors were amazing, for example Leonel Velarde and Micky [Aguirre] as professors of Painting, and those of Projects such as Natalia [Iguiñiz], Max [Hernández] and Haroldo [Higa]'.

Luis Lama, Elena adds, 'gave me my first show at the Miro Quesada'. She graduated with honours from Corriente Alterna in 2005 and only months later was having a dual exhibition with the artist Iliana Scheggia at Miro Quesada Garland, which was again under Luis Lama's management.

She also started to work with the Vértice Gallery. It was Miguel Zegarra, the gallery curator, who had introduced Elena to the gallery owner, Rosario Orjeda. She had her first solo exhibition in 2007. Elena says, 'And that's another thing too, I've been very lucky, many people have given me opportunities'. She has never had any other job than being an artist. She recounts an anecdote about when she was at the artists' dinner at the Vienna Biennial and artists were talking about the most bizarre jobs they had done before they started making a living from their art; when it was Elena's turn she didn't have any stories to tell. She confesses to me, 'I even felt guilty'.

That year, Elena won the French Embassy in Peru's Passport for an Artist Award. But instead of accepting the award of a residency in Paris, she proposed 'visiting biennials and fairs. I'd never been to any and I thought this would be important research for me'. The organisers agreed. Elena went to Paris for two weeks, <span id="page-191-0"></span>and then to the Venice Biennale, and to Art Basel and Documenta, which were held at the same time. From this experience, she tells me, 'I returned with the idea that I wanted to leave'.

The possibility of studying abroad was always latent in her plans. England was in her mind, and more specifically London, where she had been twice while she was on a cultural exchange to learn English when was 17. She explains, 'London made a big impression on me, and I said "At some point I want to live in this city"'.

Elena felt that staying in Lima for two years after her studies ended was good for her because she gained experience: 'Being an artist involves both practical and studio work, to understand all the logistics of how to work with a curator, with a gallery'.
