During our conversation, I ask Elena if the artist-run space Drama had played any role in her career. I had already drafted [Chapter 2](#page-65-1) and I wanted to know Elena's perspective. She replies, 'Of course – for example those who were there were Alberto [Borea], Miguel [Andrade], Ana Cecilia [Farah], Mariana [Riveros]. There was Drama and Tupac in Barranco'. Elena recalls that curators visited Drama. She met Adriano Pedrosa and Cuauhtemoc Medina there. 'My first approaches to collectors were at Drama, with the organization of the open studios. I met Armando Andrade, Jorge Basadre, Manuel Velarde and Marco Testino there. All this came from Drama. Look, you're making me remember; I didn't remember this'. In talking about the foreign curators who were visiting Lima, she adds, 'I was very lucky that they came to me, because that doesn't always happen. Unfortunately when curators go to Lima I think it's a limited group of people which they recommended to visit, I mean they don't circulate everywhere, as they should. And so I was lucky to be in a place that gave me the opportunity to be visited by curators'.

Elena applied to Goldsmiths and was accepted for an MA in Visual Arts in 2008. It was a challenging experience. For the first two months she didn't like it: 'It takes two months to adapt for someone like me who had never lived abroad, who'd lived with their parents, and is living alone for the first time. That was a culture clash. A totally different culture. And you get to a university that highlights everything that is conceptual, and the critical side is without filters, unlike <span id="page-192-0"></span>at Corriente. I saw my shortcomings and I realised how limited my initial knowledge of the history of art was and my knowledge of philosophy and the theoretical part of art, of contemporary art critique, which is very useful, not only to know your practice but to give a context to your practice. I was not very aware of the international benchmarks that could serve or inform my practice. And that was something that began to happen at Goldsmiths, not only at the university, but also in London'.

 At first, she took the criticism personally, but then she understood that it was about her work. She explained that Goldsmiths has two different types of seminar: one where everybody talks about a student's work and the student does not speak, and the other where the student explains his or her own work: 'For each work you present there is this pattern: one in silence and the other where you can talk. … What Goldsmiths did to me was to destroy everything I had thought about myself, and I thought I'd accomplished something in my career. … They deconstruct you and in the last months of your MFA [Master of Fine Arts] you put yourself together again, and they clearly say that would happen, as the director did. I was lucky that Gerard Hemsworth was the Master of Fine Arts director and my professor when I was at Goldsmiths. I was in the last year he taught – he retired after that. He was also a key person for me. And then there was Suhail Malik, who is very clever. He takes your work to other levels and gives you some amazing suggestions. He can link your work with the most elaborate book, or to Britney Spears. His spectrum is broad, and that also makes you tease out your ways of thinking'.

At Goldsmiths, Elena was nervous about her studies and so she focused hard on them. For one more year she continued her relationship with Vértice Gallery, but did not participate in any exhibitions. At the same time the new gallery, Revolver, invited her to work with them, but she delayed accepting. 'Because this was 2008, when Revolver was created, but I still didn't want to leave Vértice as a matter of loyalty. It wasn't a question of risk, the risk that may be involved in leaving to go to a young gallery that's just starting out. It was primarily a matter of loyalty. It was the first gallery I'd worked with. They gave me a chance just after I graduated and I felt I owed them'. She told <span id="page-193-0"></span>me that during her studies at Goldsmiths she was always thinking, 'My next exhibition in Lima must be very powerful for all the effort that the MA is demanding of me'. That show happened in 2012 at Revolver Gallery, with which she has worked since 2009, and she called it *History Decomposes into Images, Not into Narratives*.

Describing Revolver Gallery, she highlights how 'it was a totally different format; a rather informal format, if I can say that. It's not so structured but nevertheless it has a mechanism that works. Revolver was able to be at art fairs, to put on solo and group exhibitions locally and abroad and with its own structure and tools, which are still very different from those of the other Lima galleries'. According to Elena, the gallery's dynamism is based on the teamwork between the gallery owners and the artists, who are mostly of the same age. The gallery owner, Giancarlo Scaglia, is also an artist: 'He understands exactly the kind of concerns you have as an artist … he understands you perfectly'. She works predominantly with Revolver, but she also works with other international galleries.

Her relationship with the other galleries began with her Degree Show: 'At Goldsmiths Gerard would repeat that you're working towards that night for two years, for Exhibition Graduation Night, because the media and the gallery owners come and pick who they want to work with. Of course everyone believed that and was very nervous; I didn't believe it. … I worked extremely hard, though. I almost ended up in hospital because working I filled my throat with rubber. I didn't have any high expectations that a gallery would start working with me, but three galleries contacted me. … One in France, one in London and one in Brussels. And I started working with London and Brussels'.

Since then she has worked with Selma Gallery from London and started working with Elaine Levi Projects but stopped as the gallery was focusing on a different type of work. She was happy with the new opportunities. In the interview, she exhales and says, 'This is where personal issues came in'. Her mother wanted her to return to Lima. But she stayed in London: 'It made little sense to just graduate and not stay and see the local artworld'.

Selma Feriani's gallery has a space in London and has just opened another in Tunisia. Elena showed a project <span id="page-194-0"></span>there in 2017. She explains that Selma Gallery has important Arab collectors. 'Now [Selma] participates at art fairs in Hong Kong and Dubai every year, and every year we sell everything. It's amazing. I want to go some time'. Selma took part in the first PINTA London, but for Elena the art fair was not in the best location.

Elena also works with Varo Gallery in Sao Paulo, with Francesca Minini in Milan, and with Nordenhake Gallery in Stockholm, which has a showroom in Berlin. When I ask her if the galleries have problems competing for the market in the same areas, she tells me that Selma works primarily in Asia and has just started with Minini and Nordenhake. Revolver and Varo Gallery, coincide at many art fairs, but as Revolver is her main gallery she gives it priority, and she also adds, 'Brazil is a universe apart, and different from the rest of South America; also it is very close. And I wanted to work with a gallery in Sao Paulo'.

Curators have been key in providing mobility for Elena's work. Among them she mentions Sofia Hernández Chong Cuy, who was the director of the Museo Tamayo (Mexico) and since 2011 has been the curator of the Colección Patrica Phelps de Cisneros at its New York office. Elena met Hernández Chong Cuy at the Mercosur Biennial in 2012, and says, 'I think every chance she has, she shows my work to someone. I frequently get emails: "Sofia Hernández showed me your work, and I would like to meet you." In that way many opportunities have come my way'.

Elena does not enjoy going to openings. She thinks of herself as a shy person. She panics when giving talks, but knows they are an important way of connecting to the public, so she does it. According to Elena, people get to know her work at exhibitions or because someone has told them about it, 'but networking is not my thing. I think most exhibitions in which I have participated happened for me because I got an email. They ask me for my portfolio, then there's a studio visit, or if I'm in another country it's a Skype call, and then a subsequent personal meeting, and if all goes well the exhibition takes place. Obviously not all of them turn into exhibitions. But this has more or less been the way I've participated in most of the exhibitions I have been in'.

In addition to Hernández Chong Cuy, Elena thinks her relationship with her contemporaries on the curatorial <span id="page-195-0"></span>studies course at Goldsmiths is quite important. Among the curators with whom she works are Matthew Polini and Luisa Texieros Freitas. She had just had a show with Polini in Verona at the time of the interview and has been in several group exhibitions with Luisa Texeiros since the latter focused her graduation project on Elena's work. She adds, 'Right now we have a show in Guadalajara'.

She got to the Venice Biennale in 2015 by spreading the word of her work among curators. Jesus Fuenmayor, who was the curator at Cisneros Fontanals Art Foundation (CIFO), talked about her work to the Biennale's organisers. 'The Venice Biennale was due to Jesus, who in fact I did not know was interested in my work. … The general producer of the Venice Biennale wrote to me. She's the right hand of Okwui Enwenzor. … She told me "Jesus Fuenmayor has given your name to us, and you're one of the people whose portfolio we would like to review. We are just at the initial stage of reviewing; please if you could send us your material"'. Elena was amazed by this email and thought it was a very remote possibility. She only asked if they needed a short or a long portfolio. They required a complete portfolio, including future projects.

There are also key people in Lima on whom Elena relies. Apart from the early support of Luis Lama and Miguel Zegarra, she mentioned the support of the curator and art promoter Jose Carlos Mariátegui, who is a good friend. 'He helps me not so much by moving my work but with making decisions, for example if I'm dubious about whether I should participate at an exhibition. … I appreciate Jose Carlos's unfiltered sincerity when he talks about my work'. In addition, the collector Armando Andrade gave her a big boost at the beginning of her art career. Elena insists, 'That's super important to me. People who've been very clear and have told me what they really thought'. She tells me how Andrade suggested that she stop painting and devote herself to her digital collage. 'One day he came, and I think he bought the whole studio – it was in Drama. And when I did my solo show at Vértice he bought two works and told me "Elena, I will organise a lunch at my house in Los Condores and I'll put your work there. I'm organizing this lunch so you have the opportunity to meet more collectors"'. When Elena arrived, she noticed that <span id="page-196-0"></span>two of her works were there. And she adds, 'If I'm shy and timid now, I was more so then. And he introduced me to so and so. He was key in introducing me to people'.

A collector who has a central role for her today is Carlos Marsano. 'First, he's a collector with a lot of mobility outside Peru. He moves from fair to fair. He has direct and very personal contact with artists, gallery owners and curators. He's not like other collectors, who can be distant. He has incredible curiosity and is very passionate about collecting'. When Elena participated in the Solo Projects with Revolver at ArtBo in 2013, Marsano told her, 'Here I'll introduce everyone'. 'And I said, "Thanks Carlos!" [laughs]. And he did'. Two stands away was the Nordenhake Gallery. The gallery owner approached Elena to tell her he liked the stand. They were not yet working together. Elena told Marsano about this, and he said he would introduce them; she continues, 'And Carlos has emailed them for two years saying: "You have to follow Damiani, look at her work, it's advancing": that's how he is, and not just with that one gallery'. Elena does not have a strong relationship with the Museo de Arte de Lima as an institution, although she does with some of the people involved with it.

In our conversation she constantly mentions the importance of the portfolio as a means of enhancing mobility. She tells me that she had learned to make a solid portfolio in everyday practice by first dropping information that 'was just making noise' and observing what caught people's attention. She adds, 'It's also a matter of asking; it depends on the type of curator and the type of exhibition. Some people want five images, and there are people who absolutely want to review everything'. She has developed a portfolio arranged in chronological order and another with chapters divided by her interests: 'it seems an easy way for someone who doesn't know my work to approach it'. Her portfolios also contain her texts. Elena usually writes before starting a project, 'and that almost always ends up in the portfolio and on my webpage'.

I ask her about the process of her work, which leads into the second part of our conversation. She explains, 'I write to give myself certain parameters. Usually it's the first thing I do. I sit at the computer and start writing, and then I begin to investigate. I begin <span id="page-197-0"></span>to gather links to things I want to read and images I want to see, and from that a project takes shape. Also, I have things like loose annotations. I think if they last a long time on my computer or my phone without being deleted it's because they deserve to be used'. She has a folder called Ideas for Future Projects which is divided into subfolders: 'Right now I have seven folders that are like projects'. This system helps her because she does not always have time to research. She has a lot of commitments and planned exhibitions. Today she declines some invitations, but that was not the case during the first two years after she graduated from Goldsmiths, when she accepted all invitations with the aim of getting known. 'I think I've become more selective; I think I say no to two-thirds and yes to one-third'.

With some nostalgia, she refers to how things have changed for her: 'That's something I miss, for example; I miss being in my studio and working, not knowing if the work will be exhibited, just doing it, and not for something specific, not doing it for an exhibition with a particular deadline, or a fair. Or the excitement of opening your mail and saying, 'Wow, I have an email or an invitation to such and such.' Obviously there are emails that I get excited about and say 'Good!' but some things eventually become a little more common and lose their initial power to surprise, to elicit emotion. You have to become more selective'.

I ask her if she feels that her work could lose something, and she answers: 'Yes. One has to be very careful. First, there is the danger of producing a lot and burning out, because you cannot have 100 ideas a year. … If you have a very large production you'll end up doing ideas that don't necessarily need to occur. Just as you become selective with exhibitions, you have to be selective with the projects and works that you do because of the danger of repeating yourself and the commercial danger of overselling'.

Elena can work on her collages anywhere. She thinks there are more major sources of material in London and Copenhagen with their antique shops than in Lima. However, she primarily works on her sculptures, to which she has devoted the last four years, in Lima. Now that she has started working with Minini, she will start producing in Milan with a local marble workshop. <span id="page-198-0"></span>She thinks that in terms of logistics and time it is useful to have a production site in Europe.

She produces her sculptures in Lima because it costs less than doing it abroad, but also because she knows the people she works with: 'I know my technicians because I've had a four-year relationship with them, because establishing a working relationship takes quite some time. And a team is not easy. I have my assistant in Lima'. In the marble workshop her work passes through many hands, but two technicians work continuously with her. She works with Jean Pierre Castro on the design of metal structures. She works with the shop Bati to frame her work or to fix little things. She adds, 'I like the familiarity. I like that it's always the same people. … I like to have my team and establish a relationship, because to establish that communication takes years'. As I listen to her talking about the importance of relationships and the different people involved in producing her work, Howard Becker's (1982) study resonates in my mind.

Elena is constantly travelling to Lima to oversee the making process. 'First, definitely I'm at the beginning of the production. I'm on Skype during the process and go a couple of times during the making process and when the work is being finished and for packing up'. For instance, the project that she is presenting in Mexico at the time of our conversation took a year and a half from the initial idea to its materialisation. 'And', she adds: 'it continues. … Today we were looking at how to display the work in the new space, because it was a site-specific installation, and because it's travelled to another museum, it's no longer site-specific. That reframes some pieces that were made to dialogue with the architecture of a building such as the MUAC, which is quite neutral, while a building like the Amparo is colonial. So they're completely opposite architectural languages, and the works that I created for a space, which had a lot to do with the architecture and the place where the MUAC is located are going to have a completely different reading and a completely different life at the Amparo, no matter how you hang them – this is beyond its assemblage'.

Elena's work focuses on landscape. That is her thread. 'Without being fully conscious of it, it's been my main interest'. For her, that interest may derive from when she studied architecture or from family camping trips: <span id="page-199-0"></span>'that always caught my attention. I am not a person who lives with nature all day, but I like investigating of the landscape'. She has researched the landscape from different perspectives, for instance as a collective memory when she does historical reviews in collage using found material that is mostly related to the landscape. She has noticed that her own sources of research were concentrating more on certain issues: 'I found that the books I was buying were getting closer and closer to specific subjects: archaeology, geology, geography. Then this was leading my work to expand in the direction of certain issues and research. I always ended up with books about ruins, landscaping or mineral composition. 'Well', I said, 'there's a common denominator here' … and I started to find some logic in my practice, and when you find that logic you already know how to channel yourself in that direction'.

When I ask her why she had decided to live in Copenhagen, her answer is, 'Because I married a Danish man'. She married in 2013 and her husband was pursuing an MA there. She adds, 'It's not that I thought "This is the city that I have to go to." It's not. But I tell you, I'm doing well'. She wants to become a mother soon.

In Elena's view, the art scene in Lima has grown considerably and there is less fear of doing things. However, she points out that she feels there is a gap in terms of the generations and their representation in galleries: 'Artists who have come after us – or maybe I'm not informed – have not had much exhibition space. They haven't been welcomed by the galleries so far. We started working with galleries when we graduated. And what about the young artists? There have been the initiatives of young artists' alternative art fairs such as APUFF [A Cool Art Fair]. That's very important. But there's no gallery doing this job, which I think would allow young artists to make a living from their art. One can decide not to be a commercial artist and not to engage only with galleries, but the gallery is an important tool for keeping your production active. Selling work enables you to produce work. I think you can do it without a gallery, but it's more difficult'.

She concludes our conversation by highlighting: 'Many people tell you "oh this is talent." But no, first one must be a hard worker […] The opportunities that come

#### <span id="page-200-0"></span>170 *Trajectory*

in life also have a certain luck factor, and I think I have a lot. Hopefully it will stay that way'.

In 2016, after living abroad for eight years, Elena and her husband decided to go back to Lima. Her priorities have changed: she has a three-year-old daughter. Today she produces her artwork mainly in Lima for her international art commitments and invitations, and she no longer produces just for art fairs.

# <span id="page-201-1"></span>**6 The celebration of culture**

<span id="page-201-0"></span>[Creative Lima and the growth of the art](#page-7-13)  market

Latin America's best kept secret and unexpected new art world star is Lima. The city has been drawing visitors to its doorstep with its hot new fairs Art Lima and PArC. The art scene in the Peruvian capital is split between manicured Miraflores and bohemian Barranco and in this city gallery openings are cocktail parties not mere beer fuelled nod fests.

<span id="page-201-2"></span>(Neave 2015[\)1](#page-219-0)

In a celebratory narrative that pairs a vibrant art market with a creative city this succinct quote provides a good way of opening this chapter, in which I explore the growth of the art market in Lima in interrelation with the new urban lifestyle and celebration of culture. I start by discussing the launch of two government policies that located culture at the forefront: Marca Perú, at the national and international levels, and the New Lima and Lima for Everyone municipal policies at the local level, and argue that despite their apparent differences, both reiterated culture across templates of the creative industries. I connect here with my argument in [Chapter 2](#page-65-1) about the gradual move from interculturality to creativity and the consequent loss of the early political strength. Then I turn my attention to how the rise of 'creative Lima' promoted the growing consumption of art and look at the art market. I trace the strategies and responses of art galleries to the local art circuit, to the new place of interior designers and to the requirement of participation in art fairs at the local and transnational levels. Lastly, I analyse the launch of two art fairs, Art Lima and PArC (Peru Arte Contemporáneo), and the confrontations among their organisers, showing how their struggles activated alliances in Latin American art networks and amongst local agents. I argue that Art Lima is closer to the project of the 'creative city' project, and PArC to that of the 'global art scene'; both, however, reinforce the neoliberalising logics impacting the art scene and the city.

### **'How difficult it must be not to be Peruvian': Between the new Peruvian brand and the 'Lima for everyone' urban policy**

Amid camera flashes and glamour, two years after its closure for refurbishment, the Museo de Arte de Lima reopened its temporary exhibition rooms in <span id="page-202-0"></span>April 2010 with a solo show of the world-famous photographer Mario Testino's *Portraits*. This exhibition, previously held in London, Milan, Amsterdam, Edinburgh, Tokyo, Mexico City and Boston, featured Testino's most famous celebrity portraits, including images of Lady Diana, Kate Moss and Julia Roberts, among others. Kate Moss herself was Testino's main guest at the launch. In his speech, after talking about Peru's creativity, the photographer remarked: 'How difficult it must be not to be Peruvian' (*Qué difícil debe ser no ser peruano*). At the reception waiter-models wore T-shirts with this phrase in an orchestrated performance.

Despite some negative criticism of the exhibition, *Portraits'* immense international media coverage attracted worldwide attention to the MALI and reaffirmed its institutional place on the Lima art scene. The two-month exhibition received 50,800 visitors, high attendance for MALI's exhibitions. *Portraits* was also the consolidation of Mario Testino in the new Lima. Since 2004 the local media, and especially the right-wing press, had begun to mention Testino's international achievements and his re-engagement with Peru. In 2007, as part of the new attention to Lima, discussed in [Chapter 2](#page-65-1), the photographer published his edited volume *Lima, Peru,* in which he proposes to highlight: 'Lima's positive chaos. … I wanted to find a book which conveyed this; the way Lima's artists and photographers have captured the colour and complexity of the city, its art, its people and its buildings, its street life and its interiors. But such a book didn't exist, so I decided to edit one myself' (Testino 2007).

The new art scene lacked an iconic brand figure or marketing narrative that worked with the Peruvian creativity discourse. In the already booming field of gastronomy the key figure was chef Gaston Acurio, who promoted Peruvian cuisine with an emphasis on the combination of native ingredients and traditional recipes, creating new food concepts. The annual international gastronomy fair, Mistura, started in 2008, and in 2014 it attracted 400,000 visitors. The gastronomy boom was followed by the fashion industry with a focus on local products, especially alpaca wool, in combination with contemporary design. In 2012 the entrepreneur Efrain Salas founded LIFWEEK (Lima Fashion Week), which attracted around 8,000 people. With his significant international reputation and his aim of promoting Peruvian art and creativity, Mario Testino stood as an agent able to combine fashion and art and became the top figure and branded reference for the arts, at least during the years that were crucial to the consolidation of a creative narrative.

In 2012 Testino founded his cultural centre, MATE, in a refurbished early-20thcentury house in the gentrified bohemian district of Barranco. Testino's stated aim was to protect Peru's heritage by renovating the building, promote Peruvian art abroad, bring international projects to Lima, and boost tourism in the capital. Taking gastronomy as his model, he said 'Due to our gastronomy we know very well that [Lima] is a place that people can visit and have an amazing weekend' (*Gestión,* 10 July 2012). Among the celebrities and guests from the cultural and political spheres, was Nadine Heredia, the president Humala's wife, who said at the inaugural celebration: 'I invite the citizens to visit this place. This centre <span id="page-203-1"></span>not only brings art and culture to the people, it also relocates Peru in the eyes of the world' (*Peru 21*, 10 July 2012). A year later, this cultural centre became a museum.

'How difficult it must be not to be Peruvian' would not have had any resonance 12 years earlier, with a large part of society struggling to recover democracy and Lima's blindness to the civil war and its consequences. This phrase could only stand in the post-dictatorship, post–civil war context, and through the repackaging of Peru – mainly Lima – with the 'creative' discourse. In Lima, the creative project began to be implemented and to spread in 2004, with the important participation of publicists and publicity campaigns such as for the Peruvian soda Inka Cola (now part of the Coca Cola corporation) emphasising Peruvian creativity: 'Our creativity conquers the world' (see [Chapter 2](#page-65-1)).

These publicity cultural projects and narratives of Peru conquering the world were consolidated and magnified with the Peru's new national branding, Marca Perú, which was presented in Lima on 10 March 2011. A day later the brand was displayed on Wall Street in New York. It was rapidly appropriated by Peruvians both in Peru and abroad, and promoted by diverse businesses and individuals (see Cánepa and Lossio 2019). The promotional video features a group of famous Peruvians travelling to the small US town of Peru in Nebraska to teach the inhabitants how to enjoy life as 'real Peruvians' through cuisine, music, surfing, handicrafts, etc (Marca Peru 2010). In contrast to the previous national branding, which was based on Andean archaeological heritage with the slogan *Perú: País de los Incas* (Peru: Country of the Incas), the new brand was based on Peru's cultural industries with the slogan *Whatever you need is now in Peru*, and on creativity, entrepreneurship and cultural ambassadors. Under its new brand the Peruvian government sporadically began to support exhibitions of contemporary art such as Fernando Bryce's touring 2011–2012 *Drawing Modern History* exhibition and Milagros de la Torre's simultaneous exhibitions in New York and Lima, *Observed*  and *Evidence* (2013). Yet Marca Perú primarily fosters a specific type of visuality: a multicoloured Peru and Lima supposedly derived from its multicultural and creative ethos. Jaime Travezán and Morgana Vargas Llosa's photographic project *Mirame Lima* (See me, Lima) became Marca Perú's paragon, showing Lima's diverse population, their dreams and ways of living through a set of colourful theatrical pictures in which, following Zukin (1995), conflict, inequality and poverty are aestheticised and made invisible in a coherent composition[.2](#page-220-0) Like Marca Peru, *See Me, Lima* also reached New York City. I was studying in New York when I saw in a Peruvian newspaper and on social networks that it was to be projected in Times Square from 10 to 16 June at 9 pm. I went to see it. Despite the projection not lasting more than a minute – a fact that nobody mentioned – it had immense impact on the Peruvian media, and the circulation of the images fed the celebratory dream of Lima and Peru 'in the world'.

<span id="page-203-0"></span>In parallel to Marca Perú, from 2010 to 2014 another cultural and branding project emerged in Lima, this time was promoted by the municipality of Lima with the slogans 'la Nueva Lima' (the new Lima) and 'Lima, Ciudad de Todos' (Lima, the city for everyone). The election of the socialist Susana Villarán de la <span id="page-204-1"></span>Puente as Lima's mayor from 2010 to 2014 saw the start of a strong municipal cultural policy. Villarán's predecessor, Luis Castañeda Lossio, who had ruled the city for two terms from 2003 to 2010, had focused on infrastructure; indeed, he had cancelled the Lima Art Biennial and other cultural events. In contrast, Villarán's strategy was to work on the non-material and symbolic aspects of the city, however since the effects of her agenda were not easy to measure this almost led to her removal from the mayorship. Villarán's municipal agenda resembled that of Mayor Alberto Andrade, in the sense of placing culture as a key aspect of the municipality's work. However, while Andrade's policy was based on *criollo*  (white descendant) culture, as discussed in [Chapter 1](#page-31-1), Villarán's urban policy focused on the wider Lima population, replacing the municipal agenda with its focus on colonial heritage with the slogan *Lima, the City for Everyone* and an urban policy on contemporary Lima and community culture. Moreover, the campaign logo showed a graphic of the wider Lima, in which different coloured, previously peripheral areas – already renamed Lima North, Lima East, and Lima South – joined together in Lima Centre. Reinforcing the commitment to culture, in 2013 the Office of Culture (*Gerencia de Cultura*) was created as part of the municipal structure. The first person who held the position of director was Pedro Pablo Alayza, previously director of the Museo de Arte de Lima and assistant director at the Cultural Office of the Instituto Cultural Peruano Norteamericano (ICPNA)[.3](#page-220-1) As Pedro Pablo Alayza explained to me, for him '"Lima, the City for Everyone" is not an empty, sterile slogan, it's a real theme; actual management. … Culture is understood as a service and a political need to articulate social, urban and grassroots groups as part of a programme, so that their integration generates citizenship: that's the spirit' (interview, December 2014). When I asked him his view of municipal policy on contemporary art he said:

<span id="page-204-0"></span>The theme of the contemporary has had a privileged place, in the broadest sense of the term. We worked on the theme of living community culture [*cultura viva comunitaria*] with groups from different Lima neighbourhoods in all the peripheral areas of the city. We've taken films, graffiti, and mime artists … and the guys have participated in photography workshops, drawing workshops and mural-making. We have produced several events for graffiti artists in Metropolitan Lima, and even two abroad [the Latidoamericano event]; a lot of urban art has been produced. Then the Pancho Fierro gallery, which has been completely refurbished, is a gallery of the first world ... We have launched the Lima Photography Biennial: two editions of the best in the country with hyper-contemporary shows. The whole issue of contemporary dance, FAEL [Lima's Performance Art Festival]. Far beyond pictorial or avant-garde work, this is a reflection of the arts in general.

For me the contemporary is the street and the people living today. People living on the street, live the contemporary. Exhibiting Elliot Tupac is supercontemporary. For example producing an Andean music festival in Lima is super-contemporary. William Luna's narration of Andean music, that's <span id="page-205-2"></span>contemporary. Now whether this is or isn't included in a fair in London or New York, that's another thing. That I don't care about. Producing a mural with some guys in Barrios Altos, that's contemporary.

(interview, December 2014)

Therefore, the Museo de Arte de Lima's and the Municipality of Lima's promotion of contemporary art involved different actors and different values: the former focused on art tendencies resonating in the international art world, and the latter worked through the dissemination of culture and the promotion of grassroots groups' cultural work, migrant aesthetics and urban art. The Cultura Viva Comunitaria (Community Living Culture) programme recognised and fostered cultural work from diverse local organisations. Nevertheless, instead of presenting its work in new terms as Brazil's Puntos de Cultura (Culture Dots) policy that aimed to move away from how the dominant culture has represented diversity by bringing in alternative representation from below (Turino 2011), the Municipality of Lima promoted culture with the colourful and celebratory creative template. The emphasis on Lima's diversity and the work in the public space with citizens from various social sectors pushed the process of social recognition and of indigenous and *cholo* empowerment (see [Chapter 2\)](#page-65-1), to its materialisation in the municipal cultural management, and moreover in the visibility of Lima Centro, traditionally portrayed as a colonial site. The new urban cultural policy became clear with the mural-painting programme and Latidoamericano festival: policies and events that created a privileged platform for artists, some of whom had first participated in the Lima art scene in the first half of 2000s at the moment of the development of new urban subjectivities and curatorial narratives[.4](#page-220-2) From 2010 these artists received support and recognition from the municipality giving more visibility for their work and fuelling their individual agency as cultural promoters. This applied, for instance, to graffiti artists such as Entes y Pésimo, Elliot Tupac, and the photographic collective Lima Foto Libre, among others. Elliot Tupac transited from his small-/middle-sized lettering and drawings to murals with his emblematic self-defining work at Lima Centro: '*Cholo soy*' (I am *cholo*).[5](#page-220-3) The Municipality of Lima fostered the expansion of contemporary art by including artists not yet fully connected to the art institutions and the market, who found a site of participation at the cityscape. I argue that while the urban art murals policy has enabled the expression of diverse artists and the redesign of the city by including more voices and aesthetics, at the same time these policies have channelled the political experimentation of the mid-2000s, encapsulating it via formulas of 'diversity' and 'the political'. The capacity to disrupt and reimagine the city has been subdued under a controlled strategy for cosmetic diversity and close related to gentrification strategies in which art also plays a key role today.

<span id="page-205-1"></span><span id="page-205-0"></span>Lima's municipal cultural work included the launch of the Photography Lima Biennial. As part of her campaign to become mayor, Susana Villarán had promised to bring back the Lima art biennial cancelled by Luis Castañeda Lossio in 2003. However, rather than a broad art biennial, which would have implied not only a larger budget but also dealing with different art agents already engaged in

<span id="page-206-3"></span><span id="page-206-1"></span><span id="page-206-0"></span>strong disputes, as I will explain, the municipality opted to organise a photography Lima biennial with the private school Centro de la Imagen, which also coorganises the photography art fair Lima Photo.[6](#page-220-4) A photography biennial was less problematic for the municipality than an arts biennial and it was a field with which Pedro Pablo Alayza was familiar from his previous work[.7](#page-220-5) The first biennial had as its principal curator Gustavo Buntinx, and the second, Jorge Villacorta and Carlo Trivelli. Both editions included work from migrant Lima collectives and photographers that had not yet circulated on the dominant Lima art circuit, such as the LimaFotoLibre collective and the work of Nicolas Torres.[8](#page-220-6) 

<span id="page-206-2"></span>Villarán's focus on culture was lauded: Lima was awarded Plaza Mayor de la Cultura (Main Plaza of Culture) status in 2014 by the Union of Ibero-American Capital Cities. Eighteen years earlier the mayor Alberto Andrade had gained recognition for Lima as Ibero-American Capital of Culture – the platform that motivated the creation of the Ibero-American Lima Biennial – from the same institution. This award increased the media exposure of Villarán's cultural work in her last year as mayor, and the sense of pride, already boosted by Marca Perú, among Limeño inhabitants. It was only a matter of time before Lima would also have its own brand, as announced by the municipality. It is worth noting that the agency selected to undertake this project was that of the publicist and art collector Armando Andrade, which had managed the successful MALI branding. However, this project did not come to fruition: recognition as Plaza Mayor de la Cultura was not enough to appease the criticism of her government. Luis Castañeda Lossio, was elected Mayor of Lima once more in 2014, rejecting Villarán's cultural strategy and starting by erasing the murals in Lima Centro. However, this time his cancellation and erasure policy led to a vast rejection by the media, cultural agents and others. Lima was already framed on the narratives of creativity.

# **New art consumption in the creative city: Interior designers and the Desenfranquiciados project**

Peru's economic growth over the last 20 years reached a peak in 2008 and made Peru one of the fastest-growing economies in Latin America and the Caribbean (Paira 2009; International Monetary Fund 2012). Peru and other Latin American countries' rising economies became more noticeable during the global financial crisis of 2007–2008. The number of people on a high income increased (*El Comercio* 2015a; *Gestion* 2018). The new lifestyle of medium- to high-income Limeños was evident in the busy new sophisticated restaurants, fashion boutiques and design shops, expensive brand shops at malls, and in the acquisition of contemporary art and luxury apartments; and 'Peruvian creativity' suited the new upper-middle-class tastes and urban styles perfectly.

In [Chapter 4,](#page-123-1) I explained how the Museo de Arte de Lima boosted the art market with the 2007 launch of its Contemporary Art Acquisition Committee (CAAC), which fostered the emergence of new young collectors, and of its Summer Auction at beach clubs that promoted the consumption of art by high- and upper-middle -income residents (Borea 2016). Newspapers and the socialite magazine *Cosas*  <span id="page-207-0"></span>covered the rise of new young art collectors and MALI's auctions, highlighting how collecting was not just an issue of money: 'buying artworks of Peruvian artists is an intelligent form of investment' (*Somos*, 5 June 2010). As Dayana Zdebsky de Cordova (2018) explains in her research on the Brazilian market, the 'growing art market' rhetoric also created the market itself. As a result interest in buying contemporary art spread from art collectors to upper-middle urban income people seeking to distinguish themselves in the 'creative' Lima. Owners of new luxury apartments no longer decorated them with mass-produced products but began to invest in luxurious finishes and distinguished objects. As the curator Jorge Villacorta explained, 'We Limeños have become more hedonistic; we find pleasure and satisfaction in what we consume. We are more *picky*, so to say' (*Somos* 2011). This growing demand for exclusive consumption promoted the opening of small shops operating as a cross between art galleries and design shops, which in 2010 included for instance La Galería Del Barrio (2009), Reencarnación (2009), Centro Colich (2010), the pioneer Deadalo (1992), and exclusive larger design stores like Tendencias: Architecture and Design (2006). In my interview with the cultural and political analyst Mirko Lauer he did not hesitate to point out that 'the apartments are the factor that are moving art in Peru: there are walls that need to be filled' (interview, May 2011). To hire interior designers became more common among those who could afford it. Moreover, interior designers increasingly gained power on Lima's art scene, challenging the role and knowledge of art agents such as gallerists. In a conversation with a collector, jokingly he referred to interior designers – usually called *decoradoras* in Spanish – as '*de-curadoras*' (as 'de-curators'), a play on words about their supposed new activity of curation of homes and offices, while the association of interior design as a female occupation was also indicative. The tension with gallerists was due to interior designers' preference for working directly with artists, bypassing the galleries. They gave new buyers the confidence to choose from artworks that they pre-selected according to their client's taste. For some apartment owners their art acquisition can be one or two works for decoration purpose only, while others may develop an interest in buying art and building symbolic capital (Bourdieu 1984).

The Desenfranquiciados (Unfranchised) project emerged from the niche market supplying people's growing consumption of selected objects and in a direct challenge to the ways in which art galleries operated. In 2004 the art promoter Maria Elena Alvarado grouped together different artists and exhibited their work over a short period of time. The artists did not have to pay a commission to the organiser if their work sold. The experience was very successful. Alvarado then went to New York for four years, and when she returned to Peru in 2008 she joined with the artist Sheila Alvarado and photographer Nicolas Figari to create Desenfranquiciados. For each of their events they sent out an open invitation asking for proposals and works with a price of up to \$100. The selected artists had to pay a fee and were expected to help with various tasks during the event. Desenfranquiciados' slogan was 'aesthetic independence and low prices without mediators'. The project had a huge impact from the start as it articulated the interests and desires of young upper-middle-class consumers. Desenfranquiciados also <span id="page-208-3"></span>provided a market for artists who were not represented by a gallery. Although Desenfranquiciados was not in direct competition with art galleries or interior designers, tension between the galleries and this project permeated the atmosphere, particularly when it was invited to the first edition of the photography art fair, Lima Photo[.9](#page-220-7) Desenfranquiciados ceased in 2011.

# <span id="page-208-0"></span>**Art galleries in redefinition: Local work, regional scope and participation in art fairs**

In June 2011 I returned to Lima with a grant to study the art market. To my surprise, when I visited the art galleries I found exhibitions of not only the work of Peruvian artists, as had been the case when I left in 2008, but also that of international artists. The 80m2 Gallery, which had just come under new ownership, was exhibiting the work of the Honduran artist Adan Vallecillo, and in its new alternative space was featuring *Miradas sin coordenadas* (Views without coordinates) curated by Jorge Villacorta and showing work by artists from Mexico, Brazil, Cuba, Guatemala, Honduras, Chile, Colombia, Argentina and Peru. When I asked for the gallery owner at Vértice, her assistant told me she was in Chile coordinating the next show with the artist Ismael Frigerio. Two weeks later, Enlace Gallery launched *El Anverso de las Nubes* (Clouds' obverse) curated by José Falconi with the participation of the Peruvian artist Nani Cardenas and the American artist Kyle Huffman. Of course it was not the first time galleries had presented exhibitions of international artists: what was new was that many galleries were looking at and acting in a regional arena, showing foreign artists as well as locating Peruvian art abroad. This new multi-scale work was clear on the galleries' websites, which started to appear in both Spanish and English.

<span id="page-208-1"></span>As I explained in [Chapter 1](#page-31-1), throughout the 1990s the Lima art scene comprised various diverse exhibition/selling spaces, from art galleries to restaurants with spaces for exhibition. Banks ran cultural programmes in their exhibition rooms and numerous awards invigorated the small, local but dynamic Lima art scene.[10](#page-220-8) However, by the turn of the 2000s, with political conflict over the restoration of democracy and especially the economic downturn many cultural-market venues and awards were withdrawn. In addition to political and economic factors, advanced specialisation in services and spaces began to appear, and on the art scene this led to the professionalisation and renovation of art spaces (see Chapter 3). By early 2000, nine art galleries were operating in Lima,[11](#page-220-9) with Forum Gallery, created in 1974, the oldest and an a milestone on Lima's art scene in a country in which institutions do not last long. Forum played a pivotal role in the 1980s and 1990s, yet I argue that it was the Lucia de la Puente Gallery with its new space in Barranco that brought about the turning point for the Lima art gallery scene for contemporary art.

<span id="page-208-2"></span>Established in 1995, the Lucia de la Puente Gallery achieved an enormous influence on the art scene in 2002 when it moved to its new 700 m2 space in a restored *casona* in Saenz Peña Street in Barranco. As a spacious, high-ceilinged, adaptable and open-plan exhibition space, the gallery was ideal for showing and <span id="page-209-2"></span>selling contemporary art. Its space permitted to present works in different formats and dimensions, including installations. The gallery's infrastructure reflected Lucia de la Puente's aspiration to make it the leading art space, capturing the interest of artists, other art agents and the media. In addition to the main exhibition room the gallery included a smaller space for special projects, a shop with affordable art and design objects, and a café, making it attractive to clients. Lucia de la Puente explained that the gallery 'was conceived as a small museum' in a city that in 2002 did not yet include contemporary art on its radar. She added, 'It was different from now. We were fewer galleries; there was no museum of contemporary art and galleries somehow filled that role. The MALI was not as organised as it is now' (interview, March 2014). The attention and dynamism that the Lucia de la Puente Gallery started to articulate, impacted Barranco, which gradually became not only the Lima's cultural district but also its art market district, and Saenz Peña Street became into an art hub. The two-block-street of Saenz Peña and its surroundings include art galleries, design shops and artists' studios, and at one corner stands the luxurious Hotel B, a boutique hotel with a large collection of contemporary art, owned, with other partners, by the De La Puente Wiese sisters: Caridad, Lucia (the art dealer) and Susana, the influential businesswoman and art collector who, after living in New York as Chair of JP Morgan for the Andean, Central American and Caribbean Region, returned to Lima in 2013 to occupy a leadership position on the Wiese Group Board (*Gestión*, 2 December 2013)[.12](#page-220-10)

<span id="page-209-1"></span><span id="page-209-0"></span>During the second half of the 2000s, a period that saw the reinforcement of the Museo de Arte de Lima and its fostering of a younger generation of art collectors, new galleries opened their doors: the Enlace Contemporary Art Gallery (2006); 80m2: arte & debate (2006); Vértice (2007), Star (2007) and Revolver (2008). Since its opening, these galleries work together with curators for thinking their exhibitions, and two were run by male art dealers in what was, and remains, a women-driven art gallery scene[.13](#page-220-11) The galleries also, momentarily, expanded perspectives on art in the market arena. Located in San Isidro district and directed by Rosario Orjeda with the active participation of the curator Miguel Zegarra, Vértice Gallery opened with new challenging views about art. It launched with *Emergencia* (Emergence) exhibition of the artist Harry Chávez. This exhibition derived from Chávez's experiences with ayahuasca and his relationship with the Amazon Shipibo people. In the work after which the exhibition was named, Chávez had reproduced the Shipibo artist Herlinda Agustín's *kene*, a Shipibo symbolic design, which she had embroidered on a skirt and explained to him in detail. Chávez's exhibition materialised a relationship with indigenous artists that developed later in co-authored works. Vértice was a barometer for various emergent intercultural projects. Unfortunately the early death of the curator Miguel Zegarra affected the initial perspective and strength of the gallery. In a similar way, 80m2: Arte & Debate started as a project of the young artist Antoinette Arévalo, who worked closely with curator Emilio Tarazona. The name of the gallery announced its aim and form. It literally began as 80 square metres conceived as a market space and an arena for debate. Artists presented their work followed by discussion sessions. The gallery's target was emerging artists with a commitment to art and <span id="page-210-0"></span>politics, and it gave a chance to artists not only from Lima but also from other Peruvian regions and of various cultural backgrounds. In 2010 the gallery closed temporarily and with the intention of reopening as a cultural space. However, this did not happen, and it reopened with a new owner, Livia Benavides, now called the 80m2 Livia Benavides Gallery. Benavides moved the gallery to a bigger location in Barranco's waterfront and has intensified its local and international agenda, maintaining the focus on politics but dropping its decentralised, intercultural perspective.

The creation of Revolver Gallery in 2008 introduced, I argue, a new directionality and way of working to the Lima gallery scene. While the Lucia de la Puente Gallery's new 700m2 space which responded to artists' explorations using different media created a turning point towards contemporary art in 2002, Revolver introduced a new focus on international articulations and markets. It included flexible organisation and mobility, new standards for Lima's art galleries, and the supporting of artists whose work, according to the gallerists, prioritises the concept and 'creates a dialogue with art of the first world' (Scaglia in Blouin Artinfo 2013: 20). It distanced itself from traditional galleries' operations as shop spaces to concentrate primarily on participating in art fairs and on a system of international artist residencies in Lima to create interconnection. In 2010, Renzo Gianella, one of the gallery's owners, told me:

Our gallery hardly works as a shop. We have no place to store work and almost never sell our exhibitions. What we sell is almost always at art fairs, or works from previous exhibitions: buyers spread the word to others. … From the beginning we had the idea of going out as well as bringing in. To make exchanges that flows in both directions.

(Interview, August 2010)

Revolver was launched by two young art agents, the artist Giancarlo Scaglia and art manager Renzo Gianella, who left the project in 2011. They planned to come together promoting artists differently when both being with a group of Peruvian art collectors at Buenos Aires' art fair, ArteBa, saw how Peruvian artists were represented. Their target was artists of their own generation. As an artist himself, Scaglia was familiar with the needs and aspirations of many young artists, and Gianella, as personal assistant to the collector and MALI board chairman Juan Carlos Verme, knew about strategies and connections for the circulation of art. A few months after its launch, Revolver was participating at ArteBA, and since then it has been at the main Latin American art fairs as well as other world art fairs including various editions of Art Basel Miami. I argue that Revolver operated as the MALI's market partner, and that both have had a decisive role in boosting the Lima contemporary art scene transnationally.

Since its launch, to ensure the vigorous promotion and expansion of its artists' markets on an international scale Revolver has represented a small number of artists via the notion of representation and splitting profits 50/50. Both of these mechanisms, already employed abroad by art galleries focusing on art fairs, were <span id="page-211-1"></span>unusual in Lima in 2008, where galleries operated mainly locally with a large group of artists, and sporadic international work, splitting their profits 35/65 per cent in favour of the artist. Five years later other Lima galleries were working via the notion of representation and splitting profits 50/50; but change also caused friction.

To enable consistent representation, galleries have reduced the number of artists that they work with. In doing so, art dealers evaluate artists not only in the light of how their work performs in the local or international arena but also with reference to, what they call, the 'loyalty of the artist' and the 'informal market'. For art dealers loyalty means a long-term relationship with the gallery, and an artist must not sell work to art collectors or interior designers from their studio but only through the gallery; if they do otherwise they support, according to the gallerists, the informal market. However, to sell work exclusively by the gallery is problematic if galleries do not have a good record of sales throughout the year and do not provide artists with a contract and a regular living, which is the other side of the informal market. Although art dealers acknowledge this problem, they include neither this nor tax evasion in their definition of the informal market. Some of these issues were raised in an incident between the established Peruvian artist Ramiro Llona and the art dealer Lucia de la Puente in 2013 when, days before Llona's show was due to open at Lucia de la Puente Gallery, in parallel to the launch of the two new Peruvian art fairs, he was informed that the gallery's fee was going up to 50 per cent. Ramiro Llona turned this conversation between private gallery owner and artist into a public controversy by opening up a debate about the contractual relationship between artists and art galleries in Lima. Opinions were aired about whether galleries have the right to retain so high a percentage of the cost of artists' work, and whether Lima's galleries were working internationally, but little was said about the two coexisting markets[:14](#page-220-12) the more local art market, in which established Peruvian artists could obtain high prices locally but were not repeated in international markets, and the regional-global market in which young and more conceptual artists were increasingly participating, with more similar prices across scales, and for which they were implementing multiple individual strategies for amplifying their transnational circulation and value. Ramiro Llona was one of the artists in the first group: the Lucia de la Puente Gallery promoted his art mainly in Peru and the rise in its fee was not related to the gallery promoting his art any more than before. However the same gallery was taking other artists, mainly younger and conceptual artists whose art resonated with international expectations of Latin American contemporary art to international art fairs.[15](#page-220-13) Lucia de la Puente told me in an interview with me on March 2014 that 'art fairs are a different phenomenon' and she was considering her strategy:

<span id="page-211-0"></span>The budget for the fairs is high, and we all know that sometimes you make a great investment and don't make a profit from it until later. It is also true that, apart from a certain few, most artists do not have a price level that is high. Then, going to the big art fairs is very expensive. We make the effort anyway. I also think that there is a good synergy in Latin America now: for example, <span id="page-212-0"></span>ArtBo is fine; ArteBa I'm going back to after many years, and we'll see how it goes. There are the Brazilian art fairs that are pretty good. … The region is rising; I think that's interesting, it wasn't like that a few years ago. It's relatively new. … There's an economic factor that you always have to evaluate. And the United States will always be important because it's the biggest market we have, it is a market that we are interested in, definitely, and in ARCO too. I went to ARCO like for 7 years and then came the crisis. But I know that the last edition especially was great, and with its FOCUS Latin America. And I'm thinking about whether I should go back. That's the other market that is related to us.

(interview, March 2014)

Galleries expand their international profiles and networks in two main ways: by participating in art fairs and by collaborating with foreign galleries. In collaborations, foreign artists are presented at the local gallery and the artists represented by the latter are shown at the partner gallery abroad. In this way both galleries and artists increase their international profiles and the artists can be included in new markets. Artists also implement other strategies for transnational circulation such as applying for an art residency, working directly with galleries in other countries, and placing themselves on the radar of transnational art collectors and curators in the hope of participating in projects, exhibitions and biennials. With aspirations to participate at top art fairs such as Art Basel Miami, Art Basel or Frieze, the Lima art gallery scene's current possibilities and work are predominantly on a regional scale, with a few exceptions. The increase in the number of art fairs in Latin America [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1), pp.153–154) has enabled a stronger regional multi-market platform that intensify the circulation and visibility of artists, galleries, collectors, curators and other art agents, and the articulation of the region. This regional platform makes it possible for art galleries to build their international profiles and for some to apply to participate at leading world art fairs.

In 2011 aspirations towards and anxieties about internationalisation prevailed on the Lima gallery scene. It was the moment that I had returned to Lima. Although these aspirations and anxieties persist, they are not as they were a few years ago: there has been a diversification of strategies on the art gallery circuit. Vértice decided to act locally with sporadic participation in regional fairs and in close relation to art designers. Impakto has opted for more abstract art in tune with the demands of the art market and interior design. Revolver and 80m2 have an agenda of internationalisation even beyond the regional scope. Enlace primarily works with foreign artists at the local level. La Galería and Forum intend to maintain a more local and traditional way of operating, with some participation at the regional level. Wu Gallery promotes projects of lab exploration and site-specificity, working mainly with Peruvian artists and in collaboration with international galleries. The Lucia de la Puente Gallery had a split strategy at first, promoting established local artists in the local market and emerging and conceptual artists internationally; and later, in 2018, this key gallery closed down, and Lucia de la Puente's focus became the exclusive boutique Hotel B.

<span id="page-213-2"></span><span id="page-213-0"></span>The launch of the two Lima art fairs in 2013 fuelled, at least initially, the already growing art market and promoted a rise in the number of art galleries[.16](#page-220-14) Yet the art gallery circuit in Lima is still limited, both in the number of galleries and in the diversity of what they promote: the *petite circle* of high class gallery owners promote upper-middle class, mainly Limeño, artists and international artists, with little inclination to include artists with other backgrounds or to look at Peru at large, and lack interest in expanding their potential market to emerging sectors. As artist Valeria Ghezzi reminds us it is a *Pequeño Mundo* (Small World).

# **Forging Lima's art fairs: Alliances, tension and emotions in the market**

I was at PINTA, the Latin American Art Show, in New York in 2012 when some Peruvian art stakeholders asked me if I knew about the two art fairs in Lima. These art fairs were part of their conversations. A week before PINTA, the Peruvian newspaper *El Comercio* (28 October 2012) had announced 'PArC: Peru Contemporary Art, the international art fair that will arrive in Barranco in April', with a picture of the Argentinean organisers Diego Costa Peuser (also PINTA's director) and Gaston Deleau, and the director of the recently opened Museum of Contemporary Art-Lima, Alvaro Roca Rey, which had hosted the art fair's first five editions. During PINTA, *El Comercio* (18 November 2012) made another announcement: 'The best show-case for our art. To celebrate, in April 2013 we will have not one but two contemporary art fairs. Some weeks ago, we presented Peru Contemporary Art; now we announce Art Lima'. I remembered that the Peruvian stakeholders had called Art Lima 'the other'. Who then were the people who created Art Lima challenging the already consolidated international art promoters who were activating art projects in Lima, Latin America and the United States in conjuncture with local art agents? Before answering this question I will explain the group that manage PArC.

<span id="page-213-1"></span>As discussed in [Chapter 5,](#page-171-1) Diego Costa Peuser is the co-founder of the PINTA Latin American Art Show and director of the journal *Arte al Día*. He and the Argentinean art promoter Gaston Deleau were already supporting important art projects in Lima such as Art Week, launched in 2005. This event replicated Buenos Aires Art Week (2003), directed by Costa Peuser and sponsored by Argentina's Secretary of Culture, in which with an immense media campaign people were encouraged to attend galleries, museums and other cultural venues throughout a week. In Lima, whose art galleries and museums are predominantly located in four districts – Miraflores, San Isidro, Barranco and Lima Centro – each district was assigned a day for gallery and museum visits with a suggested walking circuit and a programme of talks. Lima Art Week continued until 2012, when it was taken over by PArC. In addition, in 2010 Costa Peuser and Deleau replicated a their Buenos Aires Photo fair in Lima at the Centro de la Imagen, his local partner, under the name Lima Photo.[17](#page-220-15) It was Lima's first art fair and its editions have been successful in terms of both sales and visitor numbers.

<span id="page-214-1"></span>On the other hand, those behind Art Lima were mainly Peruvians who approached the arts through their experience in design, the arts and the fashion industry. Art Lima started with the impulse of the businesswoman and manager Rochi del Castillo. Born in the coastal city of Trujillo, del Castillo lives in Lima and owns the large store Tendencies: Architecture and Design, grouping ten design companies, and created in 2006. At Tendencias she began to work closely with interior designers and artists who found a niche in art and design shops. Tendencias also advices apartment owners on interior decoration to suit their wallets. Del Castillo's relation to the arts was therefore through interior design and the new urban lifestyle, which Peru's economic growth was making possible for a growing sector in Lima. To promote her business and expand her market, she started a free fanzine *De Boca en Boca* (from mouth to mouth)*,* providing tips and information on the upmarket lifestyle, and occasionally travelled to art fairs to get a sense of the art world. According to her, she perceived that Peruvian art stakeholders were scantly grouped together, that Peru's only art events were promoted by foreign agents, and that Peruvian artists needed a better platform on which to show their work (interview, August 2014). So she convened a group of people to work on an art fair project: the entrepreneur, founder and organiser of Lima Fashion Week Efrain Salas, with expertise in managing major events in Lima for a Peruvian audience; the artist, curator and promoter Christian Bendayán, a leading figure in the promotion of Amazonian contemporary art, to contribute to the artistic direction[,18](#page-220-16) and the Lima-based Argentinean lawyer Erika Lang to help with legal and customs issues. For two years prior to Art Lima's launch, they travelled together to various art fairs in Latin America and to Art Basel Miami, talking to the organisers and learning from their experiences.

<span id="page-214-0"></span>While there were confrontations between the MALI and the MAC-Lima about the management of contemporary art in Lima at the museum level in 2007, as discussed in [Chapter 4](#page-123-1), this time the confrontations were at the art market level in an already booming art scene. Disputes and alliances were about issues of belonging: 'belonging' to the art world and 'belonging' to Peru and Lima. The Argentinean PArC directors' credibility was based on their ten-year experience with Lima Art Week and Lima Photo and their organisation of international art fairs such as PINTA New York and Buenos Aires Photo. With no previous experience of how art fairs work, Art Lima announced itself as an art fair organised by people who lived in Lima, and whose credibility was based more on their roles as promoters of culture and their individual careers. The strongest initial opposition to Art Lima was not from galleries abroad but from galleries in Lima. Gallery owners preferred to support those who already had experience of managing art fairs and with whom they had art connections. Some art stakeholders tried to persuade del Castillo to create a design fair, arguing that the market was not big enough for two art fairs. But del Castillo's plan was not to maintain the existing market but to open it up in new directions. She told me: 'Either you have an event such as Lima Photo, or you institutionalise art as a cultural industry for the whole of Peru' (interview, August 2014), an aim that was not a priority for the others. While PArC was support by most of the people behind the MALI and MAC-Lima and <span id="page-215-1"></span>the gallery owners, Art Lima worked in conjunction with the powerful right-wing newspaper *El Comercio* using publicity that resembled campaigns about Peruvian creativity[.19](#page-221-0)

<span id="page-215-0"></span>Although Art Lima did not gain the support of Lima's gallery owners in 2013, the media announced other qualities of this fair. Art Lima's core supporters were individual artists such as the Peruvian Belgium-based artist Jota Castro, who reestablished his relationship with Peru after a long period in Europe. *El Comercio*  focused on artists' support as one of Art Lima's strengths. However, the art critic for *Caretas* magazine, Luis Lama, highlighted how almost all of the gallery owners were backing PArC. In summary, in the first edition of both art fairs only three Peruvian galleries participated in Art Lima, while thirteen took a place in PArC. The second editions were held in different months, Art Lima in March and PArC in April, and eight local galleries participated in Art Lima. According to my interviews, galleries' presence at Art Lima increased in the second year as a result of its success in the first, because the art fairs were held at different times; and because the organisers made it possible for art dealers to participate in different sections of the fair. In following years the fairs were held at the same time, with local art galleries participating not only in PArC but also in Art Lima.

With the lack of a fairground in Lima, the organisers of both art fairs needed to find sizable suitable spaces. PArC's first five editions were held at the Lima Museum of Contemporary Art, which after a decade of conflict had finally opened in January 2013. Three months after its launch the museum was housing PArC in an explicit intersection of the museum with the market, similar to that of MALI and its auction (see [Chapter 4](#page-123-1)). All of the Art Lima's editions have been held in a military school. Some artists openly protested against this choice of venue. The condemnation of soldiers for human rights violations during the internal armed conflict re-emerged. During Art Lima the military base was transformed to resonate with the aesthetics of upper-class Limeño social events. This fair targeted not only collectors and people on the art scene but also ordinary people and the new high-income sector, many of whom had little experience of buying art. This spacious and open venue provides a comfortable atmosphere for people unrelated to the art world. At the first edition people queued outside to get in, something unusual in the Lima's art scene, and the visitors' interest in attending ranged from the many artworks that had been brought together and with easy access to see them, to a suited man who had returned for a second day so that his son could take a picture of him with the Armani model. In contrast, PArC is held in a mainly closed space with continuous booths in the style of the international art fair prototype, but on a smaller-scale and attracting people more related to the art scene.

Art agents' differences and alliances with the art fairs were also reflected in their programmes of talks. For the initial edition of PArC, the Peruvian curator Miguel Lopez organised the conference programme and the MALI's director Natalia Majluf brought a special panel together to discuss museums and international networks. The whole programme had an art-related audience. On the other hand, Art Lima's conference took a didactic approach. As part of the programme the Spanish curator Octavio Zaya held conversations with the Peruvian artists <span id="page-216-1"></span>Milagros de la Torre, Aldo Chaparro, Fernando Bryce and José Carlos Martinat, each time emphasising 'It is time that Lima's public got to know their artists with an international presence in a natural way, without feeling intimidated, that they can have access to them'. Octavio Zaya asked each of the artists 'What does it mean to you to come back to Peru, to come back as a renowned artist? What does what is going on in Lima mean for you?'

<span id="page-216-0"></span>Beyond the confrontations between the art fairs there was great enthusiasm for the holding of an art fair – two art fairs – in Lima: 'We are hosts', gallery owners pointed out, 'when previously we have always been guests'. Artists also celebrated the advantages of having the fairs in Lima. The strengthening of the local art market and its transnational expansion via art galleries and art fairs allowed young artists that seek to attain an international profile to remain in Lima. It also motivated some artists who were living and working abroad to make Lima a constant node for not only their personal – visiting family – but also their artistic circulation[.20](#page-221-1) To Octavio Zaya's question at Art Lima about what coming back to Peru meant to her, the New York–based Peruvian photographer Milagros de la Torre answered: 'Ever since I started twenty years ago I never thought that in my lifetime I would see an art fair like this in Lima, or even in Latin America. I've always believed in the idea of the 'forgotten', that nobody cares about what has been done' (Art Lima 2014). In parallel to her participation at Art Lima, de la Torre also had exhibitions running at the MALI and the Americas Society in New York, this time with the support of Marca Perú.

# **Wrapping, expanding and iterating: The work of the market**

As explained in Chapters 3, [4](#page-123-1) and [5,](#page-171-1) the mechanisms of the contemporary art scene include curatorial practices that mediate and promote artworks and narratives not only at museum exhibitions and biennials but also at art galleries and art fairs. At the same time, the market regime and art collector's agendas and aspirations permeate the organisation of museums and foster the work of curators. Here I want to bring attention to how art fairs have positioned themselves as spheres of cultural debate, not only through the exchange of art but also as arenas for discussion. My argument is that art fairs have become in catchment entities of other art agents and practices participating on the art scene. Like rituals that happen at a specific site and time and that condense information, practices and meaning, the whole dynamic of art fairs, during the days that they take place, become special scenarios for the exchange, consolidation and spread of information, practices and value, both on the art fair sites and through the multiple parallel events. In these 'market rituals' the types of participant range from ordinary visitors to VIP guests with different targeted events such as studio visits, parties, museum cocktails and exhibition previews. Moreover, art fairs today provide researchers, artists, curators and collectors arenas for the exchange of ideas through art projects themselves and formal and informal talks, and even for the criticism of the very art market processes. However, in showing the contradictions and critique of the market-driven art scene, these participants are at the same time fuelling the art <span id="page-217-0"></span>fairs and reinforcing the market, turning the latter into a 'sphere of debate' that fuels the art fair itself.

Some of the curators and researchers at PArC and Art Lima had an initial reluctance to participate in art fairs but agreed as, they say, there were few other platforms for debate. Referring to his participation in the organisation of the talks at PArC, Miguel Lopez explained that there were several contradictions in taking part of that project, for instance, as it was an art fair taking place in a museum. The New York–based researcher Gabriela Rangel commented critically that 'art fairs are opening like McDonald's, one here and another there', arguing that 'the global jet set is in Lima. … Now Peru is on the map and I am nostalgic: I want to go to a more marginal country' (PArC 2013). In discussion about museums the Mexican curator Cuauhtémoc Medina criticised the strong role of art collectors on museum boards and their museum policy, while other agents, such as the Peruvian art historian Natalia Majluf, recognised the need for them for the museum sustainability (PArC 2013). In other debates, the Mexican curator Pablo León de la Barra focused on the possibility of experimenting with art fairs by addressing their fast speed and short duration, qualities that demand types of exploration different to those for longer exhibitions, and emphasised that not everything is either marketbased or alternative: there are combinations. In discussion with the long-time Spanish gallery owner and founder of ARCO Madrid, Juana de Aizpuru, who defended the idea of art fairs as a just marketplace without the inclusion of cultural projects, León de la Barra replied that there are people who feel intimidated to go to museums and galleries, and that art fairs also play a role in places where there is a lack of such institutions: 'We need to see our own processes' and 'We are in Latin America, not in Spain, Juana' (Art Lima 2014). In another debate, Gustavo Buntinx pointed out that it was not about opposing the spectacle but moving to the margin: being outside art, outside its spaces of circulation, being in silence (Art Lima 2014). These crucial debates on the arts and the Latin American art scenes were discussed in the platforms that PArC and Art Lima opened up. Yet the question is, how many of these discussions influenced the processes debated? Are art fairs a sort of 'black holes' that trap such discussions making of them just art-political narratives? By letting the market sphere do what institutions and the state are – supposedly – not doing well, is that not a neoliberalism?

As Foucault (2008) explains, the normalisation of societies in the form of market value is an enterprise at all levels that shapes common sense. I argue that art fairs have become powerfully seductive arenas for other art agents and practices on the art scene; and moreover wrap the imagination in an art-market-driven logic in which discourses of discontent or alternatives practices announced at art fairs fuel the art market sphere as the prevalent site-time of cultural debate. Art fairs are a key arena for the dissemination of market logics, and I suggest they operate as neoliberal global 'prototypes' – using Jamie Peck, Nik Theodore and Neil Brenner's (2010) notion of 'prototype' – whose activation in many parts of the globe enables the circulation and spread, via path-dependent processes, of neoliberalising logics and Western art forms.
