These prototypes provide the framework for showing, dealing with, talking about, and valuing art, and also constitute the <span id="page-218-3"></span>engines and sites for the flow, the participation and the visibility of transnational art agents. However, the emergence of art fairs in different parts of the globe does not necessarily mean that they involve different formats, bodies and voices.

<span id="page-218-0"></span>In trying to become a node on the regional art market circuit and to attract art agents' transnational circulation, the art fairs dates are programmed in relation to the calendar of local festivals and especially to an international calendar of art events. PArC and Art Lima take place around the third week of April with the aim to be a date in the regional 'ritual' calendar of fairs and biennials, and a node on the map of transnational trajectories of art dealers, collectors, curators and artists. To achieve a position on the art map, art fairs promote the reiteration of key art stakeholders of the region and the globe. Art Lima and PArC compete to convene reputable art galleries, artists and collectors, as well as talented local and international curators and speakers, currently predominantly from Ibero-America[.21](#page-221-2) The platforms of visibility that the art fairs claim to be have their specific individuals and art practices. The art market makes visible, mobile and profitable the art of those who are allowed to be 'contemporary' for the market and for the 'global' museums and obscures the work of others (Borea 2017a) – and the Lima art market is strongly engaged with leading global museum discourses, such as those of the Tate Modern, the MoMA, Reina Sofia and the Guggenheim. The overwhelming visibility of ones comes with the invisibility of many others. For instance, before 2018, indigenous artists were not represented in Lima's galleries or participated in art fairs – with the exception of the Micromuseo booth at Art Lima -, and even now there participation is limited.[22](#page-221-3)

<span id="page-218-1"></span>To attend artistic events, be seen and registered by the media, participate on these art platforms, connect with others and move on again, shape an art subject; a subject that is created through its performance across multi-scale circuits and with media visibility (McKenzie 2001; Cánepa 2006). Art openings as well as private parties are stages for celebrating the artist-elite 'friendship'. Some of these events are reported in the social media and socialite magazines in the celebratory tendency of art today, as this is mainly where art is presented and discussed due to a limited space for art critique elsewhere[. 23](#page-221-4) The overwhelming visibility in the media of the Lima art fairs, gallery openings and its mobile individuals across different international stages expands the sense that this is the path to take, and creates anxieties in artists and other art agents to becoming part of this 'global art world'.

# <span id="page-218-2"></span>**Two overlapping projects: The global art world and the creative city**

As I have explained, the struggles between the two art fairs revolved around issues of belonging: belonging to the art world and belonging to Lima. I want to emphasise here that these two art fairs materialised two neoliberalising projects, which overlap but have their own logic: the 'global art world' and 'the creative city', with PArC working predominantly through the first and Art Lima through the second. Throughout this book I have shown how these projects unfolded and are connected, organising work and aspirations on the Lima art scene and in the

<span id="page-219-2"></span><span id="page-219-1"></span>city. What it is interesting in the case of Lima is that it is not just one fair changing or expanding its focus over time, as Daniela Stocco (n.d.) explains about the transformation of Rio de Janeiro's art fair, Art Rio, from a project that first aimed the democratisation of art to a more art-based elite fair. In Lima two different groups of agents launch two simultaneous art fairs, neither of which locates itself as a satellite of the other, and both claim the leading place.[24](#page-221-5) Art Lima is connected to the promotion of urban and national cultural industries and policies, and consequently to the politics of the creative city and tourism which invite Lima's citizens to participate and enjoy the arts. Like Susana Villarán's municipal cultural work, Art Lima added disturbance and dynamism to an art scene that was being shaped and articulated primarily by the Museo de Arte de Lima, Centro de la Imagen, some curators, art galleries and the Argentinean art promoters. Yet Art Lima's project has been implemented through protocols of showing, speaking about and managing – the neoliberal – creativity.

The creation of an art market in Lima has grown in parallel, and in mutual relationship to, the consolidation of the creative city ideology and the branding of the nation, where Peruvian gastronomy has become the example of successful Peruvian creative industry being included in the art stakeholder's narratives to provide confidence in Peruvian contemporary art and Lima art scene. Yet celebrations of Peru, and particularly of the spectacular Lima, efface recognition of major social problems and deep-rooted social inequalities. For instance, on 3 December 2013, the front cover of El Comercio announced, 'Results of the PISA (Program for International Student Assessment) Test: We are the last among the 65 countries evaluated. Education in Peru Falls to the Last Position,' and below this, announced "We are the Best Culinary Destination in the World." The news about gastronomy was marked as the News of the Day.

As geographer Jamie Peck (2013) suggests, the creative city paradigm fits hand in glove with the neoliberal program and in which the strengthening of urban eventism is an effective way of celebrating the market success. With their different strategies and engagement with the city policies and the larger art circuits, PArC and Art Lima comprise and fuel this urban eventism in the modernising Lima, while continually announce themselves as showcasing Peruvian contemporary art to the world, generating local and international articulations and locating the Lima art scene 'on the art map'. But as I have demonstrated in this book, these art fairs appeared late in the processes of configuration of the new Lima contemporary art scene. However, the way in which the market-driven projects announce their work resonates strongly, flattening the historical process and amplifying their influence. Today the celebration of the market shapes the city, art stakeholders' imagination and the various forms of art articulations.

Villar's curatorial narrative opted for a dichotomic confrontation of the official and the "popular" by tipping the balance towards the 'popular' and the street; while at the same time looking to validate his project on the institutional art circuits. ![](_page_222_Picture_0.jpeg)

# <span id="page-223-0"></span>**[Epilogue](#page-7-14)**

In writing this monograph I have felt the process of time upon my research and upon my body and live. An anthropological analysis of contemporary times always has that sense of past; yet beyond the rapid changes and various events, the larger logics, networks and projects that the work exposes may remain. This epilogue provides the reader with a glimpse of what has happened in the Lima art scene from 2015 to 2019.

'Madrid Will Be the World Showcase for Peru' (*El Comercio*, 21 January 2019); 'Contemporary Art Will Position Peru as a Tourist Destination'; 'Peruvian Art Leaves ARCO With Its Best Edition in Ten Years' (*Gestion*, 8 March 2018), were some of the numerous headlines celebrating Peru as the guest country at the 2019 Madrid Contemporary Art Fair, ARCO. This invitation to a major European art fair – one that fosters Latin American art in Europe and beyond – was seen by Peru's contemporary artists, collectors, curators, museums and art dealers as the perfect arena for showing the rest of the world their achievements in the creation of a compelling art and contemporary art scene. Peru's government also saw it as an opportunity to strengthen the positioning of Marca Perú through its support for contemporary art. Importantly, ARCO became the first big event at which private agents and the public sector worked together to promote contemporary art boosting Peru's global performance.

<span id="page-223-1"></span>ARCO Madrid selected Sharon Lerner as the Curator for the Peruvian Programme in ARCO, a female curator who sits as the MALI curator of contemporary art since 2012, rather than one of the Peruvian well-established independent male curators ( Chapter 3). Her role at MALI was acquiring increasing visibility as the museum director for the previous 17 years, Natalia Majluf [\(Chapter 4](#page-123-1)), was leaving the job.[1](#page-231-0) Lerner selected 23 Peruvian artists represented by galleries in Lima and internationally. Her selection showed the international market's growing interest in Peruvian artists and put in evidence the still few Peruvian galleries with an international work (Lerner 2019: 28–29). It also exposed two other factors: on the one hand, the strong centralisation of the Peruvian art scene in Lima, with all of the Peruvian galleries selected being based in the capital; and, on the other, the lack of indigenous artists in the market arena with the exception of Ginsberg Gallery that included Primitivo Evanán's work. Yet, paradoxically, indigenous art was one of the strongest images and narratives framing Peru's participation at ARCO.

ARCO's parallel program of events at different venues throughout Madrid was organised by the Spain-based cultural promoter Fietta Jarque. The MALI, and the people behind the museum, showcased their central institutional position ([Chapter 4\)](#page-123-1) with the organisation of three exhibitions covering different historical periods. None of the other Peruvian museums organised exhibitions – the Museo Pedro de Osma, directed by Pedro Pablo Alazya since 2015, featured a colonial masterpiece of the marriages of an Inca descent woman with a Spanish captain and that of her mestiza daughter at the Museo del Prado, while LiMAC organised a party. The MALI's exhibition included: *Nazca*, curated by Cecilia Pardo and Peter Fux, highlighted Peru's rich archaeological legacy; *Avantgarde Networks: Amauta and Latin America 1926–1930*, curated by Natalia Majluf and Beverly Adams at Museo Reina Sofia, resituated *Indigenismo* in the history of Latin American avant-garde and exposed the regional and international networks that José Carlos Mariátegui's *Amauta* journal permitted in a dialogue of art and politics – a dialogue that is also in the interests of Museo Reina Sofia, especially in its approach to Latin America ([Chapter 5](#page-171-1)); and the show *Amazonias* featured contemporary art at the Matadero art centre.

<span id="page-224-0"></span>Curated by Gredna Landolt and Sharon Lerner, *Amazonias* approached the Amazonia through the work of both non-indigenous and indigenous contemporary artists. Landolt is a key figure in the promotion of Amazonian art (see pp. 75) and is currently the curator at the Ministry of Foreign Affairs' cultural centre, which co-organised the exhibition. The MALI started a collection of Amazonian art in 2015, yet never held an exhibition of Amazonian art on its own premises during the long process of Amazonian art and artists insertion into and shaping of the Lima art scene (Chapter 3). However, the prominence of ARCO positioned this museum as the main institution dealing with Amazonian art, obscuring a long history of other agents and museums' commitments and achievements.[2](#page-231-1) In addition, the Matadero art centre included the participation of the Bora-Uitoto Brus Rubio as one of its artists in residence,[3](#page-231-2) and the Shipibo artists Olinda Silvano, Silvia Ricopa and Wilma Maynas with a mural – the latter promoted by Peru's Ministry of Culture. Despite the Shipibo artists not participating at the ARCO fair itself, they were recurrently at the centre of Peru's ARCO promotional imagery. Thus both the government and the MALI used indigeneity as common ground in their promotion of Peru at ARCO. Yet the Peruvian government had previously done very little in terms of cultural policy on contemporary art and the MALI had done very little to promote Amazonian art, as explained. But acting and performing in the spotlight on this global stage tended to erase and reframe processes, resituating art players and cultural policy at the international and local ground.

<span id="page-224-1"></span>ARCO became an ideal international arena for the government's publicity for Marca Perú throughout Madrid's public spaces. Buildings were covered with the Elliot Tupac's *Chola Power* lettering [\(Chapter 2](#page-65-1)), who also ran workshops at Casa Encendida, and with the amplified Javier Silva's photographic portrait of an Amazonian woman. The latter provoking criticism on issues of authorship and consent; but other questions also arise from this type of publicity: why did the government select only those particular images to promote contemporary Peruvian art internationally? Why did not also include the work of Fernando Bryce, Milagros de la Torre and others? Was Peru playing with the *tropos* of 'otherness', this time imposed not by Spain but by its own government in the 21st century? Importantly, ARCO was announced as the official opening activity of the bicentennial commemoration of Peru's independence.

This bicentennial opening activity saw indigenous people widely represented but rarely invited. Showing their already acquired knowledge and experience of the art scene and governmental proceedings, the indigenous artists Rember Yahuarcani, Santiago Yahuarcani, Elena Valera, Roldán Pinedo, Harry Pinedo, Lastenia Canayo and Nereyda López criticised Peru's organisers use of indigeneity at ARCO and demanded public funding to attend ARCO and the *Amazonias*  exhibition – an exhibition produced with public funds – at which their work was being shown: they did not want to be there only via curatorial representation; they wanted to be there having an active participation. The government replied that it had no funds for that. ARCO became an arena not only for demonstrating achievement but also of important debate. However, the art stakeholders promoting Peru in ARCO wanted to quickly turn the page. On the other hand, many Peruvian white-mestizo artists and art players attended. While many of them had participated in it previously, this was the first time the focus was on Peru, giving their work special visibility. The travel expenses of those art stakeholders selected to represent Peru at the ARCO venue were funded by the government; others were invited by art collectors, and still others, including myself, took themselves there. In Madrid, I heard, 'It's like being in Lima'; others more explicitly said, 'It is like being in Barranco', referring to the high attendance of Lima's art players – a white mestizo and high and upper-middle-class group –, who constantly ran into each other at the various art events.

In ARCO, and through the same process of organisation of Peru's participation, Peruvian art collectors reaffirmed their role in shaping Peru's art scene and their networks as global art players. Eduardo Hochschild, Jan Mulder, Juan Carlos Verme, Alberto Rebaza and Armando Andrade, featured some works of their collections in exhibitions at the hand of key curators such as Luis Pérez-Oramas and Estrella de Diego, Alejandro Castellote and Max Hernández. Peru's participation at ARCO was the clear achievement of private Peruvian art agents including art collectors, curators, artists, art dealers and private museums. It is also important to say that the invitation to Peru as guest country is part of ARCO's – and –Spain's at large – strategy to retake their standing in Latin America after the Spanish crisis, this through the launch of ARCO Focus which highlights one of the Latin American countries' contemporary art ([Chapter 5\)](#page-171-1). While the Peruvian government's new interest in contemporary art was welcomed by the private agents, the government's overwhelming visibility as a prominent actor in Peru's contemporary art scene also received criticism by some art collectors in media interviews – although this critique was also minimised by the strong celebration around this event. Jan Mulder emphatically announced: 'We arrived at ARCO by the art and desire of the private agents' (*El Comercio*, 04 March 2019). Juan Carlos Verme announced his discomfort at the Martín Vizcarra government's withdrawal of financial support for the expansion of the MALI's new contemporary art section; support that was initially offered by President Pedro Pablo Kuczynski as part of the bicentennial commemoration.[4](#page-231-3) Verme stated 'The dream is postponed' (*El Comercio*, 26 February 2019).

<span id="page-226-0"></span>MALI saw other dreams vanished. After receiving notice that its long-termed director was stepping down, the board started to look for a new candidate with a strong international profile and global connections. Chris Dercon, ex-director of Tate Modern, was almost appointed, but the French president offered him the directorship of the Union of National Museums, fronted by the Grand Palais at a bigger salary. After an international invitation for applications, the position was assumed by Bartomeu Marí, previously director of the Barcelona Contemporary Art Museum. The MAC-Lima also experienced change in its organisation when the businessman and art collector Eduardo Hochschild, a directors of the new Engineering and Technology University located in front of the MAC-Lima in the gentrified Barranco district, stepped into the position of chairman replacing George Gruenberg. With the new board, the Colombian artist and curator Nicolas Gómez Echevarri became the new museum director and the curator Giuliana Vidarte, who has a focus on Amazonian art and a strong relation to Art Lima, the museum curator. The Museo de Arte de San Marcos welcomed back the curator Augusto del Valle as its new director – Del Valle previously sat in this position between 2001 and 2004 (Chapter 3). Del Valle has set up a curatorial competition programme reflecting on current and more historical artistic practices with the museum's national accountability. Other middle-sized museum and with a national perspective has recently become an interesting actor in Peru's contemporary art scene during the last years: the MUCEN, the museum of the Banco Central de Reserva del Perú, whose with its new brand has attained visibility to the museum and to its contemporary art competition through which the museum is building a contemporary art collection. This turn has taken place with the museologist Ulla Holmquist (2015–2018) and the cultural manager María del Pilar Riofrío (2018 until now) as directors.

<span id="page-226-1"></span>The last years have also seen the closure of important art venues on Lima's contemporary art scene. The influential Lucia de la Puente Gallery closed in 2018 after almost 20 years; the owner opted to embark on boosting the exclusive boutique Hotel B, located next to the gallery and in which she is a partner.[5](#page-231-4) In my conversation with Lucia de la Puente in 2014 she was already analysing the new art market landscape to decide her next step. The demand to participate in art fairs and the emergence of art gallery enterprises with showrooms in different cities have challenged the big galleries rooted in one location [\(Chapter 6\)](#page-201-1). The gallery closed with a posthumous exhibition of the renowned Peruvian artist Fernando de Szyszlo, who died at the age of 92 before a planned exhibition could take place. The show included his last painting. The gallery art scene also suffered from the death of Claudia Polar, the Forum Gallery's founder and director for more than 40 years. Today her partners continue to run the oldest gallery on the Lima art scene.

On the other hand, the artist-run space Tupac, which played a crucial role in shaping the Lima contemporary art scene as a site for experimentation ([Chapter 2](#page-65-1)), closed in 2015 and now houses the Michelin-starred restaurant La Central. New independent art spaces such as Bisagra and AMIL opened. Bisagra is an artist/ curator-run space, led by Andrés Pereira, Eliana Otta, Florencia Portocarrero, Iosu Aramburu, Juan Diego Tobalina and Miguel López, that situates itself as a meeting point and organises talks, parties, exhibitions and other types of art initiative. The AMIL project was founded by the art collector Juan Carlos Verme and Joel Yoss, an associated director of the Hauser & Wirth gallery – a gallery operating in Zurich, London, New York, Hong Kong, Los Angeles, Somerset and Gstaad – who became AMIL's artistic director. AMIL opened in the abandoned San Isidro shopping mall owned by one of Verme's corporations. As Verme explained, 'This project seeks to give artists and the public an alternative space to the institutional discourse of museums or of commercial galleries' (*El Comercio*, 9 September 2015b), and to link the local and international art scenes. The space hosts exhibitions and artistic residencies, and its public programme is organised by the young curator with transnational experience, Florencia Portocarrero. In a similar direction, the lawyer, art collector and MALI's board member Alberto Rebaza and his wife the layer and art collector Ginette Lumbroso launched Lado B (Side B) artistic residence program, held in a site next to their San Isidro luxury apartment. Art collectors are therefore promoting other areas of the art scene, but: how much of an alternative space and out of the market a platform led by these art players can become? Today the art market, the museum and the current promotion of many alternative art spaces are promoted by the same art agents – collectors and also curators – who act in and drive the dominant art scene: through their agendas, networks and dreams. Much of the 'alternative' scene is not alternative today as it revolves around the same larger programs and dominant actors. These art spaces and initiatives are not like the artist-run space described in [Chapter 2,](#page-65-1) which was not yet a defined directionality in the art scene and the art market, and museums with transnational aims were not driving the art scene. The problem is that the art scene has been overtaken by iterations of the same art agents in different art spaces: from museums ([Chapter 4](#page-123-1) and [5\)](#page-171-1) to art fairs [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1) and [6](#page-201-1)), non-institutional art spaces and biennials (Epilogue), in both local and transnational spheres.

Since 2015 Peru has had its own national pavilion at the Venice Biennale; before it shared the Italo-Latin American Institute with other Latin American countries without their own space. The Peruvian Pavilion is an initiative of the Wiese Foundation Bank and *El Comercio* newspaper, with the support of the Ministry of Foreign Commerce and Tourism through its Peruvian brand campaign, and is very much led by the art collector, MALI's board associate, and president of its auction, Armando Andrade. The Patronato Cultural del Perú (Peru's Cultural Patronage), a private non-profit organisation created in 2010, is in charge of organising the exhibition in Italy and its reverberations in Peru. It is of interest to know that the Patronato's directors are Armando Andrade, Alberto Rebaza, Alfredo Barreda and José Orrego, being the first three top Peruvian art collectors, with Andrade and Rebaza actively participating in the MALI and Barreda in the MAC-Lima.

<span id="page-228-1"></span><span id="page-228-0"></span>Before the launch of the pavilion, the Peruvian artists selected to represent Peru at the Venice Biennale received very little fundin[g6](#page-231-5) ; yet, with the pavilion and its promoters, artists and curators now receive financial support for their projects and their travel expenses, and Peru's participation in Italy is widely covered by the media. However, until 2017 the selection of artists to represent Peru lacked transparency. Artists and curators called for less secrecy in the whole process after friction arose between the curator Rodrigo Quijano and the exhibition organisers over the presentation of Juan Javier Salazar's posthumous exhibition at the 57th Venice Biennale – the artist having died in 2016 at the age of 61[.7](#page-231-6) Juan Javier Salazar was a member of the Huayco collective, and a reference for younger Peruvian contemporary artists. He viewed the new arrangements of the contemporary circuit with smart irony and did not *adjust* to its protocols facing financial difficulties; however, in a paradox of opportunism after his death much of his work was included at the 2017 MALI summer auction and in the Venice Biennale, both events feeding into each other and engulfing his work in bigger prices. Quijano resigned as curator of the Peruvian pavilion and the situation drove the creation of the Association of Curators, promoted by José Carlos Mariátegui and Jorge Villacorta, with the aim to reinforce the institutionalisation of the curatorial practice.

In 2019 the Peruvian Pavilion at the Venice Biennale featured the work of Christian Bendayán with the curatorship of Gustavo Buntinx on a project entitled *Anthropophagus Indians*, which explored Peru's identity through the history of the Amazon and was based on the artist and the curator's long relationship. Thus, 2019 saw Peru's Amazonian art on the global stage through ARCO's parallel events and at the Venice Biennale.

<span id="page-228-2"></span>Major museums are already revising and enlarging their collections. Latin American art has entered the collections and exhibitions of top museums due to active multi-scale work of Latin American art collectors and curators [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1)) and of artists' themselves (Trajectories). In 2016 Patricia Phelps de Cisneros donated a hundred works from her collection to MoMA and in 2018 a further 200 to various museums including MoMA, the MALI and Reina Sofia. MoMA's new rooms opened with an exhibition of the Cisneros collection of Latin American abstract art in 2019. In addition, top museums and art platforms are now including indigenous art, particularly via the transnational presence of Australian and North American indigenous artists and curators who have stimulated readjustments in wider art historical narratives. Until 2014 the category of Latin American art in its circulation at a transnational scope did not include indigenous art (p. 148), but the last five years have seen a considerable change in a recalibration with changes at the mainstream circuit. The MALI's exhibition *Other Histories are Possible: Rethinking MALI's collection* (2019[\)8](#page-231-7) and, for instance, the Sau Paulo Museum of Art's *Indigenous Histories* exhibition planned for 2021 with its previous conferences on the topic, are joining in this 'global moment' of rethinking the collections and including indigenous art. In addition to the formula of the political [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1)), we are witnessing perhaps a new encapsulation, this time of indigeneity. The fast ways in which Latin American indigenous art is being accommodated to the new interest of certain art institutions, curators and entering 'onto the map' needs a more critical analysis.

A growing number of Amazonian artists are participating in today's art circuit, however, Andean art is still predominantly circulating as '*arte popular*'; although, the work addressing political issues, mainly with a focus on the internal armed conflict, have entered the dominant art circuit connecting with its interest in politics and memory [\(Chapter 2\)](#page-65-1). In the last years, the Association of Popular Artists from Sarhua (ADAPS)'s painted boards (*tablas de Sarhua*) have received particular attention, especially in the work of Primitivo Evanán and his daughter Venuca Evanán. In 2017 the US-based non-profit organisation CON/VIDA-Popular Arts of the Americas, donated the ADAPS's *Piraq Causa* (Who's to Blame?) series to the MALI. It comprises 31 painted boards expressing the violence inflicted on Andean indigenous people by terrorists and soldiers during the internal armed conflict. They also show their forced migration to Lima and their returning after the war ended. However, when this series reached Peru the police confiscated it for the alleged offence of 'apology of terrorism'; as anthropologist Olga González (2018) affirms, these actions 'suggests that Peru's legacy of violence against indigenous communities is alive and well.' Such violence against indigenous communities can also be traced in other current forms: while the government and privates are promoting Amazonian art, at the same time the government promote investment projects that affect the lands, waters and life of Amazonian – and Andean – indigenous people, such as the Amazonian Hydroway project (Borea and Yahuarcani, 2020).

<span id="page-229-0"></span>As mentioned, Peru's government has started to support contemporary art through its Ministries of Culture and of Foreign Affairs and Tourism, the latter being in charge of the Peruvian brand, into a relationship between culture and branding – previously this relation was forged between culture and education, a change that is indicative of the current policies. The Ministry of Culture's Visual Arts Office has started to become an art agent, opening competitions for exhibition and travel funds, and granting awards for best research. Competent cultural managers are being appointed and employed, including Santiago Alfaro with his broad perspective of art, Felix Lossio with his critical view of branding policies[,9](#page-231-8) and David Flores-Hora with his extensive experience of documenting the contemporary art scene. As part of Art Lima's interest in engaging with urban and national cultural policy ([Chapter 5](#page-171-1)), the Ministry of Culture was offered a booth at the art fair. In 2018, the proposal curated by Flores-Hora and Alfredo Villar included the work of Primitivo and Venuca Evanán (ADAPS members), who usually take part in *arte popular* fairs, rather than Art Lima or PARC. Venuca Evanán has reflected in her painted boards the new experience of participating at Art Lima with her compelling *Self-Portrait*, and today she addresses issues of sexuality in the Andes, gender inequality and reflexivity becoming a pioneer in this approach and uses of the boards.

<span id="page-230-1"></span><span id="page-230-0"></span>The relationship between the Ministry of Culture and Art Lima was consolidated with the signing of an agreement in 2019 in which the government collaborates with Art Lima and its new project, the Art Lima Gallery Weekend, which was launched a year before, creating circuits for the people to visit the different art platforms[.10](#page-231-9) To demonstrate the Gallery Weekend's impact, the organisers, using cultural management language, produce reports with quantitative information that analyse and show its economic and social benefit to the art sector and cultural industries. A similar type of report was produced to evaluate and highlight the impact of Peru's participation at ARCO, this time fostered by the influential art stakeholder José Carlos Mariátegui who at its presentation in the Ministry of Culture also launched the social media hashtag: #PeruPotenciaCultural (#PeruCulturalPower). Such reports not only analyse the economic, social and cultural impacts of these market events but they also produce and reinforce the art market itself (Zdebsky de Cordova 2018) and generate trust for further private and public investment in the art scene and its market. While the agreement between Art Lima and the government can be seen as a productive joint public–private effort, there is something more to say: so far the Peruvian government, with its new interest in contemporary art, is undertaking its promotion through the same pathways and platforms of the art market and of the market's extensions upon the different arenas of the art scene; thus, reinforcing the market-driven art scene.

The recent turmoil in South American countries such as in Chile and Ecuador at the end of 2019, uncovered the strong inequalities that prevail in the region despite years of booming economic growth and of its narratives; however these protests does not – or at least not yet – have taken place in Peru. The sociologist Omar Coronel (2019) sees three reasons for this: the majority of Peruvian people are in the informal economy and do not expect the government to solve their problems; president Vizcarra does not have a strong political party so he tends to negotiate or cede to strikes gaining public approval; and the dismantling of networks of corruption that have put powerful people in prison along with the closing of the parliament and the election of a new one have given Peruvians the impression that certain justice is being done. To these reasons I add that, Peru's participation at big events such as Peru's classification to the Football World Cup Russia 2018 after 36 years without classifying; the media celebration of Peru's participation at ARCO Madrid; the hosting of the 2019 Panamerican Games, and the coming bicentennial celebrations in 2021 provide Peruvians with a sense of pride and stability that overlook social inequalities.

Many artists who were leaving abroad and have contributed with the multiscalar making of the Lima contemporary art scene and the fostering of Peruvian art internationally have returned to live in Lima, a place where many of their family and friends lives – a strong reason that after all shapes trajectories and dreams. Today is no longer about artists in the diaspora. Lima's art scene has become an arena that allows artists to fuel their local and transnational mobility, to strengthen their networks and to raise the value of their work – yet if they are aligned with the right gatekeepers of the transnational connections. It has become a node on the regional art map and many of its artists, curators, collectors and art dealers now move in wider circuits. Today the arts actively participate in the making of Lima; – yet this increased participation requires responsibility and attention to what such positioning of the art scene is driving, debilitating or permitting to imagine.

- <span id="page-231-9"></span>[10 U](#page-230-0)ntil 2012, PARC's organisers led the Lima Art Week, cancelling it to launch PARC. Six years later, it is Art Lima that promotes a similar event in the city by bringing together different art platforms.

# <span id="page-232-1"></span><span id="page-232-0"></span>**[Conclusion](#page-7-15)**

This book has explored the configuration of the Lima contemporary art scene over the last 20 years. I have shown how this transformation intersects with neoliberal changes, the renovation of Lima and Peru after a civil war and dictatorship, and recalibrations of the art mainstream with simultaneous work by transnational Latin American actors. Based on ethnographic and historical information, I have shown when, how and by whom new conditions, assemblages, and directionalities have taken place. I have paid attention to the specific work and aspirations of multiple individual and institutional actors that play out in the Lima art scene. This book has not been about contestations in the cultural field. It has been about the dominant art scene, which has not been understood as a monolithic entity but instead shaped in the congeries of different groups, agendas, visibilities, authorities and narratives. This book has been about articulations and power through the spread of protocols, values and forms of participation at different levels.

I have traced the transformation of the art scene by looking at different modes and moments of multi-scale articulations and at the work of actors. I have intended to make visible processes, actor's strategies and dreams, at the time that I have focused on tangible situations in which they have taken place. By focusing on arenas, I have given the texture, the aesthetics and the materiality in which assemblages have been produced and projects engrained. I have looked to what is legitimised as contemporary art not only with a focus on struggles among the actors, but with a focus on what articulations do in defining contemporary art today. I have traced processes, networks and directionalities, not through an abstract scale, but a human-object-based scale: in the messiness of the social interrelations and emotions. I have reflected on my position and how I have acted across arenas. As the reader may have noticed, I have been witness to several of these transformations and I have participated by small and large degree depending on my position in the field: as a student, as a lecturer of art and anthropology, as a museum manager, as a curator, as a friend, and as an anthropology researcher. Some of these positions do not exclude the others. I have shown my memories, my work and agenda in the cultural field, my dreams and anxieties, my connections and social position, and my interventions in the field. However, to show dynamic processes and connections making use of words challenges the decisions on how to organise and write the text. As Gregory Bateson insists:

since words must necessarily be arranged in lines, I must present the culture, which like all other cultures is really an elaborate reticulum of interlocking cause and effect, not with a network of words but with words in linear series. The order in which such a description is arranged is necessarily arbitrary and artificial, and I shall therefore choose that arrangement which will bring my methods of approach into the sharpest relief.

(Bateson 1958 (1936): 3)

Through my chapters, trajectories and visual-curatorial section, and through the people and artworks I have brought in dialogue, I have approached the Lima art scene throughout a period of time. In this way the argument of this book is built through juxtapositions, detours and vanishing points. The form of this book itself, in terms of its organisation and how processes unfold – in terms of its structure and different rhythms – turns into a central aspect of my work. The reader has seen that there is a horizontal reading of the different periods I have identified – first period ([Chapters 1](#page-31-1)–3), second period [\(Chapters 4](#page-123-1) and [5\)](#page-171-1) and third period [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1) and [6](#page-201-1)). There is another type of reading in which I have located [Chapter 5](#page-171-1) as a vertical axis where I explicitly interconnect local, regional and larger processes on the art scene. The processes of transformation of Lima and the changes and readjustments of big projects are traced primarily in [Chapters 1,](#page-31-1) [2](#page-65-1) and [6.](#page-201-1) I have intersected the synchronic and more analytical voices of chapters with another type of report: long interviews with specific artists in which we talked about their trajectories. Here the reader can better grasp how processes of decision-making are not only based on calculated strategies; many decisions also follow the heart, love and longing. I have shown what artists do and dream by providing texture to their résumés. I hope that the reader has noticed how the voices of the trajectories and the visual-curatorial section interlace, add and discuss with my voice in the chapters. In combination, through these three types of approach I have intended to show the transformations on the Lima art scene and the mechanisms of articulation. In making this organisation of the text explicit, my aim is to show how my argumentation has been built and to give greater sense of what I want to present with this book.

In my approach to actors, arenas and articulations I have emphasised the need to locate actors in relation to issues of class, race, gender and generation: aspects that facilitate or hinder their actions. In [Chapters 1](#page-31-1) and [5,](#page-171-1) I have shown how the promotion of contemporary art in the current conditions has facilitated the mobilisation of artists who started to work professionally during the mid-1990s. In Chapters 3 and [6,](#page-201-1) I have demonstrated how curatorial authority has been based on a male voice, while the market arena has been the domain of women's activity. In [Chapter 1,](#page-31-1) I have suggested, that the enormous contributions of women artists expanding not only the topics of concern but more importantly the ways of making-thinking is still a research that needs to be done. In Chapters 3, [5](#page-171-1) and [6,](#page-201-1) I have demonstrated the important scale of mobilisation of Amazonian indigenous art at a national level, with little mobility on larger scales at least until recent years. In [Chapters 4](#page-123-1) and [6](#page-201-1) I have shown the struggles between different <span id="page-234-0"></span>generations of elites in the management of the contemporary, as well as struggles between the traditional Lima elite and a new elite group in the management of art. In [Chapter 5,](#page-171-1) I have shown how participation in transnational arenas requires to follow the Euro-American protocols, and how Latin American elites participate and spread them, insisting as well on not losing the perspective of their European descendant condition. Therefore, my book, without withdrawing from the existence and notion of a dominant art scene, has sought to unpack its construction, work and values.

Specifically, in this book I have argued that two processes with different directionalities triggered the transformation of the Lima art scene. One was led by artists' re-connection with the social and political situation, moving from a more introspective period of the arts to connect, apprehend and act in the local milieu. I have explained how this relocation of the arts was triggered by the larger awareness of Fujimori's dictatorial regime at the end of the 1990s and by artists' active participation in the political transition to democracy. However, I have argued that what this relocation brought was not exactly an 'ethnographic turn' in the arts as in Hal Foster's (1995) terms of dealing with alterity and culture, but had more in common with the terms of Carlos Ivan Degregori's 'diverse we' (2000). This is a time of social renewal, and also of utopias, in relation to the attempt by the arts and academia to move towards interculturality as a transcultural dialogue. I have suggested that through the recovery of democracy there was a mobilisation of agendas and emotions in terms of citizenship, memory and the transformation of urban subjectivities. I have shown how this social renovation intersected with a growing neoliberal economy and the material transformation of the city. I have demonstrated how the transition to democracy brought a moment of enthusiasm. In this scenario, I have shown how artist-run spaces become crucial arenas for exploration, creativity and art dynamism, at a time when Peruvian contemporary art was not yet on Lima's radar, or the media, nor on the radar of leading art institutions abroad. I have also shown how during this first moment of the transformation of the art scene and the city there is a renewal and growing cultural production about the city of Lima.

The transformation of the Lima art scene was also triggered by the Lima Biennial, as I explained. I have shown how the launch of the Biennial became a key mechanism in the making of the contemporary art scene and the modernising city working across Alberto Andrade' s urban neoliberal policies. As I have demonstrated the first editions of the biennial took place in a solipsist and socially disengaged art scene, despite that the topic for its first edition was based on cultural diversity. However, I have shown how the 2000 National Lima Biennial and the subsequent 2002 Ibero-American Lima Biennial became arenas that articulated on-going redefinitions of the art, although not yet of new actors – before it was cancelled. I have suggested that the Lima Biennial not only mediated aspirations to global engagement but also opened up arenas for exploring social articulations. I have demonstrated as well that during this time artists living abroad started to forge their own paths that later would enlarge and show directions and strategies to other art agents.

<span id="page-235-0"></span>The biennials fuelled the consolidation of the figure of the curator in Lima in the early 2000s, and curators became the principal mediators of what art is. I have shown the ways in which curators expanded the notions of what contemporary art is in a moment of the reconfiguration of the city and its subjectivities. I have also demonstrated how the role and modes of operation of independent curators linked with the need to mobilise art and culture for the renewal of Lima. Specifically, I have shown how the renovation of art spaces played out with the rise of the figure of the curator and the growing role of culture in imagining the city. Apart from the more encompassing curatorial narratives about different aesthetics, I have indicated the role played by indigenous as political and cultural actors in reassessing the limits of culture, art and the contemporary. However, as I have explained, the initial indigenous participation in managing the representation of their culture at the level of exhibition-making lost its dynamism and was overtaken by the work of middle-class, non-indigenous curators.

The second period of the recasting of the art scene, which I have explained, is of institutionalisation with the reinforcement of the notion and the figure of the museum. I have demonstrated how the institution of the museum moved from a site of silence to an arena of debate in which confrontations regarding the museum and the public space, as well as the management of the contemporary, were at stake. I have shown how five museum projects consolidated at this moment. I have explained how debate was mobilised through the powerful platform of blogs, which later became less important with the growth of Facebook. I have also argued that much of the debate that occurred at this time concerned both large and micro museum projects and left aside the work of intermediate-sized and public museums – more precisely, the Museo de San Marcos; I have suggested that this invisibility responds to aspects of class and race.

I have shown how the Museo de Arte de Lima articulated – part of – the dynamic art scene in terms of art production and curatorial work, and through the MALI Contemporáneo program, its auctions, and its acquisition committee of contemporary art became the leading institutional actor. I have explained how the museum fostered the figure of the transnational collector and worked as a learning apparatus and as a means of articulation with the international sphere. Moreover, I have suggested that with the reinforcement of the museum in this period there is a change in the institution of the museum in which the curatorial authority and narrative prevailed over other forms of museum practice, and art collectors started to have a growing and eager participation on museum boards and in the museum practice.

I have shown how the Museo de Arte de Lima and its actors intersect with other Latin American agents in the repositioning of Latin American art abroad, where abroad principally means Euro-America. I have shown how networks of agents expand the mobilisation of art and agents and have reinforced the notion and more over the agency of Latin America. My work, however, has pointed out that there are previous agendas of internationalisation and explained the particularities of this moment based on multilevel participation in the region and in the mainstream. I have made visible the participation of Latin American collectors as <span id="page-236-0"></span>trustees on top museums' boards and in supporting the creation of new curatorial posts in central institutions with the consequent mobilisation of Latin American curators. I have also shown, through my analysis of the Pinta Latin American Art Show, how the networking of Latin American art at a market-curatorial level and its mobilisation towards central nodes such as New York, London and Miami take place. I have exposed how to be global is not only an endeavour to achieve international participation but requires simultaneous work on multiple scales – at local sites, regional platforms and mainstream nodes. This process is a crucial factor that has enabled a growing international awareness of Latin American art and its art scenes.

The third period that I have identified is of the rise of the market in Lima. I have traced the growth of the market in relation to a new urban lifestyle and the promotion and management of Lima as a creative city with the celebration of diversity and the production of the multicultural image. I have shown how this repackaging of culture based on market-governmental logics of branding correlates with and mutually influences the growth of the art market, in which new urban dwellers look for distinguished objects for their new urban life status. I have explained how the rise of the interior designers driven by this select urban life competes with art gallerists in terms of access to artists and guides of taste. In this situation, I have shown how galleries have reorganised their strategies with increasing participation at a regional level. The growth of the art market became materialised and further promoted by the simultaneous launch of PArC and Art Lima. By looking at the tensions between these art fairs, I have demonstrated that the traditional art elite has supported PArC which operates through the global art project, while individual actors and the right-wing press supported Art Lima, based on the perspective of the creative city and the promotion of Peru. I have explained how these art fairs work primarily on a regional scale and that their disputes have activated alliances across the region, showing how both art fairs reinforce similar bodies, and protocols of art with the aim to be the leading art fair and part of the regional, or even larger, art circuits.

In other words, the three different moments that I have identified in the configuring of the Lima's contemporary art scene into its current form stand in relation to how different individual and institutional actors come into play and activate the art scene: the today's Lima's contemporary art scene has emerged through a process of artistic and curatorial explorations; of institutionalisation and networks; and of the rise of the art market. As I have argued the force of the art market tend to nullify previous processes of formation, which this book has intended to shed light on.

These three moments also stand in relation to a change of project in addressing and imagining social interrelationship and the city – from expectations and dreams of an intercultural dialogue, to a project of multicultural visibility based on creativity, in which the political utopia has given place to the celebration of culture. With this I do not say that the intercultural proposal is finished, but rather that it has lost significance in the public discussion. Just as a final example: after the creation of the Ministry of Culture in 2010 which opened with a section <span id="page-237-0"></span>for intercultural affairs, the then Minister of Culture Diana Alvarez Calderon announced that interculturality was as an anthropological concern, while she called for investment in culture (*El Comercio*, 22 December 2013).

These three moments are also marked by different relations towards global conditions. In the first moment, globalisation is mainly a topic of discussion and aspiration, and there is an attempt to attain it through the biennial form; while later there are active agents, particularly transnational art collectors, who have the financial and social means to enlarge and facilitate the regional and larger assemblages of the art scene while fostering the mobilisation of art and agents. It is this moment when the region itself has grown as an actor that mediates the mobilisation of art and articulates local efforts to act in the 'global' scenario: this is still the Euro-American mainstream. This moment of larger assemblages has brought new curatorial, academic and journalistic attention to different art worlds, which have been placed as 'new' art worlds.

These moments of the reconfiguring of the Lima art scene are also marked by a transformation from an art linked to political processes and specific contexts to an art linked to the political and the contextual as a recurrent topic: as a formula. The art linked to social issues and social interrelationships – in its last social reengagement of Peruvian art – was triggered in a moment of transition and renovation of the city and the country, and with the possibility of experimenting with new technology and different mediums. With an innovative, transgressive and politicalcontextual work, this art would impact locally and permit its mobilisation abroad. However, the mainstream's expectation for Latin American art – and for other post-colonial Third World countries – as dealing with political and social issues has become a *tropo* of otherness, draining its strength. For some artists – those of the mainstream – this turn to the social and the contextual is an option: for Latin American art has been a 'must'.

In this reinforcement of the political and the contextual, the value of anthropology has also changed, and in this book the moments of the changing relationship between art and anthropology for this specific case have been exposed. I wish to add that in the early 2000s, when I started to teach anthropology of art in the art department at La Católica University, a compulsory but minor course in terms of credits and schedule (Monday 8 am to 10 am), students, as many others, had very little idea of anthropology; although the work of many started to be close to anthropological practices and debates, while some young curators approached anthropology to look for forms of analysis, as I have explained. Later, with the reinforcement of this strand in the arts, and also with the need of higher accreditation to those who teach, some artists have opted to pursue La Católica's Visual Anthropology MA Programme that opened in 2009. However, the shift in the value of anthropology for the arts from being its link to 'other' art forms, such as 'primitive art' and '*arte popular*'*,* and its exclusion, and self-exclusion, from explaining mainstream art practices mainstream art practices, to today being recognised as a provider of methodological and conceptual tools for artists and curators in their approach to specific contexts and to being asked to comment on contemporary art still needs further reflection and less enchantment.

<span id="page-238-2"></span>I hope that I have achieved explaining what happened between the statement of the previous Museo de Arte de Lima's director that 'Peruvian art is not on the map'in 2007 (*Peru 21*, 19 June 2007) and the sense today that Peru *is* on the map (see pp. 187). I want to conclude this book by addressing 'this map' and others topographical metaphors such as 'new geographies of art,' 'the rise of new art worlds,' or the 'global art map,' which circulate as part of the current language of contemporary art. These notions celebrate a new order that is announced to have taken place in the arts: one in which the Euro-American art world is decentred and various interconnected art worlds have arisen providing multiple centralities and narratives of what contemporary art is. Hans Belting, Andrea Buddensieg and Peter Weibel (2013) promote the understanding of this new condition as 'global art,' a proposal that has had a great impact in museums and amongst art historians and curators. The proliferation of multiple biennials in different part of the world, the growth of the art market, and the major museum programmes and politics to expand their collections to include those 'new art worlds', result as part of the overarching evidence provided for these scholars and practitioners. Moreover, the anthropologist Thomas Fillitz adopts the perspective of 'global art' in his analysis of contemporary art in West Africa and questions the lack of involvement of anthropology with this perspective and debates (Fillitz 2013: 300; 2015). I engage with these debates but stand at a critical distance to what this concept suggests and add to Terry Smith's (2013) criticism of it.[1](#page-239-0) I found two main problems.

<span id="page-238-1"></span><span id="page-238-0"></span>The first concerns art theory and knowledge production, and asks where these cartographers stand to draw the maps and to see the rising of the 'new art worlds' – an idea that despite its 'decolonial spirit' sounds similar to the colonial discovery enterprise. These art scenes have long histories with processes of reconfiguration and formulation of art theories and narratives. The scholars that promote 'global art' discard one grand history narrative and recognise two events as developers of this discourse: the *Magiciens de la Terre* exhibition (Paris, 1989) and the second and third edition of the Havana Biennial in 1986 and 1989, which featured art from Latin America, Africa, Asia and the Middle East. But to locate the Havana Biennial as inaugurating 'global art' is to grab and dislocate this arena from a set of other key events that developed at the time in the region – such as the First Latin American Biennial of Sao Paulo in 1978, and the First Colloquium of *Arte No Objetual y Arte Urbano* in Medellin in 1981, explained in [Chapter 5](#page-171-1),[2](#page-239-1) and particularly from the continental art theory that was being produced: that of the Latin American Social Theory of Art, explained in this book. An art theory that located at the central of its analysis the arts in its broad perspective exploring the construction of its different delimitations and addressing issues of class, forms of articulations and power relations to understand the art system; in which art theory and anthropological and sociological analysis were in dialogue. The Latin American scholars already alerted of the lack of interest in the research undertaken by Third World countries on the arts, and on the contrary the immense production and attention on Euro-American literature (Eder and Lauer, 1986: 8). It then seems paradoxical to insist on decoloniality, and in the importance of region-making, disarticulating events from a network of regional production and circulation of theoretical ideas, and obscuring larger contributions.

<span id="page-239-2"></span>The second important problem I want to address is the fostering of the idea and narrative that what exists today is a multiplicity of interconnected art worlds, and the emphasis on the emergence of 'new art regions' as 'proof of formation of specific artworlds where art meets different conditions and cultural traditions (Belting and Buddensieg 2013: 28). I have highlighted the powerful process of region making in terms of platforms and networks, and the agency of the Latin American art category to mobilise current multi-scale participation [\(Chapter 5](#page-171-1)) – mentioning also previous moments of art region-making; but the proof of various 'new' art worlds and art regions should not lead to the conclusion of diversity *per se* as 'global art' does. A situated analysis considering the political economy as well as actors' practices and issues of class can take us to other scenarios.

I have shown in this book that new connections and geographies of art do not mean new art values, protocols or epistemologies *per se*. The articulation with the larger art world requires the 'emerging' art worlds to recalibrate their ways of working, talking, showing and creating value in art, and therefore to remove from the public eye local battles that have worked in the reconfiguration of specific art scenes. I have explained that to be 'on the map,' or *be seen* as part of the new geography of art, implies not only the reorientation of the scale but also the recalibration of its forms: adjusting to 'global' standards in order to have the accuracy to perform in the mainstream art circuits and for its arenas to become nodes of transnational art trajectories. While different narratives of art – 'other histories' – are being addressed, similar protocols are being spread; and the celebration of 'global art' facilitate this expansion. Arjun Appadurai calls us to see not only the forms of circulation but also the circulation of forms (2013: 64). The frames and forms through which articulations happen and circulations take place are too narrow. What we are witnessing is less about 'the global' and the multiplicity, and more about the expansion and connection of the upper class power (Harvey 2006) in different parts of the globe – usually dominant groups linked to Western forms and neoliberalising projects, and who in Latin America tend to be of European descent – who are playing a crucial role in the transnational articulation of art scenes and transformation of the cities through art projects. The art map has expanded: Latin American art, Peruvian art and the Lima art scene are now 'on the map,' but it is still a transnational white elite urban map, despite the narratives on the political and indigeneity that many of the Latin American art scenes forge today.

### **Notes**

- <span id="page-239-0"></span>[1](#page-238-0) Terry Smith whose contribution is included in the Belting et al. volume, affirms that the diversity currently seen in contemporary art circuits is not 'as some claim, best understood as "global art", a "world art", or a "geoaesthetics"' and he suggests to see 'the contemporaneousness of different kind of contemporary art'(the contemporanousness of their awareness of being on the world) and the 'multiscalar layering of worlds' (Smith 2013: 188).
- <span id="page-239-1"></span>[2](#page-238-1) Miguel López offers a similar critique to this first point in his book *Robar la Historia*, warning to avoid anachronism and discontinuities in the art history discourse by picking and reordering events (2017: 22).
